HEADLINE: MARKET PLACE;
Amid the Slump in Housing, A Rally in Builders' Stocks

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS

 BODY:
Housing starts are at their lowest levels since 1982, and home builders are suffering, with bankruptcies expected among the weakest of them. There is talk of a real estate bust that in magnitude will rival the post-World War II boom.
But, in the face of that stream of bad news, the stock prices of home builders are rising sharply. In the last month, an index of shares of home building companies has climbed 28 percent. Many investors, it would appear, do not believe the gloomiest of the forecasts. 
 The rise in prices has an obvious explanation. Interest rates are coming down, as the Federal Reserve tries to end the recession that most economists say began some time this year. Lower interest rates traditionally presage a revival both in building and in buying, but housing starts have lagged and the general consensus is that they will continue to do so, as prices fall and scared banks hold off from making mortgage loans.
"Quarterly earnings will be awful," said Barbara Allen, the housing analyst at Kidder, Peabody, referring to profits for both the current quarter and for the first three months of 1991. She added that some privately held builders were likely to be forced into bankruptcy.
But she said that by spring, home buying is likely to rise, and the stock prices of the leading public companies will take off. She is recommending every home builder she follows.
The prices of most housing stocks bottomed at the end of October, but did not start to rally rapidly until a month later. The upturn has been led by some of the largest home builders, especially those with solid balance sheets.
Such companies are the ones most likely to benefit from an increase in demand simply because they still have access to the banks and can get credit to step up building, Ms. Allen said in an interview yesterday.
"The money may not be available to most of their competitors," she said, noting that the decline in credit availability began in the second quarter of this year for most builders, as regulators put pressure on banks to mark down problem loans and improve the quality of new loans.
Even with the fall in construction, Ms. Allen said, most major builders will show profits for the current quarter, although the earnings will be sharply below those of a year ago.
The Lennar Corporation, a major builder in Florida, has been the star performer since Oct. 29, when an index of home building stocks bottomed at 48 percent of its level at the start of 1990. Lennar shares are up 75 percent since then, although the price fell 37.5 cents, to $16.375, yesterday.
Florida's housing market has suffered as the recession in the Northeast makes it more difficult for people there to sell homes and buy new ones in Florida. But Ms. Allen noted that Lennar has one of the strongest balance sheets in the industry, with debt making up only 29 percent of capital at the end of the third quarter.
Lennar, like almost every other builder, continues to sell well below book value, which is almost $27 a share in Lennar's case. The bearish case has held that book values will come down as home builders are forced to write down the values of unsold houses and, most importantly, the value of real estate awaiting development. But share buyers evidently have decided that risk is worth taking.
The index, which includes 10 home builder stocks and is weighted by market capitalization, is up 41 percent since Oct. 29, but is still off 31 percent since the end of last year.
The second-best market performer has been the Kaufman & Broad Home Corporation, the largest builder of houses in California, which slipped 12.5 cents, to $9.25, yesterday but has risen 64 percent since the index bottomed. Other stellar performers over the period, both up more than 50 percent, are the Ryland Group and the Centex Corporation.
In the 1981-82 recession, Ms. Allen noted, the best time to buy housing stocks was just as the trend in housing construction, measured on a year-over-year basis, turned up. Such a turn may have occurred in November, when housing starts were reported at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.13 million units. That was 16 percent below the level of the previous November, but it was up from October's rate of 1.03 million, which was off 27 percent from a year earlier.
The rapid rise of the stocks recently would seem to indicate a belief on the part of buyers that this recession will not be that different from previous ones and that the Federal Reserve's efforts to force down interest rates will succeed in reviving the economy before too much damage is done. And the buying is a bet that the speculation in land and housing prices did not drive prices so high that the pain from the drop in prices has only just begun.

HEADLINE: Couples Honor Commitment With Gifts to Neediest Cases

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ

 BODY:
Several weeks after their engagement party, Michelle Flacks and Steven Rosenbaum sent $57 to The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund. "We don't need much to live on," the two wrote. "We've already got each other."
The couple, who live in the Bronx, said they had received such lovely gifts that they "had to share their good fortune with others whose need is so much greater." In their letter to the fund, they wrote that they hoped their gift would start a long tradition of giving to charity. 
 For many couples beginning life together -- as well as for those who have celebrated their golden anniversaries -- a contribution to the Neediest Cases honors the commitment that a husband and wife have to each other by sharing with those who are less fortunate.
The 79th annual appeal for the Neediest Cases began last month. The proceeds are distributed to seven charities to aid the sick and the poor. All of the campaign's overhead costs are paid by The New York Times.
 
'An Important Symbol'
 Among the more than 4,500 organizations and people who have donated to the fund this year are dozens who see it as the perfect wedding or anniversary present. In several interviews, donors said that a gift to charity, which expresses the ability to think of others, is an ideal symbol of the love that two married people hold for one another.
Lisa Limpel-Sander, 31 years old, and her husband Elliot, 34, of Forest Hills, Queens, decided to donate to the Neediest several months after he proposed to her over hot chocolate in a lodge in upstate New York. The first gift that the couple received -- a check for $75 -- was passed along to the fund.
"The gift came out of the blue from a close friend, and it was an important symbol for us," Mrs. Limpel-Sander said in a telephone interview yesterday. "We wanted to infuse the tone of our marriage with the spirit of giving."
Both of the Sanders work in New York City -- she as a lawyer and he as a deputy commissioner for the state's Division of Housing and Community Renewal. They are well aware of the city's problems with poverty. They said their engagement had made them feel more strongly about giving something back to the city in which they were raised.
"When you love somebody, you learn about focusing outward," Mrs. Limpel-Sander said. "It is a natural extension of that love to want to express the feeling beyond your husband or wife, but also to friends and to the community."
 
'Give Something Back'
 Another donor, Leslie Van Orsdel Eddy, 30, said her recent gift of $50 was an extension of her wedding vows. She and her husband, John, 31, were married in Greenwich, Conn., in May, and she said they had chosen readings from the Bible that talked about concern for others -- not "mushy stuff" about love.
"We felt that the marriage was not just about us," she said. And in the letter attached to their gift, the couple wrote: "Life has given us so much -- we'd like to give something back, however small. In this, our first year of marriage, we're trying to establish life patterns of service to others."
Not all of the presents honoring marriage were from newlyweds.
Dorothy Anger sent $100 to honor the 50th anniversary of her parents, Henry and Shirley Goldberg of Merrick, L.I.
"They have been blessed with so much throughout their married life -- both materially and spiritually," Ms. Anger, 48, wrote. "I would like to think that this gift in some way will allow them to share those riches with someone of less fortune."
Ms. Anger said she had personally given to the Neediest Cases for decades but that this year she was inspired by the love between her father, 91, and her mother, 81.
"This gift represents the fullness in their life and the hope that it can be passed along to someone else," she said.
 
HOW TO AID FUND
 Checks should be made payable to The New York Times Neediest Cases
 Fund and sent to P.O. Box 5193, General Post Office, New York, N.Y. 10087, or to any of these organizations:

HEADLINE: Start of Recession at Issue

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
The National Bureau of Economic Research said today that it would probably identify a month between June and September 1990 as the beginning of a recession.
The private research organization is used by the Government as the official arbiter of dates for peaks and troughs in the business cycle. 
 The bureau's Business Cycle Dating Committee conferred in a conference telephone call on Friday, after several of the committee's members suggested that a recession had already begun.
One committee member, Geoffrey Moore, director of international business cycle research at Columbia University, had said he believed a recession started in June.
But another committee member, Victor Zarnowitz, professor emeritus of economics at the University of Chicago, said in an interview that opinion was divided among the seven committee members as to when a recession had begun.
Based on third-quarter gross national product statistics, Mr. Zarnowitz said, a recession did not appear to have started in the third quarter. But monthly economic data pointed to a downturn beginning then, he said. Annualized G.N.P. growth registered 1.4 percent last quarter.
Mr. Zarnowitz said committee members agreed that in all likelihood, a recession had begun, but they were not absolutely sure. The dating committee is unlikely to confer again before the release of fourth-quarter G.N.P. data on Jan. 25, he said.
The bureau defines a recession as "a recurring period of decline in total output, income, employment and trade, usually lasting from six months to a year and marked by widespread contractions in many sectors of the economy."

HEADLINE: STANDOFF IN THE GULF;
SHIP WITH IRAQ AID IS SEIZED OFF OMAN

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: RIYADH, Saudi Arabia, Dec. 26

 BODY:
An Iraqi cargo ship carrying more than 50 self-described pacifists and tons of food bound for Iraq was seized in the Arabian Sea early this morning after crew members tried to grab the weapons of a boarding party made up of American and Australian sailors, Defense Department officials said.
The incident near the Omani island of Masira appeared to be the most serious to result from the international blockade imposed on Iraq after its invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2. 
 A group, the Arab Women's Union, organized the trip more than a month ago, saying its aim was to deliver milk and medicine to Iraqi children suffering as a result of the embargo.
Pentagon officials said the American sailors had to use stun grenades, which emit smoke and a loud blast, to restore order on the 11,300-ton Iraqi vessel, the Ibn Khaldoon.
 
Crewmembers Lunge for Arms
 The Pentagon said no one was seriously hurt in the scuffle, although the ship's captain had initially asserted that a Swedish woman asserted that she had suffered a heart attack and that two Iraqi women traveling on the ship said they had miscarriages as a result of the incident.
American officials said a Navy doctor boarded the Iraqi vessel this morning and determined that the Swede suffered from a chronic heart problem and did not require emergency care and found no evidence that the two Iraqi women had miscarried or were otherwise seriously hurt.
At a news conference in Riyadh, the Saudi capital, a Pentagon spokesman, Lieut. Col. Gregg Pepin, said the ship, which had a crew of 42, stopped only after shots were fired across its bow.
When a boarding party landed on the ship by helicopter and moved toward the pilothouse, several unarmed members of the ship's crew lunged for the weapons carried by American sailors, Colonel Pepin said.
 
Medical Supplies Found
 One of the American or Australian sailors then fired his gun into the air as a warning, while comrades threw the stun grenades to restore order, according to the account.
Pentagon officials said the ship was found to be carrying tons of rice, milk, sugar and cooking oil, all of which are prohibited for export to Iraq under the United Nations embargo. It also carried what one Pentagon official described as "a relatively small amount of medical supplies" for Iraq. Medical supplies are exempted from the United Nations sanctions.
After the boarding, the ship's master agreed to unload his cargo at the port of his choice outside Iraq, a Navy spokesman said.
The passenger list was not available, and it was not clear whether all of the 250 passengers reported to be aboard the Ibn Khaldoon were associated with the Arab Women's Union.

HEADLINE: Prices for Oil Climb Above $27 as Threat of War Grows

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Crude oil futures prices posted their biggest gains in three weeks yesterday, rising above $27 a barrel on increasing fears of war in the Middle East.
Contracts for home heating oil, unleaded gasoline and natural gas also rose sharply in subdued New York Mercantile Exchange trading after a four-day Christmas weekend. 
 Crude oil for February delivery rose $1.30, to $27.22 a barrel. It was the biggest single-day gain since a $1.51-a-barrel jump on Dec. 4.
 
Off in 3 Previous Sessions
 Oil market strategists said increasing tension between the United States and Iraq since the market closed last Friday, after three straight daily declines, helped to lift prices.
"I think the market is starting to reinternalize the fact that war seems very, very likely at this point," said Peter Beutel, an oil analyst with the Pegasus Econometric Group in Hoboken, N.J.
President Saddam Hussein of Iraq said yesterday that the American Army would be defeated in any war because it does not have "right on its side."
In an interview on Mexican television, Mr. Hussein also said he would not allow the United States to set a date for talks. The United Nations has set a Jan. 15 deadline for the withdrawal of Iraqi forces from Kuwait.
The Navy said that American sailors, backed up by sailors from other nations enforcing a United Nations trade embargo, fired warning shots yesterday when crew members and passengers on an Iraqi cargo ship tried "to hold the boarding party members back and grab their weapons." Traders said other American and British reports also increased the prospects for war.
"All those things underscore what people are fearing: They're not moving any closer to any resolution by Jan 15," said Randall K. Rothenberg, a broker with Dean Witter Reynolds.
The market discounted the importance of an Israeli newspaper report -- later denied by the State Department -- that a date for talks had been set.
The oil market opened sharply higher, following the gains in trading in London on Monday after Mr. Hussein threatened to attack Israel first if Iraq were a target.
Volume yesterday was light, however, with less than half the usual number of crude oil contracts changing hands, traders said. Contracts for delivery in later months rose.
 
Oil Products Also Advance
 Traders last week sold contracts to hedge against sudden changes in the gulf situation over the holiday weekend. In addition, traders said a belief that diplomatic efforts to solve the crisis might bear fruit had depressed crude oil prices in recent weeks.
If war breaks out in the Persian Gulf, oil prices are expected to rise sharply because of anticipated limited supplies from the region.
Technical factors and cold weather around the country pushed up home heating oil futures prices.

HEADLINE: A Big, Sprawly Bank Shapes Up

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
Earlier this month, Marvin Davis, the oilman turned stock speculator, wrote to the management of First Interstate Bancorp. Demanding that the company shape up or put itself up for sale, he said First Interstate's shareholders were distressed with the company's "deteriorating financial condition and results of operations over the past few months."
The only problem with Mr. Davis's message was its timing. First Interstate still has a host of problems, and its depressed stock price may yet attract an unwanted takeover bid. But rather than deteriorating in recent months as many other banks have, the company has shown signs of a turnaround under its new chairman, Edward M. Carson. 
 By shrinking First Interstate from $59 billion in assets to $53 billion, raising money in a stock offering earlier this year and retaining earnings, First Interstate has substantially improved its capital position. Its level of bad loans and real estate on which it has foreclosed is declining although still high. The company stopped borrowing money in the form of commercial paper and has instead raised more in the form of stable deposits. And unlike last year, the company has been profitable every quarter this year, thanks in part to the absence of any huge write-downs from problem loans.
"We see a slow but steady pattern of improvement developing," said Thaddeus Paluszek, an analyst at Kidder, Peabody & Company. "We are not saying the company is totally out of the woods. But dealing with the problems will in my opinion be manageable, and we are not expecting more of the kinds of huge loan-loss provisions that have battered earnings and shattered investor confidence."
Mr. Carson, 61 years old, who has spent his entire career with First Interstate, succeeded Joseph J. Pinola as head of the company on June 1. Mr. Pinola built First Interstate into the nation's 10th-largest bank holding company through acquisitions in the West and Southwest, including an ill-timed push into Texas in 1988 that has plagued it ever since.
The company Mr. Pinola built has the tremendous advantage of having more than 1,000 retail branches spread through 14 states, giving First Interstate the ability to raise vast sums at relatively low cost in the form of consumer deposits.
But its network of banks has also been hobbled by inefficiencies, with many of the bank subsidiaries having little more in common with their counterparts in other states than the First Interstate name. And geographic sprawl has proved more a curse than a blessing for the company in recent years; while its fortunes have not been tied to the economy of any one state, they have suffered from problems in Texas and Arizona and are now threatened by a downturn in California.
Mr. Pinola began the process of mopping up the mistakes and transforming First Interstate, as industry executives put it, from a company of banks into a banking company. But it is Mr. Carson who must prove the company can live up to its potential.
 
Moves in Recent Months
 In recent months, Mr. Carson has started or accelerated moves to cut First Interstate's costs, trim the company's size, root out and deal with bad loans, force the bank subsidiaries to work more closely with each other and build capital, which is the financial buffer against losses.
After losing $124.5 million last year, primarily because of huge reserves it was forced to set aside to account for troubled loans in Texas and Arizona, First Interstate has been steadily profitable this year, with net income of $377.7 million through the first three quarters.
The level of capital -- tangible common equity as a percentage of assets -- rose to 3.89 percent in the third quarter, from 2.45 percent in the fourth quarter last year. And nonperforming assets -- loans not being repaid and foreclosed real estate -- fell to $1.88 billion in the third quarter, from $2.21 billion a year earlier.
Even so, earnings are still being held down by the level of problem loans and foreclosed real estate. The company calculates that the $1.9 billion of bad loans and real estate on its books costs it $700 million a year in administrative and carrying costs and forgone interest income.
First Interstate's share price, which had been sliding for a year, plummeted this summer along with other bank stocks, reflecting worries about the national economy and weakening real estate markets, but it has since recovered. [It closed Wednesday at $25.125, down 25 cents but up from its low several months ago of $15.625. That increase is larger than the gains of most other major bank stocks since the summer.]
Mr. Carson has won a degree of grudging praise from First Interstate's largest institutional shareholders, many of whom have grown hostile to the Los Angeles-based company over the years as repeated promises of improvement were followed by further shocks like last year's big loan-loss provisions.
"Carson seems to be doing a pretty good job under the circumstances," said John B. Neff, who manages the Windsor Fund, a major holder of First Interstate shares.
The question, analysts say, is whether Mr. Carson can improve First Interstate's bottom line and stock price sufficiently and fast enough to avoid a revolt among the company's largest shareholders that would lead to a sale of the company. The company's biggest holders include Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, the leveraged-buyout firm, which owns just under 10 percent, and Sanford C. Bernstein & Company, the Wall Street investment house, which owns just over 10 percent.
So far those institutions have been patient. But as Donald K. Crowley, an analyst at Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc., put it, the heavy concentration of stock in the hands of a few owners is "gasoline spilled around waiting for a match." Analysts said the letter from Mr. Davis, who has not disclosed his stake in the company, was clearly an attempt -- so far unsuccessful -- to muster support from other big holders for a sale of the company.
 
Question of Time
 "I see a proactive effort on the part of Carson and the people around him to work down existing problems and deal with any new ones up front," Mr. Crowley said. "The real issue is whether they will be given enough time. There are a lot of discontented shareholders around who have held that stock for a long time."
In recent years there has been widespread speculation that one of the other major California banking companies -- the Security Pacific Corporation, Wells Fargo & Company and even the BankAmerica Corporation, which Mr. Pinola tried and failed to acquire four years ago -- might be interested in an acquisition of all or part of First Interstate. But Mr. Carson probably has a little breathing room because the other big banks are preoccupied with their own problems as the economy slows, real estate markets crumble and regulators get tougher.
"Now is not a very good time to break up First Interstate," Mr. Neff said.
 
The Immediate Wild Card
 The immediate wild card in the company's future is the California economy, and specifically the ability of homeowners and commercial and residential real estate developers to weather a downturn. Mr. Carson has been warning since this summer that First Interstate's loan troubles would grow if the situation deteriorated substantially in California, which is by far the company's biggest market.
"But we don't see a major hit like Arizona," Mr. Carson said, referring to the big write-down on loans in that state last year. "We think all of our credit problems are manageable."
In an interview at the company's headquarters here, Mr. Carson did not rule out being interested in a merger offer, although he said one was unlikely in the current environment. For now, he said, "We need to sort out those things that are truly making us money and those that are not, to sort out those work procedures that might have worked in another era but that today are inefficient."
 
Effort to Improve Efficiency
 Because the company's bank subsidiaries are all separately chartered and must adhere to different state laws and regulatory requirements, the network will never operate as efficiently as if it were a single bank. But Mr. Carson has been pushing harder to eliminate duplication of management and back-office functions and to encourage heads of subsidiaries to take more responsibility for the performance of the entire corporation.
One example is the company's data-processing operations. First Interstate previously operated nine data-processing centers, each with its own computers, operating systems, budgets and staffs. The company is now paring the number to two, hoping to save $40 million annually.
Mr. Carson has also eliminated some layers of corporate management and pushed more responsibility to executives in subsidiaries. The banks in Montana and Idaho now report to the bank in Washington State rather than to corporate headquarters. The First Interstate bank in Oklahoma reports to the bank in Texas.
At the same time, Mr. Carson has made the chief executives of the six largest banks in the system members of a corporate management committee, with their compensation tied both to the performance of their banks and to that of the company as a whole. Each of the executives has also been given responsibility for overseeing a particular corporate function. James Curran, the head of the First Interstate Bank of Denver, is in charge of all data-processing for the company.

HEADLINE: Pacific Telesis Quits Real Estate

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
The Pacific Telesis Group announced yesterday that it was leaving the real estate business and would take a $60 million after-tax charge against its fourth-quarter earnings to offset expected losses on property sales.
The decision is the latest recognition of losses by a regional Bell company that diversified into industries very different from the provision of local telephone service, only to find that its new investments were far more vulnerable to an economic slowdown. 
 "What it shows is they ought to stay at home, ringing up the revenues from local telephone service," said Jack B. Grubman, a telephone industry analyst at Paine Webber.
 
Plan to Invest Proceeds
 Pacific Telesis will sell its entire $475 million portfolio of office buildings and industrial sites over the next several years and invest the proceeds in telecommunications services, particularly wireless telephone businesses overseas, Gerald A. Beatty, a company spokesman, said. The company had said in March that it would not invest any more money in real estate and would begin reducing its holdings gradually.
Mr. Beatty said Pacific Telesis now believed it could earn better returns in telecommunications than in real estate.
The BellSouth Corporation disclosed on Dec. 7 that it had taken a $30 million loss on the sale of its computer-leasing business. The Bell Atlantic Corporation announced on Oct. 26 that it would take a onetime charge of $40 million to reflect the shrinking market value of its non-telephone financial services, which include equipment leasing and lending to other companies and real estate projects.
U S West Inc. said earlier this year that it would seek to sell its $1.4 billion real estate portfolio within five to seven years. Poor returns on investment and volatility in the real estate market prompted the decision, Sharon K. Price, a company spokeswoman, said.
Although no charges against earnings have been taken, the weakening economy is now making it hard to sell the properties, she said, so that it may take more than seven years to sell them all.
"It looks like we'll be in the landlord business for a while," Ms. Price added.
 
Structure Has Changed
 A few Wall Street analysts, notably those at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, have been warning that the local telephone business is much more vulnerable to setbacks now than in previous economic downturns. The structure of the telephone industry has changed, and the seven regional Bell companies, which were created by the breakup of the American Telephone and Telegraph Company in 1984, have invested in a variety of industries that offer potentially higher returns than traditional local phone service, but are also more risky.
More local phone concerns, like the New York Telephone Company, are charging customers by the call or by the minute of calling, instead of charging a fixed rate for unlimited calling. Some analysts warn that while very few consumers disconnected their phones to save money during previous recessions to save money, they may forgo some local calls in the next economic downturn to trim their phone bills.
A third of the regional Bell companies' revenues now consists of fees from long-distance carriers for carrying calls from customers' homes and businesses to the nearest long-distance switching center. The number of long-distance calls has historically been more sensitive than the number of local calls to the health of the nation's economy, so that these revenues may grow very slowly in a downturn.

HEADLINE: Stocks Rise Slightly in Sluggish Trading

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
Stocks moved slightly higher in the wake of stronger bond prices and lower interest rates yesterday, as traders ignored the biggest gain in oil prices in three weeks.
In the stock market, activity picked up a little but remained sluggish because many buyers and sellers were taking a long holiday. Program trading continued light. 
 Food, drug and utility stocks were strong, while technology stocks were mixed.
Oil shares advanced strongly after George F. Friesen, oil analyst for the Deutsche Bank Capital Group in New York, was quoted as forecasting that fourth-quarter profits at Chevron, British Petroleum, Phillips Petroleum, Texaco and Unocal would "more than double."
Asked why Texaco was so strong -- it had jumped 2 1/4 at one point during the day -- Constantine Fliakos, oil analyst for Merrill Lynch, said he thought the profit forecast was "very conservative."
Blue-chip stocks, as represented by the Dow Jones industrial average, advanced 15.84 points, to 2,637.13. But in the broader market only 774 stocks were ahead and 639 declined. Smaller stocks, as represented by the Nasdaq composite index, slipped slightly, by 0.01, to 372.40.
 
Volume at 78.73 Million Shares
 Post-holiday trading rose to a weak 78.73 million shares, from 57.2 million the day before Christmas, when trading ended two hours early for the first time since 1975.
Thirty-six stocks fell to new lows, while 11 rose to new highs.
Three of the 10 most-active Big Board stocks yesterday were oil shares, with Exxon climbing 1/2, to 51 3/4, and Texaco rising 1 3/4, to 60 1/2. Mesa Limited Partnership fell 1/8, to 2 5/8, a little more than a week after Seagull Energy called off a $350 million deal to buy natural gas properties when it failed to get financing.
Chevron rose 2, to 73 1/4, partly after Pennzoil, which already owns 9 percent, said the Internal Revenue Service had extended its tax deadline to buy more until May 31. Pennzoil, which rose 1 3/8, to 65, is using the proceeds from its 1988 lawsuit against Texaco to buy the stock.
British Petroleum advanced 1, to 76 5/8; Phillips 5/8, to 26 7/8, and Unocal 3/8, to 27 3/8.
ARCO rose 1 7/8, to 124 3/8; Mobil 1 1/8, to 58 1/4; Burlington Resources 1, to 38 1/8, and Oryx Energy 7/8, to 36 1/2.
With oil prices down from their levels during the fall, despite yesterday's increase, transportation stocks edged a little higher, with UAL rising 2, to 115, although it had jumped to 117 earlier in the day. UAL said it had not announced anything and knew of nothing to account for the gain.
Tiffany slid 4 3/8, to 37 1/4, after reporting an 8 percent holiday sales gain. David Williamson of Advest cut his earnings estimate, saying Wall Street had expected a 15 percent rise.
Birinyi Associates, which monitors program trading, said that one lone selling wave late in the session cut the Dow blue chips a little more than 2 points.
Except for a brief drop in the first hour, the Dow rose briskly for most of the day, with bargain hunters pushing the market ahead more than it might if trading activity were heavier. By 2:47 P.M., and for the next 16 minutes, program selling helped move the market lower.

HEADLINE: Korean Entrepreneurs Find Their Dream Is Tarnishing

BYLINE: By RICHARD LEVINE

 BODY:
Amid glistening greens and pyramids of produce in the small store in Flushing, Queens, that he hoped would insure his family's prosperity, Sun Yong Back assesses a future that has suddenly dimmed. The odds seem as long as his workdays.
As New York City's economy turned anemic, he says, his sales have dropped by more than 30 percent. Taxes are rising. He missed last month's rent. Mr. Back is holding on, but his time, if not his resolve, seems limited. 
 In the last decade, Koreans like Mr. Back opened stores of every description in virtually every city neighborhood, includingsome forsaken by other merchants, becoming emblems of a new immigrant entrepreneurialism.
But now, business leaders and shopkeepers say, the Korean commercial empire in New York is faltering, and Mr. Back and others talk about moving to the suburbs or to California or even back home. The business leaders say the slump is made worse by sometimes unsympathetic landlords, insensitive politicians, high taxes and oppressive regulations, but the troubles are also at least partly a consequence of Koreans' past success, since their aggressive expansion in the 1980's has in many places pitted one Korean merchant against another.
As they branched out from fruit andvegetable stands to fish stores, dry cleaners, liquor stores, nail salons and a dozen other kinds of businesses, the number of Korean-owned stores is thought to have reached a peak of about 10,000 three years ago. There are no authoritative statistics, but B. J. Sa, secretary general of the Korean Association of New York, estimated that there are perhaps 7,500 Korean-owned stores in the city now, although some put it higher.
"The Korean-American community here is now in trouble," said Sung Soo Kim, president of the Korean American Small Business Service Center of New York, which has an office around the corner from Mr. Back's store in Flushing.
All types of businesses seem to be slumping, from delis to clothing stores to stationery shops. Mr. Kim said he expected that 100 of the 1,300 Korean fruit and vegetable markets now in the city would not survive the winter. The rate at which stores are being sold between Koreans has risen to 20 percent a year, another sign of instability, Mr. Kim said. The figure might be higher still, but many stores are losing money and are not easily sold.
In response to problems in New York, Koreans are moving into the suburbs. Philip K. Y. Young, an economics professor at Pace University in Westchester County who studied Korean greengrocers in the early 1980's, said they already own many dry cleaning stores in the county and have also opened produce markets. On a recent trip to Los Angeles, Mr. Kim met many ex-New Yorkers.
Thousands of Korean merchants are doubtless still succeeding, but the difficulties that many of them are now enduring, especially given the Koreans' devotion to their stores, is a measure of the distress that some of the almost 200,000 small businesses in the city are suffering. "They're the universal standard for all small business," said Steven L. Null of the Coalition for Fair Business Rents. "There's nobody immune."
 As they opened more and more stores in the city, Koreans roiled tensions among black and Hispanic customers who sometimes found them curt or resented their apparent success.
"We don't like people praising us," says Mr. Kim, who calls the merchants' prosperous image a "mainstream myth" that obscures their exhausting workdays and, now, the struggle of many to stay in business.
To Korean greengrocers, the turn of fortunes must seem perplexing. What is a bread-and-butter business if not fruits and vegetables?
Mr. Back learned the trade working in a market in the Bronx. He opened his own store in Queens three years ago, replacing a Chinese restaurant. At first, he said, sales were robust at $3,000 a day and his store was profitable. Now he has fallen behind on the $9,000 monthly rent and faces extra late charges.
"I read in the Korean newspapers that next spring will be much better," he said, without much hope. "But I don't think so."
In a pinch, a small labor-intensive business like a fruit-and-vegetable stand has only one real option: reduce labor costs. Mr. Back said he had trimmed two people from his payroll. But that has meant even more work for him. He said he now puts in 14 or 15 hours a day, six and a half days a week, taking only Monday afternoons off.
This is not the kind of new life he hoped for seven years ago, when he left Korea. His father owned buildings there, he said, but liked America, and so the family sold its holdings at a loss and moved.
"We had enough money in Korea," Mr. Back said. "Not here."
 
Start-Up Loans
 Many Koreans, even those with high levels of education or professional training, are drawn to small businesses because of limited English. Often, they obtain start-up money from revolving credit associations formed by compatriots. They share business information and advice but, Professor Young said, have "no qualms about opening up a store across the street from each other."
Mr. Back does not think competition has much to do with his predicament, but just half a block down Roosevelt Avenue is the Sung Hoon Fruit and Vegetable Market.
A few blocks away on Main Street -- too far to be yet another competitor -- Ike Shin watches over his market from a small perch in the back. As business slowed, he, too, reduced his staff. Now he sleeps at the store on many nights and has little time with his wife and children, who live out on Long Island.
Mr. Shin said he owned a garment factory in Korea but sold it to follow his family to America. He was lonely in Korea, he said.
He used the proceeds from the sale to start the store in Flushing. It now appears to be a losing gamble. He thinks about returning home, but Korea has prospered since he left and the standard of living and prices have soared. "My country developed faster than I thought," he said.
His friends in Korea are all rich now, he went on. "If we go back, I would be a beggar," he said.
 
Criticism of Dinkins
 Mr. Back also thinks about leaving New York. He favors Korea or California, which his wife prefers. But, he adds, "I don't have the money."
So Mr. Shin and Mr. Back are both trapped by the stores that were supposed to provide them with a better life but are now adrift in the business doldrums.
The city's economic downturn is bad enough, but some Koreans feel Mayor David N. Dinkins also let them down. At a City Hall rally in September, thousands of Koreans booed the new Mayor, who they believe has been ineffective in ending the bitter boycott of two Korean stores on Church Street in the Flatbush section of Brooklyn.
The protest began in January after a Haitian-American customer said she had been assaulted by an employee of one of the stores. The store owners said the customer had refused to pay for groceries.
Mr. Kim said Mr. Dinkins had also failed to honor a pledge to push for a bill that would provide for binding arbitration of lease renewals. He said 170 Koreans had lost leases -- and businesses they built -- in a recent six-month period.
On top of that is the burden that New York City imposes on small stores through an average of 14 different taxes and a variety of sidewalk regulations that are hard for some merchants to understand.
"The sanitation men are 'kings of the street," said Mr. Kim, who recently persuaded city officials to forgive about $1.5 million in fines for 150 merchants, but still knows of one store in Brighton Beach that was cited for 270 violations in just 11 months.
But now the city's weak economy is the major problem, and it does not seem likely to improve soon. The downturn is even affecting what may be the fanciest Korean market in New York, at Park Avenue at 75th Street.
Business at the store, which opened six years ago over the objections of some neighborhood residents, is down by 20 percent to 25 percent, said the owner, Kyu Sung Choi.
"Everybody worries about next year," he said. "Everyone is crying now -- everyone."

HEADLINE: Natural Gas Use Slowed By Weather

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD

 BODY:
Winter, or the lack of it, is sending a chill through the natural gas market. And after a string of mild winters in the Northeast, slack demand is starting to erode gas prices and squeeze drillers and pipeline operators.
It was not supposed to be this way. The invasion of Kuwait made oil, gas's big competitor, double in price, and was a reminder that a growing fraction of the nation's oil is imported, while nearly all gas comes from the United States and Canada. And geologists say that unlike oil, large quantities of natural gas almost certainly await discovery in North America. 
 For residential consumers, the effect has been stable or declining prices. At Brooklyn Union Gas, the fuel charge, which represents about half the customer's total bill from the gas utility, is down 5 percent this year from last, for an annual saving of about $20 for a typical single-family house. Public Service Electric and Gas, the largest utility in New Jersey, is now refunding $80 million to its customers because the price of gas was lower last winter than expected.
The warm weather has also contributed to a drop in wholesale heating oil prices, though it is difficult to determine how much of the drop is a result of lower demand and how much to the decline in crude oil prices. The drop is oil prices came about because of higher world production and a decline in war anxiety since October.
According to the most recent report by the Energy Department, wholesale heating oil prices in the region from Washington to New York had fallen to 90.4 cents a gallon on Dec. 3 from 103.2 cents on Oct. 1. Last year at this time, the price was just over 60 cents, though the intense cold in December and early January later pushed those prices up to about $1.10.
Yesterday, crude oil futures prices posted the biggest gains in three weeks, rising $1.30, to $27.22 a barrel, on renewed fear this week of war in the Middle East. [Page D2.]
 
January Prices Slide
 Weather has been the biggest factor holding down gas demand, and with it, price. With last winter a mild one and with a warm start to the current heating season, New York is close to having the warmest year since the Government began keeping records 121 years ago. Heating requirements so far this heating season have been 22 percent lower than normal. And the rest of the Northeast, where a huge population usually faces a cold winter, has also been mild.
The weather has changed the psychology of the buyers, the producers say, keeping prices down. In a move that the Energy Department earlier this week called "dramatic," the price of natural gas for delivery in January -- usually the peak month -- slipped lower than the December price in futures trading on the New York Mercantile Exchange. February gas, now the spot month, closed yesterday at $1.999 for a thousand cubic feet, up 3.7 cents.
With gas sales in 1990 down 2.1 percent from 1989 and prices flat or down slightly, executives in the business are slouching into a wait-until-next-year mode.
But some experts see no wintry cold at the end of the tunnel. There have been so many warm winters over the last few years that as Paul R. Ferretti, a senior vice president at Dillon, Read & Company, put it, "What's abnormally warm is now the norm."
The natural gas industry was also expected to benefit from amendments to the Clean Air Act, which put a penalty on both oil and coal.
The Clean Air Act will eventually help. To meet progressively stricter deadlines in 1995 and 2000, some utilities will switch from coal to gas, because gas does not emit sulfur dioxide. Some utilities will probably modify coal furnaces to burn coal and gas in a mixture that cuts pollution sharply. And the tailpipe provisions of the new law may finally help gas crack into the transportation market, probably with buses, delivery trucks and other fleet vehicles.
 
Hurt by Weaker Economy
 But the gulf crisis has been no help to gas. Some analysts think it may have even hurt.
C. J. Lawrence, Morgan Grenfell Inc. has predicted a 1 percent shrinkage in the gross national product because of a recession brought on in part by the oil-price run-up. Its natural gas analyst, Paul J. Milbauer, said the Persian Gulf crisis would be a negative for gas. He said demand will rise to 18.4 trillion cubic feet in 1991, up from 18.1 trillion cubic feet this year. But the 18.4 trillion figure would have been higher in a stronger economy; the reason for the rise is the expectation of normal weather in 1991.
In addition, some analysts think that the price of oil will collapse when the gulf crisis ends because so many users have been building up inventories. If that happens, it could crush the price of gas.
According to Barbara Shook, associate editor of Natural Gas Intelligence, a weekly newsletter, "A bunch of independent producers were secretly wearing 'I Love Saddam' T-shirts until they realized that natural gas prices weren't going to go up along with oil."
Mr. Ferretti said, "When oil prices shot up because of the situation in Kuwait, the gas industry was not in a position to benefit." Any consuming company that could switch its fuel from oil to gas had done so long ago, he said.
But the period that led up to the Iraqi invasion was one of surplus oil production. George Baker, a natural gas analyst at Merrill Lynch, said the surplus had helped hold natural gas prices down. Another factor was the warm spring that left storage fields full of natural gas in late spring when they are normally depleted.
The industry had hoped that in January a thousand cubic feet, roughly a million B.T.U.'s, would fetch $3. Prices had been very strong in October. On the New York Mercantile Exchange at the beginning of this month, contracts for delivery in January traded at $2.45. But by last Friday, they had fallen to $2.05. And the Energy Department said on Monday that while January prices are usually the highest of the year, this year they will be about $1.70 for a thousand cubic feet, down from $1.96 in December. B.T.U.'s, or British thermal units, are often used in comparing the cost of gas, oil and electricity.
 
Pipeline Limitations
 Gas prices are far more seasonal than those for oil, but what the industry needs is sustained cold weather, not a concentrated dose of cold that raises fuel requirements briefly. In a recent study, Sanford C. Bernstein & Company found that because of pipeline limitations, January consumption has been flat for the last 10 years at about 2.3 trillion cubic feet.
Industry leaders say that events will turn the world in their direction. In a year-end statement, Michael Baly 3d, president of the American Gas Association, pointed out that several big pipeline projects were approved this year and several others were approved by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission, including the Iroquois line, which will bring gas from the Trans Canada Pipe Line to the New York City area.
A quarter-million homes converted to natural gas heat this year, mostly from oil, and the rate will rise to 300,000 homes next year, he said, nearly double the rate of recent years. The gas share of home heating has reached 62 percent, from 58 percent four years ago, Mr. Baly added.
And technical refinements will make gas-powered air-conditioners popular. These will be promoted, experts say, by combined electric-and-gas utilities whose electric systems are stretched to the limit by summer demand for cooling, while their gas systems sit idle.
In addition, analysts expect gas to capture the lion's share of the fuel market for new electric generating stations.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:
The Economy
 
There is no sign of a collapse of the financial order, banking regulators and most private economists say, despite the steady decline in the banking industry's earnings along with the assets of the Federal program that protects depositors. For now, officials do not foresee widespread failures among the nation's largest banking companies, almost all of which will post some profits. [Page A1.]
 
 The natural gas market is getting a chill from the warm weather. And after a string of mild winters in the Northeast, slack demand is starting to erode gas prices and squeeze drillers and pipeline operators. [D1.]
 
 The Korean commercial empire in New York is faltering, business leaders say, in a slump made worse by unsympathetic landlords, insensitive politicians, high taxes and oppressive regulations, but also partly by Koreans' past successes. [B1.]
 
 Insurance companies are beginning to deny health care coverage to people with genetic abnormalities, even if the defects have not caused any health problems. [B9.]
 
 Companies
 
 First Interstate Bancorp still has a host of problems, and its depressed stock price may yet attract an unwanted takeover bid. But the company has shown signs of a turnaround under its new chairman. [D1.]
 
 For Pan Am and T.W.A., the chess game continues. Pan Am's management sent a letter to Carl C. Icahn, T.W.A. chairman, seeking to clarify whether he was still willing to pursue his $375 million takeover offer. [D1.]
 
 Pacific Telesis said it was leaving the real estate business and would take a $60 million after-tax charge against its fourth-quarter earnings to offset expected losses on property sales. [D1.]
 
 Bank of New England directors agreed to continue studying a proposal to give bondholders 92 percent of the stock in exchange for much-needed capital. [D14.]
 
 Pennzoil said it had bought $90 million worth of Chevron shares since Dec. 3, increasing its stake to 9.4 percent. [D4.]
 
 The sale of MCA to Matsushita may have the side effect of forcing resolution of many difficult issues concerning commercial activities in America's national parks and other public lands. Of immediate concern is what happens to the company, now an MCA subsidiary, that controls the businesses in Yosemite. [A14.]
 
 International
 
 Selling Xerox copiers in Czechoslovakia is a study in the difficulties of doing business in a land that is in between Communism and capitalism. [D1.]
Markets
 
 The Treasury auctioned a record $12.5 billion of two-year notes at an average yield that was the lowest in almost two years. The 7.25 percent notes sold at an average yield of 7.32 percent. [D14.]
 
 Stocks moved slightly higher in the wake of stronger bond prices and lower interest rates, as traders ignored the biggest rise in oil prices in three weeks. The Dow gained 15.84, to 2,637.13. [D8.]
 
 Orange juice futures prices rose, reflecting worries that the heavy frost damage to the orange crop in California would damage juice supplies. [D15.]
 
 The dollar drifted lower in quiet post-holiday trading. Gold and silver prices rose. [D15.]
 
 Today's Columns
 
 Stock prices of home builders are rising sharply, even in the face of a stream of bad news. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]
 
Mexico's sale of a controlling stake in its telephone company, a regulatory pitfall may await the buyers. Talking Deals. [D2.]

HEADLINE: Census Bureau Places Population at 249.6 Million

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 26

 BODY:
After one of the most disputed head counts ever, the Census Bureau today put the population of the United States at 249,632,692, an increase of more than 23 million people, or 10.2 percent, over the official 1980 total.
The increase reflects almost a million overseas Federal workers, including military personnel and their dependents, who were included in the 1990 total under a new law. When they are eliminated from the count, as they were in 1980, the 1990 population comes to 248.7 million. The 1980 figure was 226.5 million. 
 While the Constitution requires that a population figure for each state be reported to the President by the end of the year for apportioning the number of representatives from each state, the Commerce Department can still decide that the count was so flawed it should be adjusted by a statistically based formula. The department, the Census Bureau's parent agency, has said that it will determine by July 15 whether to do so.
 
Power and People Shift
 The total released today is the bureau's calculation of the population as of April 1, 1990, and confirms the continuing movement of people and political power to the West and South.
The total was 3.8 million higher than the preliminary total released in August, a result both of normal census operations, which had not been completed when the early figures came out, and of recanvassing in localities where officials complained.
The changes since the preliminary figures did not alter the expected apportionment of Congressional seats. From these counts, it appears that 19 seats will shift among the states next year, with California, Texas and Florida among the eight states to gain. Thirteen states, all but three of them in the Northeast and Midwest, will lose representation. New York, which is expected to lose three seats, would be the biggest loser. [Page B6.]
The preliminary figures, which did not include 922,819 Government workers and dependents then overseas, put the population at 245.8 million; the doublechecking since then raised every state's total, particularly those with large urban populations.
Experts say that if there is a statistical adjustment of the count, one or two states among the expected losers might find their losses reduced, or even eliminated.
 
Boom-and-Bust Decade
 Today's population figures reflected both the boom-and-bust decade in energy-producing states like Texas, Louisiana and Wyoming and the renewed loss of population in rural areas, which had experienced a small comeback in the 1970's. All four of the states losing population over the decade were either rural or energy producers or both: West Virginia, which lost 148,000, (7.6 percent of its population) Iowa, which lost 126,000 people, (4.3 percent), Wyoming, which lost 14,000 (2.9 percent), and North Dakota, which lost not quite 12,000 people (1.7 percent).
The 1990 law pertaining to Americans overseas requires that people posted abroad be included in determining each state's population, and thus its Congressional representation. Because the figures announced today embodied the bureau's calculation of the population as of last April 1, the military buildup spurred by the Persian Gulf crisis had no effect on the numbers.
The sharp increase in Hispanic and Asian immigrantion over the past decade was reflected in the dramatic population gains of the states where many of these immigrant have settled. California, home to many of the Asian immigrants and a large proportion of Hispanic people, gained 6.1 million people, for a total of 29.8 million. Texas, the destination of many Mexican and Central American immigrants, gained 2.8 million people, for a total of 17 million. Florida, where South American and Caribbean immigrants tend to cluster, gained 3.2 million, to 13 million.
The figures released today drew immediate protests from officials in New York City and New York State, who predicted that the undercount in 1990 would exceed the undercount of a decade ago, when the bureau estimated that it missed about 3.2 million people. Their comments made it clear that they would continue their legal efforts to force the Commerce Department to adjust its population totals.
Officials of the Commerce Department said today that it would be premature to assess the accuracy of its figures until the evaluation of a post- census survey is completed. "It'll be early summer before we know enough to intelligently answer your question," said Michael R. Darby, Under Secretary of Commerce for Economic Affairs.
 
Looking Back to October
 The chairman of the Census Bureau's Congressional Oversight Subcommittee and a spokesman for the Democrats in state legislatures around the country -- the arenas where the reapportionment battles will be fought -- were quick to point out that as recently as October the Census Bureau's Population Division had issued a report estimating the total resident population at 253.4 million, an estimate which, if correct, would mean that the bureau's counting had missed about five million people.
Over the years, demographers and other experts have used estimates to guide their work between censuses, but they have placed more faith in the actual once-a-decade head counts.
Because of the stakes involved, no census has been free of dispute. But the political pressure has increased in recent decades as more and more Federal legislation tied aid to the states and cities to population.
"What was obvious to every American in 1990 is true: the population of the United States has become much more difficult to count," said Representative Thomas C. Sawyer, the Ohio Democrat who heads the House Census and Population Subcommittee of the Post Office and Civil Service Committee. "It is a larger, more diverse and more mobile population than it has ever been. This figure may indicate that time-honored techniques are simply not sufficient."
"At this point it's fair to say that the real question is not whether we've counted everyone," Mr. Sawyer added. "We know that the nation has never counted everybody. The real question is how close has the Census Bureau come to the truth, and can they come closer?"
 
Lawsuit Is Pending
 New York State, as well as New York City and several other major municipalities, have sued in an attempt to force the Commerce Department to use statistical sampling techniques adjusting the final population totals to reflect the presumed undercount. Historically, undercounts have affected members of minorities and inner-city residents most severely. Organizations representing black and Hispanic people have joined the suit as plaintiffs.
"When large segments of our resident are left out of the political process, democracy is devalued for all of us," New York State Attorney General Robert Abrams said in a statement released shortly after the census figures were made public. Urban officials around the country have contended in recent years that census undercounts have cheated them of both political power and millions of dollars in Federal aid that is distributed on the basis of population.
"This underscores the need for a correction," said Peter Zimroth, the New York attorney representing New York City in its lawsuit. "We're in the range of a five-million undercount. That kind of undercount is really unacceptable in a democracy that depends on the accuracy of these figures to allocate legislative power and billions of dollars."
 
'Full, Fair and Efficient'
 Mr. Darby, in a press conference at the Commerce Department, said, "I believe that we have seen here a full, fair and efficient census." Census Director Barbara Everitt Bryant, commenting on widespread local protests on the release of the preliminary totals in August, said, "Virtually every large city will find that its numbers have gone up."
Over all, the population count, including the overseas personnel, was about 1.1 percent greater than the preliminary figure of 245.8 million. But some states, like New York, saw a more pronounced increase between the preliminary figure and the final total. New York gained more than 400,000 people, or 2.4 percent. One Census bureau official said most of the gain between the August figures and those released today came in urban centers like New York City.
The only figures released today were the state-by-state totals needed to apportion Congressional representation. Population figures for some minority groups on a state-by-state basis should be available next spring. Other figures derived from the census, including the number of "unmarried partners," the figures obtained from the count of homeless people on the streets and in shelters, will not be available until late 1991 at the earliest.
Under terms of an agreement reached 18 months ago, the Commerce Department must decide by July 15 whether or not any undercount in the 1990 census will be corrected by statistical techniques to insure that urban areas and minority groups are fairly represented in the final figure.
Despite New York's protests, however, it was clear that many officials were relieved to find that, as the preliminary figures indicated, the state seems about to lose three Congressional seats, not four as some had feared. Among the other states apparently losing representation were Michigan, Pennsylvania, Illinois and Ohio, each standing to lose two seats. The three losers outside the Northern and Midwestern industrial regions were Kentucky, Louisiana and Montana.

HEADLINE: A Red Flag for New York's Bridges

 BODY:
Bridge engineers call them "red flags": structural deficiencies that can threaten public safety. Since July, state and city inspectors have identified 416 red flags on New York City bridges, raising serious questions about Mayor David Dinkins's plan to cut preventive maintenance and rebuilding.
There's no denying the city's gloomy fiscal outlook. And the case for bridges always seems less compelling than the case for pressing social needs. But that's part of the problem; bridges have no constituency. Many past mayors ignored them and the city has paid a fearful price. 
 Two years ago, New York closed the Williamsburg Bridge. That prodded City Hall into an ambitious 10-year plan to rehabilitate all structurally deficient bridges by the year 2000. Now, it turns out, the problem is worse than it seemed: all the water, salt, dirt and debris that's been collecting for so long has accelerated corrosion. The city now rates 56 percent of its bridges deficient, compared with 42 percent in the past.
Though more energetic city inspections mean fewer hazards will go undetected, near-disasters have become all too common. Earlier this month, for example, city engineers had to close three lanes of the Williamsburg Bridge; in one lane, steel girders supporting the floor beams beneath the roadway were about to collapse from corrosion. The bridge was reopened after all-night repairs, but the episode was a timely reminder of the fragile state of the city's bridges, especially its overtaxed East River crossings.
Lucius Riccio, New York City's Transportation Commissioner, freely acknowledges the terrible condition of the city's bridges, but says the situation hasn't reached "a crisis point." His confidence is puzzling; many experts in and out of government strongly disagree with him. Mr. Riccio's own Deputy Commissioner for bridges, David Steinberger, says that even without the additional cuts City Hall is now considering, his repair crews can't keep pace with the rising number of red flags.
Even in these hard economic times, it's difficult to justify scrimping on vital, inexpensive preventive maintenance like painting and salt removal. Cutting back on the rebuilding program would be no less hazardous. Today's neglect will carry a far higher price tag tomorrow.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Restore Unemployment Insurance That Works

 BODY:
To the Editor:
While "Unemployment Insurance Aiding Fewer Workers" (front page, Dec. 2) exposes the inadequacy of the unemployment insurance system in meeting the needs of the growing numbers of jobless Americans, you omit some important points.
While an average of 48 percent of the unemployed collected benefits in 1982, the figure fell to 39 percent in 1983, 37 percent in 1984 and now hovers around 33 percent, according to the Department of Labor. In 1975, the average was 69 percent.
You note stricter eligibility requirements, along with a changed work force, as major causes for the 35 percent decline in the numbers using the unemployment insurance system, but you omit another obvious cause: long-term unemployment, coupled with fewer weeks of benefits available. The drastic reduction in the number of benefit weeks from a high of 65 weeks (not 52, as you state) in 1975-77 to 26 weeks has rendered the unemployment insurance safety net increasingly irrelevant to millions of jobless Americans. More than two million workers exhausted all benefits last year; almost 200,000 in New York alone. Though these workers are counted as unemployed, they do not receive benefits.
Your assumption that sentiment against higher taxes could automatically lead to calls for further limits on unemployment benefits also ignores the basic point that wage-earning taxpayers do not pay for the unemployment insurance system. Funded by a tax on employers, unemployment insurance is considered by wage earners as an indispensable benefit and not a tax. If anything, unemployment insurance has lagged far behind other taxation. As the taxable wage base for Social Security has risen over the years from $3,000 to more than $54,000, the Federal wage base for unemployment insurance taxes has only gone from $3,000 to $7,000.
This is now the fourth recession we have had in just 15 years. The fundamental issue is society's ability to provide decent-paying, secure jobs for all able to work. But the immediate question is whether or not we can afford not to protect the jobless; the issue is priorities, not just finances.
 
KEITH BROOKS, MANNY NESS
New York, Dec. 12, 1990
 
The writers are coordinators of the New York Unemployed Organizing Committee.

HEADLINE: Restore Unemployment Insurance That Works;
March of Folly

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Unemployment Insurance Aiding Fewer Workers," you report (front page, Dec. 2). It is not, however, as you state, "because of the reluctance to raise the taxes" that the Federal unemployment insurance program, "meant to ease the pain of recession," is much less effective than it used to be. It was the elimination of the uniform Federal tax and the substitution of the right of states to set the level of the tax they imposed on businesses that started this debacle.
The states largely substituted a regressive tax on business, easing the burden on larger employers, transferring costs to smaller companies. In addition to the regressive taxes, the ceilings on taxable wages bear little relation to the benefits paid to a company's laid-off workers.
In Pennsylvania the maximum annual taxable wage is $8,000. Consider two industries suffering layoffs, garment and steel. The garment workers making $160 a week could have drawn $82 a week in unemployment insurance in 1990. The steelworkers earning $550 a week would be entitled to $181. The employers of both would contribute to the fund on the first $8,000 of wages, and their experience-rated tax percents could be the same, even though their employees received vastly different benefits.
This regressiveness and disproportion came about because the unemployment system was delegated to the states. The low level of benefits, about 35 percent of what workers were earning at their last jobs, and the limited duration of benefits also result from relegation to the states.
In other countries, a uniform national unemployment insurance system recognizes that the national economy has an impact on employment levels, with states having little influence. Benefits in most developed economies (except South Africa) are set at 60 percent or more of previous earnings, and durations of benefits are longer. Also, in other countries there is an employment system focusing on training, education, counseling and relocation, with unemployment assistance truly an adjunct to re-employment.
This interstate competition system has encouraged a bidding war. "Businesses argue," you state, "and many politicians agree, that a lower unemployment insurance tax improves the business climate of a state and makes it more attractive to companies that might wish to come there." When smaller businesses argue thus, they are being seduced by a class argument; their economic self-interest would be better served by a more equitable system -- shifting the tax burden to larger and less competitive enterprises better able to pay.
When politicians agree, they are misdiagnosing the problem and prescribing the wrong cure; if they seek job creation, they should ease the burden on smaller enterprises, which create jobs while larger ones are shedding employees.
One of the many reasons unemployment insurance is aiding fewer workers is that more workers are discouraged -- by employers, because most states have tightened eligibility requirements and because many unemployment compensation bureaucracies are employer-sympathetic and user-unfriendly. In most states it is not illegal for an employer announcing a layoff to threaten those who apply for benefits during temporary layoffs with not being recalled. Because of funds cuts many offices are understaffed, lines are long and counseling (benefit rights and vocation guidance) is nonexistent.
The Keynesian counter-cycle concept underlying the 1935 legislation -- collect taxes when times are good, pay benefits to sustain purchasing power during downturns -- is undermined by the present system. As soon as the funds build up a substantial balance, business presses for givebacks. Reserves built during periods of full employment, such as World War II, were given back to employers through refunds and credits. That benefit funds are in shaky financial condition is not an accident; it is inherent in a system that fails to treat the money as a true trust fund.
In 1985, the Pennsylvania fund faced a fiscal crisis. As part of a tripartite deal, business and labor agreed with government that they would come up with a solution: 60 percent of the deficit reduction would come from business and 40 percent from labor. When the A.F.L.-C.I.O. proposed, instead of the conventional business-political solution of cutting benefits and eligibility, to generate its share through a one-tenth of 1 percent of payroll tax, the business community balked. They did not want any worker contribution (routine in other countries) that would encourage the idea that unemployment benefits were a normative right. They pay for the system and thereby exercise decisive influence over it.
Representative Thomas J. Downey is right to say, "It's a classic economic march of folly." It may be too late to correct the situation for this recession; it's not too late to revise this nonfunctional unemployment compensation system for layoff victims and a society ill served by the present system.
 
MARTIN J. MORAND
Indiana, Pa., Dec. 17, 1990
 
The writer directs the Pennsylvania Center for Study of Labor Relations, Indiana University of Pennsylvania.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: Measuring Recession

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Holding Recession at Bay" (editorial, Dec. 3) makes important points about the need for better indicators of future economic activity. You state that the recession "has begun primarily in the service sector, for which economists have not yet developed indices of future activity."
The Columbia University Business School's Center for International Business Cycle Research developed leading and coincident indexes for service-sector activity in 1988. The leading index has predicted a slowdown in service sector growth for nearly a year. This index dropped again last October, as you reported on Dec. 1.
 
JEAN MALTZ
ANIRVAN BANERJI
New York, Dec. 14, 1990
 
The writers are economists and research associates at Columbia's Center for International Business Cycle Research.
