SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; National Desk

LENGTH: 1584 words

HEADLINE: Census Bureau Places Population at 249.6 Million

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 26

 BODY:
After one of the most disputed head counts ever, the Census Bureau today put the population of the United States at 249,632,692, an increase of more than 23 million people, or 10.2 percent, over the official 1980 total.
The increase reflects almost a million overseas Federal workers, including military personnel and their dependents, who were included in the 1990 total under a new law. When they are eliminated from the count, as they were in 1980, the 1990 population comes to 248.7 million. The 1980 figure was 226.5 million. 
 While the Constitution requires that a population figure for each state be reported to the President by the end of the year for apportioning the number of representatives from each state, the Commerce Department can still decide that the count was so flawed it should be adjusted by a statistically based formula. The department, the Census Bureau's parent agency, has said that it will determine by July 15 whether to do so.
 
Power and People Shift
 The total released today is the bureau's calculation of the population as of April 1, 1990, and confirms the continuing movement of people and political power to the West and South.
The total was 3.8 million higher than the preliminary total released in August, a result both of normal census operations, which had not been completed when the early figures came out, and of recanvassing in localities where officials complained.
The changes since the preliminary figures did not alter the expected apportionment of Congressional seats. From these counts, it appears that 19 seats will shift among the states next year, with California, Texas and Florida among the eight states to gain. Thirteen states, all but three of them in the Northeast and Midwest, will lose representation. New York, which is expected to lose three seats, would be the biggest loser. [Page B6.]
The preliminary figures, which did not include 922,819 Government workers and dependents then overseas, put the population at 245.8 million; the doublechecking since then raised every state's total, particularly those with large urban populations.
Experts say that if there is a statistical adjustment of the count, one or two states among the expected losers might find their losses reduced, or even eliminated.
 
Boom-and-Bust Decade
 Today's population figures reflected both the boom-and-bust decade in energy-producing states like Texas, Louisiana and Wyoming and the renewed loss of population in rural areas, which had experienced a small comeback in the 1970's. All four of the states losing population over the decade were either rural or energy producers or both: West Virginia, which lost 148,000, (7.6 percent of its population) Iowa, which lost 126,000 people, (4.3 percent), Wyoming, which lost 14,000 (2.9 percent), and North Dakota, which lost not quite 12,000 people (1.7 percent).
The 1990 law pertaining to Americans overseas requires that people posted abroad be included in determining each state's population, and thus its Congressional representation. Because the figures announced today embodied the bureau's calculation of the population as of last April 1, the military buildup spurred by the Persian Gulf crisis had no effect on the numbers.
The sharp increase in Hispanic and Asian immigrantion over the past decade was reflected in the dramatic population gains of the states where many of these immigrant have settled. California, home to many of the Asian immigrants and a large proportion of Hispanic people, gained 6.1 million people, for a total of 29.8 million. Texas, the destination of many Mexican and Central American immigrants, gained 2.8 million people, for a total of 17 million. Florida, where South American and Caribbean immigrants tend to cluster, gained 3.2 million, to 13 million.
The figures released today drew immediate protests from officials in New York City and New York State, who predicted that the undercount in 1990 would exceed the undercount of a decade ago, when the bureau estimated that it missed about 3.2 million people. Their comments made it clear that they would continue their legal efforts to force the Commerce Department to adjust its population totals.
Officials of the Commerce Department said today that it would be premature to assess the accuracy of its figures until the evaluation of a post- census survey is completed. "It'll be early summer before we know enough to intelligently answer your question," said Michael R. Darby, Under Secretary of Commerce for Economic Affairs.
 
Looking Back to October
 The chairman of the Census Bureau's Congressional Oversight Subcommittee and a spokesman for the Democrats in state legislatures around the country -- the arenas where the reapportionment battles will be fought -- were quick to point out that as recently as October the Census Bureau's Population Division had issued a report estimating the total resident population at 253.4 million, an estimate which, if correct, would mean that the bureau's counting had missed about five million people.
Over the years, demographers and other experts have used estimates to guide their work between censuses, but they have placed more faith in the actual once-a-decade head counts.
Because of the stakes involved, no census has been free of dispute. But the political pressure has increased in recent decades as more and more Federal legislation tied aid to the states and cities to population.
"What was obvious to every American in 1990 is true: the population of the United States has become much more difficult to count," said Representative Thomas C. Sawyer, the Ohio Democrat who heads the House Census and Population Subcommittee of the Post Office and Civil Service Committee. "It is a larger, more diverse and more mobile population than it has ever been. This figure may indicate that time-honored techniques are simply not sufficient."
"At this point it's fair to say that the real question is not whether we've counted everyone," Mr. Sawyer added. "We know that the nation has never counted everybody. The real question is how close has the Census Bureau come to the truth, and can they come closer?"
 
Lawsuit Is Pending
 New York State, as well as New York City and several other major municipalities, have sued in an attempt to force the Commerce Department to use statistical sampling techniques adjusting the final population totals to reflect the presumed undercount. Historically, undercounts have affected members of minorities and inner-city residents most severely. Organizations representing black and Hispanic people have joined the suit as plaintiffs.
"When large segments of our resident are left out of the political process, democracy is devalued for all of us," New York State Attorney General Robert Abrams said in a statement released shortly after the census figures were made public. Urban officials around the country have contended in recent years that census undercounts have cheated them of both political power and millions of dollars in Federal aid that is distributed on the basis of population.
"This underscores the need for a correction," said Peter Zimroth, the New York attorney representing New York City in its lawsuit. "We're in the range of a five-million undercount. That kind of undercount is really unacceptable in a democracy that depends on the accuracy of these figures to allocate legislative power and billions of dollars."
 
'Full, Fair and Efficient'
 Mr. Darby, in a press conference at the Commerce Department, said, "I believe that we have seen here a full, fair and efficient census." Census Director Barbara Everitt Bryant, commenting on widespread local protests on the release of the preliminary totals in August, said, "Virtually every large city will find that its numbers have gone up."
Over all, the population count, including the overseas personnel, was about 1.1 percent greater than the preliminary figure of 245.8 million. But some states, like New York, saw a more pronounced increase between the preliminary figure and the final total. New York gained more than 400,000 people, or 2.4 percent. One Census bureau official said most of the gain between the August figures and those released today came in urban centers like New York City.
The only figures released today were the state-by-state totals needed to apportion Congressional representation. Population figures for some minority groups on a state-by-state basis should be available next spring. Other figures derived from the census, including the number of "unmarried partners," the figures obtained from the count of homeless people on the streets and in shelters, will not be available until late 1991 at the earliest.
Under terms of an agreement reached 18 months ago, the Commerce Department must decide by July 15 whether or not any undercount in the 1990 census will be corrected by statistical techniques to insure that urban areas and minority groups are fairly represented in the final figure.
Despite New York's protests, however, it was clear that many officials were relieved to find that, as the preliminary figures indicated, the state seems about to lose three Congressional seats, not four as some had feared. Among the other states apparently losing representation were Michigan, Pennsylvania, Illinois and Ohio, each standing to lose two seats. The three losers outside the Northern and Midwestern industrial regions were Kentucky, Louisiana and Montana.

CORRECTION-DATE: December 28, 1990, Friday

 CORRECTION:
Two articles yesterday about the 1990 census incorrectly described a provision of the Constitution, and one of them incorrectly described a proposed corrective survey in some copies. The Constitution requires only that a census be taken every 10 years. Federal law stipulates that the count be given to the President by the end of the year. The corrective survey would involve 150,000 households, not individuals.
A map accompanying the article gave incorrect figures in some copies for the percent changes in each state's population between 1980 and 1990. A corrected map appears on page A16.

HEADLINE: U.N. Choice: A Signal of a Bigger Role for Japan

BYLINE: By STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 26

 BODY:
With understandable pride, Government officials are commenting these days that the recent selection of a Japanese woman as the new United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees sends a signal that Tokyo is ready for a leadership role in a vexing global problem.
But for Sadako Ogata, who takes office as High Commissioner on Jan. 1, an equally important message should be read at home: that Japan's record of contributing funds to the refugee problem, and to international humanitarian causes generally, has been inadequate. 
 "I think it would be pretty good if Japan, in becoming an economic power, becomes a humanitarian power as well," Mrs. Ogata said in an interview. "I'm sure we can do more, and we have to do more. Our record is better than it used to be, but it should be much, much more."
Mrs. Ogata, dean of foreign studies at Sophia University in Tokyo, will be the highest ranking Japanese at an international agency at a time when Japan has clamored for a role commensurate with its growing status as financier of international activities.
 
15 Million Refugees
 The agency in charge of 15 million refugees is in a financial crisis, which could worsen if war breaks out in the Middle East or chaos grows in the Soviet Union. Agency officials have made it clear that they expect Japan to do more to help.
Japan is second only to the United States in contributing to the agency's $500 million annual budget, but the ranking is deceptive. In fact, Japan's contribution has been steady at $55 million to $60 million a year, despite the yen's being worth twice as many dollars as five years ago.
That means that in terms of the Japanese budget, appropriations for refugees have gone down sharply, not up. Critics assert that Japan's lagging efforts on refugees are a mirror of its failure elsewhere to provide aid to developing countries or to aid the global environmental problems.
Although Japan is the No. 1 foreign assistance donor in terms of dollars, it achieved that status because of the high yen and because the United States has cut back. On a per capita basis, Japan is well behind many European countries as an aid donor.
 
Indochinese Resettlement
 Nor has Japan moved dramatically on refugee problems beyond giving money. Under pressure to do more, the Government agreed in 1985 to permit the resettlement of 10,000 Indochinese refugees in Japan, but so far only 7,000 have been resettled.
Mrs. Ogata said she felt there was room for more immigrants and refugees to resettle but admitted that the Japanese themselves were wary.
"Change takes a long time in Japan," she said. "I think you should always look at Japan in a medium-term perspective -- at least 5 or 10 years. Japan moves slowly, but if you take a longer time span, it moves an awful lot."
Mrs. Ogata was among many Japanese intellectuals critical of Prime Minster Toshiki Kaifu's handling of the national response to the Persian Gulf crisis. Mr. Kaifu promised $4 billion, but was unable to win legislative approval to send personnel to the region.
 
Long a Male Bastion
 There are many ways in which Mrs. Ogata's appointment is unusual, not least because only a tiny number of women have risen to prominence in Japan. What seemed like a possibility of gains in politics last year fizzled out when very few women ended up running or being elected to office this year.
The Japanese Foreign Ministry has also long been a male bastion. Mrs. Ogata, who is 63 years old, is the granddaughter and daughter of a diplomat, but she freely acknowledges that when she was deciding on a career, it was virtually impossible for a woman to join the foreign service.
Instead, Mrs. Ogata went into teaching. After graduating from the University of the Sacred Heart in Tokyo, a Catholic institution, she got her Ph.D. in political science from the University of California at Berkeley and a master's from Georgetown in Washington.
Mrs. Ogata speaks in crisp unaccented English, a product of five childhood years in the 1930's in San Francisco and Portland. She and her husband, Shijuro Ogata, a highly respected economist, are familiar spokesmen on Japanese issues for diplomats, journalists and Government officials.
 
Outside the Foreign Ministry
 In fact, Mr. Ogata, now the deputy governor of the Japan Development Bank, has himself been frequently mentioned as a possible director general of the International Monetary Fund, should the fund ever abandon its tradition of choosing only a European.
To many Japanese, it is significant that the Foreign Ministry had to turn to someone outside its own precincts to nominate for the United Nations post. Japanese are not known as big volunteers at the United Nations. Only nine of the refugee agency's 364 professionals are Japanese.
Japanese diplomats are said to feel that even a short stint at the United Nations could derail their careers. Few Japanese also know English well enough to feel comfortable at international agencies, and there is little training in Japan for jobs relating to the developing world.
But Mrs. Ogata feels that Japanese are discouraged from working at international agencies because the senior positions have been reserved for Americans and Europeans. "Japan is a latecomer at the U.N.," she said. "It's not easy for countries to give up the top jobs."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Sadako Ogata, who is to take office Jan. 1 as United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees. (Ellen Rudolph for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: METRO MATTERS;
Would Giuliani Have Done A Better Job?

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS

 BODY:
Had just 50,000 New York City voters changed their minds in 1989, Rudolph W. Giuliani would be finishing his first year as mayor. Would his election have made any difference? Given the dimensions of the city's budget gap, can any mayor make a difference?
"I guess I'd be working for sure today had I won," said Mr. Giuliani, who was working between holidays yesterday but at his midtown Manhattan law office, not at City Hall (where Mayor David N. Dinkins held several meetings, though his only scheduled public appearance was a midday courtesy call by two Good Samaritans from Brooklyn). 
 Predictably, Mr. Giuliani, for one, says he would have made a difference, and for the better. The second question is more complicated, particularly when mayoral discretion is so confined by fiscal impeatives.
After promising to be the city's toughest mayor on crime, Mr. Dinkins postponed hiring more police officers -- a decision that was applauded at the time as fiscally prudent but that would haunt him during a summer when crime surged almost as much as crime coverage in the media. Later, he was able to claim credit for adding only about $60 million -- mostly for drug treatment and preventive services for children -- to the city's $29 billion budget.
Mr. Dinkins succeeded as a candidate, though barely. His performance as a mayor has been more problematic.
Mr. Giuliani, who was Mr. Dinkins's Republican rival, is hardly a disinterested observer. But his views seem to echo the sentiments of those New Yorkers -- including some who voted for Mr. Dinkins -- who believe that their doubts about the Mayor have already been vindicated by his first-year record.
"Another person would have helped the city through this situation more effectively," Mr. Giuliani said. "A lot of what the city is missing is leadership and a sense of direction."
Any mayor would have faced constants like crime and fiscal constraints. A second set of obstacles, Mr. Giuliani said, was created by Mr. Dinkins himself. "There is no consistent vision or sense of where the city is going," Mr. Giuliani said. "It is not as if they're saying we're cutting the budget to create a better economy in one or two years. We're cutting the budget to respond to immediate crises."
He drew a stark, if strained, comparison between Mr. Dinkins and Winston Churchill during the Battle of Britain. "There was not much Churchill could do to stop the bombs from falling," he said, "but he talked about making sacrifices for the future. He didn't just say that things were worse in Czechoslovakia."
In part, Mr. Dinkins's problem has been one of tone. Billie Holiday may croon the same tunes on both, but the clarity is greater on the compact disc player that the Mayor's wife, Joyce, gave him as a Christmas gift than on the cassette player in his City Hall office.
His problem, at times, has also been one of content, which Mr. Dinkins hopes to clarify in his State of the City speech next month. Mr. Giuliani described the Mayor's early decision to delay police hiring as "a terrible mistake" -- a political gesture that had negligible impact on the budget but an immediate effect on the police force.
Not surprisingly, Mr. Giuliani said he was surprised that Mr. Dinkins had been "considerably weaker than I thought he would be," particularly in negotiating with municipal unions and in dealing with Albany legislators -- jobs for which the Mayor's political ties might have been an advantage.
Similarly, Mr. Giuliani said that Mr. Dinkins's promise of racial harmony, as the city's first black mayor, had been largely unfulfilled (although the subdued reaction by blacks to the shooting of an unarmed black woman by a police officer responding to a family disturbance in the Bronx last month might be attributed, in part, to a presumption among blacks that Mr. Dinkins would not countenance a cover-up). The Mayor's tentative response earlier this year to the black boycott of a Brooklyn market owned by Korean immigrants, Mr. Giuliani said, demonstrated a failure to rise above racial politics.
Yesterday, Mr. Giuliani said, as he entered the Third Avenue office building where he works, a police officer lamented to him, "You should've won." Two days before, Mr. Giuliani recalled, another Dinkins detractor approached him and said, "Don't you feel fortunate that you have a political future and he doesn't?"
Futures can be even more volatile in politics than in petroleum, which is why, despite the challenges, few politicians are so self-effacing as to either eschew the chance to impose their imprint on an office or accept the notion that it makes no difference who is mayor.
"I would've preferred to win," Mr. Giuliani said.

HEADLINE: More Rate Cuts Are Urged

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The chairman of the Joint Economic Committee of Congress said today that the economy needed stimulus from lower interest rates, not from temporary tax cuts or Government spending programs that would worsen the budget deficit.
"The appropriate policy response to weakness in the economy is a more stimulative monetary policy," said Representative Lee Hamilton, Democrat of Indiana, in his annual economic report. 
 The Federal Reserve has already pushed down interest rates, cutting the discount rate by half a point, to 6 1/2 percent, and pushing down the Federal funds rate four times since July. But Mr. Hamilton said these actions were just a beginning.
"Temporory tax cuts can be quicker, but temporary tax cuts have a way of becoming permanent, and the budget deficit is already too large," his report stated.
Mr. Hamilton said the long-term health of the economy required further reductions in the budget deficit to promote savings. The report said cuts of about $80 billion in the 1995 fiscal year are needed to restore the savings rate to the level seen in the 1960's and 1970's. It made no specific proposals on how the cuts might be achieved.
The report also said that unemployment benefits should be extended in times of high joblessness.

HEADLINE: Crotone Journal;
New U.S. Base: City Boon or NATO Boondoggle?

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CROTONE, Italy

 BODY:
Nothing would please him more, Francesco Sottile said, than to have Fiat plunk down a huge plant and start churning out cars in this outpost on the arch of the Italian boot. It would be great if Olivetti were to produce computers here.
But Mr. Sottile, a realist as well as president of Crotone's Small Industries Association, recognizes that none of that is about to happen. 
 And when you must deal with a 30 percent unemployment rate among local youths and with household incomes that are half the national average, when you face a serious drug problem and a hungry local Mafia, you sometimes settle for what you can.
So Mr. Sottile, like other Crotone business owners, is delighted that ground-breaking has finally begun five miles outside town on a NATO air base that will spread across 3,500 acres of scrubby farmland overlooking the Ionian Sea. It is to become home for 72 American F-16 fighter jets that are supposed to leave Torrejon, Spain, outside Madrid, by mid-1992.
 
A 1988 Agreement
 The businessmen do not think of it as merely another military installation. Rather, they see an economic opportunity staring at them that was unimaginable until Italy agreed in early 1988, as part of its commitment to the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, to accept planes that Spain no longer wants.
"We're not expecting this base to take care of all our problems," Mr. Sottile said. "But we think it can solve some of them."
While that is a widely shared view in this weathered Calabrian city of 65,000, it is far from universal. "They're fooling themselves if they think that there will be a shower of dollars, that this will produce miracles," said Mayor Giancarlo Sitra, a Communist.
If the air base is such an economic blessing, the skeptics ask, why was Spain glad to let it go?
The planned transfer here of the United States Air Force's 401st Tactical Fighter Wing has provoked disputes that stretch all the way to Washington, where critics insist that with the cold war over, a built-from-scratch $800 million base is no longer needed.
 
Grumbling in Congress
 Some members of Congress dismiss it as a NATO boondoggle, and ask why they should help an isolated region of Italy when dozens of military installations are being shut down in the United States. The House of Representatives showed its displeasure last summer by blocking all Crotone funds for the fiscal year that began Oct. 1, although it could not prevent the start of construction with previously authorized money.
But NATO foreign ministers insist that even though the base will not be ready for at least five years, forcing the F-16's to scramble for temporary locations after 1992, it remains vital to protect the alliance's southern flank. The Persian Gulf crisis underlines the need, they say, and they recently reaffirmed their commitment in Brussels. Washington is to pay 28 percent of the costs -- roughly $225 million, assuming present estimates hold -- while the other 15 NATO members cover the rest.
To Mr. Sottile, price tags and geopolitical debates are best left to politicians and generals.
What he sees is an instant community of 10,000 -- military men and women and their families -- that will mean an estimated 1,000 on-base jobs for Italian civilians. It will mean new opportunities for local restaurants, hotels, shops and repairmen. The base has already been a boon to 74 farmers who had their property expropriated for the first phase of construction, each receiving as much as $14,000 an acre, three times what the land is believed to be worth.
 
City Has Two Plants
 Such payments have helped fuel Congressional complaints that the installation amounts to a bailout for Crotone. This city, with a chemical plant and metal-processing factory, is more industrialized than most places in largely rural Calabria. Nevertheless, it has hardly escaped the economic stagnation and ingrained resentments that plague much of Italy's tumbledown south.
Here, Rome is widely regarded as simply the latest colonizer in a long string that began 27 centuries ago with the ancient Greeks. Perhaps, the business leaders say, a NATO air base might force the central Government to take notice by building needed highways and rail links, if only to avoid giving the foreigners a bad impression.
Doubters abound, however.
Pacifists and political activists, mostly from the left, have held protest demonstrations, denouncing the base as unnecessary and provocative to Italy's neighbors in northern Africa and the Middle East. Some worry that the presence of fighter planes able to carry nuclear weapons could expose them to a military attack. Others offer a catalogue of concerns: environmental pollution, possible damage to archeological ruins and potential cultural clashes with the Americans, not to mention age-old worries that a military base may mean prostitutes and an even bigger drug problem than now.
Regardless of political persuasion, everyone here is waiting to see how long it take before the 'Ndrangheta, the Calabrian version of the Mafia, gets its hands on construction contracts.
 
Prosecutor Watches Companies
 So far, the major work has been undertaken by outside companies, but subcontracts worth a total of $5.5 million have gone to two Crotone companies. Elio Costa, the public prosecutor, says he is keeping an eye on both, although he will not disclose what, if anything, he suspects.
At heart, the decisive question for most Crotonese is the economic potential, and Mayor Sitra argues that the base will hurt rather than help. "We feel that this is seen by Rome as an alternative to industrial development," he said.
But business leaders are betting differently. Mr. Sottile, for one, has no doubts that sooner or later Congress will provide the funds to complete the job.
"In the end," he said, partly in jest but also part seriously, "the Americans wind up paying everything."

GRAPHIC: Photo: A square in Crotone, a city in southern Italy with high unemployment and household incomes that are half the national average. Opinions differ on whether the local economy will improve with the completion of an $800 million NATO air base on farmland outside town. (Associated Press)
 
Map: Map of Italy showing location of Crotone.

LENGTH: 873 words

HEADLINE: Hard Days For Liquor Merchants

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE M. FISHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Dec. 27

 BODY:
For sellers of wine, beer and spirits, the last two weeks of the year are normally hectic but profitable. This year, merchants are just plain harried.
Facing a special one-time tax on all inventory on Dec. 31, some retailers and wholesalers are scrambling to reduce stock, while others are loading up in anticipation of huge price increases coming from producers in January. And all are watching the economy with a wary eye. 
 "If you notice retailers with their hair falling out, it's because they've been pulling it," said Gordon Mott, editor of Market Watch, a trade magazine. "They're confused."
 
Excise Tax to Rise
 As part of the agreement this fall between Congress and the Bush Administration, the Government is raising the Federal excise tax: on wine from 3 cents a bottle, to 21; on a bottle of 80-proof spirits from $1.98, to $2.14, and on beer from 16 cents a six-pack, to 32.
These increases go into effect Jan. 1, and are expected to generate $8.8 billion over the next five years. But the agreement also calls for a one-time tax expected to generate $300 million.
The timing of the new tax adds to liquor store woes. Because Christmas and New Year's Eves are the two busiest days of the year, inventories are kept high. Many stores close altogether or work shortened hours New Year's Day, but not this year, when they will have to count and reprice every bottle in stock.
 
'A Big Pain in the Neck'
 "It is going to be a big pain in the neck," said Chip Cassidy, wine buyer for Crown Wine Merchants, a chain with 21 stores based in Fort Lauderdale, Fla. Employees will have to spend all New Year's Day counting more than 100,000 bottles in some stores. Still, he continued, the tax is more a nuisance than a catastrophe. "Everybody can moan and groan and complain, but six months from now we're going to still be in business," he added.
Some retailers have gone all out to greet the New Year with low inventories and, hence, avoid much of the tax. But many suppliers have announced large price increases for January and offered substantial discounts in December, creating an incentive to stock up, especially since the tax is not due until June. In addition, prices will increase by even more than the amount of the tax because producers, wholesalers and retailers will each apply their standard markup to the higher costs.
Among retailers "there seem to be two contradictory philosophies that have an almost equal number of adherents," said Paul Gillette, publisher of The Wine Investor, an industry newsletter.
Complicating matters, "many suppliers have seized the opportunity of the tax to put on price increases of their own, which they were planning to do anyway," Mr. Gillette added. He continued, "The reasoning apparently is, one, if you put it on at the same time, people are prepared and likely to accept it and blame the tax, and two, suppliers can induce wholesalers and retailers to stock up at the old price."
 
Top Brands Plan Price Increase
 Producers increasing prices include big brewers like the Anheuser-Busch Companies and the Stroh Brewery Company, leading liquor brands and wine makers, particularly premium French and Italian producers. Some increases reflect the dollar's weakness against foreign currencies, and some are the result of price rises that had been deferred because producers feared compounding the trend toward lower alcohol consumption.
"Instead of $2-a-case increases, you're looking at a lot of $10 increases, so nobody's looking to lower inventory," said John Sander Block, vice president of Southern Wine and Spirits in Miami, the nation's largest wholesaler. "If they have the cash flow available, they'll buy merchandise."
But the general economic malaise has many retailers wary of building stock, particularly in spirits. While alcoholic beverages are often thought to be recession-proof, retailers in areas where the downturn hit early, like the Northeast, report significantly lower sales in 1990. Those in more buoyant areas are still not inclined to load up.
 
Slow January Sales Expected
 "Because January, February and March are the absolute slowest months of the year, it doesn't make sense for us to load up on inventory," said David Wilce, manager of Pacific Wine and Spirits in Los Gatos, Calif.
While Pacific had record one-day sales on Christmas Eve, "with the economy headed the way it is, January could be the all-time worst," he said.
One factor that could further weaken first-quarter sales is a "tax rush" in December brought on by the impending excise tax increase and price rises. Customers who buy heavily now may not buy more for some time. But retailers and wholesalers say there has not been a large trend toward such buying, except perhaps at discount club stores, which always do a high volume in case sales.
Here, too, timing is a factor. These latest increases "have come out in a period when the consumer usually has pretty good stocks of wine and spirits because of gift giving," said Michael Aaron, president of Sherry-Lehmann Inc., the Manhattan retailer. Tax increases have little impact on premium wine and spirits sales, he continued, adding, "You don't see tax rushes on Chateau Lafite."

HEADLINE: Seoul's Leader Shuffles Cabinet In a Housecleaning for Elections

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: SEOUL, South Korea, Dec. 27

 BODY:
President Roh Tae Woo named a new Prime Minister and shuffled his Cabinet today in an effort to polish his party's image before a series of elections starting next year.
The appointment of the new Prime Minister and nine new Cabinet ministers indicates little substantive change in policy, either domestic or foreign. 
The authorities also announced today the arrests of more than 50 people as part of a continuing crackdown on anti-Government activity.
Some opposition and dissident groups assert that the arrests are part of a plan to suppress dissent as the Democratic Liberal Party of Mr. Roh approaches its first local elections in 30 years.
 
Talks With North a Priority
 The new Prime Minister, Ro Jai Bong, immediately issued a statement calling for law and order and said the conservative Government would make further talks with North Korea a priority.
"The priorities will be on establishing social discipline as well as law and order, progress in inter-Korea dialogue and multilateral diplomacy," said Mr. Ro, a former presidential aide.
Kim Dae Jung, head of the opposition Party for Peace and Democracy, said he was disappointed at the Cabinet changes. He said they made the Government more conservative and rightist.
President Roh is midway in a five-year term that ends in 1992. He has made dramatic progress in foreign relations by expanding Seoul's ties with Communist and former Communist nations. But support for domestic policies is at an all-time low as he prepares for next year's local elections, which are expected to be held in the first half of the year.
Three national elections, including those for Parliament and a new President, will be held in the next two years.
 
Job's Significance Grows
 The largely ceremonial post of Prime Minister has become increasingly significant because the holder acts as chief delegate to high-level talks on easing tensions with Communist North Korea, the fourth round of which is scheduled for February.
In a move seen to reflect changing relations with the North, Choi Ho Joong was moved from Foreign Minister to Unification Minister and will serve as Deputy Prime Minister. He was instrumental in helping Seoul forge dramatic new relations with former Communist allies of North Korea. The Government hopes its actions will force the North from its isolation and lead to eventual unification.
The new Foreign Minister is Lee Sang Ok, a career diplomat now posted in Geneva as chief of mission to international agencies.
Lee Bong Suh, an American-educated economist, was named Trade and Industry Minister.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Ro Jai Bong after being named the Prime Minister of South Korea yesterday. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off in Thin Trading; Gold Prices Are Also Lower

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar settled mostly lower in extremely thin trading yesterday.
Gold prices also declined broadly. On the New York Commodity Exchange, gold bullion for current delivery slipped 30 cents a troy ounce to settle at $385.30. The Republic National Bank quoted a late bid for gold of $384.40, up 20 cents an ounce. 
 A Government report showing that United States durable goods orders fell a steeper-than-expected 10.5 percent in November had some impact on trading, participants said. Analysts had been expecting a decline of about 2.2 percent, and the much larger drop fueled concerns that the United States economy was weakening significantly.
 European markets, which had closed for the Christmas holidays, reopened yesterday to find traders mostly selling the dollar.
Domestic trading was extremely light. "We had about an hour's worth of trading after the durable goods numbers, and that was it for the whole day," said Stephen Flanagan, a vice president at Mitsubishi Bank Ltd.
Despite the weakness of the durable goods report, it was "not enough to make people reopen their books and dump dollars before the New Year," said Michael Malpede, an analyst at Refco Group Ltd. in Chicago. He noted that a good portion of the decline reflected auto-plant shutdowns rather than declines in a variety of industries.
The German mark was helped by the confirmation of Gennadi I. Yanayev as Soviet vice president. He was President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's choice for the newly created office, and the approval offered an air of stability to the politically torn Soviet Union. Against the German currency, the dollar weakened in New York to 1.5255 marks, from 1.5348 late Wednesday.
"The focus was on U.S. economic fundamentals, the Soviet Union and the Persian Gulf crisis," Mr. Malpede said. "The dollar has been bouncing off that triad, with no clear direction in trading."
In Tokyo, the dollar closed at 136.65 yen, up 0.40 yen from Wednesday's close. Later in London, it was quoted higher at 136.75 yen. In New York, the dollar settled at 136.40 yen, up from 136.10 yen on Wednesday.
In London, the British pound was quoted at $1.8870, compared with $1.8740 late Monday. In New York, it cost $1.8895 to buy one pound, more expensive than Wednesday's $1.8815.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Wednesday's rates, included: 1.3040 Swiss francs, down from 1.3082; 5.1935 French francs, down from 5.2080; 1,151.75 Italian lire, down from 1,155.00, and 1.1616 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1595.
Gold was quoted at a late bid price of $382.25 a troy ounce in London, down from $383 bid an ounce late Monday.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Veil of Corporate Secrecy Is Lifted Somewhat in Japan

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Foreign investment experts active in the Japanese stock market have long said that following rumors could be more important than serious research, since companies disclose so little solid information.
"It has always been part of the fun of this market that even if you started a rumor, nobody could disprove it," said Robert G. Zielinski, an analyst at Jardine Fleming Securities and co-author of a recent book, "Unequal Equities," that is critical of the Tokyo market for consistently benefiting companies at the expense of their shareholders. "You could never calculate the real net worth of any company." 
 In the last few weeks, the Finance Ministry has quietly instituted several measures that may start to change that. Giving investors a rational basis for selecting stocks, as the measures are intended to do, has been taken for granted on Wall Street for decades, but is a potentially historic shift here.
The most recent change, announced on Tuesday, will force companies and banks to report the gains or losses on their portfolios of securities, as well as futures and options holdings. They will have to provide the current market value of their holdings at the end of the fiscal year, March 31.
Those portfolios have been a crucial means by which companies, banks and other financial institutions conceal hidden reserves of cash -- or, as in the last year of falling share prices, mounting losses. For instance, Mr. Zielinski estimates that as a group the major Japanese banks have lost about $15 billion on their bond holdings.
"We'll finally really know which banks are good at trading and which are not," said Robert Alan Feldman, an economist with Salomon Brothers Asia.
In addition, the ministry has enacted a rule to force affiliated companies, members of secretive corporate families known as keiretsu, to disclose more about transactions within the group. And earlier this month a new rule took effect that obliges investors to report holdings of 5 percent or more in a company, a measure similar to the 13D disclosure rule in the United States.
 This last rule has already caused several sensations: T. Boone Pickens, the Texas financier, was forced to disclose that he got the money to acquire a major stake in Koito, a maker of auto parts, from a well-known Japanese raider, and several other companies discovered that some of their biggest shareholders were investment groups controlled by organized crime families, or yakuza.
By themselves, none of the rule changes will radically alter the view that only a small number of Japanese insiders have access to accurate data on corporations' real financial status. But they are regarded as representing a trend that, most foreigners hope, will gain momentum.
"This is a new step forward, and an important one, but not the last," said a Finance Ministry official involved in instituting the measures.
These changes follow last year's tightening of the laws against insider trading. In the long run, however, the changes in the disclosure rules may have a greater impact for the simple reason that few expect the insider trading rules to be rigorously enforced against everyone: manipulation and insider trading are widely seen as too important to political fund-raising to be halted.
The perception that Japan's stock market serves a special function in politics was heightened by the Recruit scandal last year, in which the Prime Minister then, Noboru Takeshita, and a large portion of his Cabinet were disgraced for having accepted low-priced shares in a company about to go public, and a more recent case in which a former Cabinet minister, Toshiyuki Inamura, was indicted on tax evasion charges because the authorities said he had not reported profits earned from trading on inside information.
Although banks and other companies have at times objected to greater disclosure, they have muted their protests now because many of Japan's larger corporations want to gain access to foreign capital markets with stricter standards.
When the Mitsubishi Bank decided to seek a listing on the New York Stock Exchange last year, for example, it issued a very un-Japanese disclosure document with data never made available to Japanese investors.
The latest rule change, requiring listed companies to disclose the value of their securities portfolios, is to be phased in. That could provide banks in particular with a crucial advantage.
As of March 31, they must report the market value of their holdings of stocks, bonds that are convertible into stock and bonds that come with warrants or options to buy stock later at a preset price. But companies do not have to report their considerably larger holdings of regular bonds, including Government bonds, until March 31, 1992. That gives them an extra year for the markets to recover before having to report how badly they have been battered by this year's slide. Nor are updated reports on their large land holdings required.
All the same, the veil is being lifted slightly in what is expected to be an irreversible process. "It will make this market efficient, but a little more boring," said a nostalgic Mr. Zielinski.

HEADLINE: Pacific Telesis Decides to Quit Real Estate

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
The Pacific Telesis Group announced on Wednesday that it was leaving the real estate business and would take a $60 million after-tax charge against its fourth-quarter earnings to offset expected losses on property sales.
The decision is the latest recognition of losses by a regional Bell company that diversified into industries very different from the provision of local telephone service, only to find that its new investments were far more vulnerable to an economic slowdown. 
 "What it shows is they ought to stay at home, ringing up the revenues from local telephone service," said Jack B. Grubman, a telephone industry analyst at Paine Webber.
 
Plan to Invest Proceeds

 Pacific Telesis will sell its entire $475 million portfolio of office buildings and industrial sites over the next several years and invest the proceeds in telecommunications services, particularly wireless telephone businesses overseas, Gerald A. Beatty, a company spokesman, said. The company had said in March that it would not invest any more money in real estate and would begin reducing its holdings gradually.
Mr. Beatty said Pacific Telesis now believed it could earn better returns in telecommunications than in real estate.
The BellSouth Corporation disclosed on Dec. 7 that it had taken a $30 million loss on the sale of its computer-leasing business. The Bell Atlantic Corporation announced on Oct. 26 that it would take a onetime charge of $40 million to reflect the shrinking market value of its non-telephone financial services, which include equipment leasing and lending to other companies and real estate projects.
U S West Inc. said earlier this year that it would seek to sell its $1.4 billion real estate portfolio within five to seven years. Poor returns on investment and volatility in the real estate market prompted the decision, Sharon K. Price, a company spokeswoman, said.
Although no charges against earnings have been taken, the weakening economy is now making it hard to sell the properties, she said, so that it may take more than seven years to sell them all.
"It looks like we'll be in the landlord business for a while," Ms. Price added.
A few Wall Street analysts, notably those at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, have been warning that the local telephone business is much more vulnerable to setbacks now than in previous economic downturns. The structure of the telephone industry has changed, and the seven regional Bell companies, which were created by the breakup of the American Telephone and Telegraph Company in 1984, have invested in a variety of industries that offer potentially higher returns than traditional local phone service, but are also more risky.
More local phone concerns, like the New York Telephone Company, are charging customers by the call or by the minute of calling, instead of charging a fixed rate for unlimited calling. Some analysts warn that while very few consumers disconnected their phones during previous recessions to save money, they may forgo some local calls in the next economic downturn to trim their phone bills.
A third of the regional Bell companies' revenues now consists of fees from long-distance carriers for carrying calls from customers' homes and businesses to the nearest long-distance switching center. The number of long-distance calls has historically been more sensitive than the number of local calls to the health of the nation's economy, so that these revenues may grow very slowly in a downturn.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF: The Talk of Bahrain;
In an Island Playground in the Gulf, The G.I.'s Let Down Their Guard

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANAMA, Bahrain

 BODY:
At first glance, it could be Kansas.
The lobby of the Regency Inter-Continental is filled with young American servicemen and a few Britons in blue jeans or running shorts and Reeboks. Given their crew cuts, tans and telltale tatoos, they could only be soldiers. 
 About half the rooms at this and the four other luxury hotels on this relatively relaxed and tolerant island in the austere and unforgiving Persian Gulf are now occupied by soldiers, some living here on "T.D.Y.," temporary duty, or in from the Saudi desert on "R n R."
Bahrain, the smallest and poorest of the gulf states, is accustomed to being host to the alcohol deprived. Saudis have been coming here in droves ever since 1986 when a causeway costing more than $1 billion opened, linking the Saudi mainland with this island of 500,000, of whom 165,000 are foreign.
"We'd like to make Bahrain the Florida of the gulf," said Tarek al-Moayyad, Bahrain's Minister of Information, Culture and Tourism. Judging from the scene in Bahrain, he is succeeding.
Even before the crisis, Saudis came to Bahrain for the Islamic weekend, Thursday and Friday, to enjoy the island's beaches, its bars and the Philippine women who bump and grind their way through ear-shattering electrified rock music.
Now the Saudis have largely been replaced by Western soldiers. American officials declined to discuss how much money was being spent on housing soldiers in luxury hotels, where room rates for business travelers start at more than $150 a night. The United States worked out a package deal for the military at a huge discount, they said, and military personnel have been filling not only the five-star hotels, but every other hotel on the island, as well as some newly pitched tents.
Just how many military men and women are here is also a secret. They number in the thousands, but many are on the 65 ships that make up the two aircraft carrier battle groups here, some of which are anchored offshore.
Though there has been no official announcement, more than 8,000 service men and women, mostly American marines, are camped out at a Bahraini base at the southern end of the island, Bahraini officials say.
In the first weeks after the Iraqi takeover of Kuwait in August, Bahrain, traumatized by the invasion, turned away most journalists. But now both the military and civilians are welcome here, and it is the American military that is reluctant to talk.
Public affairs spokesmen for the Navy, which has had a command post here since 1949, said they were too busy to provide a briefing. But one spokesman said at an informal briefing over beer at a local bar that there would soon be 5 aircraft carrier battle groups in this "area of responsibility" -- that is, more than 120 American ships.
Navy ships enforcing the embargo against Iraq had intercepted more than 5,500 ships by mid-December, boarded more than 500 of them and diverted about 24.
Most of the troops seem well behaved. But incidents do occur. There were two drunken brawls in two nights at the Inter-Continental disco recently.
So the American Embassy has decided to begin an International Courtesy Patrol, or in normal parlance, shore patrols, consisting of three servicemen -- one British, one American, one Bahraini -- all in civilian clothes. The Americans and British hope these patrols will soon add a touch of decorum to the more than 20 hotel bars and discos that are filled each night to capacity.
 
A Welcome for Kuwaitis
 Bahrain is not only playing host to the military. Since the crisis began, some 10,000 Kuwaitis have found shelter here. With little fanfare, the Government turned over some 200 housing units just built for Bahrainis in Hamad Town, a spiffy new compound of 5,000 white duplex houses with red shutters, only a 10-minute drive from Manama.
Soon after arriving, groups of families put up tents outside their new temporary housing in which they could hold their diwaniyas, or majlises, informal assemblies on weekends and in the evenings that are a mainstay of Kuwaiti social and political life. Their new houses are sparsely furnished, but the tents have all the comforts of home -- incense burners, thermoses of tea and coffee, endless supplies of Marlboro cigarettes, cassette recorders and Persian carpets.
In one tent just off Road 1423 of Hamad Town, Jaber al Mari, 35 years old, mused about his country's plight and his own future. "We tried to fight the Iraqis, but there were too many," said the former soldier, who lived before the invasion in Mina Abdullah Fehaheel, near the Iraqi border. "We weren't ready."
Unlike many of his countrymen, he and his eight children had no villa in Cannes or house in London to which they could flee. So they came here, and are now living in free Bahraini housing with a stipend provided by the Kuwaiti government in exile.
"The Bahrainis have been very kind to us," he said. "They've given us what we needed, thanks to Sheik Issa," he added, referring to Bahrain's ruler, Emir Issa bin Sulman al-Khalifa. "It's almost like home."
But Mr. al-Mari will not be here long. He said he was leaving his family here soon to join several thousand Kuwaiti soldiers who have been training at Hafer al-Bater, near the Saudi border. "I want to fight with the Americans this time," he said, ruefully. "This time we will fight, not lose."
 
A Lift for the Economy
 Bahrainis do not seem to mind their new guests. Quite the contrary. They have provided a degree of assurance and a lift to the economy of this trading and banking center, which was badly hit by capital flight immediately after the Iraqi invasion.
Bahrain has benefited from price increases for what is still its largest source of income -- 40,000 barrels a day of oil and the refining of some 250,000 barrels a day, mostly for Saudi Arabia. Some capital has returned to the more than 70 banks here in the gulf's only offshore financial capital, which had record assets of some $73 billion in March.
Bahraini and Western officials here said there were no military targets to speak of on this island. But nerves are still frayed, since Bahrain is only 30 minutes across the gulf from the giant Saudi air base at Dhahran.
But Mr. al-Moayyad insists, and Western diplomats adamantly agree, most Bahrainis support the large Western presence here. Islamic Shiites, who make up about 70 percent of the population and have close ties to Iran, have taken a "see, we told you so" attitude.
Things were politically difficult for many of them during the Iran-Iraq war, so they enjoy reminding officials that Bahrain was among Iraq's most ardent supporters. And Sunni Muslims, who include the ruling family, see the soldiers as protection for their interests and position.
There are few public demands for greater democratization and accountability, as there are in some parts of the gulf.
"At the moment, our main concern is security and defense," Mr. al-Moayyad said. "The man in the street right now would rather have security than democracy, and given the way things are, can you blame him?"

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Looking Ahead, Guided by the Past

BYLINE: By Leonard Silk

 BODY:
WHAT'S ahead in the New Year? The crystal ball (clouded as usual) turns up these items:
The Bush Administration is backing an overhaul of the banking laws, written during the Depression, to permit American banks to compete aggressively all over the country and against giant international competitors. The commercial banks would be allowed to move into investment banking, securities dealing, life insurance and other areas. They might even begin to play the dominant role in guiding big nonfinancial corporations, as they did in the 19th century. 
 Would this be a good thing for the economy?
David Hale, chief economist of Kemper Financial Services, said: "America abolished universal banking in the 1930's on the grounds that it represented a dangerous concentration of financial power and encouraged underwriting abuses. But as a result of the current banking crisis and the public dissatisfaction with Wall Street's stewardship of the corporate sector during the 1980's, the Congress may have to rethink the economic role of the banks."
The proposed changes in banking legislation, Mr. Hale suggests, raise some fundamental issues:
Would a bank-based form of capitalism encourage American business to take longer perspectives than the stock market brand of capitalism?
With the wider diffusion of information today, could universal banks dominate American finance in the 21st century as they did in the 19th century?
And could we achieve the long-term investment horizons provided by the bank-dominated form of capitalism, present today in Germany and Japan, without losing the flexibility and entrepreneurship of a form of capitalism driven by a stock market?
Mr. Hale's answer is to let the two forms of capitalism coexist in the United States by lowering the barriers between banking and commerce while creating fiscal incentives for American businessesto use more equity and less debt. And he foresees a wave of bank mergers in 1991 to head off failures.
How long will the recession last?
Geoffrey H. Moore, director of Columbia University's Center for International Business Cycle Research, concludes that the current recession began in June. Since the eight preceding postwar recessions lasted 6 to 16 months, the odds are that if it is an exceptionally long recession, it will end in the final quarter of 1991, and if it is an average one, it does not have more than another quarter to run.
But William H. Tehan of the brokerage firm P. R. Herzig & Company, contends that the economy is not in a typical recession at all. "Recession is the wrong description," he said. "The present situation, with its attendant financial deterioration, bears no resemblance to recession." The United States, he maintains, is in "a liquidity squeeze induced by a historically high level of debt combined with a sharp contraction in credit demand." And he argues that this is "an entirely different situation not seen in the U.S. since the early 1930's."
Another worrisome parallel with the 1930's is thestrain on open trading relations and the threat of protectionism that followed the collapse of the Uruguay Round of trade talks earlier this month.
Richard McCormack, Under Secretary of State for Economic Affairs, who played a part in the trade talks, said that before they ended, the talks actually accomplished two important things. First, they advanced the political education of the American team on how not to negotiate: not by laying down ultimatums, as on agriculture. And they also showed the European Community what is at stake: the preservation of an open world trading system and the health of every country's economy.
The dangers of a return to Smoot-Hawley protectionism and retaliation are so clear that the stalled negotiations are likely to be revived in 1991.
Soaring oil prices after Iraq's invasion of Kuwait knocked investors for a loop. Will their spirits recover in the new year?
Rosanne M. Cahn, an economist at First Boston, said: "The animal spirits of the economy are unraveling more dramatically than thought, given the factual consequences of the third oil shock. Even if oil prices were to have remained at $35 per barrel, the net drag on U.S. G.N.P. growth, measured by the higher oil import bill, would have been around half a percentage point, once OPEC's imports from the U.S. picked up. That would be serious but insufficient to cause a steep recession. Furthermore, oil prices did not stay up, implying an even smaller negative."
But oil shows no signs of losing its power to fire the imagination, and the anxiety, of investors. A leading petroleum economist, M. A. Adelman, professor emeritus at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, offers "the comments of some brighter people than myself" to make the point:
Edgar Faure: "Whatever concerns oil is at once adorned with romance. No other raw material so engages the imagination, and inspires so many fine phrases."
And Dr. Samuel Johnson: "The mind which has feasted on a luxurious diet of fiction has small taste for the insipidity of truth."'
If the oil market is putting its money where its intelligence is, the odds are against a gulf war.

HEADLINE: At the Bar

BYLINE: By David Margolick

 BODY:
A teary-eyed, bleary-eyed actress stands in a yuppie kitchen, like the ones in "War of the Roses" or "Fatal Attraction." The distraught woman stares forlornly out the window, runs her fingers through her hair, fidgets with a phone line.
"My husband and I can't even talk to each other!" she laments. "I'm not willing to lose the children or my home! I have so many questions. I have no answers!" 
 Then there is the calm of a man's voice. "A Maryland attorney is standing by to assist you," he purrs in fluent disk-jockese. "Privately. Confidentially. The Legal Help Line till 10 P.M. Only three dollars a minute, first minute free."
Cut back to the woman, now tranquilly lifting the window shade and letting the sun shine in. If the commercial had a classical score, it would now consist of the post-thunderstorm scene from the "William Tell Overture." "I understand my options," the woman says. "Things are looking brighter. I'm starting to see things in a whole new light."
 The tale is only one of three half-minute melodramas that have grown familiar to Maryland cable viewers this month from Annapolis to Havre de Grace. In the second, a woman is fighting with her landlord but cannot afford a lawyer. In the third, a businessman in whose angry hands a telephone receiver could become a lethal weapon curses his perpetually unavailable lawyer. "I've left messages for two days!" he splutters. "I can't wait any longer!" They, too, are rescued by the Legal Help Line.
The line is the brainchild of David Sellman, a 34-year-old practitioner in Baltimore. He is among several lawyers now colonizing the demimonde of the 900 number, that telephonic red light district dominated by purveyors of dates, confessions, and fantasies. Slowly but inexorably, the rapidly growing 1-900 directory lists 4LAW alongside GALS, HOT, HUNK and LIPS.
Already, dial-a-lawyer services are available in California, Texas, Illinois, Georgia and Oklahoma. Then there is Mr. Sellman, who hopes to be to cheapo legal services what Kirk & Kaye were to televisions in "Avalon," the film by his fellow Baltimorean Barry Levinson.
"Although there are a tremendous number of lawyers out there, there are a lot of people who don't have access to one," Mr. Sellman said. "The majority of the population can't afford $150 an hour. And most legal problems can be resolved in relatively short order."
If the quest to perfect dial-a-lawyer services were the space race, Mr. Sellman might be Alan Shepard. But Michael Cane of Huntington Beach, Calif., would be Yuri Gagarin.
That is, while the Baltimore operation is still in its suborbital stages, Tele-Lawyer, Mr. Cane's pioneering enterprise, has begun circling the globe. In California, Hawaii and Alaska already, with Illinois and perhaps New York to follow, people are saying what the faithful Bernstein shouts in the classic Orson Welles movie: "Mr. Kane! Mr. Kane!"
This Mr. Cane, 36, had his epiphany in early 1989, while on sabbatical from the University of Hawaii Law School. While traveling one day to a weed abatement hearing, he was struck by how often he was stopped for curbside legal advice. Everyone had legal problems, he figured; why not create a telephone service offering answers?
Over the next nine months, he set up his operation. It includes an advanced computer system, one with a data base in domestic relations, criminal law, wills and other garden variety areas of concern, as well as an electronic check for conflicts of interest. That complete, he found lawyers to field the calls.
"I advertised a job with no stress, without the gripes, the bitching, the litigation and the tension," he recalled. "They stand in line for this kind of job."
Mr. Cane charges $3 a minute, and the calibrations get even finer than that: if the caller uses A.T.&T., for instance, he is charged five cents a second. Together, Mr. Cane and his lawyers are handling 50 to 80 calls a day, and have yet even to face their first malpractice suit. Indeed, to him the market is, as the economists say, infinitely elastic: Many people cannot afford lawyers, he says, and those who can cannot find them.
"Attorneys have forgotten about service," he said. "They don't call back their clients and when they do, they treat them like idiots."
The new service has not been welcome. When another California company tried to penetrate Oklahoma this month, the State Bar Association started an investigation into possible unauthorized practice of law. But the establishment bar has mostly been quiescent -- perhaps, Mr. Sellman speculated, because it is still napping.
"They've not recoiled because it hasn't come out and hit them yet," he said. "The potential of this thing is tremendous."

HEADLINE: Credit Markets;
Treasury Securities Rise in Price

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
 A plunge in new orders for durable goods during November helped to push up prices of Treasury securities yesterday, as interest rates fell.
The 10.5 percent drop in new orders was at least twice as large as most analysts had forecast. The reported decline accentuated problems currently being experienced in the nation's manufacturing sector. 
 "Everybody was looking for a drop in durables, but not the widespread and steep decline that we got," said William V. Sullivan, Jr., a senior vice president at Dean Witter Reynolds.
The numbers "created the attitude among market participants that the economy was weaker than expected," he said.
 
30-Year Bonds Increase

 The benchmark 30-year bonds rose nearly a half-point and the yield fell to 8.23 percent, compared with 8.28 percent late Wednesday.
The surprising durable goods report was the focal point of the session, but traders and analysts said that action by the Federal Reserve Board to soothe tattered nerves in short-term money markets was also important.
In addition, market participants said that demand at the final auction of new four-year notes was about as expected.
At the auction, where $8.5 billion worth of securities were sold, bids on the notes produced an average yield of 7.66 percent, down sharply from the 8.53 percent average yield at the last four-year note auction on Sept. 26.
As previously announced, beginning in January the Treasury will replace the four-year note sales with monthly auctions of five-year notes.
In other developments, early yesterday the Federal Reserve Board moved to calm participants in short-term money markets by adding what analysts said were significant amounts of reserves to the banking system.
The move came a day after rates on overnight bank loans in the Federal funds market had traded sharply higher for all of Wednesday, reaching a record 100 percent late in the session.
Analysts said the big move in the funds rate was attributed in part to the fact that Wednesday marked the end of a two-week reporting period for the nation's banks, a time when they must report reserve positions to the Fed.
Demand for overnight money is often heavy at such times. In addition, those pressures were compounded by year-end funding needs.
"Year-end funding pressures are nothing new," said Neal M. Soss, chief economist at the First Boston Corporation. "But the intensity with which those pressures have hit this year underscores the nervousness that people are feeling about lending money right now."
 
Fed's Addition of Reserves

 Even with the Fed's addition of reserves yesterday, the funds rate never fell back to the 7 percent level that most analysts see as the Fed's target for the rate. During the day funds traded in a wide range of 10 percent to 7 1/4 percent.
"Even though it didn't have an enormous influence, the Fed's move calmed the market somewhat," Mr. Sullivan said.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered in late trading at a price of 105 5/8, up 15/32, to yield 8.23 percent.
Among note issues, the 8 1/2 percent ten-year notes were offered at 102 7/8, up 9/32, to yield 8.07 percent. And the 7 1/4 percent two-year notes auctioned on Wednesday were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 7.23 percent.
 
 Short-term Treasury bill rates rose slightly.
 In late trading, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 6.47 percent, up one basis point, or hundredth of a percentage point. Six-month bill rates rose by three basis points, to a late offered rate of 6.57 percent.
Activity in the secondary markets for investment-grade corporate bonds and tax-exempt municipal securities was exceptionally slow, dealers reported.
Prices of most investment-grade bonds rose 1/4 point to 1/2 point. Prices of widely quoted municipal bonds rose by about 1/8 point.
Because of the Christmas holiday, the Federal Reserve Board will not release its weekly money supply numbers until today.
Following are the results of yesterday's auction of four-year Treasury notes:

HEADLINE: 3 Youths Seized In 75 Robberies In Last 2 Weeks

BYLINE: By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
A third teen-ager was arrested yesterday and charged with being part of a gang that investigators believe held up at least 75 people at gunpoint in Queens and Nassau County in the last two weeks, the police said.
Two other suspects were captured on Wednesday night after a wild chase and shootout on the Belt Parkway. Investigators said last night that they were searching for two other men they believe took part in the robbery spree, which they said was the most holdups committed by a single gang in Queens in recent memory. 
 "They seemed to accelerate as they went along, doing more and more each time they went out," said Inspector Charles Prestia, commanding officer of the Central Robbery Unit.
Since Dec. 13, when the police first noticed a string of similar robberies in Queens, five teen-agers are believed to have stolen at least 15 cars and cruised throughout Queens and western Nassau County in search of Christmas shoppers loading presents into the trunks of cars, Inspector Prestia said.
Working in pairs or in fours, the gang robbed all the victims at gunpoint on streets or parking lots between 7 and 11 P.M., the police said. They usually began robbing people in northern Queens and worked their way south to the Richmond Hill and South Ozone Park neighborhoods, where most of them live, investigators said.
 
Items Recovered
 The police say they believe that the gang held up at least 75 people before they were caught, taking packages, purses, wallets, jewelry and coats. In two-thirds of the robberies, the victims were hit over the head with a pistol before the robbers spoke a word, Inspector Prestia said. In three cases, the victims were shot without warning before they were robbed. Two of those shot were hospitalized. None suffered life-threatening wounds.
The police did not release the names of the victims yesterday because investigators had not yet conducted lineups to see if the victims could identify the youths. At least 60 victims were brought to the 109th Precinct Station house in Queens last night for the lineups.
Inspector Prestia said the gang switched to a new tactic as Christmas neared. They feigned accidents by bumping into the rear of victims' cars and then robbed the victims when they got out to inspect the damage to their cars, he said.
The two suspects arrested Wednesday night were captured while driving a gray Ford Taurus that had been stolen at gunpoint from a retired police officer earlier that afternoon.
 
Dozens of Police Vehicles
 Two detectives spotted the Taurus outside a Burger King on Rockaway Boulevard and South Conduit Avenue in Jamaica, Queens, about 8 P.M. After a fifteen-minute chase on the Van Wyck and Belt Parkways, the two suspects were penned in by dozens of police vehicles a half-mile from the Lefferts Boulevard exit on the Van Wyck.
The driver of the stolen car was identified as Harold Marshall, 18 years old, of 133-14 Linden oulevard in Richmond Hill, Queens, investigators said. Mr. Marshall, who is unemployed and does not attend high school, was arrested on attempted murder charges in April 1989 in the Bedford-Stuyvesant section of Brooklyn in a case that is still pending, the police said.
The passenger in the car, Michael Wells, 17, of 133-08 132d Street in Richmond Hill, also was arrested in August in South Jamaica, Queens, on an armed robbery charge.
Items said to have been stolen in 11 robberies earlier Wednesday evening -- handbags, a leather coat, money and gold chains -- were found inside the Taurus, the police said.
The police yesterday afternoon arrested a third youth, Norris Thomas, 17, in a car outside his mother's home at 790 Eldert Lane in the East New York section of Brooklyn. Items from at least 13 robberies was found in the car's truck, the police said.
Mr. Thomas's parents said he had been arrested several times on robbery and burglary charges in the past, had served seven months in prison on one conviction and had dropped out of school. His father, also Norris Thomas, said he had bought his son a set of turntables for Christmas so that he could start a business mixing rap music tapes.
"He's been trying to stay out of trouble," Mr. Thomas said. "He's been doing good. We give him enough money so that he doesn't have to go out and rob people to get it."
