HEADLINE: AIDS in Women Rising, but Many Ignore the Threat

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO

 BODY:
After two years of marriage and three children, a 21-year-old woman in the Bronx learned six months ago that she carries the AIDS virus. Her 40-year-old husband is a prime suspect in the infection, but so is her first boyfriend, the only two sexual partners she said she has had.
To this day, as she copes with the stunning discovery and feelings of guilt and anger over the poor health of her 17-month-old son, who was born infected, the woman still does not know whom to blame. Her former boyfriend can't be found, and her husband refuses to be tested. 
 "You hear about AIDS on the radio or on TV and you don't pay attention," she said, explaining why she had never given condoms a thought."All I heard," she said, pulling up two wisps of her long black hair and rolling her eyes upward to illustrate her state of mind when she fell in love, "was little birds carrying me."
The story of this woman, who spoke on the condition that her name not be disclosed, is a familiar one in New York and other cities, where the disease is concentrated largely in poor minority communities. But eventhough reported AIDS cases among women are growing faster than among men, many women still do not perceive themselves as vulnerable. Even when they do, those who treat and counsel women say, factors like traditional sex roles and economic hardship make it difficult for many to change the practices that put them at risk.
The use of condoms, which offer protection for the sexually active, is not in many cases the woman's prerogative, these professionals say. They note that many women, in their role as nurturers, tend to think last of their own health.
Even more daunting, how do you convince a woman who is married, or in a long-term monogamous relationship, that she, too, could be at risk? In large cities like New York, which has the largest number of AIDS cases in the country, geography itself is arisk factor for any woman, AIDS experts say.
"I can engage in high-risk behavior in the Dakotas with very little likelihood of acquiring AIDS," said Dr. Howard Minkoff, professor of obstetrics and gynecology and also obstetrics director at the State University of New York Health Science Center in Brooklyn. "If I practice the same behavior in the South Bronx, my chances of acquiring the disease increase tremendously."
Infected mothers often have infected children. Officials at the Federal Centers for Disease Control in Atlanta estimate that nearly 6,000 women with the HIV virus, which causes AIDS, gave birth last year in the nation. About a third of the newborns whose mother carry the virus become in fected themselves. In some areas of New York City, as many as 2 percent of all infants were born of infected mothers between November 1987 and September 1990, the New York State Department of Health has found.
Because of the misperception of risk, many women find out they are infected only when their infected child becomes ill. Advocates like Act-Up, the AIDS Coalition to Unleash Power, blame the Centers for Disease Control in part for this, because it does not include in its list of AIDS-defining illnesses the symptoms often seen in infected women -- for example, pelvic inflammatory disease, an infection of the fallopian tubes or the uterus.
Many doctors, in turn, fail to associate gynecological disorders like this with the HIV virus, critics argue, depriving many women of early diagnosis and treatment and of benefits for people disabled by the virus, like Social Security disability payments.
 
Most Victims From Poor Areas
 Dr. Anthony S. Fauci, director of the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious Diseases, acknowledges that research on AIDS in women has been limited, although he says it is receiving increased attention. One problem, he notes, is that most women with AIDS come, as most drug addicts do, from poor, minority areas with inadequate health care.
But whether for lack of early recognition or access to treatment, studies show that women with AIDS do not survive nearly as long after diagnosis as men do.
Federal and city officials say reported AIDS cases are growing faster among women than among men. And for women, cases attributed to intercourse with infected men are rising faster than those stemming from the sharing of needles during drug use.
In New York City, where AIDS has become the leading cause of death in women 25 to 34 years old, reported cases in women rose 36 percent in the last 12 months, compared with 28 percent for men, said Dr. Polly Thomas, director of AIDS and HIV surveillance for the city's Health Department.
 
Heterosexual AIDS Threat
 From 1988 to 1989, officials at the Centers for Disease Control say, the number of AIDS cases in women nationwide increased 29 percent, compared with 18 percent in men. Women account for 4,142, or 14 percent, of all adult cases in New York City and 10 percent, or 15,133 cases, nationwide.
Although the overwhelming majority of AIDS cases in New York City women involve intravenous drug use rather than sex with infected men (61 percent versus 25 percent), female cases attributed to heterosexual sex rose by 39 percent over the last 12 months, compared with 37 percent for those from drug use, Dr. Thomas said.
The same trend is seen nationwide, said Ruth L. Berkelman, chief of the HIV and AIDS surveillance branch at the Centers for Disease Control. She said heterosexual AIDS cases in women rose 34 percent, compared with 19 percent for drug-use cases.
New York City Health Department figures show that 46 percent of AIDS cases among women under the age of 24 in the city stemmed from sex with an infected man, double the rate found among women who are over 25.
Health professionals say that as a group, adolescents face a high risk of infection because they are more likely to have more than one sex partner, usually someone older.
 
Warnings Often Dismissed
 Many women, AIDS prevention workers said, dismiss safe sex warnings because they link risk to "groups" rather than individual behavior. Some women, for instance, mistakenly believe that if they quit intravenous drugs, they are no longer at risk, overlooking sexual transmission.
The New York State Department of Health projects that in 1995 there will be more new AIDS cases among women who are sex partners of intravenous drug users than among homosexual and bisexual men, the group first hit by the epidemic.
"I knew you could get it from being an intravenous drug user, and I wasn't, and I wasn't sleeping with a homosexual," recalled Tina B., a 21-year-old Bronx woman who was infected by a man she lived with for two years and who now attends an AIDS program for adolescents. "I knew how it was transmitted, but I didn't think he had it or that I'd get it."
In recent interviews, infected women cited other factors for not using condoms or asking questions about a partner's sexual history or submitting to the test for the HIV virus before pregnancy.
 
Feeling of Invulnerability
 For some, particularly teen-agers, there is a feeling of invulnerability to the disease. For others, the risk is too intangible and the disease seems too distant, particularly if they are more preoccupied with finding a place to live or putting food on the table.
And as with many men, some women simply refuse to deal with the responsibility.
A 28-year-old Brooklyn woman who is awaiting the results of her HIV test said: "He informed me. I was aware. We were using condoms, but I didn't like them. I figure if God is going to take me, He's going to take me anyway."
But many other women know the risk and want protection, yet are not able to negotiate or demand measures against infection, AIDS prevention workers say. Many women depend on men for economic support and fear jeopardizing the relationship by insisting that their partners use a condom or be tested for the AIDS virus.
Because of these complexities, outreach organizations like Life Force in Brooklyn are happy to raise the issue for discussion at least, so women can start thinking about HIV infection and make informed choices. Life Force, some of whose members are women infected with the AIDS virus, goes into beauty shops, housing project lobbies, health clinics and out on the streets to reach women. Its representatives, armed with literature, dispense practical advice.
 
Overcoming Opposition
 The group tells mothers to think of their children and what would happen to them if the mother got sick. They tell women where to go for help if they are battered, need shelter or a drug program. They share ways to get resistant partners to use condoms.
"I personally tell women to lie to their husband and tell them they have a vaginal infection," said Iris de la Cruz, a 37-year-old former drug addict and the sexual partner of an intravenous drug user. She was diagnosed as having AIDS three years ago.
Ravinia Hayes-Cozier, director of AIDS education for the New York City Department of Health said that "the kind of attention that needs to be given has not been given to women and AIDS" but that her office was preparing to focus more on the problem. She stressed that putting out prevention messages was not enough. Educating women about the AIDS risk must deal with everything from acquainting them with a condom to teaching them how to bring up the subject with a partner, she said.
"If we're going to have an impact, we have to treat the relationship," she said, adding that women were "going to get resistance."
 
Love and Anger
 In the Bronx, the 21-year-old mother who recently learned of her infection with the AIDS virus has yet to convince her 40-year-old husband to be tested for the virus. She strongly suspects him, and their relationship now alternates between love and anger, the kind that one day led her to throw her wedding band out of an apartment window.
"When I heard HIV, I said, 'My kid doesn't have that -- I know I don't have it,' " she said in tears, recalling the moment when she received the results of the baby's test. "And then I thought of my husband. He's the one that's had a lot of relationships."
But she also desperately needs him, she said, to take care of her other children, a 2 1/2-year-old daughter and a 17-month-old boy, if she becomes ill. Both have tested negative for the virus.
Her other 17-month-old boy, the infected twin, has been hospitalized three times with pneumonia and other ailments, although the mother remains in good health. The mother hopes to live to see her baby through the disease.
"My son is my main thing right now," she said.

HEADLINE: LBJ Stations Up for Sale

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: AUSTIN, Tex., Dec. 27

 BODY:
 The family of former President Lyndon B. Johnson has put the KLBJ radio stations on the market, a company executive said today.
John Barr, executive vice president of the LBJ Company, said the family had retained the Ted Hepburn Company to consider offers for the AM and FM stations in Austin. He said their value had been placed at $13.5 million. 
 The Johnson family has owned the business for more than 40 years. The family tried to sell it in 1987 for $27.5 million, but the deal fell through when the Chase Group, a New York-based investment company, did not meet the deadline to complete the sale.
Bill Jamar, whose Jamar & Associates handled the 1987 sales attempt, said it would be hard to get $13.5 million in today's economic climate.
But Mr. Barr said: "The stations are basically making money. They always have."

HEADLINE: Dow Declines by 11.63 After a Fire Delay

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
Stocks sagged yesterday in a session shortened by a transformer fire that delayed trading until 11 A.M., or an hour and a half after the normal opening.
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 11.63 points, to 2,625.50. In the broader market, 892 stocks fell while only 598 advanced. Activity in the truncated session rose to 102.9 million shares from 78.7 million the day before. 
 A Fire Department spokesman said 100 to 200 workers in an office building at 55 Water Street were evacuated between 2:30 and 3 A.M. after the explosion of a Con Ed transformer enclosure outside the building sent flames eight stories high, broke windows on three floors and filled parts of the 54-story tower with acrid smoke. The building houses computer operations shared by the New York Stock Exchange, the American Stock Exchange and regional exchanges throughout the country.
Officials of the exchanges said that after inspecting the site for health hazards, the city's Environmental Protection Department permitted workers to go into the building at 10 A.M.
Ronald J. Hill, investment strategist for Brown Brothers Harriman, said newly released data on big-ticket durable goods orders for November were "significantly below expectations," helping to push bond prices higher and, for a time, lifting stock prices. He said that except for holding volume down, the fire had "not much effect on prices."
Birinyi Associates, which monitors program trading, said two selling waves in the afternoon had knocked 16 points off the Dow.
"Stocks sold off because of a lack of interest," Mr. Hill remarked. "People just don't want to do anything at the end of the year, facing the uncertainty in the Middle East."
He noted that oil and financial stocks were strong Wednesday but gave back some of their gains yesterday.
Crude oil, which rose $1.30 a barrel on Wednesday for its biggest gain in three weeks, fell 21 cents yesterday, to $27.01.
Occidental Petroleum fell 5/8, to 19; ARCO 1 1/2, to 122 7/8; Phillips Petroleum3/8, to 26 1/2; Chevron 5/8, to 72 5/8, and Columbia Gas 7/8, to 46 1/2. Chemical Bank fell 1/4, to 11 1/8; Chase Manhattan 1/2, to 10 1/8, and General Electric 1/8, to 57 1/4. Bankers Trust New York slid 2, to 42 1/8, and J. P. Morgan 2, to 44.
In yet another sign of Wall Street nervousness over weak sales or earnings, Tiffany lost 2 1/4, to 35, on top of a 4 3/8 decline a day earlier after it reported holiday sales gains that Wall Street found disappointing. It was the ninth-most-active Big Board stock and the only one of the 10 to move as much as a point.
UAL, which rose 2 on Wednesday with no news to account for it, fell 2, to 113 yesterday. It was the only significant move among the Dow transportation stocks.
Digital Equipment fell 1 5/8, to 56 1/4, in what seemed like profit taking. Other technology stocks were mixed, with I.B.M. up 1/8, to 113 5/8, and Motorola down 1, to 52 1/4.
Ralston Purina, which had been lagging behind other food stocks over concern about its growth, rose 1 1/4, to 101. John M. McMillin, food analyst at Prudential-Bache, said early this month that growth prospects would improve with lower grain costs and declining interest rates.

HEADLINE: Big Gain in Nursing Students Lifts Hopes Amid a Shortage

BYLINE: By TAMAR LEWIN

 BODY:
Nursing school enrollment rose sharply this year, according to a new study, raising hopes that the four-year-old nursing shortage may be easing.
Preliminary results from the study, by the National League for Nursing, found that about 230,000 students were enrolled in registered nurse programs this fall, a 14 percent increase over last year's enrollment of 201,458. The highest enrollment in the past 20 years was 250,000 students in 1983. 
 While the current shortage still has many hospitals scrambling to find enough qualified nurses, experts in the field say they expect it to ease gradually over the next few years.
 
'Beginning to Subside'
 "I think the nursing shortage is beginning to subside, not just because of rising enrollments, but because of increased sensitivity to the idea that nurses are a scarce resource, and should not be used to fill in for orderlies, bookkeepers and dietitians," said Peri Rosenfeld, of the National League for Nursing, a nonprofit research and advocacy group based in New York. "Hospitals are rethinking what nurses should do, and freeing them from other tasks to do real nursing."
She and others said the revamping of nursing jobs, plus rising salaries and an improving image of the nursing profession, had combined to make nursing a more attractive career.
At the University of Texas nursing school in San Antonio, there have been far more applicants than the school could handle for the last few terms.
"We admit about 90 full-time students and 15 part-time students twice a year, and for the fall term, we had 121 students on our waiting list," said Barbara Lust, the associate dean of student affairs. "And we've probably got 70 on the waiting list for the spring term. Back in 1985, we admitted everybody who was eligible and still had a few spaces."
With the economic downturn limiting job opportunities in other fields, experts say, nursing school is likely to become an even more popular option.
There is a consensus among nursing educators and students that the image of nursing is improving because of television shows like "China Beach" and other favorable representations and because of the increasing responsibilities that nurses bear.
"My grandparents' initial reaction was, 'Why do you want to change bedpans for the rest of your life?' and when I first told my friends, they said I should be a lawyer or a doctor, but that attitude is changing," said Valerie Rotter, a 20-year-old junior at Rutgers University College of Nursing. "In my class, we're a pretty smart bunch of people. Nurses are a whole new breed these days, with our own theorists and our own set of diagnoses. It's a lot more than bedpans, or waiting to hear the doctors' point of view."
 
More Older Students
 Nursing schools, where programs can last two, three or four years, are also attracting a new and older breed of students, the National League of Nursing says. The average age of new nurses is now 31, as against 24 or 25 in the early 1980's. More than half are married, Ms. Rosenfeld said, and about half of the married ones have children.
"We're seeing many more older, second-career women, many of them with young children at home, who see that nursing can be a challenging and flexible job for moms," said Ms. Rosenfeld. "What we're not seeing is much growth in the number of men who become nurses."
Men have long made up less that 5 percent of registered nurses. While a few universities, like the one in San Antonio, have more than 10 percent male students, most see little growth in the number of men in the field.
Ken Dion, 29, who is in his last year of a four-year nursing program at the University of Central Florida in Orlando, was a paramedic for several years before enrolling.
"I enjoyed being a paramedic, but I saw nursing as a way to expand and refine my skills, and give me more latitude," he said. "I talk to men my age, and I tell them when I get out of school, I can travel all over the world. There are all kinds of opportunities for me."
 
Continued Demand Is Seen
 Indeed, just as nursing school enrollment was dipping below 185,000 in 1987 and 1988, the demand for nurses in non-hospital jobs soared. Thousands of nurses left low-paid, stressful hospital jobs for work with insurance companies, health maintenance organizations or home care agencies that offered better hours and less demanding work at comparable pay.
As a result, many hospitals faced a sudden shortage of nurses, in some cases so serious that beds in some units had to be closed, and others were staffed largely with nurses hired by the day, or those newly recruited from overseas.
"There are certain kinds of shifts, and certain kinds of nurses, and certain inner-city hospitals where the shortage is still very acute," said Judith Huntington, director of governmental affairs at the American Nurses Association in Washington. "It's excellent that enrollment is rising. But with an aging society, the demand for nurses is going to continue to grow, well beyond the year 2000."
The Bureau of Labor Statistics has estimated that the health care industry would create 350,000 new jobs for registered nurses by the year 2000.
"It's great that enrollment is up, but that's not enough," said Connie Curran, a private consultant on nursing who lives in Chicago. "For one thing, for every 10 people who enroll, only five graduate, and of those five another doesn't pass the qualifying exams. And also, even a good new supply of nurses doesn't solve the real issue of creating a work environment that will keep people."
While nursing shortages have generally been cyclical, there was serious concern that the sharp drop in the number of students enrolled in nursing programs in the mid-1980's signaled a more basic professional reordering, because of more opportunities for women in higher-paying fields.
As a result, hospitals wooing nurses raised salaries substantially, from a national average of $14,000 for starting nurses in 1980 to $25,000 now. Many began offering sign-up bonuses, tuition and housing credits and other incentives. In some large cities, experienced nurses can now earn more than $60,000.
"I've wanted to be a nurse ever since I was eight," said Kami Zartman, 21, who will graduate from Ohio State University's nursing school in June. "It's a really hard, professional fulfilling job, and I think we are goin to have to do better at getting that image across, especially in high schools."

HEADLINE: War Effect On Energy Minimized

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Bush Administration officials contend that if war erupted in the Persian Gulf, it would pose few disruptions to energy supplies, even though the region holds two-thirds of the world's oil.
Government and industry planners say the risk of damage to oilfields there is only minimal. They say that even some loss of output from Saudi Arabia could be sustained with little effect on consumers or the world economy.
By drawing upon strategic and commercial stockpiles and buying oil already aboard tankers and awaiting customers, these analysts say, it should be possible to avoid any oil shortage, as well as to keep prices from rising, as they did after Iraq's invasion of Kuwait in August. 

 
Adequate Protection
 "I am confident that the superb military force we have in the gulf is more than adequate to protect Saudi and United Arab Emirate oilfields from Iraqi military attack," Energy Secretary James D. Watkins, a former admiral, said earlier this month upon returning from a trip to the Middle East. "While there may be a temporary, even anticipated, reduction of activity in the fields adjacent to the Kuwaiti border to assess the situation, this should have virtually no effect on the availability of supplies to the market."
Nevertheless, Mr. Watkins said the markets would probably not behave rationally -- that is, share the official view that there would be no shortage if war broke out. He said he would promptly recommend, therefore, that President Bush begin to sell oil from the nation's Strategic Petroleum Reserve, now holding about 590 million barrels, if war broke out.
 
Keeping Prices Down
 This drawdown, at an eventual maximum rate of 3.5 million barrels a day and in coordination with other consuming countries, would presumably keep prices in check, Mr. Watkins and other Government planners maintain.
Industry and other private analysts, although perhaps less sanguine about prices, do not generally dispute the Government's optimistic assessment about oil's availability in the event of a military showdown with Saddam Hussein.
"An oilfield is not something that's easily blown up," said Marshall W. Nichols, executive director of the National Petroleum Council, an industry group that advises the Government. The council was asked by Secretary Watkins in October to make an assessment of supply and demand through mid-1991, including "an analysis of the factors that can affect those estimates," presumably war would be among them.
Mr. Nichols said this week that while there could be "fear of damage," which might hamper transport -- for example, the inability of tankers to obtain insurance -- he remained confident that world inventories would prove adequate.
 
Reliable Reserves
 He said the nation's strategic oil reserve, which was tested during the fall, could safely be relied upon to move oil into the market promptly -- within two weeks being the department's stated goal. Some military analysts predict a war with Iraq would be won in as little as five days.
His only nagging concern, Mr. Nichols said, was the ease with which foreign tankers could receive waivers to the law that now restricts oil cargoes between American ports to American ships.
Vito Stagliano, a senior Energy Department policy analyst, said in an interview that while some production in northeastern Saudi Arabia would be at risk in war, the worst that could realistically happen would be the loss of two million barrels a day, barely one-quarter of that country's current, much-expanded production level.
 
Estimate of Supplies
 Mr. Stagliano estimated worldwide strategic oil supplies controlled directly or indirectly by governments at one billion barrels and commercial stocks at 80 million to 100 million barrels above minimum operating levels. In addition, he said, producing countries' ships in the Mideast and Western Europe are carrying 50 million barrels.
Since it takes a month or more for tankers to reach the United States from the Persian Gulf, it would take that long for any supply shortage to be felt, assuming the oil was not bid away from the buyers in transit.
Analysts stress that oil is a world-traded commodity, the biggest by far, and that the most important factor in any assessment is how much is reaching the total market, not how much a particular country may get from one or another source.
Mr. Stagliano held it to be "not rationally feasible to imagine" world production losses as high as 3.5 million barrels a day, the amount planners figure could be readily provided for six months from Government-controlled stocks. As much as 4 million barrels a day could be provided for a shorter period, planners say.
Iraq and Kuwait were producing 4.3 million barrels a day when their oil was embargoed. This has now been fully offset by a surge of production from other countries. More than half the additional oil during the fourth quarter came from Saudi Arabia.
 
Consumption Is Falling
 With inventories high, and American oil consumption already down 5 percent without much effect yet from recession, Administration officials say their contingency plans do not include forcing consumers to use less oil.
Higher prices would achieve much of whatever conservation became necessary, Mr. Stagliano said, adding that "we would not have any major intrusive action" by the Government, like prohibiting gasoline sales on Sundays or instructing wholesalers where to send supplies.
Nor are planners making much effort to cut deals for special emergency supplies with producers outside the Persian Gulf -- most are near maximum production anyway. Such moves are viewed as shifting supplies within a system in which oil is to be shared among members of the International Energy Agency, a unit of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development in Paris, rather than adding to total supplies.
This view is held despite Government acknowledgment last week that in partly lifting a boycott of Iranian oil, it was adding slightly to the nation's supply options.
For all its confidence about availability of oil, the Administration is not at all certain it can avoid another price spike should war break out. Thus, the jawboning by Secretary Watkins:
"I would hope players in the oil market would keep their hats on if hostilities develop," he said in his address to the Council on Foreign Relations. "Is calm, common sense and perspective in order then? Yes. But, should we expect the market to behave in such a rational manner? Probably not. And isn't this exactly why we have the Strategic Petroleum Reserve?"

HEADLINE: Cold Devastates California Crops; Fruit Prices Across Nation to Rise

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Dec. 27

 BODY:
California's worst winter cold in more than half a century has devastated the state's $8 billion fruit and vegetable industry, and agriculture officials said today that fruit prices around the country would rise as a result.
Farmers called on officials to declare parts of the state a disaster area after a prolonged and widespread freeze that brought record low temperatures to many places. More freezing temperatures are forecast for this weekend and are expected to kill some of the surviving crops. 
 "I don't think there is any question that the prices will go up and in some cases have already gone up," said Robert C. Fox, chief deputy director of the State Department of Food and Agriculture. California produces 26 percent of the nation's orange crop and 75 percent of the oranges that are eaten.
Much of the state's citrus crop has been ruined, and farmers said the cold had killed many of the trees. The damage has extended to strawberries, avocados, carrots, lettuce, artichokes, broccoli, cauliflower and celery. Agriculture officials said prices were also likely to increase for other fruit, like apples, that would be in demand as substitutes.
The state's flower crop also suffered, and people working on floats for the Tournament of Roses parade in Pasadena said they were using substitute decorations.
In Riverside, Ron Hamilton, a meteorologist at the National Weather Service's Office for Agriculture, said temperatures in the San Joaquin Valley, where most citrus crops are grown, were the coldest since 1913. He said the current cold wave, affecting broad areas of the state, was comparable to the freeze of 1937, which produced statewide crop damage.
The low temperatures were caused by an enormous blast of Arctic air that descended over the state last week before heading eastward and bringing record low temperatures to dozens of cities in Utah, Montana, Wyoming, Nevada, the Dakotas and Nebraska.
As the temperature fell to below freezing over the weekend, bringing light snow to parts of the state where it is virtually unheard of, farmers worked desperately and mostly in vain to protect their crops from the chill.
"The farmers are in shock; you can hear it in their voices," said Barbara Buck, a spokesman for the Western Growers Association, a farmers' trade association. "There was really nothing they could do. The Arctic storm came. They tried to stop it with the smudge pots and wind machines and watering the trees, and there is even some sort of polymer like an anti-frost protection, but all this technology didn't work."
Mike Stuart, a senior vice president of the growers association, said that even with advanced warning of this weekend's cold there is little that can be done to prevent crop damage because of the severity of the freeze.
Wholesale prices increased immediately, and supermarkets reported that they were beginning to raise their prices for fruits and vegetables.
 
Delayed Effect Nationally
 Jan DeLyser, executive vice president of the Fresh Produce Council, a trade association based in Los Angeles, said the retail price increases would take effect nationally after several days or even weeks.
She said the wholesale price of a box of navel oranges at California produce markets rose from about $12 last weekend to about $22 today.
Frank Limacher, an agriculture economist with the state agriculture department, said the retail price of oranges had also begun to rise and that there were reports of 100 percent increases at some markets.
Claire Peters, a spokesman for Sunkist Growers Inc., in Sherman Oaks, said: "There will be citrus fruit in the stores, but the cost will be higher. Our guess is that prices could conceivably double."
 
Herald of Price Increases
 As one herald of increases, the New York Cotton Exchange reported that the price of concentrated orange juice for delivery in March had risen about 15 percent over the last 10 days, to about $1.19 a pound. Ms. Peters said it would take 10 more days to determine the full extent of damage to oranges, lemons, grapefruit and tangerines.
With damage to fruits and vegetables expected to rise into the hundreds of millions of dollars, growers were asking Gov. George Deukmejian for disaster relief. County agriculture commissioners are assessing the damage and reporting to the California Department of Food and Agriculture, Ms. Buck said.
Declaration of a local disaster would be the first step toward asking for Federal disaster relief. The relief would offer some compensation to farmers who lose major portions of their crop and see little benefit from the increased prices.
Bob Krauter, a spokesman for the California Farm Bureau Federation, which represents 83,000 farmers, said 80 percent of the state's navel oranges were still on the trees when the frost hit and that most were destroyed. But he added that some of the frozen oranges could be processed for juice.
 
Crop Value Put at $438 Million
 California is the leading producer of navel oranges and is second, after Florida, in overall production of oranges. Ms. Buck said the navel orange crop was valued at $458 million in 1988.
"Many of the buds for next year's crop have been damaged," she said. "The farmers have not only lost this year's crop and next year's, but tree limbs have split and they have lost their trees."
Mr. Krauter said the Valencia orange crop, which is not harvested until March, is still golf-ball sized on the trees and could be devastated by the drop in temperatures that is forecast for this weekend.
He said as much as 15 percent of the state's avocado crop, which is valued at $200 million, has been damaged, forcing a rise in prices that are already steep. California produces 85 percent of the nation's avocados.
"Artichokes are also hit hard," Ms. Buck said. "They are lying on their side." The state's crop is worth $38 million.
Other winter vegetables, including celery, broccoli and cauliflower, are hardier but have also been affected. Delays in harvesting caused by the freeze will cause price increases, Mr. Krauter said.
 
Temperatures in the Low 20's
 The record cold, coming after a summer of record heat, was part of a cold wave that has gripped the Rocky Mountains and the American West.
Beginning on the winter solstice last Friday, an Arctic weather mass moved across California, sending temperatures to the low 20's and the teens in the northern and central parts of the state.
A rare snowfall was reported in a number of communities in central California, the state's primary agricultural area, including Sacramento, Monterey, Fresno and Bakersfield.
Light snow also fell along the coast of Southern California from the Palos Verdes peninsula to San Clemente, and temperatures dropped to near freezing in the Los Angeles metropolitan area.

GRAPHIC: Photo: A widespread freeze that brought record low temperatures has devastated California's fruit and vegetable crops. Above, a damaged tree on a citrus farm in Santa Paula, about 50 miles northwest of Los Angeles. (Fred Stocker for The New York Times) (pg. A14)

HEADLINE: U.S. to Start Random Testing of Milk For Residues of Agricultural Drugs

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
In response to persistent criticism from Congress and consumer groups about veterinary drug residues in milk, the Food and Drug Administration said today that starting early next year it would begin the first national program for regular random testing of milk.
The aim of the new program will be to detect antibiotics and sulfa drugs. The agency said that it had chosen 250 of the largest milk processing plants around the country and that beginning as early as February, it would randomly draw samples each week from milk tanker trucks arriving at five of the plants from dairy farms. 
 The move was welcomed by the dairy industry but was denounced as insufficient by a leading consumer group and a frequent critic of the Federal agency, Representative Ted Weiss, Democrat of Manhattan.
 
Regulation of Drugs
 Dr. Gerald B. Guest, director of the agency's Center for Veterinary Medicine, said the Government was most concerned about milk containing trace levels of eight types of sulfa drugs and three types of tetracycline, all of which are widely used by dairy farmers to fight bacterial infections of the lungs and udders in their animals.
These drugs are legal for use in dairy animals. But the Government bars all except one of them from being present in a cow's system when it is giving milk. The only exception is sulfadimethoxine, whose concentrations in milk are limited by Federal regulation.
The drugs, at least one of which, sulfamethazine, has been shown to cause cancer in laboratory animals when administered in large doses, have been widely detected in earlier spot surveys of milk conducted by states and the Food and Drug Administration.
Besides fearing a risk of cancer, health experts have also expressed concern that residues of antibiotics in milk and meat could cause bacteria to develop immunity to the drugs and reduce their effectiveness in treating illness in humans.
Until now, the burden of testing milk for safety has fallen on the states and the dairy industry itself. "We are supplementing what the states already do to test milk," Dr. Guest said in a telephone interview today. "The states look primarily for penicillin and penicillin-like drugs. We're going to use new detection methods to look for other drugs, because they aren't being routinely looked for and they have the potential for being widely used."
The drug agency said that it believed the American milk supply was safe but that a nationwide monitoring program would deter farmers from using the drugs illegally. A similar program, using the most advanced drug-residue detection system on the market, was begun in 1988 in Idaho. The proportion of milk samples in which illegally used drugs are detected there has since declined to almost zero from more than 5 percent, an official of the Idaho Department of Agriculture said.
A spokesman for the National Milk Producers Federation in Alexandria, Va., one of the dairy industry's principal trade groups, said farmers supported the new national milk testing program.
 
Plan Called Inadequate
 But Mr. Weiss, who heads a House Government Operations subcommittee that has spent years investigating the F.D.A. program for regulating the $2.7 billion animal drug industry, said the new project was an insignificant attempt to quell public anxiety about the safety of milk.
"Testing five milk samples per week means that each processing location in F.D.A.'s program will be visited only once a year," a statement issued by Mr. Weiss said. "F.D.A.'s attempt to describe this as a nationwide program is ludicrous. To even call it a modest proposal is a wild exaggeration."
When told of Mr. Weiss's comments, Dr. Guest said: "It is modest. We don't quarrel with that. It's all we can do with the resources we have for this program. But we think we can get good coverage because of the randomness, and it will be a deterrent. Where the drug is found, we can go back to the farm and take action."
The issue for consumers is whether drinking milk containing trace levels of sulfamethazine and the 10 other compounds is dangerous. Dr. Martin Terry, a toxicologist with the Animal Health Institute, a Virginia-based trade group for the veterinary drug industry, noted today that sulfa drugs and tetracycline were commonly used in human medicine at far higher concentrations to treat urinary tract ailments and other infections, without causing harmful side effects.
"If you're a mother of three, you've probably used tetracycline to treat infections," Dr. Terry said. "Does it make sense to worry about it? There are better uses for our resources."
 
Listing as Carcinogen
 But technical experts with several consumer groups noted that the agency had identified sulfamethazine as a carcinogen and that there was some evidence that sulfa drugs and tetracycline as a whole can cause allergic reactions in some milk drinkers. "The point is, Why deliberately introduce any carcinogen into the food supply?" asked Lisa Lefferts, staff scientist at the Center for Science in the Public Interest, a Washington-based consumer group that, like Mr. Weiss, characterized the new program as insufficient.
Sulfamethazine, an anti-bacterial compound, is perhaps the most widely used of the 11 drugs that the Federal agency will look for in milk next year. Because it is inexpensive and effective against an array of infections, sulfamethazine made it possible to improve the economic efficiency of producing milk by confining large numbers of infection-prone animals in one place.
But since 1980 the F.D.A. has considered sulfamethazine to be a carcinogen. Based on new studies linking large quantities of the drug to cancers in rats and mice, the F.D.A. announced in November 1989 that it was taking action to ban the sale or use of sulfamethazine in agriculture. Dr. Guest said today that final action against the drug could occur next year.

The Economy
Orders for durable goods fell sharply. In yet another ominous economic pulse-taking, the Government reported that orders to factories making all sorts of long-lived goods from watches to locomotives tumbled 10.5 percent in November, equaling a 32-year record drop established in January. The drop was particularly sharp for transportation equipment, but weakness was shown by most leading industries. [Page A1.]

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1011 words

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
GULF G.I.'S TO GET GERM WAR SHOTS

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Acting on C.I.A. warnings that Iraq has developed biological weapons, the Pentagon is planning to begin vaccinating American troops in the Persian Gulf, military officials said today.
A Defense Department spokesman refused to comment on the plans today. But senior Administration officials said the Pentagon had recommended starting the inoculation program. 
 The Pentagon plan is part of a broad effort to improve defenses against biological weapons, including the use of masks and protective garments.
 
Chemical Weapons Used
 Iraq has used chemical weapons against Kurds in its own country and against Iran during the Iran-Iraq war. But President Saddam Hussein has repeatedly denied that his nation has biological weapons.
In September, William H. Webster, the Director of Central Intelligence, said that "Iraq has a sizable stockpile of chemical and biological weapons." Government officials said Mr. Webster was reacting to intelligence reports that Iraq will be able to deliver significant quantities of biological agents on the battlefield by early next year.
Administration officials describe the vaccination plan as a precaution in light of these intelligence findings and deny that they are trying to stir new alarm about Iraq's military abilities. Washington officials have been worried that the vaccination effort could disturb the Saudis, other members of the anti-Iraq coalition and some American troops. This is because the United States does not have enough vaccine to inoculate all American troops, let alone all friendly troops and Saudi citizens.
It is unclear how many troops will actually be vaccinated under the program, but officials said the Pentagon plans to first inoculate those Americans who are most likely to be threatened by biological weapons.
Biological weapons, unlike chemical weapons, are based on living agents like viruses and bacteria. They can be used to spread diseases like typhoid, cholera and anthrax.
Vaccines for the biological agents considered most likely to be employed by Iraq would be produced in much the same way as the smallpox vaccines administered worldwide, using weakened or dead bacteria to stimulate the production of antibodies by human immune systems.
 
Effort to Expand Supply
 An Administration official said that the Pentagon plans to begin the inoculations "in a matter of weeks" and that an effort is being made to rapidly expand the production of vaccines so that more Americans can be inoculated.
But the official declined to say exactly how many would initially be inoculated, when the United States would have enough vaccine to protect all of its troops, what specific vaccines are being developed or where they are being manufactured.
Even though supplies of vaccines are limited, Administration officials defended the Pentagon plan, saying it is better to vaccinate some troops than not to vaccinate any. And they said that those who are not vaccinated can be given some protection by masks and special protective clothing. They also said that some of the vaccine is being reserved for use after a possible biological weapons attack as an antidote, and that antibiotics are also available.
 
Control Is Difficult
 Some officials say Iraq might be unwilling to use biological weapons because it is difficult for an attacker to control the biological agents and prevent them from infecting his own troops. Also, the United States has said that it would unleash devastating retaliation if Iraq employed chemical or biological weapons.
The Pentagon's plans were disclosed as President Bush vowed again not to compromise with President Hussein.
"There is not going to be a compromise with this man, because it's the wrong signal to send to people around the world who are together" against Iraq, Mr. Bush told reporters on the White House lawn.
Mr. Bush also asserted that he was "very comfortable" with the combat readiness of American forces despite the public statement by Lieut. Gen. Calvin A. H. Waller, deputy commander of American forces in the Persian Gulf, that American forces in the region may not be fully combat-ready until mid-February, comments that echoed the private views of other Army and Marine Corps officials.
White House officials have been concerned that the commanders' assessments of the readiness of their units would undercut the Bush Administration's efforts to coerce Mr. Hussein to withdraw his troops from Kuwait. Mr. Bush appeared to reflect such concern in his comments today.
"I've said all I want to say about readiness," Mr. Bush said. "I think it would be very useful if from the President and others there were fewer comments about readiness."
Several news organizations reported this week that Defense Secretary Dick Cheney and Gen. Colin L. Powell, chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, had informed Mr. Bush that General Waller's comments reflected the dominant view within the American military about the combat readiness of the expanding American force in the gulf.
Asked about these press reports, Mr. Bush said: "I'm not going to tell you what they said, but I'm going to tell you, don't believe these reports you're reading. It's under control. Don't be misled by these rabbit trails running through the snow out there."
But other Administration officials, who asked not be identified, indicated that they found the main thrust of the press reports to be accurate. And some military officials say that General Waller's assessment roughly paralleled their own thinking on the question of combat readiness.
As Mr. Bush defended his Persian Gulf policy, more than 100 Democratic members of the House of Representatives urged the President today to put off any military action against Iraq as long as economic sanctions are maintianing pressure on Mr. Hussein.
"We believe that the initiation of offensive military action by the United States unwisely risks massive loss of life," said a letter sent to Mr. Bush by Representative George Miller, a California Democrat, and 109 other House members.

HEADLINE: Review/Art;
Questions of Rhetoric, Empty and Otherwise

BYLINE: By ROBERTA SMITH

 BODY:
"Rhetorical Image," at the New Museum of Contemporary Art in SoHo, is an exhibition paved with good intentions but remarkably devoid of visually complex, fully realized works of art. This doesn't make it a failed show, but it does create, at many points, a medicinal, good-for-you zealousness that is tiring.
In keeping with today's trend toward political subjects, the works on view tackle important social problems, from economic inequity to political oppression. (In the show's catalogue, the artists don't just make artworks; they execute "interventions" in the social fabric.) 
 Good intentions are equally prominent in the structure of the show itself. Organized by Milena Kalinovska, an independent curator, this exhibition of the work of 20 artists is narrowly focused on Conceptual art, the art of wall texts, photographs and projected slide images. Even so, it has an almost admirable breadth. It samples several generations of Conceptual and related art from all over the world, including artists from not only the United States, Canada and Western Europe, but also Japan, Brazil, Poland, Yugoslavia and the Soviet Union. One of its main achievements is to bring together artists from both sides of the recently raised Iron Curtain. Thus it invites the viewer to appreciate both the continuing flexibility of Conceptual art and the way artists in different parts of the world have adapted it to the problems of their own cultures.
The show's theme is rhetoric. In the catalogue, Ms. Kalinovska cites the word's traditional definition as "the art of using language effectively and with passion to communicate ideas -- political, metaphysical or moral -- which could influence and persuade others." But she also acknowledges that the word's current rating is quite low, as in "empty rhetoric." Her mission is to examine the way artists use good rhetoric against the bad kind, reviving this maligned "art of persuasion" to expose the empty rhetoric handed down by governments and politicians, newscasters and advertisers around the world.
Certain artists seek to reveal the machinations of political rhetoric by mimicking it. Tomislav Gotovac, a Yugoslav Conceptual artist, has documented his life with the kind of idealizing photographs that are usually ordered up by the heads of totalitarian states. Braco Dimitrijevic, another Yugoslav artist now living in London, takes photographs of the faces of anonymous passers-by, enlarges them to a size usually reserved for political rallies and hangs them on public buildings. The suggestion that the symbolic authority of such images is more a case of size and presentation than actual identity is neatly made.
Some work exudes real visual sharpness and originality in its battle against rhetoric. Krzysztof Wodiczko, a Polish-born artist who lives in New York City, projects images on government buildings, museums and monuments, criticizing what might be called architectural rhetoric with a combination of wit, efficiency and eerie visual power. In recent years, Mr. Wodiczko's projections have momentarily affixed huge guns to the gun turret-like form of the Hirshhorn Museum in Washington, and nuclear missiles to the arch at Grand Army Plaza in Brooklyn. The slide-show retrospective of his efforts is one of the exhibition's most convincing visual moments.
Cildo Meireles is a Brazilian artist whose work seems even more genuinely interventionist. Since the early 1970's, Mr. Meireles has printed political slogans on paper money and Coke bottles and put them back in circulation. He is credited with helping to stir up the political opposition that led to his country's return to democracy in 1985.
Working more consistently within a museum context, Lothar Baumgarten and Lawrence Weiner both deploy words in large type across walls in elegant and provocative ways. Mr. Weiner combines one of his characteristically open-ended phrases (in this case "pushed out in front of each other") with the more dramatic term "to the barricades" in German; thus he cryptically conjures scenes of proud defiance, and even more specifically last year's opening of the Berlin wall. In "Semaphore," Mr. Baumgarten simply contrasts the names of American railroad companies, states and cities with the names of American Indian tribes; the frequent overlaps between these words tell a story of stolen names, stolen lands and cultures destroyed.
The show is also strong where it illuminates the political concerns of artists not widely known for them, especially in the case of Jiri Kolar. A Czechoslovak artist known in the United States primarily for sweetly benign collages and assemblages, Mr. Kolar is represented here by "Diary 1968," a series of quietly denunciatory collages made in response to the Soviet occupation of Prague that year.
But too often the art on view skims the surface, treating complex issues with either glib simplicity or frustrating opacity. Rose Finn-Kelcey, an English artist, comments on art's monetary allure by creating a replica of van Gogh's "Sunflowers" in a combination of Japanese, American and British coins. A visual one-liner that combines cleverness and trompe l'oeil accuracy, it's great to look at for about three seconds. At the other extreme, it's hard to decipher Dennis Adams's "Translation." It consists of two big, illuminated images of a statement about peace, printed in English and Japanese, that seem to be part of a war memorial in Hiroshima.
And at many points the show could stand a little more rhetoric -- or any other device, for that matter -- used "effectively and with passion." Judith Barry's "First and Third" consists of several short videotaped monologues about racism, poverty and terrorism, but the actors telling the stories are not very convincing. The projection screen, an open door of a jumbled storage room in the museum, is a sophomoric allusion to the difficulty of lives lived at the edges of society.
In contrast, the incessant counting of Tatsuo Miyajima's illuminated digital numbers seem to fight rhetoric with effective understatement, although the political import is unclear. The catalogue suggests they are measuring time and money and are thus subtle symbols of the fin de siecle.
Sometimes the show's main point seems to be that difficult times like these, afflicted with myriad political, social and economic problems, demand difficult, visually dull and even unintelligible art. As if aware of this paradox, Ms. Kalinovska and her colleagues conclude the show with a "resource room," a kind of walk-in catalogue that includes statements and documentation of additional work by the artists in the show, catalogues of other exhibitions of their work and even cards so that viewers can write down their responses to "Rhetorical Image." It is good to have the artists step out from behind their work and talk about their intentions, but this room also gives the exhibition a smug, self-congratulatory end.
The actual catalogue is better. Granted, its main essay, written by Nena Dimitrijevic, sticks close to an orthodox Conceptual-political reading of postwar art, connecting the work in this show to a very short list of politically involved artists. But in other ways this exceptionally well-designed publication bends over backward to avoid being polemical or "rhetorical" -- even questioning the very possibility of making politically effective art.
It includes a wonderful string of 55 interconnected aphorisms about the relationship between art and reality by the late Swiss philosopher and playwright Friedrich Durrenmatt, originally published in 1977 and translated into English for the first time. And it is peppered with notable quotes from all kinds of writers (Voltaire, Karl Marx, W. H. Auden). Their words "intervene" at random throughout the catalogue's texts, arguing the relative merits of art for art's sake and art for the sake of society in ways that often suggest that the two are not so easily separated. In this way, the catalogue delivers a message only hinted at in the show itself, that there is no single correct way to make art, political or otherwise.
 
"Rhetorical Image" remains at the New Museum of Contemporary Art, 583 Broadway, near Prince Street, through Feb. 3.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Detail of "Dokumenta," a work by Yugoslav Conceptual artist Tomislav Gotovac, at the New Museum of Contemporary Art. (Fred Scruton/New Museum of Contemporary Art)

HEADLINE: ON MY MIND;
Handle With Care

BYLINE: By A. M. Rosenthal

 BODY:
This is a news alert, and warning, for the American press and members of Congress.
The Chinese Communists plan to announce the release of many political prisoners who were rounded up after the springtime revolution of 1989. They have been kept without charge or trial. About 2,000 may be freed. The announcement is scheduled for the first months of 1991. 
 The freedom of even one political prisoner is good news. So why the "warning" about Communist China's still-undisclosed plans to allow many freedom fighters out of their cells?
These are the answers:
1. The most important prisoners -- the "ringleaders" -- will get heavy sentences. Nobody will know how many remain incarcerated. In China the public is never told how many are arrested, where they are or how many have been executed.
2. The cruel and cynical Marxist-Leninist criminal code will remain in force. It makes a crime out of any action, comment or written word that the Government decides is against its interests. The "trial" system makes a guilty verdict automatic. Even an attempt at defense is taken as an anti-Government insult and can lead to heavier sentence.
3. The massive police apparatus will remain in force. Crackdown and arrest of dissidents will continue -- with the newly freed a special target.
And finally, the reason for Americans, particularly, to beware:
4. The plan for release of some prisoners while keeping the best and the bravest incarcerated is carefully aimed at strengthening the power of Communist China's leaders through more American trade and credits.
Most of the Western sanctions imposed on China in horror at the 1989 massacres have disappeared. On Dec. 5 of this year, page A14 of The Times carried a story from Beijing about how the Communists obliterate the very memory of political prisoners. The same day on page A13 was a story reporting that the World Bank had approved $114.3 million in loans to Beijing, the first "non-humanitarian" loans since the 1989 massacres.
Still, the men who rule behind Beijing's guarded compound walls fear one possible economic weapon against their power. They fear Congress may lift their immensely valuable tariff privileges in the U.S. -- most-favored-nation status.
This is a gift prolonged every year by the Bush Administration. With these low tariffs, Communist China has been helped to flood the American market with dumped goods, piling up billions. Selling goods at low "cost" is not difficult for China, considering how much of its manufacture is turned out by labor that "costs" only a lump of bread and a bowl of thin gruel a day. Slave prison labor is an economic tool written into the very law and economic planning of the state.
Congress has voted against most-favored status for China. But so far it has failed to muster the twothirds vote to block the President.
But the Bush Administration may realize it will be more difficult to keep giving tariff gifts to China. Recently it wisely sent Richard Schifter, Assistant Secretary of State for Human Rights, to China. He is a tested fighter for human freedoms and is not the kind of man to spare the truth about any country.
The majority of Congress will fight again this year to stop buttressing Beijing by tariff privileges. Beijing and Washington both know the vote is certain to be influenced by whether U.S. media present the prisoners' release as a Communist turnaround or an effort to paint over the truth about the gulag that is China.
Congress should demand a series of specific steps before extending the tariff breaks, which allow Beijing to subsidize corrupt or miserably inefficient Government enterprises. Chinese freedom fighters plead for them: unconditional release of all political prisoners. Free emigration. Lifting the de facto martial law in Beijing and Tibet. Ending harassment of Chinese students in the U.S. and religious worshipers in China. Ending forced labor. A taste of freedom in the press and universities.
We cannot force China's Communists to take these steps so frightening to despots. But we can refuse to subsidize their economy until they do.
In Beijing, Communist officials hug the thought that the Persian Gulf crisis has shifted American public attention from human rights in China. Congress and the press have the chance to show they are wrong. So does every American who cares as much today about those youngsters at Tiananmen Square as the day they were shot down.

HEADLINE: The Census Isn't Over

 BODY:
The Census Bureau, no longer trumpeting "the best census ever," has released a set of "final" figures. They describe a nation of about 250 million people whose center of gravity is shifting from the East and Middle West to the South and West. In this broad scope the figures are no doubt correct. But those numbers are anything but final and may not be as accurate as America needs and can have.
Pursuant to a court order in a lawsuit brought by New York and other large cities, the bureau's state-by-state tally will bear an important notation, a caution light for states preparing for the decade's legislative reapportionments. It says the figures "are subject to possible correction for undercount or overcount," and says the Commerce Department will decide by July whether to correct them. 
 That's because for many decades the census has undercounted large cities, where people suspicious of authority evade the enumerators and the enumerators avoid hard counting in hostile neighborhoods. In 1980 the undercount of young black males living in urban pockets was five times the undercount rate for the population as a whole. That's more than an actuarial nicety; it skews the distribution of political power in the form of legislative seats and potentially billions in economic benefits distributed in accordance with populations.
New York City, part of a state that may lose three House seats, has been joined by other urban centers like Los Angeles and Miami, whose states are the biggest gainers in Congress, to protest the undercount. Their lawsuit has wrung from the Commerce Department an agreement to "consider" adjustments based on selective recanvassing and statistical techniques.
It's a highly technical decision that will have major political consequences. Commerce has expressed doubt that the undercount is either serious or easily corrected. The Administration's hostility seems to have a partisan as well as technical dimension, since an adjustment might benefit big cities and therefore Democrats. A Federal judge gave Commerce the burden of showing that its decision is reasonable and understandable.
The Administration's stance is not reassuring. The bureau's announcement repeatedly speaks of the figures as though they were final. Its claim to a "best ever" census was made laughable by the widespread lack of public cooperation, a woeful budget and the alarming amount of pure guesswork by enumerators in many neighborhoods. Commerce can justly claim credit for hard work under adverse conditions, but its pride in a "full, fair and efficient" census is not yet secure.

HEADLINE: Religion Notes

BYLINE: By Ari L. Goldman

 BODY:

Praying for Gulf Peace
Top officials of the National Council of Churches, who recently returned from the Middle East, have called for a day of prayer and fasting on Sunday, Jan. 6, in an effort to avert a war over Iraq's occupation of Kuwait. 
 The church leaders, representing 40 major Protestant and Eastern Orthodox denominations in the United States, returned from their visit to the region Dec. 21 and declared, "We believe the resort to massive violence to resolve the gulf crisis would be politically and morally indefensible."
"War will not liberate Kuwait, it will destroy it," they continued in a statement that appeared to be a rebuttal of the Bush Administration's policy in the Persian Gulf. "War will not save us from weapons of mass destruction, it will unleash them. War will not establish regional stability, it will inflame the entire Middle East."
The Rev. J. Martin Bailey, press officer for the National Council, said the fast had been declared because it was "important for church people to acknowledge the seriousness of the situation in the gulf and recognize that they must themselves participate in the development of alternatives."
The decision to call the fast was made a few days after the church delegation's return, but the idea was clearly present a few days before Christmas, when the council declared: "At this moment, the resolution of the gulf crisis will take a miracle. But in this season we are reminded that the Middle East is the cradle of miracles. The miracle must be acted and prayed into being."
 
Old Fast, New Meaning
An Orthodox Jewish group has also linked recent world events with fasting. Rather than declaring its own fast, the Rabbinical Council of America called on those observing an ancient fast to give it a modern dimension by using it as an occasion to protest the United Nations' resolutions condemning Israel for actions in the occupied territories.
The fast, the Tenth of Tevet, was observed on Thursday and traditionally commemorates the siege of Jerusalem 2,600 years ago by Nebuchadnezzar, the king of Babylonia.
Rabbi Marc D. Angel, the president of the council, urged that special Psalms and prayers be added to the fast "to help Israel at this time of crisis when Jerusalem is once again being besieged."
He continued, "The United Nations, known for its blatant anti-Israel and anti-Semitic pronouncements, has virtually laid political siege to Jerusalem, the eternal capital of the people of Israel."
 
New Aristide Book
About a year ago, Orbis Books, the publishing arm of the Maryknoll Fathers, decided to publish the writings and sermons of a little-known Roman Catholic priest from Haiti who devoted his life to working with the poor.
Last week that priest, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, was elected the President of Haiti. "We had no idea that anything like this was remotely possible," said Robert Ellsberg, the editor-in-chief of Orbis Books. "We're now trying to catch up."
Only 2,000 paperback copies of Father Aristide's book were printed when it came off the presses in November. Another, larger printing is planned.
The book, "In the Parish of the Poor: Writings from Haiti," was edited and translated by Amy Wilentz.
Mr. Ellsberg said one of the major purposes of Orbis had been "to amplify the voices of third-world Christians," -- especially liberation theologians like the Rev. Leonardo Boff, the Rev. Gustavo Gutierrez and Father Aristide -- who believe that a primary duty of the church must be to promote social and economic justice.
 
Presbyterians' Rebuttal
An article in the December issue of the Reader's Digest charged that the leadership of the Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.) was "out of step" with its congregations because, according to the writer, it had been embracing religious and political liberalism. Now the article has brought an eight-page response from the denomination's leadership.
The article, written by John S. Tompkins, an elder of Madison Avenue Presbyterian Church in Manhattan, said, "The people in the pews" were "fed up" with the liberal tendencies. Among these tendencies, he said, were the elimination of hymns deemed militaristic or sexist, challenges to United States military policies and various instances of substituting "social, economic and political action for the real business of religion."
The response, which was sent to all 11,505 Presbyterian congregations, was prepared by the denomination's Stated Clerk, the Rev. James Andrews, and the executive director of the denomination's General Assembly, the Rev. David Stoner. The executives argued that the leadership has a mandate from its membership to speak out on social and political issues and adds that the congregations have been consulted in the process. The response was prepared, the executives wrote, to rebut the article's "statement and interpretation of facts" and "to clarify those facts and dispute that interpretation."

HEADLINE: Leading Indicators In 5th Drop

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
The Government's main set of indicators used to predict the economy fell for the fifth straight month in November, a 1.2 percent drop that suggested no early end to a deepening contraction that now lacks only formal designation as the nation's ninth postwar recession.
"The November indicators leave no doubt that a serious and lengthy recession is under way," said William K. MacReynolds, director of forecasting for the United States Chamber of Commerce. "The only remaining questions are just how far the economy will plummet and how long it will stay there." 
 A recession would mark the end of the longest peacetime expansion in the nation's history and one that if it had continued until October 1991 would have matched the record 1961-69 expansion that embraced much of the Vietnam War.
 
Most Severe Since 1981
 The latest fall in the index of leading indicators, constructed by the Commerce Department from data collected by various Government and private-sector bodies, made the five-month drop the lengthiest period of uninterrupted decline since 1984 and the most severe since late 1981, when the most recent recession still had 10 months to run. In that year, five of the six months from June through November had sizable declines, with those in September and October both exceeding any in the current series.
Eight of the 11 components of the index fell during the month, while the other three components, paced by higher stock prices, made positive contributions.
Two companion gauges, the coincident index designed to reflect current movements in the economy, and the lagging index that tends to confirm it after an interval, also fell in November. The coincident index declined 1.1 percent, its fifth straight fall, and the lagging index dropped by eight-tenths of 1 percent after a three-tenths of 1 percent decline in October.
 
 Preceded by Three Declines
 Traditionally, a string of three straight declines in the leading index is regarded as pointing toward a recession and, indeed, recessions in recent decades have generally been preceded by three monthly declines.
But the three-decline rule has on several occasions also signaled recessions that never occurred. For example, the four-month stretch from October 1987, the month stock prices crashed, through January 1988 was not followed by a business downturn. Likewise, no recession followed the six straight declines reported between May and October of 1984.
The current decline, however, is twice as severe as the drop during the first five months of the 1984 streak -- 4.4 percent compared with 2.2 percent. And the decline registered over the first four months is slightly more severe than that in the 1987 period.
Economists warn not only about false, or misleading, signals but also about assuming a close correlation between the magnitude of declines in the index and the amount by which the economy may subsequently decline. The index is designed specifically to anticipate turning points in the economy -- that is, direction rather than momentum.
Two privately constructed forecasting indexes also moved down for November, their compilers reported today. Safian Investment Research of White Plains said its Composite Forecasting Index fell 1 percent, its sixth straight decline. A subindex that excludes stock market and monetary data fell 1.7 percent, its 20th decline in the last 22 months.
The Jesup, Josephthal & Company Macro-Economic Index, which first flashed a recession signal in August 1989 for early 1990, fell to minus 118 from 112 in October, a moderation in its rate of fall.
 
 Milder Recession Seen
 "My interpretation of the more modest -118 reading currently is that the recession of 1990-91 is likely to be milder and over by the end of the first or second quarter of next year," said Jesup's chief economist, David B. Bostian Jr. The consensus among private analysts now is for a recession to last through mid-1991. But Mr. Bostian also believes the downturn actually began in late 1989 or early this year instead of in the third or fourth quarters of this year, as most economists believe.
The components with a negative effect on the Government's index, ranked by their impact, were a decline in manufacturers' new orders for consumer goods and materials, lower prices for materials particularly sensitive to the business cycle, a decline in factory order backlogs, falling contracts and orders for new plant and equipment, a rise in average weekly claims for state unemployment insurance, a reduction in the length of the average workweek and declines in the money supply and in building permits.
 
 Deliveries and Expectations
 Components with a positive impact on the index, besides higher stock prices, were slower deliveries by corporate suppliers and a slight increase in the University of Michigan's barometer of consumer expectations.
October's decline, initially put at 1.2 percent, was revised to 1.3 percent, the Commerce Department also reported. The declines in the previous three months were eight-tenths of 1 percent in September, 1.2 percent in August and one-tenth in July.
The ratio of the coincident index to the lagging index, which is considered an alternative predictor of the economy's direction, fell slightly in November, the third straight decline.
The leading index now stands at 139.7, which is 3.4 percent below the November 1989 reading of 144.6.

HEADLINE: Bond Prices Slip as Books Are Closed

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
A spurt of selling pushed down prices of Treasury securities yesterday, as dealers made final bookkeeping preparations for the end of 1990.
The decline came even though the economic data released yesterday were generally favorable for the fixed-income market. The numbers included a report that the Government's index of leading economic indicators fell by 1.2 percent in November, the fifth straight month that the forecasting gauge has shown a decline. 
 "The numbers were pretty good for the market, but they were met with a little bit of selling," one government bond trader said. "Dealers are getting ready for year-end, because with settlement on Monday this is the last day you can do it."
 
 Unexpected Strength
 One report that may have unsettled some market participants showed that initial unemployment claims fell for the second straight week in the period ended Dec. 15. Most credit market analysts have been anticipating that the December employment report, which is scheduled next Friday, will show additional weakness in non-farm payroll employment. The fact that initial claims have dropped for two consecutive weeks suggests that perhaps the employment data will not be as weak as most analysts expect.
"The jobless claims numbers were a surprise, since most people were looking for the numbers to be flat to up a bit," said Robert Chandross, chief economist at Lloyds Bank. "But there is no indication here that the economy is doing any better. There is a widespread perception that the December data will look bad. I think that view will be validated by the numbers."
Trading was thin yesterday, as it was all week. Early in the session the Public Securities Association, an industry group, recommended that activity in the government securities market end at 3 P.M., to give traders in New York, which was hit by a fairly heavy snowstorm on Thursday night, time to make their way home.
 
 Secondary Market
 In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered late yesterday at a price of 104 31/32, down 21/32, to yield 8.29 percent, up from 8.23 percent late Thursday, but little changed from a yield of 8.27 percent a week ago.
Among note issues, the 8 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 102 17/ 32, down 11/32, to yield 8.12 percent. And the 7 1/4 percent two-year notes were offered at 100 1/32, unchanged on the day, to yield 7.23 percent.
Short-term Treasury bill rates fell slightly.
By late in the session, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 6.42 percent, down 5 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point. Six-month bills were offered at 6.53 percent, down 4 basis points. And one-year bill rates were offered at 6.48 percent, down 2 basis points.
Year-end pressures continued to buffet the overnight rate for bank loans in the Federal funds market yesterday, although not so badly as earlier in the week.
Yesterday the funds rate moved in a wide range of 9 percent to 6.50 percent, a level that is below the 7 percent rate that most analysts see as the Federal Reserve Board's target.
In other developments, the Fed reported yesterday that all three measures of the nation's money supply rose during the week ended Dec. 17.
The report had no impact on prices in the credit markets.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Down in Slow Day On Bearish Economic News

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar fell sharply on world currency markets yesterday, with extremely thin trading accentuating the downward move.
Currency traders received more negative news about the United States economy in a Commerce Department report showing that the index of leading economic indicators fell 1.2 percent in November, its fifth consecutive monthly decline. Normally three straight monthly declines indicate that a recession is imminent. On Thursday the Government reported a sharp drop in durable-goods orders for November. 
 The leading indicators figure "was much below expectations," said Marc Chandler, an analyst with IDEA, a New York consulting firm. As the economy weakens, pressure builds on the Federal Reserve to lower interest rates to stimulate growth. But lower interest rates are bearish for the dollar because they erode the value of fixed-income investments like bonds.
The dollar began declining in Asian trading and continued to fall when dealings shifted to Europe as investors took profits.
Shortly before the United States market opened, "the dollar fell hard across the board" and was unable to stage a convincing recovery, Mr. Chandler said.
The sharp decline came despite a political backdrop that would appear to support the currency. Tensions in the Persian Gulf as well as political strife in the Soviet Union might encourage investors to seek a haven in the dollar.
Mr. Chandler maintained that the currency would trade still lower if not for these political developments. "The full dimensions of the current economic contraction aren't being fully appreciated by the market," he said.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell to a closing 136.05 yen, from 136.65 yen on Thursday. Later in London, it traded at 135.05 yen. In New York the dollar settled at 134.90 yen, down from 136.40 yen on Thursday.
In London, the pound rose to $1.9220, from $1.8870 late Thursday. In New York it cost $1.9282 to buy one pound, more expensive than Thursday's $1.8895.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Thursday's rates, included: 1.4935 German marks, down from 1.5255; 1.2745 Swiss francs, down from 1.3040; 5.0810 French francs, down from 5.1935; 1,126.50 Italian lire, down from 1,151.75, and 1.1601 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1616.
