HEADLINE: Mourning Soviet Reform

BYLINE: By BILL KELLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Dec. 28

 BODY:
If the First Congress of People's Deputies, a year and a half ago, was a baptism of infant democracy and an exorcism of Stalinist demons, the fourth Congress, which ended on Thursday night, was a wake.
 With desperate noise and little cheer, it marked not only the passing of this year of sunken spirits, but in the minds of many participants, the end of the period of great advances. 
 "The Congress reflected the trends that have been developing in the society around us, the sunset of democratization and the onset of conservative forces," said Aleksei M. Yemelyanov, a professor of agricultural economics and an opposition deputy.
 
Gorbachev More Isolated
 President Mikhail S. Gorbachev emerged from the Congress more isolated than ever from the innovators who helped him remap Soviet society, and dependent on traditional institutions of power -- the Communist Party, the military-industrial complex, the K.G.B. -- for his chosen priority of the moment, which is order.
He seems to have run into the limits of what he started. Trying to unleash the creative energy of a suppressed society, he set loose forces that threaten the union.
Trying to break the paralysis of fear, he now finds himself afraid of the consequences. The word he used repeatedly on the closing day was "razval" -- breakdown, disintegration, collapse.
 
Press Corps Is Worked
 People argue whether Mr. Gorbachev has reached his own personal limit, or is accommodating the hard-liners panicked by the mess of democracy; whether this is a temporary cooling-off period or the beginning of a more drastic retrenchment.
Mr. Gorbachev's spin controllers, who now adroitly work the press corps in the Kremlin lobbies, proclaimed the Congress a victory because the Soviet leader got most of what he wanted: a reorganized Government, his choice as Vice President, and a rousing endorsement of the general idea that the Soviet Union should continue to exist.
He won these victories because the Congress as a whole was not very assertive, and had no consensus of its own about how to confront the problems of economic decline and clashing sovereignties. He won because he catered to the Communist Party bloc, the military officers and military-industrialists and other hard-liners who make up a majority in the hall.
 
Two Ballots and a Tantrum
 Even so, as American pols say, he won ugly.
It took him two ballots and a temper tantrum to win approval of his undistinguished vice presidential choice, a lifelong apparatchik named Gennadi I. Yanayev, whom he plucked from the Communist Party Politburo after two candidates of stature had publicly spurned the post.
The Congress refused to give him the one element of his proposed government reorganization that actually conferred upon him a conspicuous new power -- a presidential Supreme State Inspectorate that was to police compliance with orders from Moscow.
What he won was a streamlining of the Government that theoretically makes it easier for him to call Cabinet members and other obstructive bureaucrats to account. This is useful for political infighting, but it is hard to see how it helps resolve the great power struggle going on outside the Cabinet, between Moscow and its rambunctious republics.
As the Congress ended, the Russian Republic calmly announced that it would not pay its usual share of the federal budget. This was a challenge as real as the Boston Tea Party, and nothing in Mr. Gorbachev's ostensible new powers equipped him to solve it.
 
Original Team Breaks Up
 This was the Congress that witnessed the breakup of Mr. Gorbachev's original team, the strong and independent thinkers who helped shape the reforms.
Eduard A. Shevardnadze, the Foreign Minister who helped win his President the Nobel Peace Prize, quit his post, saying he could not stand to watch the country lurch toward dictatorship -- although the hard line on domestic affairs seems to have no effect on the improvement in Soviet relations with the United States.
The Shevardnadze resignation remains such a sore point that the broadcasting authorities today canceled tonight's post-Congress edition of the irreverent, late-night weekly show "Vzglyad," or "View," which was to be devoted to Mr. Shevardnadze. The program has been a periodic target of censorship by broadcasting officials sensitive to Kremlin moods.
"People like Shevardnadze would never have turned away from Gorbachev unless they felt they weren't needed any more," Mr. Yemelyanov said.
Three other key figures lost their places in the inner circle, at least for now, when the Congress abolished the Presidential Council. They are Aleksandr N. Yakovlev, a co-architect of perestroika; Vadim V. Bakatin, who battled the Communist Party for control of the Interior Ministry, and Stanislav S. Shatalin, the economist who almost persuaded Mr. Gorbachev to try a 500-day demolition of Communism.
Some of them may be recycled into the new team, but Mr. Gorbachev's first recruit for that team did not inspire hope among the campaigners for reform. Judging by his public record and the word of his colleagues, Mr. Yanayev, the new Vice President, is an honest, loyal, unimaginative man, an obedient lieutenant to tend the fortress but not a companion for new frontiers.
 
Dependent on Old Guard
 The appointment seemed to confirm that Mr. Gorbachev is more than ever dependent on the institutions of old power, and reinforced the impression that the period of reform has passed and that the period of law and order has arrived.
Mr. Gorbachev's isolation from the people in the insurgent opposition and his mistrust of their ambitions have grown steadily over the last year.
The self-styled democrats have long ago grown disenchanted with Mr. Gorbachev, and are in their own funk, frustrated by their inability to govern as the economy collapses and the old guard resists.
At the Congress, there was no sign left of any bond between Mr. Gorbachev and these intellectuals who made up his original cheerleading squad.
"I think he is a man with only one friend -- Lukyanov," said Vitaly A. Korotich, editor of the popular weekly magazine Ogonyok. Anatoly I. Lukyanov is the Gorbachev protege who was chairman of the Congress.
 
Media Stars From the Right
 The new media stars of the Congress came from the far right -- the two army colonels whose badgering helped drive Mr. Shevardnadze from the Government, and the striking blond party functionary who called for Mr. Gorbachev's impeachment.
The signals to the outside world were chilling: First the departure of Mr. Shevardnadze, a champion of partnership with the West even on such tricky issues as the Persian Gulf, then the platform given to the K.G.B. chief, Vladimir A. Kryuchkov, whose anti-Western tirade seemed intended to spook foreign investors.

HEADLINE: Developers End A Legal Tussle On Skyscraper

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
Americas Tower, a skyscraping distress signal in mid-Manhattan that has been frozen for more than a year while its developers battled one another, may finally be completed as a result of a settlement reached yesterday.
The 48-story office building, which covers the block of the Avenue of the Americas between 45th and 46th Streets, was about three-quarters finished last December when the developers, unable to get more construction financing, began fighting in court over who should control the project's destiny. 
 Since then, the highly visible tower -- partially clad in rose-colored granite, partially exposed as a hulking steel skeleton -- has been a kind of three-dimensional symbol of the end of the 1980's building boom.
The legal struggle pitted Joseph E. and Ralph Bernstein of New York, through their NYL Development Corporation, against the giant Kumagai Gumi construction concern of Tokyo, through its subsidiary, K.G. Land New York Corporation.
 
Bankruptcy Proceedings
 When the project began in 1988, K.G. Land paid for the initial work, with the understanding that a construction lender would be found to complete the job. With no such financing on the horizon last year and facing some $28 million in unpaid bills, K.G. Land sought to declare the project bankrupt, a step that the Bernsteins contested.
Although bankruptcy proceedings were dropped in August, the Bernsteins and K.G. Land continued to quarrel before an arbitration panel over the terms of their partnership agreement and how to dispose of the project.
Yesterday, they announced that they had concluded the arbitration and that they "anticipate construction can be resumed within the next few weeks."
However, it remains unclear under whose auspices that would happen.
As a result of the settlement, the Bernsteins have acquired a controlling interest in the development partnership. But K.G. Land has an option to buy the building, which it could exercise as soon as next Friday. The option runs for a month.
Americas Tower has 1 million square feet of space, almost as much as the Chrysler Building. In their joint announcement, the partners said that Swanke Hayden Connell would remain as architects and that KM/Turner Construction Company would remain as construction manager. Few other details of the settlement were made available yesterday by either side.
It is also unclear what prospects the project would face in the current real-estate market, where huge blocks of newly constructed space in mid-Manhattan are going begging for tenants. Americas Tower has no tenant.
The Bernsteins gained nationwide notoriety for their role as real-estate managers for Ferdinand E. Marcos, the former President of the Philippines, and his wife, Imelda.
Last week, the Bernsteins, the Philippine Government and a business associate of Adnan Khashoggi said that they had settled their five-year-old ownership dispute over the Crown Building, at Fifth Avenue and 57th Street, which was once owned by the Marcoses. But the mortgage holder was not part of this settlement, so the final outcome is impossible to predict.

The building, still a steel skeleton on part of its right side, may finally be completed as a result of a settlement reached by its developers. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Hotels Woo Cabbies to Win Their Riders

BYLINE: By ALISON LEIGH COWAN

 BODY:
Last week, in celebration of its grand opening, the Rihga Royal Hotel held a special reception for a group of dignitaries as a way to build favorable word of mouth. Invitations went out. The pastry chef started baking. And by 9 A.M. on the appointed day, the V.I.P.'s began arriving in a yellow blaze of taxicabs. One by one, they jumped out to munch on the free cookies and coffee, darted back into the cabs -- and sped off in search of their next fares.
With the local economy weakening and competition keen, the Rihga is one of a growing number of retail businesses courting New York's cabdrivers. These businesses are betting that they can reach the customer in the back of the taxi by wooing the driver in front. 
 By the end of its giveaway last week, the Rihga on West 54th Street had served up 4,000 cups of coffee -- enough for roughly one-tenth of all the licensed yellow cab drivers in New York City. Earlier this year, another luxury hotel,the Mark, spent about $4,000 to treat some 2,000 drivers to a continental breakfast of fresh-squeezed orange juice and just-baked muffins as a way to publicize its recent makeover.
No fewer than six hotels and a nightclub have offered similar hospitality in recent months. They hope drivers will learn where the businesses are located, drop by occasionally for fares and maybe even send clients their way.
"It builds a general awareness, and the more awareness you have in a market as competitive as this, the better off you'll be," said Larry Weiss, an executive with the Embassy Suites Hotel in Times Square. He feels that the $2,200 of coffeeand clipboards distributed in its October opening were worthwhile even if no referrals result.
"Very rarely do you get someone who hops in a cab and needs a hotel room," he said. "Where it helps more is for the person who gets in the cab at the airport and wants to be taken to the Embassy Suites Hotel. It wouldn't sit well if the cabdriver hasn't heard of it. There's a comfort level that exists if the cabbie knows where it is right away."
 
Boosting Morale
 For drivers, the special attention has been an antidote to complaints that many of them do not know their way around town or have an uncanny way of finding the longest distance between two points.
Even when the promotions are modest, cabbies enjoy being singled out for their valuable contacts. "It's a great idea for the drivers to be able to come here and relax for awhile," Ruberto Munoz said in front of the Rihga. Judging from the tro phies on his dashboard, he was helping himself to cookies and a souvenir mug for at least the second time that week.
And as long as the programs' thrust is to educate drivers about new destinations and improve morale, and not necessarily to induce drivers to plug products or services in violation of city regulations, taxi officials take a benign view toward the giveaways."It's a form of marketing," said Jack S. Lusk, the chairman of the Taxi & Limousine Commission, the government group that monitors the city's $600 million cab industry.
Other cities have experimented with the idea. The National Museum of American Art in Washington, for instance, wanted more attention in a city that already has a number of attractions. So it treated 500 cabbies to lunch in September on its front steps, at the suggestion of a board member who remembered that the gimmick worked when Pan American World Airways wanted to promote its new shuttle service from La Guardia Airport in 1986.
 
Free Air Fresheners
 In an added flourish, the museum's lunch guests received complimentary air fresheners for their cars, shaped like the museum's famous bucking-bronco sculpture, as a reminder of who sponsored the event. Attendance is up, and the museum expects to host a similar to-do for the city's hotel concierges in January.
Nordstrom Inc., the Seattle-based retailer, also borrowed the concept, which had been used successfully in 1988 by the operator of a San Francisco mall it occupies, when it opened a new store in Arlington, Va., late last year. About 500 cabbies showed up to claim the free box lunches.
But nowhere has the trend blossomed quite like it has in New York, a city whose 11,787 yellow cabs are vital to its daily rhythm. Cabs and car services make up nearly one out of every five trips taken on public transportation, according to Mr. Lusk.
 
'Just Ridiculous'
 Most of the promotions have been confined to the coffee, danish and souvenir variety. Some businesses, however, go further. Lawrence Goldberg, a taxi drivers union official, said that the Loews Howard Johnson in midtown had recently proposed giving cab drivers $5 for every person they brought to the hotel without a reservation.
"We didn't want to get involved," said Mr. Goldberg, the secretary-treasurer of Local 3036 of the Taxi Drivers & Allied Workers Union. The union refused to notify its members of the proposed offer, and the hotel says it is now studying alternatives.
"It was just ridiculous," Mr. Goldberg said. "Even if a driver were to get the $5, it would cost him $10 in down time and quite frankly, drivers could have gotten in trouble for it."
 
Getting the Word Out
 Strictly speaking, plugging businesses can cost taxi drivers their licenses if passengers complain. Rule 219 of the taxi drivers' rulebook states that "a driver shall not sell, advertise or recommend any service or merchandise without prior written commission approval." The rule, regulators explain, exists to protect passengers who may not appreciate a hard sell.
But even the regulators say they are apt to look the other way if a driver simply responds to a passenger's inquiry. The Commission also uses its newsletters to help "get the word out," as Mr. Lusk put it, about those promotions that it believes will keep drivers abreast of new destinations and boost esteem.
Rebecca Bowser, a spokeswoman for the commission, held out as a model the promotion that A & S Plaza ran last year to mark its Manhattan debut.
Besides giving out some 5,000 cups of coffee to cabbies who were passing by, the mall's developer also honored five drivers for distinguished service, although not too many of their colleagues attended the speeches. Finding 11,787 parking spaces around Herald Square in midday is no cinch.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Leiv Minashr, left, and Victor Alecchi, New York City cab drivers, being served free coffee and cookies last week at the Rihga Royal Hotel on West 54th Street in Manhattan. Like a growing number of the city's retail businesses, the hotel is hoping to reach the customer in the back of the taxi by wooing the driver in front. (Jim Estrin for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Ex-Follower of Ho Chi Minh Scolds Vietnam in Broadcasts

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
A longtime Vietnamese Communist who personally accepted the surrender of Saigon in 1975 has shown up in the West and, surprisingly, has begun broadcasting statements over the BBC calling on his compatriots to abandon the rigid Marxist-Leninist economic system, political repression and enmity with the United States and other adversaries.
The Vietnamese, Col. Bui Tin, a deputy editor of the party daily Nhan Dan, said in a telephone interview from Paris today that he had decided to take advantage of a stay in France to beam his appeals back to his country over the BBC because he would not have been allowed to publish or broadcast them at home. 
 Besides, he said, more Vietnamese -- three million of them -- listen to the BBC's Vietnamese-language service every day than to Radio Hanoi.
 
Returning Could Be Dangerous
 "I want to go back," said Colonel Tin, who arrived in France in September. But he acknowledged that returning to Vietnam could be dangerous for him, and he would not say exactly when he would try. "Prison is possible, and my wife and children have already been interrogated by the police."
Colonel Tin, a disciple of Ho Chi Minh, was at Dien Bien Phu when the French were defeated there in 1954, and accepted the surrender of Gen. Duong Van Minh when the Communists rolled into Saigon in April 1975.
He has a long and honored record as a Communist. He joined the Vietminh in 1945 as a teen-ager and became a member of Ho Chi Minh's private guard unit. In 1963, as Hanoi's leaders were debating whether to send major North Vietnamese troop units to join the Vietcong fighting against the Saigon Government, Colonel Tin was assigned to lead a secret scouting mission to South Vietnam.
 
Accepted South's Surrender
 He later left active duty and became deputy editor of the North Vietnamese army newspaper, Quan Doi Nhan Dan. As a correspondent for that paper in the last days of the Vietnam War, he was riding with the advance tank unit that broke into the Presidential Palace in Saigon. The tank commander deferred to him as the ranking officer present and gave him the honor of accepting General Minh's surrender.
Colonel Tin came to France at the invitation of L'Humanite, the French Communist newspaper. He said he planned to return home "in a few months."
By his account, he has been troubled by events in his unhappy country for some time, but could not say so. "I had a serious heart attack last year," he said, "which is why I felt I absolutely had to try to do something now to save the people."
 
Broadcast in Installments
 On Nov. 28, he made public what he calls "a petition from a single, ordinary citizen" to the leaders and people of his country. The BBC's Vietnamese-language service has been broadcasting his explanation and commentary on his views in installments. The last installment of the five- to six-hour interview will be broadcast sometime in January, said Richard Black of BBC radio here.
Vietnam, with North Korea, is one of the few Communist countries that still resists radical Soviet-style change, at least in economic and social policy. Last spring, its Communist Party purged Tran Xuan Bach, a senior member of the Politburo, for warning of the danger of rigidity.
The authorities in Hanoi have also menaced Colonel Tin. The Voice of Vietnam broadcast a statement by the editorial staff of Nhan Dan on Dec. 15, saying that Colonel Tin's BBC interviews contained "many points that distort our party and state policies and lines."
 
Said Aloud What Many Think
 In the interview today, Colonel Tin said, "Very well, I say, let's have a public discussion of how I did that."
"I have been thinking about the ideas in my petition for four years," he said, "and before I left Vietnam I discussed them with intellectuals and journalists in Hanoi. Some said that I would only be saying aloud what a lot of people think."
Colonel Tin has a record as something of a maverick. He tracked down Ho Chi Minh's last will and testament and proved that Ho's successors had doctored it. Ho had wanted his ashes scattered around Vietnam. But instead the party leaders built a huge mausoleum to Ho, which Colonel Tin felt was a fraud.
Colonel Tin's statement, as published in translation by the Asian Wall Street Journal on Dec. 3, said: "Our country is still confronting a grave general crisis, which has recently become more serious in a number of domains, leading to a further deterioration of our people's living conditions."
Later in the statement, he added: "Every Vietnamese is troubled by the country's present situation. There are acute economic problems, a return of galloping inflation, mounting prices and daily erosion of living conditions of civil servants and cadres. Bureaucracy, irresponsibility, egoism, corruption and fraud are becoming entrenched under an insolent reign of privileges and prerogatives.
"The decisions and measures thus far adopted remain ineffectual. There is an alarming deterioration of traditional ethical, moral and spiritual values. There is confusion among the youth, on whom the country's future depends."
Well over half a million Vietnamese "boat people" have fled the country's catastrophic living conditions since 1979, when Vietnam's invasion of Cambodia prompted China to attack it in retaliation. Several hundred thousand also fled after South Vietnam fell to the Communists in 1975.
 
Seeks National Reconciliation
 He told the British newspaper The Guardian earlier this month that a government of national reconciliation -- uniting Vietnamese refugees and exiles around the world with those at home -- could help build democracy and heal the wounds left by mass arrests and other repressive measures taken by the Communists after their victory.
"This caused many more to flee and die in the ocean," he told The Guardian. "The motive was prejudice, revenge and hatred. If Ho Chi Minh had been alive, there would have been an amnesty."
In his statement, he called for a reduction in Vietnam's army and security forces over a three-to-five-year period. The army now constitutes 2 percent of the country's 62 million people, he said, but it should only be about a quarter of its current size.
"We have to withdraw our troops from Cambodia and let the U.N. take care of it," he said today. "But the most important thing is to make the world understand that Vietnam is a factor of peace, stability and cooperation in the region. And, at the same time, we should normalize our relations with all our former enemies, in all directions -- France, Japan, the United States and China."

Colonel Tin, a disciple of Ho Chi Minh, is now broadcasting to his Communist compatriots in Vietnam from the BBC in Paris. (Associated Press)(pg. 2)

HEADLINE: Darkness and Confusion In a Smoke-Filled Train

BYLINE: By DEAN BAQUET

 BODY:
When the No. 3 train stopped in the tunnel under Brooklyn Heights yesterday morning, it seemed to Milton Puryear just another of those everyday indignities of life in New York.
But suddenly waves of thick brown smoke billowed through the car. In front of Mr. Puryear passengers collapsed to the floor and began coughing and vomiting.
"The smoke was so thick you couldn't see in front of your face," he said.
Passengers on the nine-car Manhattan-bound train described a scene of panic and confusion, of people rushing from exit to exit, amid conflicting instructions from the crew. 
"It seemed forever to get out of there," said John Casson, an economist with the Federal Reserve Bank who was in the front car. "The conductor tried to give instructions through the loudspeaker, but we could barely hear him."
 
'People Started Panicking'
 Urban Sanders, a flagman for the Transit Authority, said: "There was a lot of rumbling. People started panicking. Some people said close the doors. Other people said no, let's move back. Some people were screaming. Other people were pushing and shoving. The smoke was so black, you couldn't see what was ahead of you."
Another Transit Authority employee, Andrew Carcasole said: "I thought it was the end of the world. I didn't think we were going to make it."
The passengers said the ordeal lasted about half an hour -- from the time the train froze 125 feet short of the Clark Street station then backed up to Borough Hall again, where it was met by Transit Authority officials and paramedics.
In interviews, passengers gave varied accounts of how the incident began and how long it lasted, and whether train crews moved quickly enough to resolve the crisis.
Mr. Casson, for instance, said the people in the front car were baffled when the voice over the public-address system told them to "move back to Borough Hall."
"That was the only thing they said," said Mr. Casson. "Everybody knows front and back. Move to the back of the train would have been the thing to say. We needed better instructions."
But Mr. Puryear, an investment banker, and others said instructions to move to the rear of the train were clear, though they said they found the rear as smoke-filled as the front and middle.
 
Flashes of Light
 The passengers said the train pulled out of the Borough Hall station in Brooklyn about 9:15 A.M. They said that within seconds it stopped and that the crew announced there was a fire on the track ahead.
Mr. Casson said that after the train stopped, and before smoke filled the cars, he looked out the front window and saw quick flashes of light on the tracks ahead. He said the flashes were followed by muffled explosions.
Given the conductor's announcement, "Nobody thought anything about it," Mr. Casson said.
"But the smart people left the car" and started moving to the rear of the train, he said.
In the middle of the train, Mr. Puryear said, he could hear one transit worker over the public-address system tell another to shut the air conditioner off.
By Mr. Puryear's account, the passengers in his car still had no idea that this was anything more than a normal delay. "There was some groaning and moaning" at the announcement, he said.
Some said the smoke entered through the air-conditioning ducts, others said it seemed to come from under the doors.
Passengers in the more crowded cars seem to have panicked, while those less jammed were relatively calm.
Mr. Casson said that some of the men in his car tried to pry open the doors. Others tried to calm passengers who were frantically trying to jam into the rear cars, which quickly became packed.
"The vast majority of people were helpful," Mr. Casson said. But he added: "It was scary for a while. I thought I was going to be lying there on the floor covered by the bodies of people who died from smoke inhalation."
The train eventually lurched back to Borough Hall. Transit officials said it was idle because other trains had to be moved to make room for it.
Mr. Puryear said that another train was hooked up with theirs, so that passengers could walk through a clear car.
Mr. Sanders, the Transit Authority worker, said he waited for all the passengers to step off his car before he left.
"I was allowing the paying customers to leave first," he said.

(Dith Pran/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Great Lakes Bancorp Doubles Loss Reserve

BYLINE: By PAUL C. JUDGE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Dec. 28

 BODY:
The Great Lakes Bancorp said today that it would more than double its reserves for loan losses and write off $25 million of nonperforming good will assets, causing it to lose between $18 million and $23 million in 1990.
Great Lakes said that increasing its loan-loss reserves to $23 million, from $10.8 million at the end of September, would cause it to lose money in the fourth quarter, but it did not say how much. It also said it would reduce its 1990 net income to between $5 million and $7 million before the good will write-off. In 1989 the institution had net earnings of $13.7 million, or $3.10 a share, before extraordinary items. 
 Great Lakes, a Federal savings bank based in Ann Arbor, Mich., had $3.7 billion in assets and 52 branches in Michigan at the end of September. It increased the reserves before a routine examination by Federal regulators at the end of January, analysts said.
"This move reflects the tougher attitude among Federal regulators, as far as the amount of nonperforming loans thrifts can carry on their books," said Henry S. Peltz, an independent analyst and consultant in Ardsley, N.Y. "It's also a recognition by Great Lakes of the deteriorating economy."
 
 'A Bookkeeping Action'
 Mr. Peltz said the Great Lakes action was rare in the industry, because few savings institutions had enough capital to be able to add to their reserves. In June, Great Lakes Bancorp raised $27 million in new capital by selling preferred stock.
Good will is the difference between what an institution pays to acquire loans and mortgages, and what those assets are actually worth on the open market.
Until last year, savings institutions were allowed to include good will as regulatory capital, the funds they must set aside to serve as a cushion in bad operating years. As part of the Government's attempt to stem the huge losses arising from failing savings units, Congress voted in 1989 to require them to exclude good will from regulatory capital by the end of 1994.
Mr. Peltz said the Great Lakes good will write-off was "essentially a bookkeeping action, which has already been taken into account by investors."
The move will reduce Great Lakes' good will assets to approximately $50 million, according to Robert J. Delonis, the bank's president. Mr. Delonis said the institution did not expect to write off its remaining good will "for several years."
Great Lakes stock, traded on Nasdaq, fell $1.50 today to close at $8.

HEADLINE: Big Swings of Fortune From Rise in Oil Price

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
The jump in oil prices since the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait has produced enormous swings in the fortunes of economies across the globe. Oil-producing countries will be the biggest winners in the standoff, although some experts say that they may not fare as well as many presume.
People who follow world markets said Saudi Arabia, for example, could spend half its windfall from increased oil production and higher prices to aid needier supporters of the coalition against Iraq and to help pay for the American military operation -- obligations that could last longer than the high oil prices.
 
 'Pentagon Dollars'
 "Petrodollars are becoming Pentagon dollars," said David D. Hale, chief economist at Kemper Financial Services Inc. 
For oil-importing nations, particularly those in the developing world that can ill afford higher energy costs, the losses could be more severe than expected.
On top of these higher costs, some importers, like Turkey and Jordan, are losing billions of dollars in income from their citizens who lost jobs in Kuwait, Iraq and Saudi Arabia. These and other countries are also losing out on trade with Iraq and Kuwait.
What is more, developing countries like Brazil will not benefit from petrodollars as they did during the oil crisis of the 1970's, when American banks recycled oil exporters' profits by making loans to Latin America.
Neal M. Soss, chief economist at the First Boston Corporation, and other analysts expect much of the increase in petrodollars from the crisis to be invested by the oil exporters in United States Treasury securities.
 
 No Significant Lift
 He said the oil exporters will want to save the windfall rather then spend it because oil prices are not expected to remain high for long. For the United States, this recycling could help lower interest rates slightly but would not provide any significant lift to an American economy weakened by soaring energy costs.
Among oil exporters, Saudi Arabia is on top of the list of winners from the gulf crisis in terms of increased oil sales, followed by the Soviet Union, Iran and the other major oil exporters, including the United Arab Emirates, Nigeria, Indonesia, Ecuador, Norway, Algeria, Venezuela and Mexico.
While several of these countries, like Ecuador, are small oil exporters, the dollar increases are significant, since for all but the United Arab Emirates and the Soviet Union net oil exports account for more than 5 percent of their gross domestic product, according to Data Resources Inc., an economic forecasting concern in Lexington, Mass.
"You don't want to get the image that this is an awful shock to the whole world," said Roger Brinner, chief economist at Data Resources. "There are a lot of winners."
 
 Revenue Gains in Percent
 According to the Petroleum Finance Company in Washington, an oil consulting firm, Saudi Arabia's oil revenues since August are 99 percent above pre-invasion forecasts. Based on the same analysis, Venezuela's oil income rose 94.7 percent; Indonesia's, 80.3 percent; Iran's, 45.4 percent, and Ecuador's, 58.3 percent. In Venezuela, for instance, the oil windfall is a lift to an economy whose reforms in 1989 touched off riots in which 300 people were killed.
In terms of the net gains after accounting for contributions to support the standoff, Hossein Askari, a professor of international finance at George Washington University in Washington, argued that Iran and the Soviet Union were probably the top winners.
Starved for hard currency, the Soviet Union, which is supporting the coalition but not providing troops or money, could make an additional $1.35 billion a month in oil revenues if oil prices stay around the current level of $27.50 a barrel, he said. Iran, which also in not providing financial assistance in the standoff, could reap an additional $1 billion a month.
While Saudi Arabia gets the biggest oil windfall -- $3.5 billion to $4 billion a month -- Professor Askari said these gains could be shaved in half by its commitments to aid countries like Egypt and to back the United States military operation.
These obligations are expected to last longer than the oil price rise, he said. The reason is that the economic damage to members of the coalition will take a long time to repair, while oil prices are expected to drop if there is a settlement or even a quick war.
 
 Eventual Price Fall Expected
 The increase in production by Saudi Arabia and other producers like Venezuela and the return of production from Iraq and Kuwait should increase the available oil supply and drive down prices after the crisis is resolved. Such a decline could create serious tensions within the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries and could sharply reduce the revenue of individual producers.
According to the Treasury Department, Saudi Arabia and the other gulf states have already contributed $8.8 billion to a fund to aid countries hurt by the crisis. Japan and the European Community have given $4.2 billion to the fund. Among the countries already getting aid are Egypt, Turkey, Jordan, Lebanon, Syria, Djibouti and Morocco.
In addition to paying for the military operation, Saudi Arabia has contributed $760 million; Kuwait, $2.5 billion, and the United Arab Emirates, $250 million to the Pentagon. (Germany has contributed $272 million, and Japan, $426 million.)
 
 Aid Level Criticized
 But some American officials say that Saudi Arabia, Germany and Japan have not contributed enough to the cost of the standoff and are putting pressure on them for more money. Neither Germany nor Japan has provided troops. Representative Richard A. Gephardt, Democrat of Missouri, the House majority leader, added his voice to those of the critics last week.
Another beneficiary of higher oil prices is Britain, which is a net exporter of energy. But the gains are not enough to keep Britain out of a recession. The slowdown in Europe and the United States caused by the oil price increase will hurt Britain's declining economy by reducing export sales.
For countries like Venezuela and Nigeria, rising oil prices are nothing but good news. But officials at the World Bank note that it could become a longer-term detriment if those countries use the money to expand government spending programs and then the windfall disappears.
On the losing side, the list is much longer because more countries import oil than export it. The list includes the United States; Japan, which has virtually no oil of its own; most of Europe; Eastern Europe, and all the oil importing countries of the developing world in Africa, Asia and Latin America.
 
 List of 51 Countries Hurt Most
 The World Bank has prepared a list of 51 seriously affected countries, including Chad, India, Panama, Uruguay, Brazil, South Korea, Hungary, Poland, Romania and Yugoslavia. Of course, Iraq and Kuwait are among the most obvious losers, losing billions in oil revenues and facing the threat of devastation from war.
The countries most sharply affected, whose net imports of oil equal 5 percent or more of their gross domestic product, are Turkey, the Philippines, Greece, Portugal, Thailand, Egypt, Jordan and Nicaragua.
In the Philippines, for instance, the price jump has set off protests and strikes and left the coup-plagued Government of President Corazon C. Aquino facing its worst economic crisis since 1986.
In Turkey, higher oil prices could siphon off about $3 billion, or 3.7 percent of the country's economic growth. In addition, thousands of its citizens who worked in Saudi Arabia, Iraq and Kuwait have been sent home since the invasion.
According to the World Bank, the total pay lost by these workers could equal about 2.5 percent of the countries' gross domestic product in 1988. Turkey has also lost trade to Kuwait and Iraq, which together bought 11.7 percent of Turkey's exports in 1987, the bank said.
But foreign aid from Saudi Arabia and other members of the Gulf Cooperation Council should help offset the damage. And Turkey's support of the West could give it additional leverage in its quest to join the European Community.
 
 Losses for Jordan
 Jordan is another big loser, both economically and politically. Besides being a major oil importer, it has lost money from worker remittances, has had to cope with the refugees from Kuwait after the Iraqi invasion, has lost aid from Saudi Arabia because of its support of President Saddam Hussein of Iraq and at the same time has lost out on trade with Kuwait and Iraq because of its support of the United Nations embargoes. Its continued support of the Iraqi leader may severely damage its relations with the West over the long term.
Among the other countries that face rising oil bills and loss of remittances are Egypt, the Philippines, Bangladesh, Pakistan and the Sudan.
East European countries, struggling to build market economies after more than 40 years of Communism, face a drain of $8 billion to $10 billion when the Soviet Union ends its subsidies on Jan. 1 and sells oil to them at market prices. The jump in the oil price to an average of $25 a barrel will double those costs.

HEADLINE: Data General's Uncertain Future

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BOSTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
The Data General Corporation, a company that helped make the minicomputer business soar a decade ago and was lionized in the Pulitzer Prize-winning book "Soul of a New Machine," made a clean break with its past this month by forcing out its founder and chairman, Edson de Castro. But the company is still trying to prove it has a future, and many industry analysts are not optimistic.
Sixteen months after the company leaped from machines that use proprietary hardware and software to embrace the new wave of "open" systems that has built up around the widely adopted Unix operating-system standard, Data General has sold more than $100 million worth of the new computers. The machines -- work stations and servers that act as hosts for a network of PC's -- have been well received by consultants and customers. 
 But analysts say sales of the new computers are not growing fast enough to offset the erosion of Data General's proprietary minicomputers. Intense competition from I.B.M., Hewlett-Packard, Sun Microsystems and Digital Equipment is cutting heavily into sales, and margins are much lower than in the lucrative proprietary market. What is more, the new Data General machines are based on a Motorola microprocessor that no other major vendor has yet adopted.
But Ronald L. Skates, president and chief executive since November 1989, is optimistic that more aggressive marketing in the coming year will help the Westborough, Mass., company make the jump to open systems successfully. "We need to focus on selling something -- the days of products selling themselves are over," he said. A major advertising campaign is scheduled to begin in January.
In putting pressure on Mr. de Castro to step down, effective next Monday, Data General is signaling the end of an era that it is eager to put behind it. But people inside and outside the company say Mr. de Castro has had little to do with day-to-day operations for two years, and analysts think his leaving will have no impact. Mr. de Castro has not announced his plans for the future, and no new chairman has been named.
The contrast between Mr. de Castro, who is 52 years old, and Mr. Skates, who is 49, is stark. Mr. de Castro is a talented engineer; Mr. Skates, a Harvard M.B.A. and certified public accountant, is a strong financial manager who lacks technical credentials. Realizing that a heavy hand was necessary, he has overseen a slashing of costs and personnel at Data General since he joined the company in 1986.
A high-flying operation founded in 1968, Data General was the focus of "Soul of a New Machine," by Tracy Kidder.But it has been losing money since 1985, and last month reported a $140 million loss for the 1990 fiscal year, with a drop in revenues from $1.31 billion, to $1.22 billion. Mr. Skates said earlier this month that the company would not comment on financial results for the first quarter of 1991, which ends Saturday. John W. Adams of Adams, Harkness & Hill in Boston reports that the company "is making some progress."
But most analysts are not optimistic. "The future of Data General is not bright," says Marc Schulman, vice president of UBS Securities Inc. "Their problems are not going to ease up; they'll continue to lose money and the economic environment makes it very tough for them."
 
 'We Built This New Strategy'
 "The erosion of their installed MV minicomputer base is happening faster than the growth of their Aviion open-systems base," added Thomas Willmott, vice president of the Aberdeen Group in Boston. "The prospects are grim."
Such dire predictions are familiar to Mr. Skates and J. Thomas West, a senior vice president and the company's technology leader who was a central figure in "Soul of a New Machine" for his work on the company's minicomputers. The two look haggard as a result of the fiscal struggles, but they remain optimistic about Data General's strategy.
"We've known for the past three years that we wouldn't be profitable as we built this new strategy," Mr. Skates said. "Now we know we have to get profitable as fast as we can. We've recruited a new team, we've added new customers with our Aviion line and we believe in the strategy we're following." He noted that the company would introduce a more powerful version of Aviion in April.
The strategy, initiated by Mr. de Castro nearly four years ago, was to continue to support the company's proprietary Eclipse MV minicomputer line, but also move to the Aviion open systems line.
Under this plan, the company has been forced to endure widespread and painful cost cutting. Employment has been cut virtually in half from a peak of nearly 18,000 in 1984 to 9,300. In the last three years, there have been two major consolidations, resulting in write-offs of $278 million.
 
 Morale Called Low
 A parade of top officers left the company. Eleven of 27 corporate officers in the 1989 annual report are gone. Over the last three years, Mr. Skates has recruited 17 new divisional vice presidents and corporate officers, and they have engineered a reorganization of the company into business units.
The extensive cost cutting and layoffs have been effective on the balance sheet but have left morale low. "I left in July after 12 years," said a former Data General engineer who requested anonymity. "I recently had dinner with a guy who is still there and he told me, 'Morale is worse than when you left.' I found it hard to believe because it was rock bottom when I left."
Even good news has been tainted. Data General received a $127 million contract with the United States Geological Survey, but Congressional investigators are looking into accusations by two Interior Department employees that the contract was rigged in the company's favor by a top aide to President Bush, Ronald Kaufman. Company officials deny any wrongdoing. Data General officials say the issue is closed and the company has already begun shipping the 6,000 Aviion work stations and servers called for in the contract.
Despite this and continued bad economic news, Mr. Skates thinks the worst is over. He said that Data General had a strong balance sheet, with only $57 million in long-term debt, and a low debt-to-equity ratio of 14 percent. The company has sold off several million square feet of space and "can keep going if we go at our current rate."
Through it all, Data General has stuck to its new script.
 
 'Orphan Technology' Cited
 The results have been mixed at best. The Aviion work stations and servers have met with some good reviews. "The hardware is rock solid; we haven't needed a single service call," says Tom LaMarche, data systems manager for the Maricopa County Flood Control District in Phoenix, a Data General customer for 10 years.
First-year sales reached $100 million and Mr. Adams predicts Aviion will grow to $500 million in sales "in fairly short order." But Mr. Schulman warned of tough competition from other computer makers.
In addition, consultants note that Aviion is based on Motorola's 88000 RISC chip, a microprocessor that no other large vendor has yet adopted. "They are out with an orphan technology," Mr. Willmott of Aberdeen said. "Everyone else is at Intel."
Mr. West countered that Motorola, as a $10 billion company, was committed to the chip and that others would jump on the bandwagon soon. Besides, Mr. West said, there are already 1,000 applications from 500 third-party software vendors running on Aviion.
 
 Committed to Its Base
 Rather than abandoning the lucrative MV line, with $600 million in revenues and a reported installed base of 40,000 machines and 200,000 users, Data General says it remains committed to that base. In the last 18 months, Mr. West said, the company has revamped the entire MV line, bringing out a fifth generation of systems, including the recently announced MV/30000.
"It was simply smart business," said Carolyn Griffin, a senior analyst at the International Data Corporation in Framingham, Mass. "They had too many systems to support so they streamlined the MV line. The proprietary line gives them much better margins than the Unix line and it lets the installed base know that it is going to be supported."
Unfortunately, the installed base is eroding -- 24 percent by revenue in 1990, according to Mr. Adams.
Customers are rooting for Data General to succeed. "The performance of their products is excellent," said Robert Neese, director of management information systems for Health One, a 16-hospital nonprofit organization in Minneapolis. "I'm comfortable with the equipment I have, but for future buys, I have to look real hard at Data General's finances. Profitability is a major element of the equation."

HEADLINE: MOSCOW PROMISES TO REMAIN STEADY IN FOREIGN AFFAIRS

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
Soviet officials have pledged to the Bush Administration to continue coordinating efforts to resolve the Persian Gulf crisis and several civil wars, from El Salvador to Cambodia, in keeping with policies promoted by the departing Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze.
"They are conveying continuity and we are reasonably optimistic," said a State Department official. 
 American officials said meetings held by President Bush and Robert M. Kimmitt, the Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, with Ambassador Aleksandr A. Bessmertnykh this week, along with lower-level exchanges in Moscow, convinced the Administration that President Mikhail S. Gorbachev remains fully in charge of Soviet foreign policy, at least for the time being. Doubts had arisen because of his severe domestic problems, including economic shortages and challenges from rebellious republics.
 
Reassurances for Washington
 Officials refused to comment on the contents of a New Year's note Mr. Gorbachev sent to President Bush, but they said its friendly tenor signaled Moscow's desire to improve relations further at the summit meeting scheduled in Moscow in February.
They said the Soviets continue to express a willingness to complete work at the summit on a new treaty limiting strategic nuclear arms. That initiative was pressed by Mr. Shevardnadze before his surprise resignation last week and apparently was resisted by some conservatives in the Kremlin.
A senior Administration official said Mr. Gorbachev may have given himself more latitude in foreign affairs "at least in the short term" by appearing more open to the concerns of conservatives on domestic and security matters. Speaking of traditional elements in the Communist Party, the military and K.G.B., he said, "The focus of their pressure is much more on internal policy than on external policy."
 
Recurring Theme: Continuity
 He said that most Soviet conservatives who want to limit democratic changes at home tend to believe that Mr. Gorbachev has coordinated his foreign policy too closely to Washington's and "given away the empire," but they have not come up with a unified proposal on how to redirect foreign policy.
Mr. Bessmertnykh requested the 30-minute meeting he held with Mr. Bush on Thursday to reassure the Administration that no policy swings were anticipated in the Kremlin. American officials said the Soviet Ambassador held a more detailed 45-minute session with Mr. Kimmitt on Monday, in which he made a series of commitments that cheered the Administration.
A State Department official said Mr. Kimmitt emphasized the need for Moscow to continue promoting its "united front" with Washington with the approach of the Jan. 15 deadline for Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait or face eviction by force under a United Nations Security Council resolution. "Mr. Bessmertnykh agreed to that," the official said.
The official said "the recurring theme" of the Kimmitt-Bessmertnykh meeting was: "They want to continue working with us and we want to continue working with them."
 
Kremlin's Central America Role
 The two diplomats devoted the most time to the month-old guerrilla offensive in El Salvador, with Mr. Kimmitt suggesting that Moscow use its influence to stop Cuba and Nicaragua's Sandinista Army from sending arms, particularly antiaircraft missiles, to rebels of the Farabundo Marti National Liberation Front.
Mr. Bessmertnykh reiterated Moscow's traditional line that its influence over Cuban and Nicaraguan arms transfers is limited. But he pledged that Moscow would do what it could to restart the stalled United Nations-sponsored peace talks between the Salvadoran rebels and the Government of President Alfredo Cristiani.
Administration officials said they valued the Soviet Union's involvement in Central America now that it no longer actively supports revolutionary movements. They said Moscow had helped prove that SA-14 antiaircraft missiles deployed not long ago by the Salvadoran guerrillas came from Nicaragua by identifying the serial number on an SA-14 missile casing found near a downed aircraft as that of a missile the Soviets had donated to the Nicaraguan Army in 1985.
The Administration hopes such evidence will strengthen the hand of the Nicaraguan President, Violeta Barrios de Chamorro, as she tries to control the Sandinista-dominated military.
 
Talks on Afghan War
 Touching briefly on a series of other regional conflicts, Mr. Bessmertnykh promised Mr. Kimmitt that Moscow intended to continue nudging the pro-Soviet Government in Cambodia to compromise on forming an interim Government with a guerrilla coalition, in advance of United Nations-sponsored elections.
He also pledged that Moscow would continue discussions with Washington to settle the civil war in Afghanistan, in which rebels armed by the United States are fighting a regime supported by the Soviet Union. Those efforts have stalled in recent months over several issues, including the timing of future elections, the details of a proposed cutoff of Soviet and United States arms shipments and the future political role of President Najibullah.
Soviet-American relations in the post-Shevardnadze age will face their first test in early January, when peace talks to resolve the civil war in Angola between pro-American guerrillas and a pro-Soviet Government are to resume in a potentially decisive session in Portugal.
 
Complications Ahead?
 An American official said Mr. Bessmertnykh and Mr. Kimmitt expressed their Governments' willingness "to keep up the heat on both sides."
Leonid Sofanov, an aide to Mr. Bessmertnykh, said "we completely agree with our American colleagues that we can work together" on helping the two sides in Angola find a fair solution.
But Administration officials cautioned that complications in United States-Soviet relations could lie ahead. One warned that the appointment of one of the Soviet Government's Arabists as Foreign Minister could soften Moscow's apparent support for the use of force to drive Iraq out of Kuwait.

HEADLINE: Tough Clean Air Bill Signed in Massachusetts

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BOSTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
Gov. Michael S. Dukakis has signed a clean air bill that adopts California's auto emissions standards, the toughest in the country.
The new emission standards, to be phased in from 1995 to 2003, are intended to reduce smog-producing pollutants sharply. 
 Based on California's new tailpipe standards, the law requires auto makers to cut emissions of hydrocarbons by 75 percent and nitric oxide by 50 percent beyond the requirements of the new Federal Clean Air Act. Hydrocarbons and nitric oxide are the chief components of smog and contribute to acid rain as well as to the warming trend known as the greenhouse effect, environmentalists say.
The new law, signed Thursday, also requires that Massachusetts adopt California's new warranty, testing and recall provisions.
 
The Cost of Bad Air
 "We are paying a tremendous economic cost for bad air, and this is a piece of state legislation that has national implications," said Representative David Cohen, a Democrat from Newton who sponsored the bill in the House. "The problem with other states is just that none of them had the courage" to follow California, he said.
Mr. Cohen said the new law is expected to help reduce air pollution in the state by 25 percent.
According to environomental experts, all other Northeastern states, as well as Texas and Illinois, are considering adopting California's new standards.
California has had standards that are more stringent than those set by the Federal Government since the 1970's. States can adopt either the Federal standards or California standards.
 
Action in New York
 This fall, environmentalists applauded New York for adopting the same anti-pollution standards that are to go into effect in California for cars in the 1993 model year. Under its new law, Massachusetts will adopt the even stricter standards that California has set for cars in the 1994 model year. New York and several other states are expected to adopt the more stringent emission control standards as well.
"We hope its passing in this state will give other states the impetus to do the same," said Jenny Carter, an environmental lawyer with the Massachusetts Public Interest Research Group.
California officials have estimated that the anti-pollution equipment would cost about $170 a car. Other auto industry estimates put the price at about $300.
Under an agreement with automobile manufacturers, supporters of the Massachusetts bill agreed to postpone the date the new anti-pollution equipment must be in place from model year 1993 to model year 1995. The 1995 cars will go on sale in late 1994.
The new law will require that pollution control equipment be covered by a warranty for seven years or 70,000 miles. It would also require that the automobiles carry diagnostic equipment to alert the driver if the anti-pollution equipment was not working, Ms. Carter said.
The new law would also allow the state to order a recall if the pollution-control equipment failed at an unacceptable rate, she said.

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
The Buses That Haul In Players

BYLINE: By Ira Berkow

 BODY:
I had this dream. In the dream, buses from colleges backed up into black communities and made off with talented athletes and, despite the pressures of big-time athletics and the crassness and callousness and hypocrisy of many of those colleges, these athletes took advantage of the classroom opportunity and went on to make something of themselves.
 This dream was inspired by a segment on "60 Minutes" shown last Sunday night. The segment depicted some of the attitudes and actualities in top-level college sports, particularly in relation to blacks, black players having risen to dominance nationwide in recent years in the revenue-producing sports of basketball and football. 
 This dream was precipitated by a remark on the "60 Minutes" segment from Harry Edwards, the prominent black sociology professor from the University of California at Berkeley.
 "We still have a situation," Edwards said, "where essentially traditionally white institutions back buses up to the black community every recruiting season, haul off busloads of kids, bring them onto these traditionally white middle-class campuses, use them up, and then dump 75 percent of them right back into those same communities."
 It is true that athletic scholarships are not quite free, and that it is a veritable full-time job to play big-time sports for a university. It is also true that historically many without much funding from home -- athletes or otherwise -- but thrilled to have been admitted to a good college, have had to work long hours busing tables or sweeping floors or working on a garbage truck in summer to pay for tuition and board and books, and the occasional date to the movies.
 But too often, as "60 Minutes" illustrated, former stars like the Pancho Wrights of the University of Louisville and the Skip Hendersons of Marshall, who apparently majored in eligibility, don't earn degrees, don't get pro contracts and become lost souls, reduced to working freelance construction (Wright) or being arrested and accused of stealing a car (Henderson). And two current black players -- Dwayne Morton and Brian Hopgood of Louisville -- when whisked into the syndrome, say they are in college for just one reason. "Basketball," Hopgood said. "Make it to the N.B.A. and make big bucks."
 Meanwhile, a coach like Denny Crum of Louisville earns a salary of $250,000 a year, plus a $1 million bonus at the end of his contract, and he thinks naturally the system, which nets the school millions, is just fine, even though the dream of making the pros is achieved by merely 1.6 percent of all college athletes, with an average pro career being less than four years.
 Some school chancellors and coaches, and editorial writers, have paid attention to true schooling for these athletes, but the current system is so entrenched that it seems little will change in that nebulous period known as the forseeable future.
 On "60 Minutes," Edwards also pointed a finger at "black parents and the black community."
"In general," he said, they have "first set up young black athletes by allowing them to develop a higher priority on playbooks than on textbooks. The parents end up without the ticket out of the community they thought they were going to have."
 In the dream I had, these streetwise youths realized that, as Edwards and Arthur Ashe, among others, have urged, they must become educated in class, as a way to be extricated from the underclass. They realized by their street wits that it is a hard and cruel world and that the only people who will truly be looking out for them are not the recruiters or the coaches but themselves.
 I dreamed they comprehended that the percentages were so heavily weighted against their succeeding as pros that they began to regularly attend and even pay attention in the economics class, the philosophy class, the physics class.
 A number of years ago, Andrew Toney, a high school star in Birmingham, Ala., received a basketball scholarship to the University of Southwestern Louisiana. Before going off to school, his father, Ernest, laid down the law: you are going to college to learn in the classroom, he told Andrew. We wouldn't have the money to send you otherwise. It's a rare opportunity you're getting, and I don't want you to waste it. "My father knew of some others in the neighborhood who had received athletic scholarships," Toney recalled yesterday, "and they hadn't taken advantage of the education."
 It happened that Andrew Toney became a standout basketball player in college and one of the handful good enough to even make the pros. For several years he was a star guard with the Philadelphia 76ers, until an injury cut short his career.
 It's also true that Andrew Toney did so well in the classroom that he graduated from college in three and a half years.
 Andrew Toney's father had a dream, too, about that college bus that backed up into his community and hauled off his son, and the lad had the good sense to realize that dream for himself.
