The New York Times

PUB-DATE: December 30, 1990

SECTION: Section 6; Page 10; Column 1; Magazine Desk

LENGTH: 4030 words

HEADLINE: Daring The Impossible

BYLINE: By Glenn Collins; Glenn Collins reports on culture for The New York Times.

 BODY:
Hanging upside down under the silver dome of the Tarrant County Convention Center in Fort Worth, 18 feet from the taut grid of the safety net below, his massive arms readying for the catch, Vilen Golovko sensed that something was dangerously wrong. Pyotr Serdukov, his flying partner for nine years in the Cranes, the aerialists of the Moscow Circus, seemed out of control. Pyotr, having exploded off the fly bar to soar in the four tucked turns of the quadruple somersault, was spinning too quickly -- overrotating, a gymnast would say. Now, arms outstretched to meet Vilen's midair grasp, Pyotr was too advanced for Vilen to catch the trick cleanly.
The rapt crowd of 1,500 Texans below had no idea what they were witnessing: if missed by Vilen, Pyotr -- turning at a speed of as much as 70 miles an hour -- could break his neck by tumbling to the net in an out-of-control fall. Or, if deflected beyond the net by a bungled catch, Pyotr could be killed in his plummet to the floor. 
 The 32-year-old captain of the Cranes did the only thing he could do. From the day that Vilen Golovko began catching for his troupe in 1981, he had reassured his fliers that it was his responsibility -- no matter what the cost -- to gather them in. Vilen knew now that he was certain to be hit by Pyotr. Nevertheless, he flexed forward powerfully and attempted to ensnare Pyotr's wrists with his crushing grip.
Vilen has no memory of what came next, something of a blessing because he received a stunning blow as Pyotr's knee slammed into his cheek. The flier slipped through Vilen's senseless fingers and caromed toward the floor at a bad angle, smashing into the net and hurting his back and shoulder. Vilen's fate was less immediately painful; he lost consciousness, suffering a concussion. He hung limply, his legs braced in the catch bar, swinging slowly, upside down, until another aerialist clambered across the rigging to rescue him. The soaring music of the Cranes continued, the act's seven other fliers kept performing and only a few audience members noticed as Vilen was lowered and carried from the arena.
This near disaster, which occurred on Dec. 10, 1989, did not keep Vilen and Pyotr out of the air for long. The improbably resilient Russians were throwing quadruple somersaults again within a month. But by then they were flying with new understanding. For, as the partners analyzed the accident, as they called to memory every split-second impression and fragmentary image of that midair collision, one conclusion was inescapable: The miss had occurred not because Pyotr was out of control but because he was flying too well. He was flying so high and turning so fast, that he had done four and a half somersaults instead of the requisite four. And if Pyotr could do four and a half without even trying to, then what would he be able to accomplish with disciplined effort?
The answer was intensely exciting: the Five. That's how Vilen and Pyotr refer to it, as if it were capitalized. The Five: five back somersaults from the flying trapeze to the hands of the catcher, a feat never achieved -- and scarcely imagined -- in the 131-year history of the trapeze.

REVIOUSLY, THE DREAM of circus aerialists, the four-minute mile of trapeze flying, had been the quadruple somersault. The feat was first attempted in the late 1970's and finally achieved in performance, in Tucson, Ariz., on July 10, 1982, by Miguel Vazquez and his brother Juan, flying for the Ringling Brothers and Barnum & Bailey Circus. The Cranes, the only other fliers ever to achieve the quadruple consistently, never thought seriously of attempting five.
But after the accident in Fort Worth, the quintuple somersault became a goal. Despite failure, injury and the vagaries of the unforeseen, the Cranes have steadily pursued the Five in rehearsal during the Moscow Circus's current 20-city tour of arena America. (On Jan. 16, the Circus, with the Cranes, will begin a two-week engagement at Radio City Music Hall in Manhattan.) Their progress is a tale of state-of-the-art acrobatics, world-class athleticism and high-tech apparatus. But it is also the story of one of the most determined, innovative and curious groups of performers in the world, whose spectacular antigravity dance transcends any mere acrobatic feat.
How to describe the flight of the Cranes? Try imagining three catchers and six fliers, all of them schooled in Soviet acrobatics and all of them performing simultaneously on a multilevel, $200,000 aerial grid. They fly skyward on electrically winched cables and hurtle from swings they call levitors, performing a seemingly weightless ballet that enacts the words of a Russian song. Their soaring leaps and explosive midair exchanges are accompanied by strobe lighting, stage smoke and the music of Tchaikovsky, Bach, Liszt, Ravel, Stravinsky and Wagner.
The Cranes use their safety net as both a trampoline and, in the context of their narrative performance, a symbol of the earth. Their text, derived from a poem by Rasul Gamzatov, celebrates the souls of soldiers who have perished and who are transformed into white cranes that ascend to the sky. It is a powerful, traditional Russian image, which also informs "The Cranes Are Flying," the World War II Soviet love story that won the best film award in Cannes in 1958. On the trapeze, the last of the fallen, played by Vilen Golovko's wife, Lena, is carried skyward so that peace may prevail. Although all of this activity has been scored, choreographed and lighted for maximum effect, the act can even be inadvertently magical: at one point, as the Cranes perform aerial feats in front of a celestial backdrop, clouds of powdered magnesium from the fliers' hands begin sifting down like star drift.
"They are really astonishingly great artists," says Jerome Robbins, the legendary choreographer, "and the work is incredible from a choreographic point of view. People in the dance field have been working on ropes and ladders, and doing things in the air, but no one has ever come close to what the Cranes do. They're not just passed back and forth, as it is usually done in dance. There is real creativity here. It truly gives the feeling of flying."
I first met the flying Cranes in August 1988, in Toronto, where they had touched earth for the first time on the North American continent. Vilen Golovko was lounging under a leaking circus dining tent as a mammoth thunderstorm approached.
I asked him why the Cranes never announced the completion of the quadruple somersault during their act, as do the Flying Vazquez. "Because our greatest achievement is not the quadruple," he said, through a translator, "but the simultaneity of our aerial gymnastics and the psychological effectiveness of our acting -- all of it working together to move an audience."
The thunder and lightning got closer. The rain on the tent grew louder, but Vilen seemed to become even more relaxed. "The individual trick means nothing in comparison to the overall impression," he continued. "And our act is not a series of tricks."
Soon the rain was so torrential that we put our feet up on chairs to keep from being inundated by the rivers of water coursing under the open sides of the tent. An especially close lightning pulse produced a deafening thunderclap. Vilen seemed not to notice.
"Nevertheless," he said softly, "we do the four because we wanted to do the most difficult trick in the world. We strive to reach perfection in our own work. We want to be the best, and do the impossible."
Two years later, Vilen was in North America again, resting in the bleacher seating of the deserted arena at the Trump Taj Mahal Casino Resort in Atlantic City. This time, he was oblivious not to lightning bolts but to the scurrying of cleaning crews that were vacuuming popcorn from under the seats.
Less boyish than he'd been in Toronto, Vilen was as inscrutable as ever, his broad face dominated by sleepy eyes that belie the active mind within. The Cranes would never try for a trick "if it got in the way of our theme," he said, as if the conversation in Toronto had never been interrupted. He quoted a maxim of one of his father's teachers: "In circus, if a costume gets in the way of a trick, get rid of the trick." Still, the Cranes had always assumed that they were the best in the world at whatever they did. And the quintuple had come knocking on their door. How could they not go for the Five?
"Psychological readiness is just as important as physical readiness," Vilen said of achieving the quintuple. "Pyotr must believe he can do it. And I believe in Pyotr."
There are several key aspects of the quintuple somersault that make it so dangerous and so seemingly impossible. The flier must turn five somersaults quickly enough to "open up" -- to stretch out his body from the tucked-in position -- in time to extend his hands so the catcher can grab his wrists. The flight path of the Five is very different from that of the quadruple, whose symmetrical pattern curiously resembles a highway cloverleaf in the air. To achieve the asymmetry of the Five, Pyotr would have to complete his first three somersaults near the top of his arc. This would enable him to complete two more turns during his descent to the catcher, who must compensate for the additional speed and momentum.
"Imagine a swirling ball coming into your face," Vilen said, "and for part of a second you see the arms in their place. And you have a millisecond to catch the flier. If Pyotr is too advanced in his rotation, then I need to compensate and to contrive to catch him by moving my own body."
Is that similar to what he had tried to do in Fort Worth? "The catcher is responsible for the flier's life, and he knows that," Vilen answered. "The flier is totally involved in his own trick, and so the catcher must make the decisions. Subconsciously, you know when to catch. And you know when not to catch. There is no time for thinking."

"IS THE FIVE IMPOSSIBLE?"
Tito Gaona, the most consistently daring young man on the flying trapeze in our time, repeated my question. He smiled. "That's just what they used to wonder about the triple and the quadruple," he said.
Nearly 30 years ago, as a 14-year-old member of a Mexican trampoline act, Gaona was inspired to be a flier by the movie "Trapeze." The film starred Burt Lancaster and Tony Curtis as the two aerialists who first threw the triple somersault. (The trick was actually first thrown by Ernest Clarke to Charles Clarke in Cuba in 1909.) Gaona himself was the first modern flier to do the triple somersault consistently. Today, at 43, he estimates he has done more than 20,000 triples in his 25-year career.
It was in 1977 that Gaona attempted to become the first aerialist ever to achieve a quadruple somersault, but he never managed it in performance. Five years later, however, he predicted the success of Juan and Miguel Vazquez. "Miguel flies very high and very fast, and his brother Juan is a great catcher," Gaona told me in 1982, months before the Vazquez brothers achieved their goal in Tucson.
And now, in 1990, Gaona is still flying in the Circus Circus Hotel-Casino in Reno, and he is, as always, au courant about the art of the trapeze. "I first noticed that Pyotr was overrotating in August 1989," he said after a performance one night, explaining that he had watched his friends Pyotr and Vilen in practice. "Pyotr was so tiny and so light. I thought to myself, 'He can do five!' And Vil is a great catcher." Gaona looked very serious. "You know, the Five, it can be done by the Cranes. But they are really going to have to work at it."
Working is something the Cranes are accustomed to. They are the product of the hypercompetitive environment of pre-glasnost Soviet athletic training. Six of the Cranes have achieved the title of Master of Sport, meaning that they have arrived at the top rung of the Soviet sports academy. Recruited by Vilen Golovko and his father, Vilen Golovko Sr., a former aerialist himself, the Cranes began work almost exactly a decade ago, on Jan. 1, 1981. It took four more years to create their act, during a time when the Cranes lived on training athletes' starvation wages in Moscow.
"The early years were so very difficult," said 30-year-old Mikhail Illyushkin, recalling life in the Circus Studio rehearsal space in Moscow. "I don't think that I could withstand such pressure if I had to do it again. We would be four hours rehearsing in the air, then two hours on the floor, and then there was our class, that took place every day. This went on six days a week. There were injuries and accidents. One flier left the act after he cracked his forehead against the center perch and suffered a bad concussion. Exactly the same thing happened to another flier who was doing exactly the same trick -- a front somersault." Misha pointed to a two-inch scar on his leg. "And there was the time I ripped my knee." He shook his head. "I could stand it all, then, because I was young. All of us were so very young."
Added Vladimir Shumilin, the 32-year-old Crane who has, like Pyotr, mastered the quadruple somersault, "What kept us going was the idea that something extraordinary would develop."
That something extraordinary was, in the words of Golovko Sr., now 58, "a thematic aerial spectacle. I wanted it to be totally different from all other air acts in the world: it would have a theme. The idea was to mix theater and ballet and gymnastics and mime and circus."
The senior Golovko, who is often called Papa, is a dedicated chain smoker, with knowing eyes, iron-gray hair, a challenging jaw and a penchant for irony. For many years he made a living standing on his head on the flying trapeze. He later became the state circus administrator responsible for launching all circus acts in the Soviet Union. His son had entered the state circus school at the age of 14 and began performing as a circus acrobat at 16. After serving in the Army, he came to Papa at the age of 21, wondering what to do with his life.
"I told him, 'You continue your father's tradition and fly under the dome as I did,' " Papa recalled. "My idea was to create a flying act that no one had ever imagined before. And my son would put it together. My own experience told me that he could be a great aerialist."
To direct the effort, Papa turned to a rangy, graying director named Pyotr Maistrenko, also a former aerialist. Maistrenko, now 56, had been a private in the Russian Army during World War II and afterward suffered for a time from shell shock. He choreographed the Cranes' flight "to recapture the emotional effect that the war had on me," he recalled. "This is my story."
The Cranes speak of Maistrenko only with reverence. "I love him," Vilen said simply. "From the age of 21, he was like my father. He taught me how to be an artist, a person, a human being." Vilen had reservations about working with his own father, however. "But I thought, I cannot not recognize his experience and his knowledge."
Not only did Vilen struggle to preserve the Cranes during the years of rehearsal, but he also had to find flying equipment for the Cranes in a managed economy unaccustomed to allocating scarce materials to aerialists. Even after the act was up and ready to fly, disaster loomed. "Two weeks before the act was to premiere," said Shumilin, the flier, "after four years of practice, I went to a sports hall to practice -- and broke my hand." His acrobatic flight was essential to the premiere, which was also the athletes' final academic examination. The Cranes would have to fly before the state circus hierarchy, which approves acts and permits them to perform.
"I knew I just had to go up," Shumilin said. "And I did. I felt it impossible not to participate in the actual moment." Not only did the Cranes pass their examination, on March 25, 1985, but six weeks later they were the stars of the state Victory Day festivity in the Kremlin, celebrating the 40th anniversary of the triumph over Nazi Germany. In the audience were more than 3,000 World War II veterans. After the Cranes performed, many of the soldiers cried openly. "We always knew we could evoke such a response," Vilen recalled. "But it was another thing to actually see it."
These days, the Cranes are a circus collective of 25 -- including wives, children and technicians. They embody the tradition of circus in the Soviet Union, a mass entertainment that has the appeal vaudeville had in pre-television America. More than 100 million Soviets see the circus each year, under the aegis of Soyuzgoscirk, the monolithic state circus authority that performers call Soy.
But the Cranes have gone their own way. "Perestroika was in the Cranes from the very beginning, before it was in the country," Vilen said. In 1988 his father left Soyuzgoscirk, after being ousted with his entire administration. Papa Golovko created his own production studio, called Synthesis, sponsored by Moskva, another entertainment agency.
Papa decided he would bring the Cranes along with him to Moskva, and on April 1, 1989, Soyuzgoscirk reluctantly released the Cranes, partly because they had a fan named Mikhail Gorbachev. "Just by agreeing to break away and enter into a new arrangement, the Cranes are forcing the collapse of the old system," said Iosif Kobzon, a Moskva director, during a visit to the Cranes in Atlantic City this summer. "Prior to perestroika, all circus collectives were under the control of Soyuzgoscirk. It was an absolute monarchy. When an artist would go abroad for Soyuzgoscirk, it was not unusual for performers to get as little as $25 per diem on performance days."
But currently, performing for Moskva under the auspices of an American producer, Steven E. Leber, "the artists now get a salary, based on their contribution," Kobzon said. "The workers' collective decides, for example, that a horse tender should get $50 per show, the leading performers might be voted $120 per show. The director of the collective would receive $200 per show. This may not seem like a huge amount of money to American entertainers. But to our performers it is a great amount."
Papa Golovko puts it more bluntly. The Cranes are in "a very enviable position," he said. "No bureaucracy. No supervision. Moskva deducts 30 percent, and that's it. We have control of our own money. We are pioneering a new way of working in my country."

IT WAS THREE HOURS before show time, and Vilen Golovko, dressed, as usual, in black, was directing a Cranes practice session. They would be flying that night in the red plush spaces of the Ziegfeld Theater in the Bally's Casino Resort in Reno.
"It could take us a month, or a year, to do it," Vilen said of the quintuple. "We do it methodically, always remembering that the purpose of the act is not to do the Five. The purpose of the whole architecture of the Cranes is to tell the story line."
The Cranes have planned the assault on the quintuple in several well-defined stages, "all in strict accordance with the laws of physics," Vilen said. His eyes narrowed and he barely smiled. "Of course -- also in strict accordance with the laws of physics -- the quintuple is impossible."
Standing next to him, Pyotr Serdukov shrugged as he gestured to the twisted metal housing of a stage lamp that had broken his fall the previous week. A cable support had been improperly anchored, so that, as the music swelled and the other Cranes soared, Pyotr alone plummeted to the stage from a height of 20 feet, rebounding off the huge lamp. "I tore the ligaments in my left wrist and that was about it," he said casually. "I'll be flying again soon." But now, he explained impatiently, the quintuple was another few weeks farther from his grasp.
Pyotr's has been a life of impatience. Only six months after joining the Cranes at the age of 21, he was throwing the triple somersault. As for the quadruple, which he and Vilen have completed more than 150 times, it is "not difficult at all for me," he said. "I can do it six times a week, no problem. But I haven't been doing it, because it breaks my concentration for the Five."
The first flier to attempt the quintuple somersault was born in Chu, a city in Kazakhstan. Pyotr moved to Moscow at the age of 7 for athletic training, and started intensive acrobatics at 12. After he met Vilen, he contrived to be the first student in the history of his gymnastics school to be assigned to the circus school, which is administered by a competing Government agency.
Vilen joined Pyotr beside the battered lamp, and they began talking animatedly about the accident. Soon the conversation turned to the troupe's early days when Pyotr was trying to master the Cranes' first triple somersault.
"Pyotr didn't learn to fly with me," Vilen said impishly. "He learned on me! I still have the scars to prove it." He shrugged. "Of course, it was someone else who broke my nose -- and my jaw. Someone who weighed more."
"Yes, but in Fort Worth, I was able to knock you out, Vilya," Pyotr shot back. "That time, the lightweight won! With a knockout!"

IT WAS AFTER 1 A.M. ON A rainy Florida Saturday in late November, very late for a practice session in the Orlando Arena. Pyotr's shoulder was aching from a nasty fall in Auburn Hills, Mich., two weeks before, and Vilen's knee was acting up from a practice injury. But tonight, nonetheless, Vilen and Pyotr were going to try to put together the elements of the trick that each had been working on separately. Instead of coaching Pyotr from below, Vilen was ready to try to catch the quintuple for the first time.
Vilen, who weighs 172 pounds and is 5 feet 8 inches tall, climbed the rungs of the ladder to his catch bar, ascending in the fluid way of the Cranes. He reached his perch and languidly assumed the catcher's topsy-turvy stance.
When Pyotr danced up his ladder, he stepped onto the bridge, the elongated pedestal board from which he would push off into space. He was standing 49 feet from Vilen and 30 feet above the arena floor. His face rigid with concentration, Pyotr began visualizing the extreme exertion that lay ahead. He seemed oblivious to the belt attached to the lunge lines that would break his fall if the attempt ended badly. "Hup!" yelled Vilen, giving the signal to Pyotr to power away from the flying bridge, so he could swing out on the bar to pump up speed for his release. Pyotr, light and compact at 110 pounds and 5 feet tall, left the fly bar and arced toward the ceiling.
The world's first attempt to achieve the quintuple somersault took two seconds. It was not successful. Thirty minutes later, Vilen had shouted "Hup!" three more times. Pyotr had tried four times to throw the quintuple. Each time he had failed to connect with Vilen. Pyotr's weakened shoulder was preventing him from blasting off the fly bar with sufficient power to turn quickly enough to complete a full fifth turn and open up in time for the catch. Although he had performed five perfect turns many times practicing alone, he began to realize that this was not going to be his night.
After the fourth miss, Pyotr hit the net, hard, and looked exhausted. By now, Vilen and Pyotr were losing their concentration and their edge. That is when injuries occur, and the Cranes couldn't afford another injury. Pyotr began a slow descent to the arena floor.
As he did, a ripple of applause and shrill whistling drifted upward from the other Cranes, who had been watching. Vilen, returning from on high, looked as enigmatic as ever as he joined Pyotr. The Cranes gathered around them.
"We could have done it on the very first try," said Vilen, face deadpan. "I definitely could have caught Pyotr from behind if he'd only had hands on his backside! Will he consider surgery?"
With that, the assembled Cranes dissolved in laughter. Out came the paper cups and the vodka. The toasts began. "To Pyotr!" came first. Next: "To Vilen!" And then: "To the only performers in history ever to miss the Five!"
Certainly they were the first to miss. No one had ever tried it before. The quest for the quintuple had only begun.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 24; Column 1; Arts & Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 940 words

HEADLINE: VIDEO;
HDTV, Videodisks and Sharper Images Come to the Fore

BYLINE: By HANS FANTEL

 BODY:
In video, 1990 may be remembered as the year when high-definition television (HDTV) finally went from promise to reality. Regularly scheduled HDTV transmissions, yielding a picture as good as a top-notch photograph, went on the air for the first time this year in Japan.
Progamming is still limited to a few hours a day, and with sets costing upward of $5,000, the principal display sites are in hotel lobbies and other public places. This situation recalls the period when color television first appeared in the United States 30 years ago and sets were also too costly for most viewers. People would go to watch color TV at their neighborhood bars. 
 The historic parallel is encouraging. As was the case with color television, prices for HDTV are likely to drop with the eventual spread of the new medium. How soon will HDTV find its way to the United States? The darkening economy presents a melancholy prospect. By late 1990 it became clear that neither broadcasters nor viewers were likely to come up with the cash soon.
And there was still no hint from the Federal Communications Commission as to which of half a dozen competing methods of HDTV broadcasting would be approved as the future standard. Not even a preliminary recommendation is expected before the fall of 1992.
To break this impasse, Faroudja Research Enterprises, a small firm in Sunnyvale, Calif., has proposed an interim solution. The company has developed a method of image enhancement that requires no new broadcasting standards, no new channel allotments and not a lot of money. Last month, Faroudja announced that it would undertake test transmissions of its system on TV stations and cable systems early next year in several cities -- probably Boston, New York, San Francisco, Philadelphia and Washington.
The company's president, Yves Faroudja, is a French-born engineer whose prior inventions form the basis of Super-VHS and eight-millimeter video, concedes that his system isn't quite as good as HDTV. But with 1,025 scanning lines it is vastly superior to the present standard of 525 and comes close to the 1,125 scanning lines of full-fledged HDTV.
The main point is that the Faroudja signal can be transmitted through existing television and cable facilities, but present TV sets could not receive the enhanced picture, although they could still get the same quality picture as they do now. The cost of a home receiver capable of displaying the enhanced picture would be far less than that of a full-fledged HDTV set. A TV set capable of receiving the Faroudja system picture might cost about $300 more than present TV sets. The system might improve the quality of American television until HDTV becomes a reality in this country.
 Other video developments in 1990 are having an impact right now. One is the sudden rise in the popularity of videodisks, helped by heavy advertising and promotion. The trend confirms once again that viewers will buy better picture quality if it isn't too costly.
Disks are cheaper than cassettes and provide better pictures and sound, and the repertory available has expanded greatly. In many instances, the same program -- a full-length movie, for example -- can be had on disk for a price 30 to 50 percent lower than on cassette. According to industry estimates, nearly seven million videodisks were sold in 1990.
Also spurring the emergence of videodisks is the growing trend of combining sight and sound in a single "home theater" system. The central unit in such an arrangement is a combination (combi in trade talk) player, which plays both videodisks and compact disks.
Economic factors again figure importantly in this trend. The cost of combi-players has been dropping. Some now carry a list price as low as $500, making them competitive with VCR's.
 In television sets, there has been a general upgrading of picture quality. The better models can now produce a far sharper image than is obtainable from VHS cassettes, broadcasts or cable. The only way to take full advantage of these sets' capabilities is with videodisks.
The technical improvements in image quality -- involving digital data storage on some of the latest models -- make it possible to present larger pictures with acceptable sharpness and detail. This, in turn, accounts for rising sales among sets with larger screens, ranging from 27 to 35 inches, measured diagonally. Even the rather costly rear-projection sets, presenting images up to about 60 images diagonally, gained a larger share of the market in 1990.
While most companies applied the latest technical advances mainly to their larger models, NAD -- a Massachusetts-based company known mainly for its fine audio equipment -- took the opposite course and designed a 13-inch TV set incorporating advanced technology. Concentrated on the relatively small image area, these refinements yield a picture singular in detail and vividness. This small NAD set surely ranks among the more remarkable products of the year.
Among innovative products of 1990, some introduce new capabilities of enduring value: the electronically stabilized camcorder by Panasonic, which takes the jiggle out of videos made with hand-held equipment; Sharp's portable video projector, which throws a five-foot picture on any white wall; the tape-copying VCR by Go-Video that allows single-touch copying of cassettes; the Videonics Video Equalizer, which allows color correction of videotapes along with many other editing functions; and Gemstar's VCR-Plus, which makes it possible to program a VCR simply by punching in a certain set of digits for a specific program.

HEADLINE: SOUND;
It's the Numbers That Foretell The Future

BYLINE: By HANS FANTEL

 BODY:
Looking back is a good way to see where we are heading. In audio, the first year of the 90's has given us glimpses of technical advances that may take the rest of the decade to mature.
What may be the most important technical advance in audio -- digital broadcasting -- has attracted little notice in this country, aside from a small group of radio engineers, but this new technology has already been put into use in Europe. The development is comparable in importance to the invention of FM. 
 In an age of digital sound, conventional radio transmission, based on analog principles, has become a technical bottleneck. It is restricted in tonal range and dynamics and beset by obtrusive background noise and other interference. Digital broadcasting sidesteps these problems and delivers programs with a sound quality equal to that of a digitally recorded compact disk
The first regularly scheduled digital broadcasts went on the air last spring under the joint auspices of the German, Austrian, Swiss, Dutch and French broadcasting systems. Sixteen digital program channels are beamed over most of Europe from the satellite Kopernikus. Home receivers for decoding the signal use a small dish antenna, about the size of a salad bowl, and sell in Europe for about $1,000. Similar services for the United States are under discussion.
 For Americans, the year's most far-reaching audio event was the introduction of digital audio tape (DAT) after a long delay caused by by legal and political wrangling about possible copyright violations. The timing of the introduction was inopportune. In an increasingly cloudy economy, few people were eager to spend nearly $900 on a new type of tape recorder that wouldn't play their old cassettes. But from a technical point of view, the advantages of DAT are indisputable and make its eventual success seem certain. And, as more companies enter the market with DAT recorders, competition will inevitably cause prices to drop.
DAT is greatly superior to the familiar audio cassette. DAT is to conventional cassettes what CD's are to LP records -- both DAT and CD's make use of digital technology, with all its advantages: silent background, no tape hiss, frequency response to the upper and lower limits of audibility, rock-steady pitch that ends flutter and wow, and a dynamic range that extends from a whisper to a thunderclap.
DAT is also a lot easier to handle than conventional cassettes. There is no need for Dolby noise reduction because there is no noise. Gone is the need to deal with adjustments for different kinds of tape, because all digital tapes are the same. In addition, the wide dynamic range prevents overloading of the tape at peak passages. All this makes it far easier for casual and technically diffident listeners to obtain near-perfect recordings. And you never have to flip the cassette, for DAT has no "other side" -- yet it runs for two uninterrupted hours.
 As for digital disks, there were important developments in CD players, both economic and technical. Prices for CD players continued to drop, and it is possible to buy a competent, no-frills CD player for about $150. Only a few years ago the lowest price was about $1,000.
A growing number of manufacturers adopted a technique known as one-bit or "bitstream" decoding for converting the digital pulses from a CD into an audio signal. This decoding involves circuits able to process information at the startling rate of 33,868,000 bits a second. Bitstream is not inherently better than the best conventional decoding methods, but it makes it easier and cheaper to attain superior performance.
What characterizes well-designed bitstream CD players is the absence of the metallic hardness that often marred the sound of low-cost CD players. The bitstream technique allows even moderately priced players to achieve brilliance without stridency -- a tonal character that should help allay lingering objections to digital sound on the part of listeners partial to the gentler sonic aura of older recording methods.
Speaking of older methods, 1990 marks the virtual if not total demise of the LP record. Fewer and fewer stores stock grooved disks, and few companies still make them.
The passing of the LP may not be lamentable, for it has been replaced by something better. But those whose musical life began at the drop of a needle will cherish fond memories of the black, slow-turning platters.

HEADLINE: THEATER/1990;
Reality Nearly Upstaged a Paradoxical Year

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH

 BODY:
If you believe everything happens for a reason, then why was the star of the year's second-most boring Broadway musical, "Shogun," knocked cold by a piece of falling scenery during a press preview? Why was New York's angriest theatrical controversy of the year about a performance -- by Jonathan Pryce, in "Miss Saigon" -- that has only been seen in London and even there by few, if any, of those leading the debate about it? Why in an exceptional year for new American plays -- "Six Degrees of Separation," "The Piano Lesson," "Prelude to a Kiss" -- did December find three prime Shubert playhouses on West 45th Street (the Plymouth, the Royale, the Golden) sitting dark, with no bookings in sight for the New Year?
Don't look here for simple answers to any of these questions. (The answer to an easier question -- Which Broadway musical was more boring than "Shogun"? -- can be more quickly provided: "Aspects of Love.") This has been a more paradoxical year than usual in the eternally irrational world of the theater. The high quality of new productions has been undermined by the low number of new productions, particularly on Broadway, where the output has slowed to a trickle. The American musical, having revived in 1989 with "City of Angels" and "Grand Hotel," dropped dead once again on Broadway in 1990, only to have a small renaissance at one theater company Off Broadway, Playwrights Horizons (which originated "Falsettoland" and "Once on This Island" and is now previewing the new Stephen Sondheim-John Weidman "Assassins"). The gala relighting of two antique Broadway houses -- the Walter Kerr (with "The Piano Lesson") and, briefly, the Victory (with "Crowbar") -- has been overshadowed by the perpetual darkness of so many of their neighbors in the half-redeveloped, half-abandoned, increasingly surreal theater district. 
 More pressing still, this was a year in which some of the real-life dramas preoccupying the audience attending the theater -- racial conflict and recession, for starters -- permeated not only the year's outstanding plays but also racked the theater community, dividing it and depleting it. And sometimes the New York theater's own backstage story was as compelling as anything happening on stage.
Take, as one representative example, "The Grapes of Wrath," Frank Galati's epic adaptation of John Steinbeck's Depression novel, as performed by the Steppenwolf Theater Company of Chicago. Watching this work, one could not ignore the parallels between the homeless Okies of the 1930's and the homeless New Yorkers of 1990, some of whom were begging for change outside the theater at intermission. But "The Grapes of Wrath" itself soon became a sad casualty of hard times, despite winning the Tony Award for best play, traditionally a sure box-office boon to any production in any year. The audience for the show, though by no means meager, was simply not big enough to support its populous payroll, and within weeks of its Tony victory, "The Grapes of Wrath" closed.
Thus did a play about a distant depression become in death a symbolic victim of a new economic tailspin, adding its own large company at least temporarily to the ranks of the unemployed and leaving another darkened midtown marquee to serve as a makeshift shelter for New York's destitute. Where did art end and real life begin?
The "Miss Saigon" dispute was also part of a sociopolitical drama far weightier and broader than any single play. Ostensibly the battle was about Jonathan Pryce's right, as a white British actor, to repeat his London performance as a Eurasian pimp in the Broadway production of a West End hit. In reality Mr. Pryce wasn't the issue at all but a symbol, merely the wrong man in the wrong place at the wrong time, rather like Sherman McCoy in Tom Wolfe's "Bonfire of the Vanities." Mr. Pryce's case proved to be a catalyst for the release of racial tensions that had been building up for some time and were bound to explode sooner or later in the theater industry, with or without "Miss Saigon."
 As is now history, Actors' Equity shot itself in the foot by attempting to bar Mr. Pryce: The union both unjustly slurred a colleague's performance, inaccurately branding it a "minstrel" routine, and unwisely set itself up as a censor, restricting the creative freedom of theater artists who want to cast whoever they think will best serve their show. (The Equity position was a particularly embarrassing one in a year when the theatrical profession was fighting government censorship of the arts.) But while Equity's quixotic stand on Mr. Pryce was eventually reversed, the wounds opened up by the episode are not going to heal so easily. Though "Miss Saigon" was an absurdly ill-chosen battleground for a debate about racial equality in the theater -- the show is likely to employ more minority performers for more months than any Broadway production in decades -- the debate continues to be a real and challenging one, as yet attracting more demagoguery than solutions.
No Solomon, this observer included, has emerged with a plan about how to resolve the legitimate complaints of minority actors, especially Asians, who feel they have not had a fair shake in pursuing their art on the professional stage. There are more questions than answers. What does one say about a musical like "Oh, Kay!," which gives employment to dozens of black performers but often treats them in a patronizing fashion? What is one to make of the white theater establishment, which often supports nontraditional, or color-blind, casting with its big mouths but not with its bigger productions?
In a letter to the editor published in The New York Times during the Pryce controversy, and typical of the discourse the affair engendered, a half-dozen prominent white actors and playwrights argued that affirmative action is the best route for improving a New York casting system in which "actors of color are silently and automatically excluded from consideration for the majority of offered roles simply because they would be 'inappropriate.' " The three playwrights who signed this letter -- Craig Lucas, Terrence McNally and Larry Kramer -- are all the authors of recent hits ("Prelude to a Kiss," "Frankie and Johnny in the Clair de Lune," "The Normal Heart") in which the lead roles, all of them repeatedly re-cast during the plays' lengthy New York runs, have been exclusively played by white actors.
Talk is cheap, and, heaven knows, there has been plenty of it. One of the few leaders in the theater who tends to back up his pronouncements with deeds, Joseph Papp, helped improve the climate by taking actual affirmative action, both by continuing his career-long practice of nontraditional casting (Morgan Freeman in the summer's "Taming of the Shrew") and by installing George C. Wolfe, the young black author (of "The Colored Museum") and director, as an artistic leader of the New York Shakespeare Festival. (This year Mr. Papp also practiced his convictions on another front by refusing much needed National Endowment funds rather than submit to strictures on their use.) The results of Mr. Wolfe's tenure at the Public so far have included "Spunk," a selflessly imaginative theatrical homage to the fiction of Zora Neale Hurston, and "The Caucasian Chalk Circle," an adaptation of Brecht's epic in which the writer Thulani Davis reset the story in Duvalier's Haiti.
Whatever the individual achievements of the Wolfe productions, they are even more notable for their lavishly executed and uncompromising African-American esthetics, realized by an informal, floating company of young actors and adventurous designers. When one considers the successful Broadway run of "The Piano Lesson," August Wilson's latest, Depression-era installment in his cycle of plays about black America in this century, it is clear that this year, at least, black theater art had far more than a token representation in the major venues of New York theater. But color-blind casting -- whether of blacks, Asians, Hispanics or any other minority -- is another cause entirely, and one that is likely to continue to erupt not just in the theater but in television and film as well.
 As the real-life dramas of racial division and economic hardship pressed on the theater in 1990, so again did the tragedy of AIDS. Both in "Falsettoland," the musical by William Finn and James Lapine, and in Mr. Lucas's "Prelude to a Kiss," writers unearthed beauty and heroism in a story of hideous suffering.
"Falsettoland," whose principal lovers were bracingly played by Stephen Bogardus and Michael Rupert, is the more explicit of the two shows, charting the path of the disease in New York in the early 80's, when, as one lyric goes, "something very bad" was happening but the something still lacked a name and identity. By contrast, "Prelude to a Kiss," which initially featured Alec Baldwin and Mary-Louise Parker as its spellbinding couple, never mentions AIDS but is just as moving in its depiction of bottomless love as the only defense in a "precarious" world where the young join the old in being tested by death. Usually I am tempted to run the other way from plays that try to be uplifting about terminal illness -- see "Shadowlands" for a treacly case in point -- but "Falsettoland" and "Prelude" do leave one feeling, as Mr. Lucas puts it, that "the miracle of another human being" is "never to be squandered."
I would be harder pressed to argue that the two best of the most overtly polemical productions of the season left one with such a full heart. Eric Bogosian's "Sex, Drugs, Rock & Roll," a brilliant solo performance touring nearly every urban battlefront in contemporary America, and the director Michael Greif's sensational Public Theater revival of Sophie Treadwell's forgotten "Machinal," a 1928 "tragedy in 10 episodes" in which a mercantile New York grinds up its proletarian heroine, leave one high about the power of theater rather than sanguine about humanity. For sheer escapism, a 1990 theatergoer would be better off watching Maggie Smith's outrageous star turn of the old school in Peter Shaffer's well-mannered "Lettice and Lovage," or Spalding Gray's cool star turn of the narcissistic new school in his autobiographical comic monologue "Monster in a Box." The happiest fairy tale of the year is "Once on This Island," a show that announces the arrival of the young songwriting team Stephen Flaherty and Lynn Ahrens.
In a class by itself in 1990 is "Six Degrees of Separation," the John Guare play that is an alternately hilarious and moving attempt to uncover what feelings, ideas and people might still be authentic in a city where the con artist is king. In only 90 minutes, Mr. Guare surveys a New York landscape that stretches from the Upper East Side to Rikers Island and that encompasses everything from racism, recession and homelessness to the spiritual qualities of art and love to the cultural significance of Sidney Poitier, Wassily Kandinsky, J. D. Salinger and "Cats." As directed by Jerry Zaks, "Six Degrees" is also a dizzying feat of stagecraft blessed with its own triumphant star performance, by Stockard Channing. In a year that has seen classical theater descend to a still lower estate in New York -- Kevin Kline's intelligent, almost passionless "Hamlet" was by default the best of a lot that included the Mabou Mines's cross-gender "Lear" and a disappointing Philip Bosco "Miser" -- Mr. Guare and Mr. Zaks have given the theater what looks like a new classic.
About the only up-to-the-minute phenomenon missing from "Six Degrees of Separation" is the supernatural. As the country reels from its various crises, not the least of which is the threat of war, Americans seem to want to believe in any world other than this one. Not for nothing was "Ghost" the most popular movie in the country this year. Is it coincidence that ghosts, poltergeists and miracles figure in so many plays this season, often to facilitate happy endings, whether in "Prelude to a Kiss," "The Piano Lesson," "Crowbar" or "Once on This Island"?
 If things do happen for a reason, and if one wants to be superstitious -- and I'm half-kidding, but only half -- maybe a message was being sent when the screen fell and beaned Philip Casnoff during that fateful preview of "Shogun." The effect of the incident on the audience in the Marquis Theater that November night, much of it theater people, was shocking. "Shogun" itself had been numbing most everyone from its opening number, "Karma," but Mr. Casnoff's accident roused the crowd to the loudest screams I've heard in a theater in memory, followed by an equally stunned silence. It was as if an alarm of some sort had sounded.
A karma alarm, perhaps? Mr. Casnoff got a happy ending, recovering from his injury within days. But the prognosis for the theater, like that for the world the stage inevitably reflects, is less clear. The deep divisions and economic darkness spreading throughout the New York theater were hardly abating as 1990 reached its final curtain. If the bizarre calamity at "Shogun" somehow seemed the perfect denouement for this whole disquieting theatrical year, so one cannot rule out the possibility that more than scenery might come crashing down in the months to come.

HEADLINE: ANTIQUES/1990;
The Bids Were for Baseball And Florentine Cabinetry

BYLINE: By RITA REIF

 BODY:
As with other global markets, the weakening economy and events in the the Middle East took their toll on antiques buying in 1990. Through July, the 1980's boom continued, with certain rarities selling at auction for the highest prices ever paid for decorative arts. The most spectacular event of the year was the sale of an 18th-century Italian cabinet, which caused a sensation in London when it became the most expensive object other than a painting ever auctioned. The lavishly gilded architectural masterpiece inlaid with semiprecious stones, was bought at Christie's in London for $15.2 million by Barbara Piasecka Johnson of Princeton, N.J., the widow of J. Seward Johnson of the pharmaceuticals family.
Commissioned in 1726 by Henry Somerset, the third Duke of Beaufort, and made in 1730 at the grand ducal workshops in Florence, the cabinet reigned for 260 years at Badminton House (where the game was born), a Palladian-style mansion in Gloucestershire, England. 
 Commenting on that sale and other million-dollar purchases of antiques, Harold Sack of Israel Sack, a New York dealer, said such extraordinary prices are bound to be far fewer during a recession. "I expect a drought in masterpieces on the market," Mr. Sack said. "In my memory it has always been so. In the 1930's when I began in this business, there was a lot of material of quality for sale but very few masterpieces. The equation still holds."
 Over the last decade Mr. Sack paid many of the highest prices at auction for American furniture. In June 1989 at Christie's in New York, he paid $12.1 million for an 18th-century American desk-bookcase -- a triumph of Newport cabinetry that was the costliest piece of furniture ever auctioned until the Badminton cabinet.
Mr. Sack was back at Christie's in New York on Jan. 20 to bid and buy for $4.6 million an 18th-century Philadelphia console table by Thomas Tuffts. The table, with an apron of Chinese-style fretwork, tall legs and slender ankles, is the world's most expensive table sold at auction.
Collectibles commanded lower prices during the year but were ruled by the same psychology of buying. One of the hottest categories was baseball memorabilia. Shirts worn by celebrity players brought some of the highest prices ever paid for sports-related items. In June a 1950 shirt worn by Jackie Robinson was sold for $16,093 by Lelands, a Manhattan auction house. A pin owned by Lou Gehrig was sold for $41,300 by Guernsey's of New York.
Antiquities were in the spotlight throughout the year -- in museum exhibitions at gallery shows, at auction and in a court dispute that spurred an investigation by Scotland Yard. One of the most publicized objects was a restored Greek vessel -- a vase incorporating a fragment of a two-handled krater signed with the name Euphronios -- which was sold for $1.76 million in June at Sotheby's -- a record for a Greek vase. The piece was bought for the New York collectors Shelby White and Leon Levy, who sent it to the Metropolitan Museum of Art. There it was included in the current exhibition of the couple's antiquities, on view through Jan. 27.
 The year began and ended with controversy over 14 pieces of ancient Roman silver -- lavishly decorated vessels that Sotheby's is trying to sell for the Marquess of Northampton of Britain. The origins and ownership of these silver objects, said by Sotheby's to date from the fourth or fifth centuries and known as the Sevso treasure after a name inscribed on one piece, have been in dispute since Sotheby's announced their existence in February.
The Governments of Lebanon and Yugoslavia each claimed to own the silver and obtained a preliminary injunction in Manhattan barring its removal from the United States. In March, at the request of the Yugoslavian Embassy in London, Scotland Yard began an investigation of the silver and reported in June that there may have been "conspiracy to defraud."
Legal skirmishes continued into November, when it was decided to try the case in this country at a date to be decided by the State Supreme Court. At issue are the two conflicting claims of ownership by Yugoslavia and Lebanon.
This past year antiques shows were dramatically affected by the downturn in the economy. Attendance and sales were down at the major fairs, including one of the nation's oldest and most prestigious events, the Winter Antiques Show, held in January at the Seventh Regiment Armory in Manhattan. Fewer socialites appeared at the preview parties, and overall attendance was off by about 2,000, down to about 29,600. And according to Mario Buatta, the show's chairman, the take was down, too -- about $240,000 below the $880,000 total of the year before.
 The International Antique Show attracted some 14,000 people -- down from just under 18,000 in 1989. Sanford Smith, who manages six art and antiques shows each year, said the gate was lower at all of them. The most popular of these bazaars, the Fall Antiques Show at a pier on the Hudson River, was attended by about 14,000 -- off from 17,000 in 1989 after eight years of escalating attendance.
"The dealers don't have any money," Mr. Smith said. "They don't buy enough new material. The need to buy something before someone else gets it isn't there anymore. The excitement that characterized shows in the 1980's is gone from the whole market."

HEADLINE: HOME ENTERTAINMENT/1990;
Video Settles Down To Its Second Decade

BYLINE: By PETER M. NICHOLS

 BODY:
Video in 1990 was a story of moratoriums and "Rocky Horror," of rising sales and so-so rentals, of "Red October," "Lethal Weapon 2," "Pretty Woman" and "Total Recall," of best sellers that were also best renters. It was a year of dropping prices, used cassettes and "Dick Tracy," of the emerging laser disk, of consolidation and Blockbuster and Warner and MGM/UA, of "The Cook, the Thief" and NC-17.
In 1990 a score of acclaimed films went on tape; others, some not so acclaimed, were put to good commercial use by a maturing industry which was finding life in its second decade to be more problematic than it had been in the early 80's when the VCR was new and the viewers enthralled. 
 Judging the year with a critical eye, the best releases included Spike Lee's "Do the Right Thing," Paul Mazursky's "Enemies: A Love Story," Steven Soderbergh's "Sex, Lies and Videotape," Michael Moore's "Roger and Me," Nancy Savoca's "True Love," Jim Sheridan's "My Left Foot" (an Academy Award for Daniel Day-Lewis) and Oliver Stone's "Born on the Fourth of July."
There were Woody Allen's "Crimes and Misdemeanors," Bruce Beresford's "Driving Miss Daisy" (Oscars as best picture and for Jessica Tandy and Morgan Freeman), Jim Jarmusch's "Mystery Train," Edward Zwick's "Glory," Kenneth Branagh's "Henry V," and Disney's "Little Mermaid" ("the best animated Disney film in at least 30 years," wrote Janet Maslin).
This fall brought Ken Burns's "Civil War," the highly praised series broadcast on PBS. Then came Peter Greenaway's "Cook, the Thief, His Wife and Her Lover," video's first title to carry the Motion Picture Association of America's new NC-17 designation.
 Ratings were controversial in 1990, but in video the real ruckus was over Paramount's "Hunt for Red October," released Oct. 25, starring Sean Connery and Alec Baldwin. Here was a rental title arriving in stores at close to $100 retail, meaning that retailers were paying about $70 a copy wholesale (as opposed to the usual $65 or so). Would stores rebel and not stock large supplies of the title? Would other studios raise prices of their own hits? And if they did, would the stores raise their rents?
The answers were no (stores stocked the film in good numbers); yes (studio prices rose a bit, if not to the $100 level), and, as for rental increases, maybe. Rents rose here and there, but generally they seemed to be holding at their accustomed levels. "The consumer wants that price," said Brad Burnside, owner of Video Adventure in Evanston, Ill., last July. "Price is one of the great things about video."
Rentals still ruled the video business in 1990, but there was softness. The total number of cassettes rented rose somewhat; the number of tapes rented per household fell somewhat. At midyear, industry leaders were expressing hope that video would be fairly recession-proof. By year's end, citing such factors as the slowing economy, weaker-renting films and wandering consumer attention, many observers had tempered their optimism.
 In 1990, the good news was not rentals but sales. This year for the first time the studios took in more revenue from low-priced cassettes for sale than from tapes for rent. Among those sales titles were a string of hit films in the $20-to-$30 price range: "Indiana Jones and the Last Crusade," "Lethal Weapon 2," Disney's "Peter Pan" and "Little Mermaid," "Pretty Woman" and "Total Recall."
Two factors distinguished some of the year's best sellers. First, R-rated films, with sex and violence thought inappropriate for the family-oriented sales market, sold fairly well to very well indeed. Second, some of these titles also rented about as well as they sold. On Billboard's Dec. 15 best-seller list, for example, "Pretty Woman" ($19.99) was No. 1, and "Total Recall" ($24.99) was No. 3; on the best-renter list they were Nos. 1 and 2, respectively.
In 1990, though, it wasn't only the hits that sold well. Sensing a growing public propensity to own and collect, the studios -- MGM/UA, CBS-Fox, Paramount, Turner, Republic and others -- issued scores of older titles from their libraries, most often in series featuring stars and genres from westerns to comedies to crime at prices that seemed to go lower as the year progressed: $19.95, $14.95, $12.95.
Prices of used rental cassettes went lower still (a used "Indiana Jones," for instance, could be had for $7.95). This year even a studio encouraged dipping into the used-cassette bin. When the initial rental rush for "Dick Tracy," released Dec. 19, has slowed, Disney's Touchstone will offer a $3 rebate on used copies.
Along with higher sales and lower prices came various marketing schemes. This was a vintage year for the moratorium, that commercial arm-twister long used in video whereby studios offer titles for limited periods and then withdraw them from the market. The idea, of course, is to increase demand during the window of availability.
On Nov. 8, CBS-Fox, a practiced hand at the moratorium, presented the year's textbook case: "The Rocky Horror Picture Show," released as a rental title at $89.98. Dealers were invited to order the cult classic until Oct. 25, whereupon the supply line was cut. Many retailers found that they hadn't ordered enough, but, as analysts noted, moratoriums work only if you make them stick. This one stuck and it worked. An instant best renter, "Rocky Horror" is also one of only two titles most video dealers can remember that has sold in such numbers at that price ("Gone With the Wind" being the other).
Whatever measures video used in 1990, major operatives tended to get bigger and fewer. In November, Blockbuster Video, the country's largest chain of video stores, announced plans to buy Erol's, the second largest, and numbered close to 1,500 stores nationwide. And on the studio end, MGM/UA Home Video and its long list of titles passed into the hands of Time Warner, giving Warner what some analysts estimated as 22 percent of the video market.

HEADLINE: ARCHITECTURE/1990;
The Important Things Were What Didn't Happen

BYLINE: By PAUL GOLDBERGER

 BODY:
The worst thing that happened to architecture in 1990 was that all the money went away. Then again, maybe the best thing that happened to architecture in 1990 was that all the money went away. There are going to be a lot fewer buildings getting started in the coming decade -- which means less of the glitz, less of the overbuilding, and less of the pretentious, overbearing excess that characterized the 1980's. Expect not a lot of huge skyscrapers being shoehorned into already crowded downtowns, as they were in the last decade. And there won't be a lot of zillion-dollar post-modern beach houses for investment bankers, either.
This will mean that there are also going to be a lot of unemployed architects running around. Indeed, there already are. Few and far between are the architectural firms that are expanding these days; the word is retrenchment, even in some of the largest and mightiest presences in the world of commercial architecture, like Skidmore, Owings & Merrill, where a slew of layoffs inaugurated the holiday season. 
 It seems in the spirit of a year like this that a lot of the most important events were things that did not happen. The cancellation of the ill-advised plan by the architect Romaldo Giurgola to expand the Kimbell Art Museum in Fort Worth -- a masterpiece by Louis Kahn that is one of the great museum buildings of the century -- ranks as one of the most positive architectural developments of the year. The plan would have stretched out the museum by replicating Kahn's design, an idea that seems on the surface to respect the masterpiece but in fact would have been a kind of vulgar parody, the architectural equivalent of a stretch limousine.
Another piece of good news in the did-not-happen category was the defeat of a proposal to erect a large jail in front of the Marin County Civic Center in San Rafael, Calif., one of the last buildings by Frank Lloyd Wright and certainly the greatest monumental civic structure of Wright's late career. The jail as originally conceived would have blocked the view of the Wright building, which is set along a freeway and is one of the first buildings ever designed specifically to be viewed from a moving automobile. As with the Kimbell, a coalition of architects and historic preservationists (who are increasingly accustomed to discovering that "modern" buildings can be in as much danger as old ones) came together to save the day.
Some of the did-not-happens were not so happy. The past year also brought the demise of a remarkable plan to redesign John F. Kennedy Airport in New York City by Henry Cobb of Pei Cobb Freed. The airport is the primary gateway to the United States -- but as it now stands it is a chaotic mess that probably gives the worst first impression of this country since the quarantine rooms on Ellis Island. Mr. Cobb's plan would have reduced the architectural cacophony of the airport's sprawling array of separate terminals by creating a vast central building, a modernist monument that would quite literally have brought order out of chaos. It was killed by a combination of economics and politics: the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, the airport's operator, lacked both the money and the political muscle to put the plan into effect over the objections of the airlines. Their competing economic interests were better served by the balkanization of the airport's present design.
Also a reminder of the extent to which the economy affects architecture was the near-demise of Donald Trump, who gave us in 1990 one of the world's most spectacular pieces of para-architecture, the Taj Mahal hotel and casino in Atlantic City, an ordinary building dressed up in so much glitz one was forced to pause before it in astonishment, if not in admiration. (Any place that has 3,000 slot machines in a single room, not to mention a 4,200-square-foot guest suite named after Alexander the Great, has to signify something.)
That not enough people flocked to the place Mr. Trump proclaimed the Eighth Wonder of the World to allow him to pay its mortgage contributed, at least indirectly, to the problems facing another, and ultimately much more important, Trump project: the vast "city" he planned to build on the site of the former Penn rail yards on the Upper West Side of Manhattan. Designed by Cooper, Robertson & Partners, Trump City is an awful scheme, not only because of the 150-story tower planned as its centerpiece, but in its underlying conception: huge buildings around a mega-shopping mall. It's a grotesque parody of urban values, more a suburban vision blown up to immense urban scale.
While the project remains officially under consideration, the combination of Mr. Trump's own money problems, the decline in the New York economy, and the forthright determination of several civic groups to fight it makes the latest chapters of the still-unfinished saga of Trump City one of the more positive developments of 1990.
High up in the sad news category was the demise of Comiskey Park in Chicago, the magnificent, rough, messy old stadium that was the oldest baseball park in the country and as genuine a ball field as ever existed -- more truly the essence of Chicago than the city's more famous old ball park, Wrigley Field. (Comiskey's replacement, not quite complete, is not as endearing.)
If not a lot is getting built in New York, building still continues apace in Europe -- though by the end of the year, things seemed to be slowing down there, too. Among the other good events of 1990 was Frank Gehry's design for the American Center in Paris, announced in midyear; it will be a powerful and intense building by one of America's greatest architects, and a testament in the midst of Europe to the continued creative strength of American architecture. Also announced was an important European architect's first major building in America: Mario Botta's design for the San Francisco Museum of Modern Art.
This was also the year that Ellis Island in New York harbor, superbly restored by the architectural firms of Beyer Blinder Belle and Notter, Finegold & Alexander, reopened to the public as a museum. It was the most elaborate and expensive restoration of a public building in the United States, and it was done with dignity and imagination.
The Pritzker Prize went to Aldo Rossi of Milan, an honoree who ranks with the best, confirming this international prize's reputation as the accolade to architects who have advanced the art of their profession. And just as the year drew to a close, the American Institute of Architects announced that its highest award, the Gold Medal, will go to the architect, educator and writer Charles Moore, an equally admirable choice.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Ellis Island, superbly renewed, reopened as a museum--the most elaborate and expensive restoration of a public building in the United States (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times); The Town Hall in Borgoricco, Italy, designed by Aldo Rossi, winner of this year's Pritzker Prize--a choice that confirms the reputations of both prize and man (Paul Warchol); Donald Trump's Taj Mahal in Atlantic City--an ordinary building dressed up in so much glitz that it gives pause (John Sotomayor/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Travel Advisory

 BODY:

La Guardia Projects Slow Airport Access
La Guardia Airport is one-quarter of the way through a four-year, $340 million redevelopment program to expand and improve its facilities. Because of construction and delays, passengers heading for the airport should set out earlier than they normally would because of detours and construction delays, suggests William Fife, manager of the redevelopment program.
The major goals of the project, scheduled to be completed by mid-1992, are expansion and modernization of the central terminal building, reconfiguration of the airport's roadway system and construction of a new passenger terminal at the east end of the airport.
"Conditions will get a little worse before they get better," says Mr. Fife. "We encourage the use of public transportation to get out to the airport during the renovation," he says, "but if you do take a car spend more time looking at the signs -- because the shapes of the roadways are different than before."
The central terminal building is being made 55 feet longer for expanded baggage claim areas, extra circulation space for passengers and new restaurant facilities and amenities. On the upper level, the number of lanes for dropoffs are being doubled to help alleviate some of the congestion on the roadways leading into La Guardia.
The new terminal will be for passengers of Continental and Eastern airlines.
This new $162 million facility is part of the plan by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, which runs the city's airports, to decentralize La Guardia's terminal facilities, redistribute passengers and relieve congestion in the central terminal area.
Information on public transportation to and from La Guardia, Kennedy or Newark is available by calling 800-247-7433.
 
London Begins Chunnel Station
 Work has started on Britain's first international railroad terminal, Waterloo International, in London, where completion is scheduled to coincide with the opening of the Channel Tunnel in 1993.
According to British Rail plans, trains will depart every hour for both Paris (a three-hour journey) and Brussels (11 minutes more).
Waterloo International is being built as a separate terminal within the site of the existing Waterloo Station.
High-speed trains, with one power car at each end, plus 18 intermediate passenger coaches, will transport passengers at speeds up to 185 miles per hour, making it possible, a British Rail official said, to leave your office in the heart of London and arrive in Paris or Brussels in time for late dinner.
 
U.S. Warning On Fez Violence
The State Department has advised Americans contemplating traveling to Morocco, particularly Fez, to check with the Department or the American Embassy in Rabat for an update on the situation there after violent demonstrations the weekend of Dec. 14-15.
Tourists fled when the Des Merinides Hotel in Fez was set afire during riots following the start of a nationwide general strike. Violence erupted in other cities, including Tangier and Rabat, but it was most widespread in Fez, the country's religious and intellectual center. The city suffered extensive damage as thousands of people rampaged through the streets, looters ransacked jewelry stores, banks and public buildings and rioters set buses and cars on fire and burned the 90-room hotel. The violence, which was prompted, according to diplomats, by hunger, poverty and unemployment, was followed by a pledge by the Moroccan Government to raise salaries, improve social benefits and resume talks with unions and employers to resolve labor disputes. Estimates of the number of people killed in Fez varied; union officials put the number at about 35, including some soldiers.
 
AIR-TRAIN PLAN FROM AMTRAK
 Amtrak and United Airlines are offering packages that permit travel one way by rail and return by air. Travelers will save about 30 percent off the regular one-way fares for each leg. Reservations for the new program begin on Wednesday.
Christopher D. Bowers, a United vice president, said the cost of the plan's packages will help overcome resistance by travelers -- because of high one-way fares -- to taking trips by air one way and by rail the other. One-way fares are the highest in the transportation business, he said.
For example, a round trip (train and plane or plane and train) under the plan between Los Angeles and New York will cost $419 a person in off-peak months and $499 during peak, which is May 24 to Sept. 16 and the Christmas-New Year's period. The regular train fare between the two cities is $307 one way. The package, called the Amtrak Air-Rail Travel Plan, will also allow travelers to fly to a city served by United, journey around the region by rail and fly home from the same United city.
The plan may be used for trans-Continental trips or regional travel between Amtrak and United destinations in the Continental United States, except Alaska. Generally, the plan allows one-way air and one-way rail between two of four zones -- the West, Mountain States, Midwest and East. It also permits two "regional tours." One combines the East Zone with one of five Midwestern and Western cities: Minneapolis-St. Paul, Omaha, Kansas City, Dallas/Fort Worth and Houston; the other links the West Zone with Chicago and New Orleans. Other price examples: for travel between Midwest and West, $369 and $399 in peak; Midwest-Florida, $299 and $349, and East-Mountain States, $399 and $439. The regional tour combining the East and five cities costs $419 and $489. The regional tour linking the West with Chicago or New Orleans is $359 and $389.
Reservations from Jan. 2: (800) 872-7245.
 
ART BRIGHTENS WALLS OF PARIS
PARIS
 Paris has blossomed with a new art form. Vivid, eye-catching paintings and metal sculptures brighten dead, windowless walls in a program organized by the city urban mangement office. The city encourages artists to submit ideas, and a wall commmitee that includes neighborhood property owners, votes on the works. The project was started by the Mayor, Jacques Chirac, in 1981 to brighten the many walls left bare when neighboring buildings disappeared during the creation of boulevards and squares beginning in the 19th century.
On a towering blank wall next to a fire station at 20 Rue St.-Fargeau (20th arrondissement), a mosaic depicts a fireman climbing a ladder to rescue a small black plaster cat marooned atop the real wall. Across from the Pompidou Museum at Rue Quincampoix and Rue Aubry-le-Boucher (4th arrondissement), strollers smile at four trompe l'oeil windows giving them a glimpse of a bird, romping children and a man playing a violin. At 58 Rue du Louvre (2d arrondissement) a giant Mona Lisa crafted from metal shimmers far above pedestrians hurrying past.
One of the largest paintings is at 103 Quai Branly (15th arrondissement), blanketing a wall bared when bulldozers leveled old buildings to construct the Eiffel Tower. Labeled "They Made the 20th Century," the wall is packed with the faces of 477 newsmakers from Albert Einstein to Judy Garland. A 14-story windowless wall at 182 Rue Nationale (13th arrondissement) glows with a cinnamon-colored door opening seductively onto a blue sea and crimson sky.
The city pays an artist $2,000 for the model of an approved design and in some cases another $8,000 in prize money on condition that he or she supervise the transfer of the work to the wall by craftsmen.
The Direction de l'Amenagement Urbain, 17 Boulevard Morland, 75004 Paris -- near the Sully-Morland subway stop -- can provide the addresses of the 73 rejuvenated walls. -- ALINE MOSBY
