HEADLINE: WHAT'S DOING IN;
Fort Worth

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO; ROBERTO SURO is chief of The Times's Houston bureau.

 BODY:
From honky-tonk dance floors to museum galleries, a cowboy spirit survives in Fort Worth. Here glass- sheathed office buildings tower over streets of antique red brick in a setting that offers the visitor gateways to the frontier past. Although its people are too numerous and too diverse to fit a generalization, they too convey the kindly flavors of an earlier time through their tastes and manners. Altogether this small prairie city has become an unusual repository of Western myths and icons.
On display are collections of the works of the American artists Frederic Remington and Charles M. Russell, and there are fine examples of Victorian architecture as interpreted by rich cattlemen. But there are more than mementos here. At rodeo time in late winter, the West of ranchers, riders and cattle comes to town. The 1991 Southwestern Exposition and Livestock Show runs from Jan. 18 to Feb. 3 and offers 28 rodeo performances, 14 auctions and almost continuous showings of cutting horses, bulls, sheep and other animals. 
 Never really a fort, Fort Worth took on its first role in Western history as a way station for cattlemen driving their herds north to Abilene on the Chisholm Trail. After the railroad arrived in 1876 Fort Worth became the end of the trail for cowboys and a major shipping point for cattle. The city then acquired its moniker, Cowtown. Aside from picking up supplies and doing business, cowboys got drunk, gambled and visited prostitutes in a neighborhood known as Hell's Half Acre. Bulldozed over, the area is now the site of the Convention Center.
Fort Worth is one of the few cities beyond the East Coast that can be visited without a car because the major attractions are close to downtown.
The most economical trip from Dallas-Fort Worth International Airport is offered by the Airporter Bus Service. The trip takes 35 minutes to an hour and costs $7 one way.
The No. 57 bus traverses downtown on Throckmorton Street and takes 10 to 15 minutes to get to the museum district. The No. 73 bus takes about the same time to get to the Stockyards. The fare is 75 cents.
 
Coming Events
 Fort Worth's Western spirit is never more evident that during the rodeo. From Jan. 18 to Feb. 3 the Will Rogers Memorial Center comes to life from 8 A.M. to midnight. More than 18,000 head of livestock from llamas (raised for wool) to draft horses are entered in competitions and auctions on 42 acres of show space. Looking at the animals close up can be a thrill. In addition there is a Children's Barnyard and Milking Parlor, a carnival and midway and a huge commercial exhibition space with Western wear, arts and crafts and ranch equipment. The entry fee is $4, $1 for children.
Rodeo tickets are $12 for weekend performances (Friday night to Sunday night) and $10 for weekday performances. There will be evening performances every night from Jan. 18 to 24 and matinees on Jan. 18, 19 and 20. Then from Jan. 25 to Feb. 3 there will be matinee and evening performances every day. Call the rodeo ticket office at (817) 877-2420 or Rainbow-Ticketmaster at (214) 787-1500 or (800) 725-7200 outside Texas.
Some rodeo events like bronco busting, steer wrestling, bull riding and barrel racing -- though not always of the same quality -- can be seen at the Cowtown Coliseum, 121 East Exchange Avenue in the heart of the Stockyards National Historic District. It schedules single rodeo events, and admission is often free. For example, there is team roping at 2 P.M. on Feb. 10, 17 and 24 and at 8 P.M. on Feb. 12, 15 and 19. For a schedule, call the coliseum at (817) 625-1025.
A variety of buildings, many of them historic, comprise the stockyards district, offering shopping, dining and lodging along with sightseeing. Billy Bob's Texas, relatively new, claims to be the world's biggest honky-tonk. It offers dancing on 13,000 square feet of dance floors to bands that appear on week nights. There are big-name acts and professional bull riding in a miniature rodeo arena on Fridays and Saturdays. For neophytes who stumble making the turns in the Texas two-step there are free lessons on Thursdays at 7 P.M. Billy Bob's is at the corner of North Main Street and Stockyards Boulevard. Cover charges vary. The phone number is (817) 624-7117.
Another stockyards establishment, the White Elephant Saloon, plays host to an annual re-enactment of an 1887 gunfight in which one of the saloon's previous owners, Luke Short, outdrew a popular marshal, Jim (Longhair) Courtright. It was Courtright's last shootout and is said to be the last one on Exchange Avenue. The re-enactment is on Feb. 8 in front of the White Elephant at 106 East Exchange. The phone number is (817) 624-1887.
 
Frontier Art
 The exhibition space for the permanent collection at the Amon Carter Museum of Western Art comprises five small rooms and a long entrance gallery in an airy building designed by Philip Johnson. Taking an hour or two to walk through these compact quarters, however, involves a trip through the history of the American wilderness. Arranged chronologically, the collection offers an array of styles ranging from the landscapes of the Hudson River School to the stark earth tones of Georgia O'Keeffe.
Big Remington canvases like "A Dash for the Timber" show the West of action and drama while "The Fall of the Cowboy" is a somber coda. Russell's paintings often provide a comic counterpoint.
The juxtaposition of Remington's grand romance and Russell's more rustic cowboy art is even more evident at the Sid Richardson Collection of Western Art. About 50 works by the two artists are displayed in one big room on the ground floor of a restored turn-of-the-century building.
Through April 14 the Amon Carter is showing 27 Remington bronzes arranged by types of castings. From May 4 to Aug. 4 the museum will offer the first major exhibit in more than 40 years of works by Carl Wimar (1828-1862). Although this artist, who chronicled Indian and frontier life, may not be a familiar name, many of his images, such as "Floatboatmen on the Mississippi," which is on permanent display here, are familiar images.
The Amon Carter Museum is at 3501 Camp Bowie Boulevard. The phone number is (817) 738-1933. It is open Tuesday to Saturday from 10 A.M. to 5 P.M., Sunday from 1 to 5:30 P.M. Tours are conducted at 2 P.M. Tuesday to Sunday. Free admission.
The Sid Richardson Collection is at 309 Main Street on Sundance Square. The phone number is (817) 332-6554. Open Tuesday to Friday from 10 A.M. to 5 P.M., Saturday from 11 A.M. to 6 P.M. and Sunday from 1 to 5 P.M.; free. Also at the museum is one of three visitor information centers. The other two are at the Fort Worth Museum of Science and History, 1501 Montgomery Street, and at the Cowtown Coliseum.
While Carter and Richardson restricted their purchases to Western art, the fellow philanthropists Kay and Velma Kimbell produced an eclectic collection from many periods. It is housed in an award-winning building by Louis Kahn at 3333 Camp Bowie Boulevard and is open Tuesday to Friday from 10 A.M. to 5 P.M., Saturday from noon to 8 P.M. and Sunday from noon to 5 P.M. Tours of the exhibitions are conducted at 2 P.M. Tuesday to Saturday. Tours of the collection and the building are offered at 3 P.M. on Sundays. The phone number is (817) 332-8451. Free general admission.
 
The Outdoors
 Besides the Water Gardens, a series of concrete cascades spread out over four blocks between Houston and Commerce Streets, there is the Japanese Garden at 3220 Botanic Garden Drive two miles from downtown. Until March 31 it is open Tuesday to Sunday from 10 A.M. to 5 P.M. Admission is $1.50 on weekends, $1 on weekdays. For joggers and bikers there is the Trinity Trail along the north bank of the Trinity River.
 
Where to Stay
 The Stockyards Hotel, dating from 1906, offers rooms in four basic decors: Indian, Cowboy, Mountain Man and Victorian. If none of them satisfy, there are a few accommodations like the Bonnie and Clyde Room, where the bank robbers supposedly stayed in the summer of 1933. If all else fails, the bar stools at the hotel's Booger Red Saloon are topped with big Western saddles. The hotel is at 109 East Exchange at the entrance to the stockyards. The phone numbers are (817) 625-6427 and (800) 423-8471 outside Texas. Rates range from $95 to $120 a night for two.
Downtown first-class accommodations are available at the 16-story Hyatt Regency Fort Worth with 516 rooms at 815 Main Street; phone: (817) 870-1234 and (800) 233-1234. In the same class is the 12-story Worthington with 508 rooms at 200 Main Street; phone: (817) 870-1000 and (800) 433-5677. Rates at both range from $79 to $124.
The Residence Inn by Marriott at 1701 South University Drive is convenient to the river, the rodeo and the museum district. Rates range from $87 to $110. Phone: (817) 870-1011 or (800) 331-3131.
Budget choice: Days Inn Downtown with 300 rooms at 600 Commerce Street, three blocks from the convention center. Rates for two range from $35 to $55 a night. There is a restaurant and a garage, but a $5-a-day parking fee is not included in the room rate. Phone: (817) 332-6900.
 
Where to Eat
 Beef is naturally a favorite in Cowtown, and the Cattlemen's Steak House, 2458 North Main, is the place for it. In dining rooms decorated with pictures of prize steers, diners feast on selections ranging from an 18-ounce Texas strip sirloin, at $21.50, to more modest cuts, available at half the price. Lunch and dinner are served Monday to Friday, dinner only on weekends. The phone number is (817) 624-3945. Reservations accepted every night but Saturday.
Every Texas city and many towns have barbecue places that are part of local lore. In Fort Worth it is Angelo's at 2533 White Settlement Road. Brisket, pork ribs, ham and links are served cafeteria style in sandwiches and on plate dinners. Prices are about $5 to $10 a person, depending on the number of side orders. Phone: (817) 332-0357. Closed Sundays.
Texan and Tex-Mex cuisine act as the great social equalizers, with customers in blue jeans and business suits sitting side by side. At Joe T. Garcia's, for example, a fixed-price meal ($7.75 at lunch or dinner) served family style includes nachos, tacos, enchiladas, rice, beans and guacamole. There is no menu, but other dishes are usually served, including fajitas. The restaurant, at 2201 North Commerce, has a delightful patio and is open for lunch and dinner seven days a week. There can be long lines, so arrive early for meals like Sunday brunch. The phone number is (817) 626-4356. No credit cards accepted.

HEADLINE: DOOMSTER VS. BOOMSTER

 BODY:
What strikes me about Julian Simon is not so much his blind optimism, but rather his laissez-faire attitude toward change ("Betting the Planet" by John Tierney, Dec. 2). Simon acknowledges that population growth will cause "short-term problems." For countries in the developing world, such "short-term problems" include lack of adequate food or clean water, lack of needed medical facilities, etc. What Simon argues is a complacent acceptance of the status quo until the status quo become untenable. What seems more logical to me would be to avoid the problems as much as possible before they arise.
 
DAVID NARUM
Madison, Wis.

HEADLINE: DOOMSTER VS. BOOMSTER

 BODY:
Tierney notes that doomsayers like Ehrlich have proved to be far more popular than Cornucopians like Simon. There is a simple explanation for this. By forecasting doom and gloom, Ehrlich energizes his audience by allowing them to assume the noble role of saving our planet.
It is much more satisfying to imagine that you are helping to shape humanity's destiny by using cloth diapers instead of disposable ones than it is to sit back and believe that somebody else will solve these problems for you.
 
DAVID HASS

HEADLINE: Fillmore Center

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I appreciated the coverage of Fillmore Center in the Western Addition section of San Francisco ("Tenanting a Big, Controversial Project," Dec. 9). Despite the problems reported, the project is now 95 percent complete. It is providing homes for an economically and racially integrated 600 families, many of whom are from the Western Addition.
[Clarifying the leasing picture as reported in the article, the Filmmore Center management said that 23 of 103 new units that came on the market Dec. 1 had been preleased, and 523 of 686 units that were previously available had been leased. Ultimately Fillmore Center is to have 1,113 apartments. In the retail component, 15,000 square feet is leased, the management said.]
The current development team has successfully moved toward completion of its several goals -- affordable housing, increased neighborhood employment, assistance to minorityes and women contractors, and the provision of neighborhood amenities.
A total of 223 of the units, or 20 percent, are affordable to low-income households, while 335, or 30 percent, are affordable to middle-income households. The development has pumped more than $13 million in wages directly to the neighborhood workers. Special use and contracts for minority and women contractors generated contracts in excess of $21 million. Fillmore Center has provided a 16,000-square-foot community center, and careful design has made it possible to preserve nearly half the site as open space.
As a 25-year resident of the Western Addition, I can attest to both the historical problems and the current successes of the Fillmore Center. Creating housing and new retail uses of this magnitude, which are good neighbors to our surrounding subsidized housing, has been a daunting task and one that is not yet finished.
The agency remains committed to achieving its goals with this development in a way that embodies the best of urban design and city living.
 
LEROY KING
 
The writer is president of the San Francisco Redevelopment Commission.

HEADLINE: Essex Incinerator: New Look at Old Debate

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO

 BODY:
AN overestimation of the amount of trash that would be delivered to Essex County's solid-waste incinerator in Newark has intensified the debate between advocates of incineration and supporters of increased recycling. The discrepancy has also focused new attention upon Gov. Jim Florio's plans for regionalized incineration.
The waste-to-energy incinerator, designed to handle up to 2,250 tons of trash a day, was guaranteed by Essex County officials to receive a delivery of 1,800 tons a day. The county, however, has been able to deliver only half that amount. 
 As a result, officials are trying to negotiate a long-term agreement with another county to make up for the shortfall. But such an agreement could require expanding the plant and the burning of even more trash than anticipated in Essex County.
Environmentalists say the situation is proof that the plant was deliberately oversized and that regionalization of incineration is no guarantee that less trash will be burned. Regionalization, they say, only concentrates pollution in the state's poorer, densely populated areas.
But state and county officials say Essex County's problem will have positive results. They say it will make cooperation between counties and the regionalization of incineration inevitable. And that, they say, will result in more efficient processing of solid waste and in lower disposal costs for taxpayers.
The debate comes at a time when the cost of solid-waste disposal continues to rise sharply and when counties are re-examining their waste-disposal plans in an effort to meet the Governor's goal of 60 percent recycling statewide.
"We are going to turn a problem into an opportunity," said the Essex County Executive, Nicholas Amato, referring to the county's effort to remedy its solid-waste shortfall.
"If we can put together a regional situation with Bergen County or with both Hudson County and Bergen County, then we will be the county to copy," Mr. Amato said.
An ideal agreement, Mr. Amato said, would require Essex County to burn another county's trash, while the other county would provide landfill space for the resulting ash. With such an agreement, Mr. Amato said, Essex County would have "the best technology, the best price, and we would have solved the solid-waste crisis in this area."
 Officials say Essex County has little choice but to enter into an agreement with another county to burn imported trash. That wasn't necessarily the plan from the beginning.
Initially, the Newark incinerator was designed to burn only Essex County's trash. But critics and environmentalists have asserted from the beginning that it was built larger than necessary to handle the tonnage the county could provide.
County officials, however, signed a contract with American Ref-Fuel of Essex County and the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, the operator and the owner, respectively, of the incinerator plant, that requires Essex County to pay for the processing of 1,800 tons of trash a day whether that amount is delivered or not. Since the plant opened in November, Essex County has consistently fallen short of the daily tonnage requirement.
 
Population Has Declined
 "In essence, we're nowhere near the tonnage that's required," said Joseph Parlavecchio, president of the Essex County Board of Freeholders. "We are providing half of what we're supposed to."
When the incinerator was designed, Mr. Parlavecchio said, engineers calculated the required capacity from estimates supplied by the Hackensack Meadowlands Development Commission. Those estimates, however, were based upon the number of truckloads of trash being processed at the transfer station operated by the commission rather than its weight, the currency in which the incinerator deals. In addition, he said, the population of the county has declined over the last several years and recycling efforts have proved more successful than had been anticipated.
And finally, Mr. Parlavecchio said, officials are suspicious that as the cost of solid-waste disposal rose steadily, hundreds of thousands of tons of Essex County trash "leaked" out of the county illegally to out-of-state landfills.
As a result of Essex County's inability to deliver the required amount of trash, the Department of Environmental Protection and the State Board of Public Utilities recently ordered Union County to divert some of its solid waste to Essex County. In addition, 13 municipalities and four private transfer stations in Bergen County were also ordered to begin delivering waste to the Essex incinerator, beginning last week.
 
Lower Rates for a Year
 At the same time, Essex County officials were able to persuade the Port Authority and American Ref-Fuel to lower the $97-a-ton rate at the incinerator to $65 a ton for December and $70 a ton beginning in January.
The lower rates, however, are effective for only one year, and the diversion orders are both temporary, each expiring in 90 days.
As a result, Essex County officials are negotiating with officials from several counties, including Bergen, Hudson and Morris, for a supply of trash and a long-term agreement.
But, officials say, to accept all the waste from another county, the incinerator might have to be expanded and a fourth boiler added to increase capacity. And that has rankled environmentalists who have opposed the incinerator from its inception.
"We predicted that something like this would happen," said Madelyn Hoffman, director of the Grass Roots Environmental Organization in Bloomfield. "Since the very beginning we have been challenging the Essex County freeholders to tell us how much would be burned, how much is available and how much it's going to cost."
Ms. Hoffman said she was not surprised that the county could not provide the amount of trash it had agreed to deliver, since the plant was obviously larger than was needed. As a result, Newark residents may end up with an even larger incinerator where no incinerator should have been built in the first place, she said.
 
'Improperly Chosen Technology'
 Similar situations are likely to occur in Camden County, Gloucester County and Passaic County, Ms. Hoffman said. These are densely populated, urban areas where large incinerator projects are now planned or under construction.
"It's the perpetuation of an improperly chosen technology," Ms. Hoffman said.
Arnold Cohen, a member of the Ironbound Committee Against Toxic Waste, a Newark organization that has opposed construction of the incinerator, said he believed that expansion of the Essex County plant had long been anticipated by both the Port Authority and American Ref-Fuel.
"We contend that they purposely oversized this incinerator and we said so in 1985," Mr. Cohen said. "The more garbage they can put into it, the more profitable it is for them."
Indeed, while Port Authority officials deny that the plant was deliberately oversized, they acknowledge that the incineratorwas built with the possibility of expansion in mind. "We built it to handle 2,250 tons per day," said Deborah Schneekloth, the project manager for the incinerator, "but we did put in some of the infrastructure for a fourth boiler, so it is able to be expanded."
 
A Debate on Regionalization
 Ms. Schneekloth said expansion of the Newark plant fitted in with the state's current policy of regionalization. "There are economies of scale and economies of efficiency," she said. "And there is something to be said for larger plants' being more efficient environmentally."
Ms. Hoffman, however, said that having fewer, larger incinerators was just as damaging to the environment as having numerous smaller ones.
"Instead of having 19 separate ones, they're going to burn the same amount of trash at six or seven facilities," she said. "We've been saying from the beginning that we don't believe regionalization is going to be the solution to our problems."
The situation in Newark, Ms. Hoffman said, underscores environmentalists' concerns that rather than reduce the amount of trash that gets burned, regionalization only centralizes incineration. That becomes even more offensive, environmentalists say, when incinerators are squeezed into densely populated areas like Newark, Camden and possibly Passaic.
At the same time, Ms. Hoffman said, recycling is inevitably discouraged when officials struggle to comply with contracts that require specific minimum amounts of trash to be delivered to the incinerator. And a question no one has answered yet, Ms. Hoffman said, is where to dispose of the ash.
 But in that regard at least, officials say, Essex County's trash shortfall may work to its advantage.
"If Essex County is going to accept some other county's garbage, then they are going to have to provide somewhere to put the ash," said Thomas J. D'Alessio, the Essex County Sheriff, who will become the County Executive on Tuesday. "Getting rid of the ash can end up being more expensive than getting rid of the garbage."
 
The Lesser of Two Evils
 Officials in Morris County, it seems, might be willing to talk business.
"We're seeking a long-term contract for our solid-waste disposal," said John Rae, director of public information for Morris County. Officials there had been considering an incinerator, he said, but the plan generated strong public opposition.
An ash landfill, Mr. Rae said, might be the lesser of two evils. "No matter what you want to put in any community, there's going to be opposition," he said.
William J. Glover, vice president of American Ref-Fuel of Essex, said an agreement between Morris County and Essex County might be "a good fit" since it would not necessarily require a fourth boiler at the Essex incinerator.
"I think this is a perfect example of how regionalization can work," Mr. Glover said. "We can make an agreement with another county, eliminate a resource-recovery project that might have been oversized and resolve two counties' requirements in a single site."
But Margo Reinhardt, vice president of One Clean World, an environmental organization in Flanders, said that even an ash landfill in Morris was unacceptable.
"I want to see Morris's trash composted and recycled and anything that's left over safely landfilled," Ms. Reinhardt said. "We'd be sending Essex a thousand tons of garbage, and they'd send us back a thousand tons of ash. That doesn't solve anything."
Indeed, Ms. Reinhardt said, nothing short of waste reduction and intensive recycling will result in a long-term answer to the solid-waste problem.
"Regionalization of incineration," Ms. Reinhardt said, "is just regionalization of pollution."

LENGTH: 1259 words

HEADLINE: WEEHAWKEN JOURNAL;
Group Fights to Keep 'Magical' Skyline View

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO

DATELINE: WEEHAWKEN

 BODY:
EVEN the most jaded Lincoln Tunnel commuter can remember at least one day when the sight of the sun rising over Manhattan into a pastel sky meant that the morning hadn't started so badly after all.
New York as seen from the western shore of the Hudson River is a sight that is seldom disappointing, often inspiring and on occasion nothing short of breathtaking. 
 So for 20 years, a group of citizens from this compact, proud community have fought to preserve as much of that view as possible. Though their concerns may seem self-serving to some, their current battle is being fought on behalf of people who may never even know that Weehawken exists.
 "We have a national treasure here; you can't put a price tag on that view," said Ruth Elsasser, president of the Weehawken Environment Committee, one of the grass-roots groups that have banded together to protect the waterfront environment. "When you see that open vista, that magical view, even if it's just for a moment, it's something you always remember."
 
Protests Before Planning Board
 So when Hartz Mountain Industries of Secaucus submitted plans for its 93-acre Lincoln Harbor development along the waterfront, Mrs. Elsasser's group, which had been monitoring waterfront development since 1970, quickly became a thorn in the developer's side. Members descended upon planning board meetings and protested almost every aspect of the project that deviated from Weehawken's zoning ordinance.
"We said that the developers were trying to plan for an extension of New York City," Mrs. Elsasser said. "We thought that the development should be an extension of Weehawken."
As a result of the group's persistence, the developer was sent back to the drawing board until it finally came back with a project that was acceptable to officials.
 
Offices, Housing and a Marina
 The final plan, approved in three phases, provided for a total of 2,202,316 square feet of office space, 99,324 square feet of retail and restaurant space, 322 rooms in two hotels, a 250-slip marina and 300 housing units.
But though township officials had accepted Hartz's proposal, Mrs. Elsasser's group took exception to the final phase, which permitted construction of two 10-story buildings that would be "mirror images" of two buildings constructed in an earlier phase. The new buildings, the group said, would block the view of the lower portion of the Hudson.
Though that view is enjoyed primarily by people driving on the ramp to and from the Lincoln Tunnel, Mrs. Elsasser's group believed that it should be preserved and that the buildings should be reduced in height.
John R. Weingart, director of the Division of Coastal Resources in the Department of Environmental Protection, the state agency whose approval is necessary for a waterfront development permit, agreed.
"I feel that this is a spectacular view that is enjoyed by hundreds of thousands of people," he said. "And the role of a state agency like the Department of Environmental Protection is to protect resources that are important to large numbers of people."
<> <> As a result, Mr. Weingart decided to reject the plan as it was proposed. But in a departure from the department's own regulations, Christopher J. Daggett, then the Commissioner, pre-empted Mr. Weingart and issued the permit himself.
 
'Momentary Glimpse' for Motorists
 In his written opinion, Mr. Daggett said that while the proposed buildings might indeed block a portion of the view, that view amounted to nothing more than a "momentary glimpse" for passing motorists. Mr. Daggett's decision was welcomed by municipal officials who were counting on the millions of dollars in anticipated property taxes the project would generate.
The Weehawken Environment Committee, however, saw the Commissioner's approval as an abrupt finale to its 20-year efforts.
"We were outraged," Mrs. Elsasser said.
A few dozen miles to the south, officials of the American Littoral Society, a national environmental group based in Sandy Hook, had been quietly watching the issue as it developed. As long as the local environmentalists were successful, the society remained an observer. But when Mr. Daggett issued his approval, officials of the society decided it was time to act.
"The society pays close attention to the permit process that takes place in the Division of Coastal Resources," said Dery W. Bennett, director of the 9,000-member society. "And when one comes up that we think is bad, we pursue it."
The Weehawken situation presented just such a case.
"This is not a private view issue," Mr. Bennett said. "We aren't raising questions of whether somebody had a condominium bedroom with a blocked view. Our concern is with the public."
State regulations, he said, require that coastal views from public roadways not be blocked. So the society filed suit in the Appellate Division of the State Superior Court to invalidate Mr. Daggett's approval. "We did not like the idea that anytime somebody up above didn't like the decision from down below, they could just yank it away and do it themselves," Mr. Bennett said.
Former Commissioner Daggett later said he believed that his decision had been warranted by the circumstances, but the three-judge Appellate Court apparently disagreed. It vacated the permit and returned the matter to Mr. Weingart for a decision.
Municipal officials were not happy with the court's decision.
"In our opinion, this is not a view issue; this is an economic issue," said Weehawken's Mayor, Richard F. Turner. "There has to be a balance between preserving views and bringing in the ratables that Weehawken needs."
The portion of the view that is blocked, Mr. Turner said, is a small area of the river near the tip of Manhattan.
"We've have tried to preserve the view of midtown and lower Manhattan, and in fact 95 percent of the view will be there forever," he said. In any event, he said, the small portion of the view that will be blocked by the buildings is enjoyed primarily by commuters who contribute nothing to Weehawken.
"While they're sitting in traffic enjoying the view, we're putting up with air pollution and noise pollution and congestion on our local streets," Mr. Turner said. "We estimate that Weehawken will lose between $2 and $3 million in taxes if these buildings are not built. So to preserve 100 percent of the view for commuters who leave nothing in Weehawken is unwarranted, unjustified and unfair."
 
'Proud of What We're Doing'
 Officials of Hartz Mountain Industries, who have appealed the Appellate Court's decision to the State Supreme Court, said they had done everything in their power to create an attractive, viable development.
"We're proud of what we're doing," said Martin Gold, director of planning and marketing for the Secaucus developer, referring to the construction that has already been completed. "But we've put things in place with the idea of going forward with the site as it was originally approved."
And with regard to the loss of the "momentary glimpse" of the river, he said: "It would seem to me that most people ought to be concentrating on driving instead of staring out the window. It's not like there is a scenic overlook where you can pull over."
But for James T. Dette, a Weehawken resident and a member of the environmental committee, even that momentary glimpse is precious.
"Everybody who passes by enjoys it, even if only for seconds," he said. "It's like looking at a bright light. Even after you close your eyes, you can still see it."

HEADLINE: Campus Life: Connecticut;
Tutoring Inmates In Literacy: A Chance to Grow

DATELINE: NEW LONDON, Conn.

 BODY:
Students from Connect icut College and inmates at three nearby prisons are learning a lot from one another.
The college is one of a handful in the nation with literacy training geared to the prison population, said Diana Hayman, manager of the Federal Department of Education's Student Literacy Corps program. The program provides money to colleges and universities to support literacy work by students, and Connecticut's corps began operating this semester under a grant. 
 Participants study the socio-economic and political reasons behind illiteracy and the skills needed to tutor functionally illiterate adults. The practical phase of the program calls on them to teach some adults to read and write.
Fifteen professors from seven departments helped set up Connecticut College's course, "Origins and Consequences of Adult Illiteracy."
Students spend three hours in a weekly seminar and six hours tutoring inmates at one of the prisons -- J. P. Gates and the Niantic Correctional Facility, both in Niantic, and the Montville Correctional Facility.
 
'Forgot I Was in Prison'
 Rachel Parratto, a junior anthropology major from Philadelphia, said she was surprised at how comfortable she became with her student, who, she said, always arrived early for his sessions and completed extra assignments.
Lorraine Boucher, a visiting instructor in sociology and the academic coordinator for the literacy program, previously taught a class in social problems at Mohegan Community College in Norwich. But this program, she said, is her first opportunity to integrate theory and practice.
The class, which is already over-enrolled for the spring semester, will be underwritten by the Federal grant for two more semesters.

HEADLINE: Setting Up an Island in the Soviet Storm

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON

DATELINE: MOSCOW

 BODY:
On a fall Friday, just after Pepsico opened its first two Soviet Pizza Huts, a small yellow Lada puttered up to the curb outside the larger one. Out stepped a slight, middle-aged woman who wired the doors shut. The woman, a sanitary inspector, said Pizza Hut had failed to file all the necessary papers, including venereal disease tests for every employee. "This is the cleanest restaurant in Moscow," protested Andrew Rafalat, Pizza Hut's regional director for the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe. She shrugged, but would not relent.
Just days before, American executives and Soviet officials had hailed the restaurants as evidence of a new epoch, the fruit of perestroika. Said Donald M. Kendall, head of Pepsico's executive committee, "We hope that today's opening sends a clear message to the world that Western-Soviet business initiatives can succeed." 
 Welcome to fast-food capitalism in the Soviet Union, where doing business requires a mastery of bureaucratic intrigue, Socialist economics, international trade and jungle survival techniques.
With 16 years of experience here, Pepsico presumably knows the system as well as anyone. After several calls to Soviet officials, its Pizza Hut reopened in time for Saturday night. Both restaurants are now attracting crowds.
But even a company as well-connected as Pepsico, maker of the most widely available consumer brand in the Soviet Union, cannot insulate itself from growing turmoil. Soviet President Mikhail S. Gorbachev has courted Western businesses, but others, including the chief of the K.G.B., have said they represent an intrusion and a security threat.
One reason Mr. Rafalat left most of his belongings in Britain is his fear of economic collapse and social chaos. As he said even before the leadership went so deeply into crisis, "There could be a time when we have to pack a few suitcases and get the family out real quickly." By last week, he was reckoning with food costs that had, in some cases, more than tripled. In coming weeks, he plans to raise menu prices as much as 50 percent.
Many American consultants and trade officials are advising caution about Soviet ventures. Without huge stocks of patience and capital, businesses thinking about coming here might best wait until the nation settles down, they say. "The obstacles are just overwhelming," said Richard E. Ericson, a specialist in Soviet economics at Columbia University. "Anyone trying to invest had better enjoy risk."
The McDonald's Corporation has armed itself against those risks by building a $45 million plant to make its own beef patties, pasteurize its own milk and bake its own buns. A 700-seat restaurant -- its largest -- opened here last January, beating Pizza Hut by eight months and generating front-page American coverage when Soviets waited for hours to get inside. But when the state raised meat prices in October, McDonald's nearly doubled many of its prices, and customers often could walk right in.
 
The Pizza Hut Way
 Pizza Hut has taken a more modest approach, spending just $4.8 million and relying on private-sector farmers and existing channels of supply. In Mr. Rafalat's words, "Our objective is to source locally, to live off the land."
A 39-year-old British subject, Mr. Rafalat joined the London office of Pepsico Food Service International in 1984. After opening Pepsico restaurants, which also include Kentucky Fried Chicken and Taco Bell, in Africa, Eastern Europe, Scandinavia and the Middle East, he knew how to create one from scratch practically anywhere. Or so he thought. Then, in 1987, he was assigned to Moscow.
Three years later, Mr. Rafalat at first seems to overflow with enthusiasm, like a head counselor leading his campers to the swimming hole. Seldom parking himself behind a desk, he constantly bounds from kitchens to storerooms to tables.
But get him to sit down for a few minutes and he talks of experiences that have made him watchful and wary, like someone who has recently been mugged. Or shipwrecked. "Setting up a business here is like setting up an island in an ocean," Mr. Rafalat said. "You hope it will survive the storm."
Pepsico began planning its Soviet Pizza Huts in 1986 at the suggestion of Anatoly F. Dobrynin, an adviser to Mr. Gorbachev, according to Mr. Kendall. When Mr. Dobrynin was Ambassador to the United States, from 1964 to 1986, he had came to enjoy riding bicycles with his granddaughter to a local Pizza Hut.
The restaurants here are joint ventures, 49 percent owned by Pepsico, 51 percent by the City of Moscow. Mr. Rafalat wanted the city to contribute two locations. First the city offered some cellars on Moscow's outskirts. "We had to explain to these guys that typically, restaurants have to be where people are," Mr. Rafalat said.
Finally, the city agreed to two spots: one, the main restaurant, somewhat out of the way but on a major thoroughfare, Kutuzovski Prospect; the other, a small space on what was then Gorky, but now Tverskaya, Street. At the Kutuzovski building, Mr. Rafalat took the two floors above as well, for offices, warehouse space and possibly the Soviet Union's first Kentucky Fried Chicken.
 
Socialist Savvy
 Mr. Rafalat hired Polish construction workers for their combination of Western work attitudes and Socialist savvy. If a truck he needed to pick up some supplies was late, Mr. Rafalat tended to fume and wait. "The Poles realized," he said, "you go out on the street and give 15 rubles for the nearest truck."
He drilled eager, inexperienced youngsters in the arts of pepperoni-pizza production and relentless good cheer. "This is something the Russians don't understand," he said. "You don't want trained cooks. You want kids off the street. Within a few weeks, they are the best pizza cooks in the world."
Mr. Rafalat found the sanitary codes arcane, with officials standing on every provision. "The Russians will drown you in papers," he said. Inspectors objected to plans for a bathroom on the main building's second floor, with dishwashers below. "They can't imagine a toilet that doesn't leak," he said. They demanded venereal disease tests for every employee. Mr. Rafalat hired his staff a month in advance to make sure all the tests would come back in time, but they didn't.
City officials also quarreled with his plans to take hard foreign currency as well as rubles, saying that Pepsico could count on the ruble's convertibility soon. "We have been here nearly 20 years and it hasn't happened yet," Mr. Rafalat told them. "We need an insurance policy."
Soviet laws required Mr. Rafalat to divide both restaurants into two sides, ruble and hard currency. The ruble side, where a large Evropeskaya, or European, pizza with ham, beef and mushrooms costs less than $4 at the tourist exchange rate, usually has long lines by night. On the hard-currency side, where it costs more than $14, there is seldom a wait. "We have got a lot of hard-currency costs," Mr. Rafalat said. "We need hard-currency earnings."
Then there was the matter of supplies. Mr. Rafalat's insistence on predictable deliveries and consistent quality mystified Soviet officials, he said. "They say, 'If it's a good winter you'll have more. If it's a bad winter, you'll have less.' "
Mr. Rafalat decided to search the growing private market, where food remains plentiful at several times the official prices. He found a middle man who drove him 150 miles south of Moscow, where the paved roads disappear, to inspect his farm.
Mr. Rafalat is educating his suppliers step by step and ruble by ruble. At first all his cabbages arrived in unwieldy sacks and wooden boxes, caked with dirt and covered with unusable leaves. Now, he said, picking one up, they come in plastic bins, cleaned, with the outer leaves peeled away. "Each time they do something better," he said, "I pay a little more."
Stepping into the Tverskaya Pizza Hut several weeks after the opening was like visiting another country. At a dowdy state-run restaurant, an hour and a half can pass while a diner is seated, waits for menus, waits to order, then waits some more to be told that the kitchen is out of what he wants.
At the Pizza Hut, the young waiters and chefs, in their red striped shirts and visors, seem cured of the Soviet knack for standing still. They sweep, they scurry, they serve. Hanging plants, brass railings, oak trim are all immaculately transplanted from the West.
But bureaucratic obstacles persist. "It's impossible," said Mr. Rafalat. "I have a problem knowing whom to call." A few months ago, he would have had no trouble winning permission from the Soviet Council of Ministers to bring in Western small change. But now, the Soviet authorities refer him to the Russian Republic. He has filed applications there, but in the meantime, engineers coming from abroad haul hundreds of dollars in coins.
Mr. Rafalat is training Soviets to take over from him, possibly as soon as the middle of next year. "The whole Pizza Hut concept is a real world business school," he said. Once he has proven that two Pizza Huts can survive the winter, he plans to launch several more.
 
Rough Start
 At first, Mr. Rafalat had cause for wondering whether his restaurants would last a week. The crowds nearly overwhelmed him. Nowhere else had he seen hundreds waiting for hours,to enter a Pizza Hut, though he admits he should have learned from the experience of the Moscow McDonald's. Within days, he assigned more guards and planted galvanized steel railings outside.
Then there were the beer busters. Rambunctious young Soviets knew from the first day that there was no easier place to get beer for rubles. By the second day, Mr. Rafalat banned beer and smoking on the ruble side of his main restaurant, keeping them on the hard-currency side. "People may criticize us," he said. "But they were turning the restaurant into a bar."
Security at the Pizza Huts remains tight. Beer and wine are locked in cages with heavy steel bars. "People would risk their future for a can of beer," Mr. Rafalat said as he walked past the storerooms above his main restaurant. But salamis and canned peas are locked up too. "There is more food in this restaurant than you will see in any food store in Moscow," he said.
Another mistake was the Moscva Seafood pizza, with salmon and sardine. Nothing wrong with the taste, given the Soviet appetite for flavorful fish. But few Russians risked ordering an apparently domestic product in an American restaurant. "Russians have this thing," Mr. Rafalat said. "If it's their own, it must be bad."
All these problems were dwarfed by the inspector's arrival in her yellow Lada.
Tass reported that sanitary problems -- dirty onions and such -- were the reason Pizza Hut was shut down. To this day, Mr. Rafalat said, he has never been shown a copy of the regulations. His other theories include a feud between the city government and the district government, which was hungry for a slice of the joint venture's profits. But according to a local joke, Pizza Hut's only sanitary problem was that its pipelines to local officials weren't working. As Marshall I. Goldman, associate director of the Harvard Russian Research Center, said, "That's what anyone coming from the Chicago region would call a shakedown."
Mr. Rafalat said that several local officials pressured him for favors, telling him, "You have to satisfy our needs." He said he responded by giving them only meals.
After his restaurant was closed down, he frantically called local health authorities. They told him to see them at 4 that Friday afternoon. When he came by their offices, they had left for the weekend.
Meanwhile, in the Bordeaux region of France, an urgent call alerted Mr. Kendall, who was bicycling there. Mr. Kendall phoned Mr. Dobrynin, who had not heard the news. "How are things going to look in the West if we have this problem there?" Mr. Kendall asked him. "This will scare people off."
On Saturday afternoon, the Pizza Hut was back in business, in time for a busy night. But how many companies have a Donald Kendall to reopen the right doors?
 
 
 
LIFE IN MOSCOW: SERIOUS BUSINESS
 Imagine what an executive's life in the United States would be like if getting the car fixed meant driving from Chicago to New York, with a visa and export license required for every trip. In Moscow, Andrew Rafalat found that the nearest garage for anything more than an oil change on his 1989 Toyota Land Cruiser was in Helsinki, 800 miles away. "It's the longest service run in the world," he said. "A real pain."
Mr. Rafalat moved from Britain to Moscow with his wife, Kristina, a 9-year-old daughter and a 7-year-old son in January to open some Pizza Huts. Like most Western executives here, he has to be as resourceful in running his household as his business.
His apartment, a half-hour from work, is on the 20th floor in a cluster of new high-rises. Rubble scattered outside and bare, concrete-lined hallways within make it look as though the construction workers quit a few months before they finished.
Neighborhood thieves treat Mr. Rafalat's Toyota as a steady source of second-hand parts. "I've had my alarms stolen. My heater stolen. My radio stolen," Mr. Rafalat said, pointing to a dashboard with gaping holes.
Mr. Rafalat first inspected the premises in October 1989, flashlight in hand. There were no light bulbs. The stove that was supposed to be included wasn't. No mirror or medicine cabinet in the bathroom. No drain pipe underneath the sink. "I never dreamed that opening a Pizza Hut would involve designing my own apartment," he said.
By Russian standards, the apartment is huge: three bedrooms, one bath, a combination living and dining room. Having the City of Moscow as his business partner helped him get more space than most foreign executives. Some live in hotel rooms for a year.
Like backpackers, the Rafalats had to carry or ship in almost everything they needed, including 100 light bulbs. They bought their furniture from a British store that specializes in inexpensive goods for export. The furniture cost $16,000, shipping $20,000.
At first glance, the apartment has the clean, efficient look of a well-run budget motel. On closer inspection, things don't seem quite right. The same bathroom tap swings to serve sink and tub. The vents are covered with tape. "We had super-mosquitoes," Mrs. Rafalat explained. Plumbing leaks had left stagnant water in the basement, perfect breeding grounds, and mosquitoes migrated up through the ducts in the spring.
Foreigners in Moscow have their own sparse network of services. Stockmann's, a branch of a Finnish retailer, offers Western groceries at twice the Western price. The American Embassy has a health club and school, which the Rafalat children attend.
But even those with wallets full of hard currency and rubles have to gather and hunt for their meals. "Day in and day out," Mrs. Rafalat said, "you have to plan a week, or two weeks, or three weeks in advance."
On a weeknight, she assembled a meal of fresh tomatoes and cucumbers from a private market, plastic trays of salads from Stockmann's, canned salmon from a former restaurant manager Mr. Rafalat knows, and a chocolate cake from a cart downtown. The Soviet Union, Mr. Rafalat said as he finished his meal, "is not a place where we can set up a house, put our feet up and feel comfortable. At the end of the day, Moscow's no fun."

 
 
PEPSI-COLA -- BY THE TRICKLE
 It took Pepsico Inc. 15 years of negotiations to become, in 1974, the first American consumer product sold in the Soviet Union. Since then, Pepsi has seldom missed a chance to expand production or introduce Western-style marketing -- or to brag about its success. It has sponsored hockey, soccer and gymnastic events, put red, white and blue "Pepsi" logos on buses and, in 1988, became one of the first Western companies to buy advertising on Soviet television.
Capitalizing on its tenure, the company signed a 10-year agreement with the Soviet Union in April, valued at more than $3 billion, to more than double the number of Pepsi plants, to 50 from 24. In return, the Soviets promised to expand shipments of vodka that allow Pepsico to take its profits in the United States, and to build at least 10 freighters and tankers that the company can sell or lease.
Pepsi sells only its concentrate to the Soviet Union, relying on state-run bottling plants to do the rest. But the company aims to make the plant managers feel like they work for Pepsi as well as the Government. Visits to the West, advanced equipment, technical advice, spare parts -- Pepsi is there to help.
But keeping Pepsi flowing has become difficult as the Soviet economy has unraveled. Every plant has had to interrupt production. Bottling-plant managers sound like harried consumers when they describe how they scrounge for materials. "The whole range of supplies. Everything," said Aleksei A. Kochetov, director of the largest Pepsi bottling company in Moscow.
Keeping bottles on hand, and persuading stores and customers to return them, is hardest of all. "I use the economic-incentives method," said Mr. Kochetov. "You don't give me bottles, you don't get the product. It's as simple as that."
Some of Moscow's ubiquitous Pepsi kiosks always have soda, but elsewhere Pepsi fountains have been disconnected. And Pepsi usually vanishes before it reaches Moscow grocery shelves. As with other goods, it pays to have a friend who knows when shipments arrive, or, better yet, can set some bottles aside.

HEADLINE: For U.S. Airlines, a Sudden Lust for Foreign Entanglements

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
The nation's airlines, many of them burdened with debt, have plunged into a crisis under the twin pressures of recession and higher fuel prices. Consolidation, through merger or bankruptcy, threatens to sweep the industry.
Amid this sudden distress, there is a growing temptation to look abroad in search of capital to save the struggling airlines and further bolster the power of the stronger companies. This has renewed the debate over whether it is time to lift the limits on foreign involvement in the domestic air travel market. 
 The question is whether it would be wise -- or helpful -- to change the legal limits that prevent foreigners from exercising financial control over domestic airlines, especially the law that blocks foreign companies from owning more than 25 percent of an American airline's equity.
Transportation Secretary Samuel K. Skinner "plans to take the first ten days of January and basically go into a full court press" on this issue, Jeffrey Shane, Assistant Secretary of Transportation for policy and international affairs, said in a recent interview.
The issue is one that Mr. Skinner has confronted before, notably in 1989, when KLM Royal Dutch Airways invested in a leveraged buyout of Northwest Airlines and British Airways flirted inconclusively with an investment group attempting to buy out United Airlines.
In both cases, strict limits on foreign ownership were invoked. But this time, the question is being addressed in a much more comprehensive fashion. Airline executives and Government policy makers alike say they hope to resolve it in a way that could have lasting and pervasive effects on international aviation.
 "We must re-examine the limits on foreign investment in U.S. carriers," said Edwin I. Colodny, the chairman of USAir, in a speech this month in Washington, where he was awarded the aviation industry's Wright Trophy for distinguished leadership.
"The current 25 percent limit is outmoded," said Mr. Colodny, whose own airline has been attempting to expand rapidly overseas.
"The U.S. is under increasing pressure to allow greater penetration by foreign carriers of its travel market, including access to U.S. domestic traffic. Alliances and cross-ownerships are spreading. If this trend is representative, it follows that access to foreign equity is just as rational. Indeed, the airlines are already financing much of their aircraft acquisition with foreign debt capital," he said.
The airlines' enormous appetite for capital is the driving force behind the quest for foreign money. Morton S. Beyer, chairman of Avmark Inc., an aviation consulting firm, pointed out that the world's airlines are expected to take delivery of about 8,000 airplanes over the next 15 years, at a cost of about $480 billion. Already, vast numbers of these planes are purchased using complex international leases financed in Japan and Europe, as well as the United States.
But airlines like Pan Am and TWA, which are negotiating a possible mutual salvation in a risky merger, or those like Continental and Eastern, which are in bankruptcy, have a hard time tapping these sources of debt financing. They have pretty well exhausted their credit. Among the United States airlines, American, United, Delta and Northwest are most able to turn to the banks right now.
"What you find is that since 1978 the capital markets have funded huge amounts of money for the U.S. airlines," said Clark Onstad, a former senior executive of Texas Air Group, which managed Eastern and Continental during the reign of Frank Lorenzo. "But now, the only people who find the synergies that make the return on investment worthwhile are air carriers, and those are mostly foreign."
The best remaining source of cash, people in the industry say, is foreign airlines eager to benefit from the huge American market, where they cannot operate except under the very limited international route agreements negotiated between nations around the world.
Foreign airlines are generally prohibited from carrying passengers in domestic American travel, a right known as "cabotage." The restriction has two effects: it prevents a British Airways jet departing London from landing at Kennedy Airport in New York and picking up passengers for a later stop. Consequently, it is more convenient for some passengers to cross the oceans on an American airline than on a foreign carrier because of the ease of making connections inside the United States.
But American airlines face similar restrictions abroad. This has made access to the lucrative routes between the world's major cities some of the most valuable, if intangible, commodities owned by competing airlines, which can buy and sell the routes for hundreds of millions of dollars.
CHANGING the rules on foreign air carriers' investments in American airlines would, in effect, change the way access to these markets is parceled out.
Consider what would happen if an airline like Lufthansa owned half of United, or if Japan Airlines were a third partner in the proposed combination of TWA and Pan Am. Whose interests would be served then by granting American airlines new access to the global hub cities of Frankfurt or Tokyo?
"What if we simply repealed the current constraints on the ability of foreign airlines to control -- and even create -- airlines in the U.S.?" asked Mr. Shane of the Transportation Department in a speech last month that signalled the department's fresh look at the issue.
"For the first time, in effect, we would be allowing foreign carriers to buy for cash the access to our market that, heretofore, they have been able to obtain only by agreeing to open up their own markets," he said.
Mr. Shane was the State Department's leading airline route negotiator before moving to the Transportation Department, where he has helped engineer a program designed to open up international aviation.
His speech disclosed little inclination to relax foreign ownership rules if that would undermine his broader negotiating agenda: "Is there anything in the history of the international airline industry outside the U.S. -- characterized by state sanctioned and even state owned monopolies, price-fixing cartels, capacity sharing agreements, and other economic anachronisms -- that should encourage us to welcome their influence on decision-making in the domestic U.S. air services market?"
IN a subsequent interview, Mr. Shane said that the Bush Administration would prefer to open up the airlines to foreign investment, if only it could first open up the world's aviation markets in the way that many other trade barriers have long since been conquered.
"The issue is, are we ever going to reinvent the global frame in a way that removes the concern about foreign investment?" he said.
The intense pressure being felt right now to allow foreign investment may falsely assume that such a step could provide a lifeline to carriers that are in financial difficulty.
 "There is the legal adage that hard cases make bad law," Mr. Shane said. "In the same way, economic crises can, if you are not careful, beget bad policies. We are not going to abandon all caution. That will not happen."
Even so, there are signs that the Transportation Department has already begun to loosen its grip on foreign investors.
The department has twice allowed KLM extensions of the deadlines to reduce its investment in Northwest under an agreement reached in 1989, when the department ruled that the investment would give the Dutch company too much control over Wings Holdings, which had just taken over Northwest in a leveraged buyout led by the investor Alfred Checchi.
The current arrangement could be allowed to stand, Mr. Shane suggested. To date, "there is no evidence of any inappropriate level of foreign control at the new Northwest," he said.
For now, the Admininstration would prefer to decide airline investment cases one at a time, which is simpler than trying to write a rule governing all cases.
If the Administration decides to rewrite its rules, it has two options, both of which could be problematic.
It could seek a change in the law, something that would require hearings in Congress and would probably face opposition from those who think airlines are national assets.
The Administration also could by executive action seek to redefine the index of foreign control in cases where an airline's equity remains in the hands of United States citizens or corporations. This would allow airlines to borrow more heavily from foreigners, perhaps using more of their assets as collateral, and would allow more foreign representatives into corporate boardrooms. But Mr. Shane said it would take complex regulations to effect such a change, which would reverse decades of case law, and that the changes would be require extensive public comment.
Bradley Larschan, the general counsel at the Association for International Investment, a nonprofit organization that works for liberalized trading rules, said that he also believes Congress could be persuaded to support foreign investment in airlines.
"The initial reaction will be, absolutely no way," he said. "Then they will think what happens if we do not allow this. Doing nothing is also an affirmative choice. Something is going to happen. The Department of Transportation is not motivated by good wishes. It is motivated by reality."

HEADLINE: Wall Street;
Ah, the Weird Whimsy of It All

BYLINE: By Diana Henriques

 BODY:
Generally speaking, Wall Street research is no laughing matter. Day in and day out, thousands of highly paid and (usually) hard-working men and women monitor the vital signs of thousands of public companies, alert for any development that might affect the market's view of a company's worth. Their findings flow from Wall Street with the force, if not always the clarity, of a mountain cataract seeking the sea.
Some of these reports make a real contribution to investors' understanding of some arcane corner of global finance, or identify a promising investment opportunity. A few even warn investors of a company's deteriorating prospects early enough for them to exit with a portion of their profits intact. (These accomplishments are even more notable given the increased workload that analysts are facing in these lean times. Barry Graubart of Nelson Publications points out that, since 1987, Wall Street firms have been paring research staffs while increasing the number of companies they monitor.) 
 It has become customary on Wall Street for those who turn out these top-notch examples of the analyst's craft to be honored by inclusion in the "All-American" teams compiled each year by Institutional Investor magazine. But where, one wonders, are the annual awards for ghoulishness? For the steadfast support of unsteady stocks? For sheer whimsical weirdness? In short, for all the qualities that make Wall Street what it is? Here, that's where.
Wall Street has long cultivated a reputation for being able to find profit opportunities where others see only grief and pain. This honorable tradition was sustained in 1990 by Jeff Adams, until recently an analyst for PaineWebber. Last January, Mr. Adams saw encouraging prospects for the Loewen Group, a Vancouver-based funeral home chain that trades over the counter. Among his reasons: "Improving death rates."
Although death rates declined in 1989 for the first time in nearly 30 years, Mr. Adams explained, they had started to "improve" as the beginning of 1990 neared. "This implies that 1989 was likely an aberration due to the warm winter and light flu season," he observed. If death rates resumed their normal bullish trend, he added, his profit estimates for Loewen Group could be too conservative.
For the record, the stock advanced more than 40 percent during the year and another PaineWebber analyst, Gerald May, decided in August that the stock was "no longer cheap," given the company's rapid growth and various other risks, including "future trends in cremations vis-a-vis traditional funeral services." Cremations are less costly, "taking into account caskets, limousines and travel time for relatives," Mr. May noted, and a shift toward this less-elaborate ritual might "place some pressure on pricing of traditional funeral services."
If Wall Street gave out a Rhett Butler Award for the swashbuckling defense of a losing cause, the walk-away winner this year would be Robert McAdoo, a widely followed aviation industry analyst at Oppenheimer & Company. The fearless headline on his Oct. 24 report on Continental Airlines Holdings says it all: "New Chairman Removes President and 4 VPs; Press Reports Fuel Costs Led Company to Review Possible Bankruptcy Filing; Stock Still Attractive."
The shares were trading on the American Stock Exchange at just under $6 each.
The management purge? "A new broom sweeps clean," said Mr. McAdoo. Those press reports of a possible bankruptcy filing? Merely one cost-saving strategy being considered by the debt-heavy company. "Interest costs are the expenses most easily cut by bankruptcy," he said. Still, he did not believe that Continental's fuel price problems were "sufficient to force such a filing."
They were, though. On Dec. 3, the airline filed. This was how Mr. McAdoo concluded his report that day, as Continental's stock fell more than a dollar to close at $3.50 a share: "We are removing Continental Airlines Holdings from our Special Research Series Recommended List. We believe . . . that holders of both the debt and equity securities will be expected to share the pain of the restructuring. At this time, we would avoid all of the company's securities except the secured debt, and would hold that debt only after closely reviewing the collateral associated with the various issues."
Continental closed on Friday at $1.875.
If Woody Allen ever felt inclined to produce a comedy about the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation, known as Freddie Mac, a useful resource would be the whimsical report issued on Oct. 15 by Eric I. Hemel, a mortgage finance industry analyst for First Boston. "Freddie Mac's stock is torturing us," wrote Mr. Hemel. Resolutely bullish on the deteriorating stock, which was then trading at $43.25 a share, Mr. Hemel had some advice for the agency's top executives, who were "obviously experiencing difficulties communicating clearly with investors."
One of the big problems, Mr. Hemel said, was that "Freddie's management is completely honest intellectually." He explained that several key executives of the company "are all former professors of economics or finance. Intellectual integrity is an almost inherent aspect of their personalities." The problem with all that inherent integrity, he continued, was that "economists have the tendency to qualify almost everything they say with a caveat (i.e., 'Our credit quality trends are positive but, on the other hand, if Saddam Hussein invades New York City, trends could turn negative.')"
While Mr. Hemel, himself a former academician, said he was sympathetic to these "personality quirks," others on Wall Street "tend to infer that a corporate official who hedges every positive point with a negative point must really mean to imply that something is seriously wrong."
The psychoanalysis may have helped; Freddie Mac closed Friday at $48.75.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY;
The Starch Goes Out of the Dollar

 BODY:
Reality caught up with the dollar this holiday week. The American currency surged before Christmas to the 1.51 level against the German mark. Last week, it jumped to 1.54 against the mark and 136 against the yen. Although still quite weak, the dollar was showing signs of strength not seen for months. But on Thursday the Government reported that orders for durable goods had fallen 10.5 percent in November. And on Friday, it was reported that the index of leading economic indicators had declined for the fifth month in a row, dropping 1.2 percent in November. These recession signals knocked the dollar down sharply to -- 1.4936 marks and 134.85 yen -- and put it back on the downward track most forecasters expect it to continue on.

HEADLINE: World Markets;
Coping With the Threat of Peace

BYLINE: By Jonathan Fuerbringer

 BODY:
With the Jan. 15 deadline for Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait and the possibility of a war afterward staring them in the face, you might expect portfolio managers and market analysts to be beside themselves with anxiety. So why are they so calm?
The answer is that since last August, when the Persian Gulf crisis began, they have become adept at coping with uncertainty about war. And when they look at the larger global picture, they see other factors clouding the outlook for the coming year far more than the situation in the Persian Gulf. 
 These managers point to the decline in world economic growth, with the United States, Britain, Canada and Australia in recessions, the problems with banking systems in America and Japan and the high level of interest rates in Europe and Japan.
"To an extent, it is still the same question," Martin Paling, international strategist for James Capel Inc. in London said about the uncertainty of war. "The deadline does not necessarily say that you will have a war after the Jan. 15, even if the United Nations requirements are not met. We have no idea what is going to happen."
"I do not treat lightly the possibility of hostilities," added Lawrence W. Harris 3d, a director of Lombard Odier International in London. "But this is the most prepared for war portfolio managers have been in my lifetime."
That does not mean that a war would not send stocks down sharply in markets around the world. This would be the likely immediate reaction, especially because some markets have rallied a bit since the lows reached in the fall. But these managers and analysts believe that a war could be quick and that markets would rebound.
In fact, one portfolio manager stands ready with cash to leap in and buy when stocks drop if there is fighting. Another is already putting some cash back into stocks in case there is a negotiated settlement.
Mr. Harris knows a long conflict is possible, but he is counting on a negotiated settlement or quick war that would do no long-term damage to oil production.
"I am convinced there is so much oil out there that it is hard for me to believe that the price of oil would go up very much," he said. Earlier this year, the uncertainity for stock markets caused him to keep 30 percent of the Worldwide Value Fund in cash. He has cut that to 18 percent so he can buy back some stocks, on the theory that there will be a settlement or quick war followed by an equities rally.
Mr. Paling of James Capel agreed. "We feel that the impact of a war on the medium-term economic performance would be limited," he said. Asked about the threat of a war, he answered, "The real question is what is happening if peace breaks out."
Sander Cseh, who runs Cseh International in Philadelphia, has about 10 percent of his small fund in cash. He expects the drop in stock prices to be so shortlived if war breaks out that he is ready to snap up stocks should it happen.
Such strategies reflect the view that a war will not mean a long-term jump in oil prices, an analysis based on the recent sharp increase in oil production in Saudi Arabia and other oil-producing countries.
But beyond that, analysts and portfolio managers fall back on fundamentals -- the outlook for the world economy and interest rates in general. And although they are generally positive that global stock markets will rise moderately in 1991, after the sharp declines of 1990, there are seeds of doubt. "The main thing that worries me is the possibility that the deterioration in the economy is worse than I thought," said Mr. Harris.
Madhav Dhar, a global strategist for Morgan Stanley & Company in New York who is much more bearish, believes that stock markets are too anxious for a rally, which he said means they are ignoring the shakey economic fundamentals.
Finally, there is the problem of the Soviet Union, the wild card in everyone's analysis. It is a country whose problems could send stock markets around the world reeling if something went seriously awry. "With Russia about to implode, that just adds to the risk premium," said Mr. Dhar.
