HEADLINE: All About/Tractors;
An Uncertain Reprieve For Tractor Makers

BYLINE: By ERIC N. BERG

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
Gleaming new tractors are becoming more familiar sights on America's farms. Sales and profits at the three leading United States tractor makers -- Deere & Company, the J.I. Case division of Tenneco Inc. and the Ford Motor Company's Ford New Holland division -- are all up, reflecting renewed agricultural prosperity after the near-depression of the early and mid-1980's.
But the recovery in the tractor business, now in its third year, is fragile. War in the Middle East could send oil prices still higher, cutting into farmers' cash flow and undermining their ability to buy new tractors. The price of wheat, one of the most important crops tended by tractors, has been slumping. Further declines could make farmers skittish about buying new equipment. 
 Chief among the longer-term concerns is competition from Japan. Some industry experts fear that the Japanese, who entered the United States market more than a decade ago with small tractors, will increasingly compete with American and European companies for sales of larger machines.
 
The Big Question
A Lull Or Downturn?
 After three straight years of increased sales, America's largest tractor makers wonder whether they are tipping into a temporary lull or a decline like the one that nearly bankrupted them a decade ago.
Dealers report that retail sales of giant row tractors used to harvest crops fell 3 percent in August, 35 percent in September and were flat in October. Although sales rose 18 percent in November, many analysts remain concerned. "The business stopped dead in August," said David Mowitz, senior editor of Successful Farming magazine.
The tractor makers say their troubles are temporary because farmers are in their best financial condition in years. "It's clear the farmer has money," said Eli Lustgarten, an analyst at PaineWebber. Added Tobias Levkovich, Smith Barney's machinery analyst, "For tractor makers, the up cycle is still intact."
Farmers have paid down debt, and interest rates on their remaining borrowings are lower. The value of their land is up due to higher commodity prices and less acreage in production. And farm incomes tend to run counter to the rest of the economy, as demand for food remains steady while farmers' interest costs and other expenses stabilize or decline. A long recession might leave farmers more prosperous and more inclined to buy new tractors, even those priced close to $100,000..
Analysts say the sudden slump in sales recently was caused by the fear of reduced Federal farm subsidies and the spike in energy prices. But Federal subsidy levels for 1991 have survived with only minor reductions, and many farmers have decided they can absorb the higher costs of energy so far.
Still, even optimists say that war in the Persian Gulf could derail the industry's long-term recovery. Energy comprises more than 6 percent of a farmer's costs, from gasoline and diesel fuel to the petroleum used in many fertilizers and pesticides.
"Barring oil prices going up, I see replacement demand continuing to go up," said Thomas G. Burns, the farm machinery analyst at Goldman Sachs & Company. "But if it's war in the Middle East Jan. 15, you won't have farmers going into dealer showrooms."
 
More Competition
Japan's Move Into Big Tractors

Having conquered the market for small tractors, will the Japanese next attack the market for mid-sized and large machines?
The Japanese developed small tractors for tiny Japanese farms, then penetrated the United States over the last decade by selling to weekend farmers. Each of the Big Three sells Japanese tractors under its own nameplate, and some Japanese makers have dealers of their own.
Farm-machinery executives in the United States would just as soon avoid the topic of the Japanese. But industry experts say tractor makers should remember that the Japanese auto companies scored their first American successes with small cars.
Two Japanese farm-equipment companies, Iseki and Shibauri, are now test-marketing large tractors in Australia. Another Japanese company, Kubota, recently opened a large-tractor plant in Portugal, and Kubota already sells a mid-sized tractor in this country. Deere, Case, Ford and European companies like Deutz-Allis of Germany and Italy's Fiat still dominate sales of large tractors. Ford has recently agreed to merge its tractor division with Fiat's.
But the market's shift toward larger machines to serve larger farms, and the wider profit margins on the bigger tractors, are bound to attract the Japanese. Mid-sized tractors are used primarily in Europe, while the largest roar across the vast farms in the American and Canadian West. "No market is secure," said John Stark, an analyst who publishes Stark's Off-Highway Ledger. "The mix of business is changing and the Japanese could change with it."
 Some analysts say the Japanese would need five to seven years to match the quality of American and European competitors. "U.S. machinery makers have been building awfully good tractors," said Mr. Mowitz of Successful Farming magazine.
The Japanese also lack extensive dealer networks. "Establishing a distribution channel would be the major obstacle for the Japanese," said Vickie L. Rickey, a marketing vice president in Case's tractor group.
Still, the Japanese might not want to expand their commitment to tractors. Profit margins have been slipping due to price cutting and promotions. And with the number of farms declining, long-term tractor demand is headed down.
 
 
Emergency Parts
Federal Express To the Rescue
The competition among major tractor makers has become a race to get parts to farmers in the field. The companies figure that with tractors increasingly alike, they can win sales by convincing farmers of fast service in case of a breakdown. Rapid delivery of parts is particularly important for so-called custom cutters, nomadic harvesters who travel in groups, like wagon trains, following the crops from south to north.
All the tractor manufacturers have developed systems that deliver spare parts within a day of a breakdown, often within hours. Farmers use mobile telephones or C.B. radios in their cabs to relay parts requests to a dealer. The dealer searches his inventory and delivers the partsimmediately if they are in stock. If not, the dealer dials the manufacturer's computer system, which uses satellite communications to comb the world for the part. The computer searches from one parts depot to the next, starting with the closest. Deere and Case both have nearly a dozen parts depots scattered throughout North America.
The computer generates a written order for the depot to remove the part from stock, pack it and ship it to the farmer. In many cases, the picking and packing is done by robots, so once the dealer keys the parts request into his computer terminal, machines do the rest.
Parts are delivered by overnight services like Federal Express or United Parcel Service. Because competition has forced these couriers to serve increasingly remote spots, tractor makers can offer next-day service to farmers in Saskatchewan and Montana. Even at $20 or $30 for shipping, the practice has become commonplace. As Ms. Rickey of Case said, "When you consider the cost of the down time associated with a $100,000 machine, it becomes pretty cost effective to deliver that part."
 
 
PLOWING BY COMPUTER
 IT is a problem farmers have suffered since the era of the ox and cart: getting stuck in the mud, with wheels gaining no ground. Most tractors gave farmers few choices besides revving their engines. Now, electronic sensors and on-board computers are giving tractors a push.
Deere, Case and Ford are all coming out with tractors that electronically sense the difficulty of the terrain and the task, and transmit this information to a computer that makes the necessary adjustments. If the computer learns that a plow pulled by a tractor is encountering a rocky stretch, it signals the hitch to raise the plow height to make the going easier. On steep slopes, the computer directs the tractor's transmission to downshift -- much like a car's automatic transmission downshifts when climbing a hill -- or engages the tractor's front-wheel drive. The same computer disengages the front-wheel drive when it senses the beginning of a sharp turn.
"Based on the operator's chosen strategy -- conserving fuel, keeping a constant implement depth or maintaining a certain speed -- today's tractors are close to being able to manage themselves," said Gary Ball, Ford New Holland's chief tractor engineer.
Extricating tractors from the mud is not the only mission where electronics are handy. Electronic sensors to monitor levels of crucial fluids like coolant and oil are already common. Many tractor makers have added shut-off systems that bring a tractor to a halt if it overheats or if the oil level is dangerously low. The shut-offs are particularly popular where farm operators hire migrant workers, many of whom cannot speak English or read dashboard monitors.
Next, perhaps: satellite-to-tractor links. Already, larger farmers sometimes use satellite photographs to determine which of their tracts, once planted, will generate the highest yields. Tractor makers hope to install computers that can digest this information, then automatically concentrate the application of costly fertilizer and chemicals on the most productive land. "Within the next 15 years, that capability will be commonplace," predicted Mr. Ball.

HEADLINE: FORUM;
Philanthropy as a Business Tactic . . .

BYLINE: By Joel Kurtzman

 BODY:
For more than a decade, companies have been asked to take on more responsibility for the nation's social programs. But how? According to Craig Smith, editor and publisher of the Corporate Philanthropy Report, a Seattle-based newsletter, they should make philanthropy part of their business plans. Mr. Smith spoke with Joel Kurtzman of The New York Times. 
 Question: What will be the impact of the recession on corporate philanthropy?
Answer: The impact will be mixed. For 1991, not much impact. Corporate contributions will keep pace with inflation. They will stay at about the $2 billion-a-year level. Usually there is a two-year lag before changes in profits are felt in corporate contributions.
Q. You have talked about making philanthropy part of a company's business strategy. What do you mean by that?
A. It means that a company puts in place a management system that allows the person in charge of contributions or corporate social policy, a term I like better, to be in close consultation with the heads of the company's other operating units and divisions. It means the social-policy person talks with the heads of human resources, marketing, research and development, new product development and so on. It means the social-policy person sits with the human resources director and asks what he or she is trying to achieve for the company. Out of this comes a dynamic, and ideas emerge about how the company's social policy can assist the company's business strategy.
Q. In practice, how would that work?
A. Well, the human resources person, for example, might say the company needs to attract higher quality recruits from the nation's top business or engineering schools. The social-policy person may then strengthen the company's contributions to the schools that the human resources people want to recruit from. At some schools, becoming a major donor means that a company can get the first crack at seeing students' resumes. At the same time, if a company makes product donations to the school -- computers, for instance -- then the image of the company goes up as the graduate students use the company's products.
Q. Does this mean that companies should be more self-serving in their giving?
A. Not really. I don't think it should be a one-way street, with philanthropy just there to support the company's recruitment efforts. That is why there must be a dynamic so that when the social-policy person and the head of another division sit down, they will create some synergy.
For example, if a company's social agenda is to advance public-school reform, the social-policy person will look for ways that the different units of the company can be orchestrated to pursue that end. The social-policy person will coordinate the resources of the company -- by getting volunteers to work for K-12 education, by using the company's research and products and so on.
Q. What about companies that are generous with their money but don't make philanthropy part of their business strategy?
A. I don't think you can achieve much just by giving money. It doesn't affect the factors that created the problem.
For example, pharmaceutical companies can just give money to combat AIDS. Or, they can pursue solutions to the crisis in ways that go beyond just the typical calculations of short-term returns. They can take on initiatives that would tap their scientific capacity. They have a lot to contribute -- products, cutting-edge knowlege, laboratories, product development.
They can contribute by fostering relationships between workers in the medical world and by helping build networks. They can go to their industry associations and become levers for bringing about industry-wide relationships. They can bring their distributors, stakeholders into the fight and mobilize their employees as volunteers. The social-policy person could orchestrate all this.
Q. Have pharmaceutical companies done this?
A. No. The pharmaceutical industry hasn't done any of this. It's an industry that hasn't gathered around a cause.
The insurance industry is different. Insurance companies set up a think tank on social issues and they have an industry forum on social policy. In the mid-1980's, the insurance industry emphasized AIDS education and put major dollars into programs that they thought would help prevent the epidemic from spreading. They responded before the Federal Government and United Way responded. They recognized immediately that it was in their interest to work strategically on AIDS education as a social-policy issue.
Q. Have other industries mobilized around causes they are familiar with?
A. Yes. As hunger was becoming a huge problem in the mid-1980's, and people were lining up at soup kitchens, the Government did nothing but give out food stamps. The food industry, on the other hand, got together and found ways to get donated food into food banks and soup kitchens. They put into place an emergency food-distribution system, and it didn't cost them much because of the tax benefits. General Mills, Pillsbury and Sara Lee worked on this. These groups of companies were the natural experts. They allowed their philanthropic strategies to work with their business strategies. A lot of companies haven't figured this out.
Q. Are there other ways companies benefit from philanthropic activity?
A. Yes. Philanthropy is also a marketing issue. In most mature industries, there are a number of products of the same quality and price. When you have that circumstance, corporate citizenship makes the critical difference between whether a company's product can be trusted or not. A recent Roper poll showed that 52 percent of consumers said they will pay more for a socially responsible product. Companies viewed as socially responsible have a marketing edge.
Socially conscious companies also have better morale. A recent study by David Lewin at the Columbia Business School looked at 188 companies. Mr Lewin found that employee morale was three times higher in companies with a strong degree of community involvement than in companies that were not involved.
Q. Which companies are the best philanthropists?
A. The most generous company, in terms of overall giving is I.B.M. It gave $135 million in 1989. It will give $150 million this year.
As far as contributions as percentage of net earnings before taxes, Cummins Engine Company at 16.5 percent was the best.
And as far as who are the best philanthropists, Levi Strauss & Company has linked all of its programs to fighting AIDS throughout every aspect of the company. The Baby Bell companies have been the leading-edge forces behind public-school reform and the Dayton Hudson Corporation and the Weyerhaeuser Company have been very good strategic givers.

HEADLINE: For Toe-Wigglers to Wear to the Office

BYLINE: By DEBORAH HOFMANN

 BODY:
Men dressing for success these days are likely to also be slipping into comfort -- on their feet.
Makers of men's dress shoes say the trend is fed by a generation used to the relaxed roominess of athletic shoes, as well as by the changing workplace of the 1990's, with its less rigid ideas about what is acceptable in business footwear. Toss in high technology and orthopedic improvements, and " 'the comfort factor' is the news in men's shoes," said Tom Julian, a spokesman for the Men's Fashion Association, a trade group. 
 To compete, the 140-year-old shoe company Johnston & Murphy, whose headquarters are in Nashville, introduced business shoes with what it calls a "trampoline" cushion system in wing tips, kilties, slip-ons and cap toes that can go toe to toe with any other dress shoe in the haughtiest board room on Wall Street. No rubber soles: the company still uses leather, as well as welt construction. The shoes are sold in its specialty shops and at Macy's and Nordstrom stores for about $215.
Allen-Edmonds makes dress shoes that are known for their cork cushioning. Virginia Riddle, a spokeswoman, said the technology hasn't changed since 1922. The shoe is welted -- stitched all the way around -- and there are no nails, metal shanks or plastic parts. It is flexible, and there is little if any breaking-in period.
"Due to the recession, a huge part of our business growth in our executive shoes has been tied to our policy of recrafting old shoes," Ms. Riddle said. It is not uncommon, she said, for customers to send back 40-year-old shoes for repair. More than 500 pairs with an average age of 10 years arrive at the factory in Port Washington, Wis., every week. The company ships about 6,000 new pairs a week.
The cost for recrafting a pair of shoes is $79. They get new soles, heels, cork cushioning and welting, and the calfskin uppers are completely stripped and refinished. This all takes three to four weeks.
Allen-Edmonds's wing tips, cap toes, loafers, oxfords and Fred Astaire-style spectators are $205 at Saks, F. R. Tripler, Macy's and Nordstrom. "Men who wear ours also use Rockport, frankly," Ms. Riddle said. She was referring to a competitor that many men say eased them into comfort shoes.
Next fall the Hush Puppies division of Wolverine World Wide will enter the dress-comfort race with five styles, at $80 to $95. "We did it as a reaction to the athletic shoe phenomenon," said Dick Simpson, a spokesman for the company, which is in Rockford, Mich. "The extreme lightweight constuction and support inherent in athletic footwear has developed a new awareness among a lot of men who have till now been channeled into stiff dress shoes."
 
Sneakers Are Stealing In, Too
 Naturalizer is also planning a men's collection that it hopes will make compromise a thing of the past. And sport brands like L.A. Gear, Reebok, Cole-Haan and Nike are tailoring their casual styles more for the business setting, using things like sueded canvas, beefier treads and soft interior construction.
The trim heels and soles of Rockport's dress shoes, introduced in October, look normal. But the leather wraps around polyurethane cushions. They are $210 at Tip Top Shoes, Moe Ginsburg, Fellman Limited, Macy's and Bloomingdale's.
"There's been this whole comfort myth that men's shoes were flat, therefore comfortable," said Reina O'Connor, Rockport's director of corporate marketing. "Men are now getting what women got in high heels last fall."

HEADLINE: ABOUT CARS;
A Decade Under the Spell of Technology

BYLINE: By Marshall Schuon

 BODY:
I see it coming. Some time in the next couple of years, not to mention late in the decade, you will need a glossary to read this column.
Right now, we have ABS (antilock braking system) and AWD (all-wheel drive) and CVT (for constantly variable transmission), among others.
But more alphabetized technology is on the way. And, surprisingly, that means that high petroleum prices and automobile sales have not stalled automotive progress. In fact, cars just keep getting better and better.
There is, for instance, active suspension, available now on Nissan's Infiniti Q45. Like so much of what is going on everywhere, active suspension is a new use for the computer, and it will be the widely accepted major technology of the middle future.
In Infiniti's case, the suspension uses two computers, a pump and hydraulic actuators to smooth the road by moving the wheels into the road's depressions. More than that, active suspension works against the laws of physics and can make a car lean into a turn like a motorcycle instead of heeling to the outside.
The flat cornering allows a behemoth to behave like a sports car, controlling roll, brake-dive, acceleration-squat and load-leveling. And, believe me, you will hear more about all of this.
Another technology for the 90's is traction control, with names ranging from the clever "Traction Control" (Cadillac Allante) to ASD and ASR. The latter two come from Mercedes-Benz and stand for the German words for automatic locking differential and automatic slip control.
Infiniti and other cars also offer the feature, and it is sort of the reverse of antilock brakes. Using the computer and wheel sensors, the system recognizes imminent wheel spin and throttles down the engine. That prevents tires from slipping, and it gets you out of the rut or up the hill.
Honda's version is called TCS (traction control system), and it is designed for front-drive cars. According to the company, it provides "ultimate cornering and acceleration on various road surfaces, including gravel." It also sends the same amount of power to driven and steered wheels, and the computer makes everything all better, even if the driver puts a heavy foot into the gas.
Computers also are keeping an eye on engine mounts. The idea here (it's experimental and doesn't yet have an alphabet-soup name) is much the same as active suspension. But the purpose is to make shaky four- and six-cylinder engines as smooth and vibration-free as V8's.
According to developers in Europe and America, the systems can breathe new life into the small-engine concept, which means saving weight, increasing economy and eliminating billions of dollars in engine-development costs. Accelerometers and actuators are used to shorten and lengthen hydraulic engine mounts so that the powerplant's throb is not sent to the car's body.
Less dramatic, but interesting in its own way is another electronic breakthrough, this one by General Electric's lighting division. According to Motor Magazine, the system uses only one bulb, which feeds a network of fiber optics to light everything from the road ahead to the ashtray in the back seat.
"A star-shaped gas-discharge bulb, called a Light Engine, would be mounted somewhere out of the way and light any time the ignition was on," the magazine says. "Light would be piped through a fiber-optic network to wherever it was needed."
The advantages would be a minimum of power to generate light and easier packaging that could cut automobile assembly costs. There is, however, one disadvantage: You're in big trouble when that light bulb blows.
Further dabbling with the magic of electronics has generated some hot ideas about cleaning up tailpipe emissions, and most of the manufacturers are working on "preheated catalysts."
Almost all of the pollution produced on short trips comes out of the exhaust in the first several minutes, when an engine is cold and not operating efficiently. And the notion here is that a catalytic converter, heated electrically before the engine is started, could eliminate the problem.
Such a system might double the cost of the converter, and it would mean a driver would have to wait 10 to 20 seconds before starting the car, but tests by the Environmental Protection Agency show that an electrically heated catalyst can reduce hydrocarbons and carbon monoxide by more than half, compared with current systems.
And then there is Volkswagen's experimentation with a sort of Thermos-bottle way of saving engine heat. Again, the purpose is to eliminate cold starts, but VW has developed a way to store warmth from the cooling system and release it when needed. It's all very complicated, and we'll talk more of it later. For the moment, just remember that it is called a "heat battery."
In fact, why don't you just clip this column? Then I won't have to write all those glossaries.

HEADLINE: IN THE NATION;
War And Malaise

BYLINE: BY Tom Wicker

DATELINE: ROCHESTER , Vt.

 BODY:
Despite a flood of Christmas sentimentality about "the kids over there" -- this time including women -- little appetite for war in the Middle East can be found among the American people; quite the opposite.
As the announced deadline of Jan. 15 approaches, the problem seems more than the natural reluctance of any nation to send its youth into war. It's more, I believe, than the obvious truth expressed by Senator Pat Leahy of Vermont to President Bush: 
 "Americans support defensive measures to contain the Iraqi Army, but there is no consensus for . . . an offensive war that would cost the lives of thousands of Americans."
The unease is palpable -- to some extent because memories of Vietnam remain so corrosive, and because Mr. Bush has not provided convincing reasons for another distant war in which, again, "allies" are likely to become fair-weather friends. No doubt, too, many Americans have heard with anxiety their military leaders' protests that U.S. forces, which will do most of the fighting and dying, are not yet ready for combat.
The end of the cold war, being celebrated only last Christmas, now seems far away and forgotten. The so-called peace dividend is fast disappearing into a military operation costing at least $2.5 billion a month even before the shooting starts.
Still, even more than all that is at the root of American malaise during a holiday season overshadowed by threats abroad and at home. Americans, it seems to me, are deeply troubled by the sheer incongruity of mounting a huge military operation abroad, the necessary purpose of which remains unclear, while the economy deteriorates at home, our children go begging and shooting, crime spirals upward, the schools educate poorly if at all, millions neither have nor can afford medical care and bridges fall down on the homeless sleeping beneath them.
A sampling of holiday domestic news makes the point:
Dec. 20 -- The Commerce Department revised its estimate of third-quarter growth in gross national product, from 1.7 percent to an anemic 1.4 percent. The fourth quarter will be worse.
Dec. 21 -- In 1990, the New York metropolitan area lost about 100,000 private industry jobs, according to a Federal survey. Three more of the nation's largest banks reported millions in bad-loan write-offs or dividend reductions. For 1991, U.S. businesses plan to expand spending by only 0.4 percent, the least in five years.
Dec. 22 -- The Federal Home Loan Bank Board's senior economist said it would take three to five years for growth to resume in New York City. U.S. health care spending was announced to have reached $804 billion last year; but though the law requires lead-poisoning tests for all the nation's 12 million poor children, only a third actually are being tested.
Dec. 23 -- In Washington, five children, one only 6 years old, were shot by other youngsters from a passing car; there have been 471 homicides in the city this year. The Federal budget office sought about $1 billion in reductions in aid to mass transit, despite a recent increase in the gasoline tax.
Dec. 24 -- Experts said the health of Americans in the inner cities had deteriorated to "critical condition," while the rest of us are healthier and living longer. Even the television networks suffered one of their worst years; all lost market share and CBS announced a fourth-quarter loss.
Dec. 25 -- Retailers reported "the poorest Christmas season in years" as shoppers didn't show up. Signs of the first U.S. price deflation since the 1940's also were reported -- bad signs for a government, businesses and people burdened with debt.
Dec. 26 -- Further signaling recession, a wide spectrum of American manufacturers, caught in a price squeeze, are deferring plans to modernize and expand. Some are laying off employees.
And on and on. It's true that spending for another war might provide stimulus to a sick economy. But that's not only a distasteful way to prime the pump; it's inefficient, in lives and money. Already the Pentagon is having to pay premium prices -- sometimes twice standard rates -- for shipping space to the gulf.
Anyway, it's not just economic problems but values that trouble the nation, as Jan. 15 nears. In Britain in 1939, leaders sought "peace in our time" and shrank from a war to defend a people "of whom we know little." Now, in ironic reversal, many Americans sense that their most pressing problems are here at home, largely unattended by leaders in the fatal grip of superpower illusions, who hasten them into ill-considered war.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Sverdlovsk Shows the Way the Soviet Economy Is Retooling

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"In Urals City, the Communist Apparatus Ends but Not the Communist Power," your Dec. 13 report on my home city, Sverdlovsk, was not only provocative and stimulating, but also itself a sign of real and deep changes in the Soviet Union and in Sverdlovsk in particular. However, readers need to know the framework in which these changes are taking place to understand the positive nature of the fast-moving developments.
Sverdlovsk was a closed city until early this year. Today it is opening rapidly, with visas widely granted to businessmen and scientists (and even reporters) from the United States, Western Europe, Japan, Korea. Free elections were held for the first time last March, bringing inexperienced newcomers in to replace previously unopposed party candidates.
Your reporter ran into the contradictions of a political and economic system in transition. Sverdlovsk's economy, and that of the whole Ural region, was heavily dependent on military production; today, there is a rush to convert even some of the military plants to a market-style economy with civilian goods.
As with political power, the size of the plant (or enterprise) determines how it is managed or controlled. Some enterprises are so large that they can only be directed at the Russian Republic or All Union central government level -- two or three levels of authority above the City Council.
The City Council has responsibility only for its small shops and enterprises. Some of the city's problems are not solvable at the local level. The large enterprises have the hard currency that is the engine that drives a free market economy. These enterprises pay taxes to the city government, but refuse to pay hard currency because they can use 30 percent of those funds to purchase (usually through the central government) supplies and consumer goods from abroad for their employees as fringe benefits and incentives.
Major changes are under way in the Urals. The regional government was granted by the Soviet Council of Ministers last August the rights to carry out direct trade in the products of the region, amounting to $1.3 billion in the next two years. This and other orders are designed to make the Sverdlovsk region a model for trade and development.
It clearly constitutes a major step in economic decentralization -- a process that was not emphasized to your reporter on his first (but, let us certainly hope, not his last) visit to Sverdlovsk.
 
VLADMIMIR GUBANOV
Evanston, Ill., Dec. 20, 1990
 
The writer, a visiting professor at Northwestern University, is professor of chemistry at the Academy of Sciences, Sverdlovsk.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Kuwaitis in Washington Plan Rebuilding of Nation

BYLINE: By MARTIN TOLCHIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Behind an unmarked door in an office building five blocks from the White House, more than 100 Kuwaiti bureaucrats are devising a master plan to rebuild their war-ravaged nation.
Working at computer terminals in two dozen glass-paneled offices, they are planning the reconstruction of everything from sewers to hospitals to airport communications systems. They are hoping for an opportunity to make some changes in the cultural structure as well. 
 "It's not going to be the same old Kuwait," said Kareem al-Saeed, a spokesman for Citizens for a Free Kuwait, a group of Kuwaitis and Americans who proposed the project and obtained sponsorship from the Kuwaiti Government in exile in Taif, Saudi Arabia. "We're going to build a very modern, very progressive, high-tech nation."
The cost of reconstructing Kuwait, which has been plundered by the Iraqi forces that invaded on Aug. 2, is estimated by Fawzi al-Sultan, the project's director, at $20 billion. He says the sum is based on the worst case scenario: the total devastation of his nation.
 
$2.8 Trillion in Foreign Banks
 The estimate may be low, but the country's vast overseas property holdings and $2.8 trillion deposited in foreign bank accounts mean that the Government in exile is unlikely to have any problem paying for the rebuilding, whatever the cost.
The planners, described by Mr. Saeed as "the cream of Kuwait's bureaucrats," were sent here about two months ago after the reconstruction proposal was accepted by the Government in exile. They are working closely with American officials and businessmen, under the supervision of Sheik Ali al-Khalifa al-Sabah, the exile Government's Finance Minister.
The planners are organized into 10 teams, each devoted to a segment of Kuwaiti society: communications, transportation, education, health and food, airports and ports, electricity and water, oil, public works, information and the legal and judicial system.
Their work is both broad and specific. Because the Iraqis have destroyed many Kuwaiti public records, they seek to recreate a national data base beyond the census data and property records smuggled out of Kuwait City on computer tapes.
 
'Institutional Memory' Gone
 "Saddam Hussein destroyed Kuwait's institutional memory," Mr. Saeed said. "If you want to rebuild a refinery, all the blueprints are gone. You have to go back to the builder of the refinery."
Mr. Sultan, a Yale-educated World Bank official, said: "We need detailed implementation plans -- which man takes which container of drugs to which medical facility. We need to provide refrigerators before we can provide meat. We need to know who can deliver what, and when."
The planners also hope to eliminate what they consider the built-in inefficiencies of the Kuwaiti way of life.
"We'd like to reconstruct our attitude toward life," said Saif Abdullah, a professor of public administration at Kuwait University, who is working with Hassan al-Ibrahim, a former Education Minister, on plans to re-create the education system. "We try to create a consensus in our society, which requires one to accept inefficiencies, and give automatic promotions. This is an opportunity to take a new direction toward competition and merit."
For instance, he said, the officials hope to demolish the national concept of higher education as an entitlement.
 
Competition Is Promoted
 "In the past, education was considered a social right," Mr. Abdullah said. "In the future we have to talk more about responsibility, competition and merit."
Mr. Sultan said: "We're reorganizing the bureaucracy and redefining the roles of the government and private sector. We had a welfare system -- anybody who came in was given a job." Such a system will not survive, he added.
A critical question is the size of the population, he said, adding, "We had a population of 2.2 million, and now we're down to half a million." He said the size of the population would determine the character of his nation, and the roles of government and the private sector.
The State Department is coordinating the Kuwaitis' dealings with other United States agencies, including the Army Corps of Engineers and Civil Affairs Corps, the Department of Commerce and the Federal Emergency Management Agency.
 
Making 'the Right Contacts'
 "They're getting in touch with contractors who will be able to go back in very quickly after the place is liberated and begin reconstruction activities," a State Department official said. "We're helping them make the right contacts."
The planners are also working closely with Pentagon civil affairs specialists, who do everything from helping restore civil order to providing public services.

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 1; Column 1;
States In Distress: The Seeds of Fiscal Calamity/A special report National Desk

LENGTH: 3264 words

HEADLINE: 80's Legacy: States and Cities in Need

BYLINE: By MICHAEL deCOURCY HINDS with ERIK ECKHOLM

 BODY:
All across America, state and city governments have been stricken by financial crises that were not even imagined a year ago.
More than half are facing serious financial shortfalls, and many are considering Draconian measures, including huge layoffs of public employees and major cutbacks in social services and public works. Some states, like Connecticut and Rhode Island, must find a way to cut their budgets by 20 percent in the next few months. 
 Making up for the cash shortage with tax increases may be political suicide for the public official who suggests it. Memories are all too fresh of the beating incumbent governors and legislators suffered in the November elections as voters registered their anger over rising taxes and reduced services.
 
'Unprecedented Cutback'
 "The fiscal plight seems to be worse than at any time during my memory," said Henry Aaron, director of economic studies at the Brookings Institution, a research organization based in Washington. "I think you would have to go back to the Great Depression to find similar anguish, in terms of the number of states that are facing an unprecedented cutback in service or significant increases in taxes."
The immediate cause of the crisis is the nation's economic downturn, which has drastically cut into tax revenues. But the seeds were sown over the last decade or so, and what public officials are now facing is the collision of several long-term trends, including these:
*Starting in Jimmy Carter's Administration and accelerating under President Ronald Reagan's "New Federalism," the Federal Government has shifted a heavy financial burden to the states and cities, cutting and eliminating Federal grants for housing, education, mass transportation and public works projects. In the late 1970's, Washington provided 25 percent of state and local budgets. It now provides 17 percent.
*The public is increasingly resistant to new or higher taxes, partly because they believe government is not wisely spending the money. In the November elections, voters in two states, Nevada and Oregon, approved initiatives to limit tax increases or roll back taxes. In other states voters showed their displeasure by ousting officials who raised taxes. Altogether 14 governors' offices changed political parties in the upheaval against the old order.
*The pressures to spend are enormous. They range from the public's demand for better schools and harsher punishment of criminals to an increased number of elderly people who need help with nursing home costs. For instance, Medicaid, the health program primarily for the poor and disabled, now accounts for 12 percent of state spending; 20 years ago it was 3 percent.
 
 Growing Responsibilities
 In many cases the pressure to spend comes from the Federal Government itself. The growing body of Federal rules and regulations often place new responsibilities on states and cities without providing sufficient money to carry them out. And the situation is unlikely to improve soon because the budget compromise worked out this fall by Congress and President Bush provides some added money for domestic programs this year but freezes domestic spending for the next two years and restricts its growth for two years after that.
Sometimes the pressure comes from the courts, which have forced states to build new prisons to ease overcrowding or to make costly changes in education programs.
Unlike the Federal Government, which can and does operate at a deficit, all states but one -- Vermont -- are legally bound to have balanced budgets. So, their options are limited to raising taxes or reducing spending.
Experts say many states will be forced to raise taxes in the next year or two. And some predict that the electoral drubbing many governors suffered in November will turn out to have been but a preview of 1992.
 
 'End of Their Political Careers'
 "Our Federal politics are gridlocked, and governors have become the ones who have to have the courage to put their necks out," said Daniel M. Sprague, executive director of the Council of State Governments, an organization of state officials that is based in Lexington, Ky.
"Unfortunately, for a number of governors who raised taxes, that has been the end of their political careers," he added, referring in particular to William A. O'Neill of Connecticut and Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts, who both decided not to run for re-election this year.
Although the converging trends have produced a crisis atmosphere in governors' offices and state legislatures, they spell special disaster for poverty-stricken inner cities, said Richard P. Nathan, director of the Rockefeller Institute of Government at the State University of New York at Albany. Worsening economic prospects, especially in the Northeast, a tapering off of state aid coming after a sharp drop in Federal aid and a "hardening and concentration" of social problems like drugs, crime, poverty, homelessness and AIDS mean "a new crunch time for cities," he said.
 
 The States
Most Ambitious Fall the Hardest

Twenty-seven states are being forced to reduce spending below what they had planned this fiscal year to head off a deficit, according to the National Conference of State Legislatures. They are eliminating programs, freezing hiring, laying off employees, raising college tuitions, increasing fees and fines, using up savings and doing almost anything to avoid raising taxes. The Northeast is hurting most: New York State is laying off 10,000 employees, Massachusetts has already laid off 1,700 and Connecticut, one of the handful of states that do not have an income tax, may impose one, something once considered unthinkable there.
In Rhode Island, Governor-elect Bruce G. Sundlun, a Democrat, predicted "a major downsizing in government." After he takes office in January, he said, he will have to make spending cuts of about 20 percent of the state budget to balance the books by June 30, the end of the fiscal year. New taxes are not an option, he said. Last year, Gov. Edward D. DiPrete, a Republican, added $200 million in taxes, and political analysts say that was a major reason he lost his bid for re-election by a resounding 3-to-1 ratio.
Rhode Island's financial experience is fairly typical of about one-third of the states, many of them in the Northeast, a region that had rapid growth in the 1980's, said Marcia A. Howard, research director of the National Association of State Budget Officers, which is based in Washington. These states were able to cut taxes, start new programs and put aside some money in the decade's strongest economic years, 1984 through 1988. The other two-thirds of the states, Ms. Howard said, saw relatively little or slow growth during the 1980's. Now the economy has pulled the rug out from under both groups of states, and the ones that started the most ambitious social programs are now falling the hardest.
Massachusetts, for instance, set up its own universal health care program two years ago, but now that the financial crisis has hit, Governor-elect William F. Weld, a Republican, has vowed to repeal parts of the program.
Some of today's problems have shallow roots. Uncertainties about how people and companies would react to the Federal Tax Reform of 1986, which eliminated many tax deductions, made it difficult for states to forecast revenue. Several, including New York, California and Massachusetts, were overly optimistic. In addition, economic forecasters did not predict that the Northeast would be on the leading edge of the recession, causing serious budget miscalculations in the region.
The states having the least trouble balancing their budgets this year are primarily those in the West with growing populations and energy-rich states that are benefiting from the rise in oil prices since the Persian Gulf crisis began, as well as slow-growth states like Arkansas and West Virginia that for years have tried to keep a tight rein on expenditures.
Ms. Howard of the National Association of State Budget Officers said that even without a recession, widespread fiscal problems were inevitable, paricularly because of spending on health care and prisons. "These problems are cascading on the states," she said.
Medicaid, the shared Federal-state program covering medical costs of the poor, and, increasingly, nursing home costs of the middle class, has become one of the states' biggest budget headaches. That is because of the aging of the population and the increasing number of middle-class elderly people who have exhausted their savings or transferred their assets to their children and are now dependent on Medicaid for nursing home care.
"Medicaid is becoming the Pac-Man of state government, eating up every dollar," said Ray Scheppach, executive director of the National Governor's Association.
Some experts argue that when the current economic downturn is over, state and local revenue is likely to pick up briskly enough to relieve the pressure. Others doubt it.
"The tax bases are going to grow more slowly in the 1990's than they did in the 1980's," said Steven D. Gold, director of the Center for the Study of the States at the State University of New York at Albany, citing projections of slower economic growth in the years ahead in many regions.
 
 The Cities
Major Burdens, Limited Options

In more than half the nation's cities, spending is outpacing revenue this year, largely because of declining economic conditions, loss of Federal and state aid and state prohibitions on increasing local taxes, the National League of Cities said.
Philadelphia, which has already cut $65 million from social service programs this year, may not be able to pay its 25,000 workers their full pay after Jan. 4 and may soon be insolvent. New York and Washington each plan to lay off thousands of city workers and teachers. Los Angeles and Louisville, Ky., have already closed neighborhood health centers.
And New Orleans dealt with an expected $42 million deficit in 1991, in part by budgeting only enough money to run its criminal justice system and neighborhood health clinics for just six months in the hope that the state will make up the difference. But Louisiana's Governor, Buddy Roemer, said, "We can't spend the money we don't have."
The plight of cities is raising old questions about shared responsibility. "The cities have a disproportionate burden to carry, and nobody wants to talk about it," said Alair A. Townsend, who directed New York City's budget office under former Mayor Edward I. Koch. "New York or Chicago or Philadelphia cannot solve these problems on their own."
One problem for New York, Philadelphia, New Orleans and many other financially troubled cities is a limited taxing authority. New Orleans, for example, collected enough money through its sales and property taxes to deal with social problems when Federal and state governments shouldered 50 percent of the city's budget two decades ago. But now that Federal and state aid has fallen to 15 percent of its budget, the city's taxes do not bring in enough money to cope.
"Pressure from suburbanites is part of the reason we can't have a wage tax or a gas tax, and the legislature just prohibited us from having an inheritance tax," said Mayor Sidney J. Barthelemy of New Orleans.
He went on, "People can't constantly tell us to stand on our own two feet if they keep cutting our legs off."
 
 The U.S. Government
The Shift In Responsibilities

Financial distress is focusing attention on a historic shift over the last decade in Federal relations with the states and cities. Federal grants, which blossomed during the Depression and President Lyndon B. Johnson's "Great Society," peaked in the late 1970's as the Federal deficit began to mount. Under President Reagan, Federal aid declined and more responsibility was transferred to the states.
As Federal grants waned, the share of total state and local spending paid for by Washingon declined from a peak of 25 percent in the late 1970's to 17 percent now. Cities lost much more than the states as programs like revenue sharing and aid for low-income housing were cut. Nor was there as much assistance as the cities and states needed to attack new problems like homelessness and AIDS.
While Federal aid to state and local governments has declined, Federal assistance to individuals has steadily climbed. The individual payments that pass through state budgets are mostly poverty-related money like Medicaid and welfare. The Federal Government pays more than half the cost of Medicaid and Aid to Families with Dependent Children, and the entire cost of food stamps. In addition, the Federal Government directly pays the elderly and others through Social Security and Medicare.
From 1984 to 1988, the strong economy helped state and local governments raise the money to continue programs that had lost Federal support. But now as the economy sours, states are saddled with heavier social responsibilities and few options to pay for them.
"We don't have New Federalism, we have New Feudalism, where every community fends for itself with a hodgepodge of responsibilities and taxing powers," said George Sternlieb, an urban specialist and professor emeritus of public policy at Rutgers University.
Optimists view the financial distress as a chance to cut the fat from government.
"There's no question that the Federal cutbacks are part of the problem. or the opportunity, depending on your perspective," said Richard S. Williamson, who was a special assistant to President Reagan for intergovernmental relations and promoted the idea of transferring more reponsibility to the states.
"There are those of us who feel you get better decision making when both funding and execution are closer to local levels," he said. "If my name were Florio, and I had to increase taxes, I'd probably disagree with that."
He was referring to Gov. Jim Florio of New Jersey. Although Mr. Florio was not up for re-election last month, another New Jersey Democrat, Senator Bill Bradley, was. Mr. Bradley, who was expected to win re-election easily, barely squeaked into office as voters expressed their displeasure with tax increases pushed through the State Legislature by Mr. Florio earlier in the year.
Mr. Florio blames President Reagan for making it difficult for governors and mayors to govern. "Folks have become very skeptical, if not cynical, about government because President Reagan convinced people that you never need to make any tough decisions," he said.
Governor Roemer of Louisiana, also a Democrat, agreed. "I think the question of Federal responsibility needs to be discussed candidly with the American people," he said. "It might be that the government closest to the people works best, and federalism, in shifting to the states more responsibility is the best thing if done carefully. But the price tag, the purpose, the goals, the methodology, all need to be discussed."
Having less money to distribute has not stopped Congress from trying to set local priorities, often necessitating large expenditures. The states say they are being forced to take on responsibilities without the ability to pay for them.
"The Federal-state story is mandates," said Gerald H. Miller, executive director of the National Association of State Budget Officers. "The Federal Register averages 200 pages a day, and much of that is for new mandates and rules," he said. These range from rules on major national goals, like whether poor children should receive welfare, to less weighty matters. One proposed rule, fiercely opposed by governors as setting a new standard for Government meddling, tells states how they can spend interest income from fees they charge for fishing and hunting licenses.
Gov. John D. Ashcroft of Missouri, a Republican who is the National Governor's Association's spokesman on Federal policy, said, "The most pernicious part about it is that when the Federal Government wants to expand a program, the Government funds its share of the program by borrowing and printing money, while the states have to come up with real money by forfeiting other things we'd much rather do."
The major destabilizing force in Missouri's budget, he said, was $143 million needed to finance additional Medicaid costs , which were mandated by Congress. The new rules extend care for more poor children, pregnant women and elderly people.
But as that example shows, Federal mandates can involve goals with which most would agree.
Defenders of the expanded Medicaid coverage like Representative Henry A. Waxman, Democrat of California, an architect of the program, agree that Medicaid is becoming unwieldy and is overburdening the states. But he argues that without broader restructuring of the nation's health system, Medicaid is the only available vehicle to meet crucial goals. "I think the Federal Government has a responsibility to be sure we provide prenatal care for low-income women and to make sure poor children have health care available," he said.
Governors also contend that the Federal Government, while saying the states should take on more responsibility, is hampering their ability to raise revenue. The new Federal taxes on gasoline, alcohol and tobacco are expected to decrease demand slightly for these products and thereby reduce state revenue from existing taxes on those products. In addition, the new Federal taxes make it harder politically for states to tax those items.
 
The Outlook
Who Should Take Which Roles?

More than just balanced budgets are at stake. Alice Rivlin of the Brookings Institution who formerly headed the Congressional Budget Office, argues that states must take the lead in some areas. "Infrastructure must be modernized," she said. "school systems dramatically improved and public services, from child development to adult retraining, made dramatically more effective."
She added, "These are the kinds of programs that work best when well adapted to local conditions and local people."
In turn, she said, the Federal Government should take more responsibility for programs like Medicaid and welfare, which can be fairly addressed only on a national scale.
There is surprising agreement among many liberals and conservatives that the Federal Government, with its unsurpassed ability to raise and redistribute money, should take special responsibility for welfare measures and leave service programs to the states. The main disagreement comes on the levels of services to be offered.
"Intellectually, I have no problem with federalizing programs like Medicaid," said Mr. Williamson, the former Reagan adviser. His fear is practical. "Politically, it's hard for me to see how in the process you won't end up ratcheting the coverage up in a fiscally irresponsible manner."
New roles for the states would mean more taxes. And Mr. Gold, the expert on state governments, is optimistic about the ability of the states to find the needed income, albeit at the cost of spilled political blood.
"If the governors are smart, they will present clearly the budgetary implications of not raising taxes," Mr. Gold said.
"They will be able to convince legislatures that there is no practical way to avoid raising taxes without spending cuts that are unthinkable."
"When the dust settles there will be many big tax increases in 1991," he predicted. "This will lead to a strong backlash in 1992."
"It's going to be real uncomfortable in state capitals," Mr. Gold said.

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 1215 words

HEADLINE: Umpires Give an Early Call: It's Recession

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Millions of Americans believe that the nation recently entered a recession, but like players in a baseball game, they must wait for the umpire, in this case a committee of prominent economists, to make the official call. Now the ruling has come, but weeks ahead of schedule.
Normally the committee, known as the Dating Committee of the National Bureau of Economic Research, issues its ruling only after observing six or more months of hard times. Just as the umpire in a baseball game waits until a pitch has crossed the plate before he calls it a ball or a strike, the committee waits until enough data has accumulated before it declares that a slowdown qualifies as a recession.
 
Uneasy About Silence
But this recession has been so anticipated by the public and so chronicled in the news media that the committee members said they felt nervous and uneasy remaining silent in the midst of the public uproar over hard times. So in a rare, jump-the-gun ruling, the committee declared that a recession probably began in the summer, most likely in August. It was as if the umpire had called a strike before the pitch crossed the plate. 
 "We did not want to be viewed as laggards," said Victor Zarnowitz of the University of Chicago, one of the seven committee members. "If we had waited a few months to say something, when most people are convinced that we are in a recession now, then we might have been laughed at."
Never before has the Dating Committee declared a recession ahead of its normal schedule -- and mindful of tradition, the committee said a final ruling was not yet possible. The same seven committee members, for example, waited until Jan. 8, 1982, to announce that a recession, the worst since World War II, had begun the previous July. Similarly, they waited until July 8, 1983, to declare that the recession ended nearly eight months earlier. In the intervening periods, the Dating Committee said nothing.
In the present case, many other economists, of course, have speculated that a recession started during the summer. But the Dating Committee, and its parent organization, a prestigious private academic group in Cambridge, Mass., operate on another plane that carries far more weight. The committee's seven members, each an expert in a different aspect of economics, are considered by Government officials and fellow economists to be impartial and highly qualified to decide when recessions begin and end.
 
Silencing Optimism
 Even this early ruling makes the recession that everyone sensed was underway more or less official -- and in the process forces Government officials and others to deal differently with the economy. For one thing it is likely to silence the optimism still coming from a handful of economists and politicians who have maintained that the current economic downturn was not severe enough to qualify as a recession.
Until recently, Bush Administration officials had not used the word recession, and the Federal Reserve has not yet explicitly described what is happening today as a recession. Now the Fed might be under more pressure to explain why it did not recognize the onset of a recession last summer and take action then to reverse the decline.
Still, the committee members normally try to function as historians rather than as "chroniclers of real time action," as Robert M. Hall of Stanford University, the committee's chairman, put it. (The committee's final rulings go into the Federal Government's record books, affecting all sorts of future studies of economic activity.)
 
Likely Date of Recession
 The early announcement, made public in a news release distributed in the past week, suggested that the final determination will not be made until January or February at the earliest. "It appears likely that the committee will identify a month between June and September 1990 as the peak of activity in the economy and onset of a recession," the committee said.
Some economists, gathered here for the American Economic Association's annual conference questioned whether the Dating Committee should have issued so early a pronouncement. One concern was that the declaration, from so important a body, might help to discourage consumer spending, business investment or other behavior that gets a nation out of recession.
"These guys may have been thinking about the relation between what they say and what the world does, in which case, discreet silence would have been better," said Robert M. Solow of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, a Nobel Laureate in Economic Science.
 
All Indicators Sag
 During a conference call about a week ago, six of the committee members (the seventh, William Branson of Princeton, was in Bulgaria) agreed that key measures of economic performance all signaled recession, including the latest figures for industrial production, employment, retail and wholesale trade and personal income.
There was disagreement as to when the recession might have started. Geoffrey Moore, director of Columbia University's Center for International Business Cycle Research, favored June. Most others thought the recession probably started in August, when jobs were clearly disappearing.
The most popular definition of a recession is one in which the gross national product, measuring the dollar value of all the goods and service produced in a given period of time, declines for at least two consecutive quarters, or a total of six months. The Dating Committee also prefers that a downturn last at least six months to qualify as a recession, but it frequently dates recessions as starting in the midst of a quarter. And the committee does not consider the Commerce Department's G.N.P. data to be the ultimate criterion of a recession. It bases its judgment on its own analysis of the weekly and monthly statistics that measure economic performance.
 
3d-Quarter G.N.P. Debated
 For example, there was discussion of whether the G.N.P. really rose by 1.4 percent in the third quarter, as the Commerce Department reported this month. The G.N.P. would have risen by only five-tenths of 1 percent if a more accurate inflation adjustment had been used, Mr. Moore said.
The Dating Committee defines a recession as "a recurring period of decline in total output, income, employment and trade, usually lasting from six months to a year, and marked by widespread contractions in many sectors of the economy."
But that definition means the Dating Committee could end up eating the words in its release. As the release itself suggested, the committee cannot know until late January or February whether the downturn had lasted long enough to qualify as a recession.
Robert J. Gordon, a committee member from Northwestern University, believes that the economy has gotten progressively worse in recent weeks. But he said he had opposed the release, in part because of the outside chance that the economy might turn around.
So why was the release issued? During the conference call, the committee, whose two other members are Benjamin Friedman of Harvard and Martin Feldstein, considered how the public might view the committee's silence and also how to handle frequent inquiries from journalists. "It is hard to control the evolution of a conference call," Mr. Gordon said.

HEADLINE: Homelessness in New York: Years of Plans, No Solution

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
A decade after vast numbers of homeless people began to be seen on New York City's streets, officials and advocates fear that homelessness has become embedded in the city's life for the foreseeable future.
Thousands of destitute single adults still roam the streets. Hundreds of families, primarily young single mothers and their children, still pass their days and nights in rundown welfare hotels. The city's huge makeshift system of overnight accommodations -- from large barracks-style shelters to small centers with private rooms -- is now anything but temporary.
 
Dinkins's Effort
 Advocates for the homeless had seen the election of Mayor David N. Dinkins as the last, best hope for transforming the machinery built to cope with homelessness into a mechanism for ending it. The Dinkins administration, which had promised a new level of innovation and commitment to New York's most vulnerable population, is in the midst of devising an ambitious five-year plan for the homeless. 
 But even before she is finished drafting the plan, the mayoral aide responsible for it acknowledges that the recession squeezing the region means that the effort inevitably will fall short.
"The flaw is that we probably won't have the money to do it all," said Nancy Wackstein, director of the Mayor's Office of Homelessness and Single-Room Occupancy Housing. "We know what the goal is. We can move closer, but we will not be able to house everybody."
At the end of his first year in office, Mr. Dinkins has been overtaken by the city's devastating financial troubles. Neither the Federal Government nor the state can be counted on for much help. Rather than expanding the city's array of homeless programs, the Dinkins administration thus far has struggled just to maintain the system it inherited from Mayor Edward I. Koch.
That has frustrated many advocates for the homeless and many ordinary citizens, who have begun to conclude that, without expensive new programs to provide alternatives to shelters, homelessness is likely to remain a part of the city's fabric.
"By and large, Dinkins says kinder things, but Dinkins, like Koch, is not moving toward any solution," said Robert M. Hayes, chairman of the Coalition for the Homeless.
"All the good-hearted people in the Dinkins administration wring their hands, rue the budget -- and aspire to do no less than Koch," said Mr. Hayes, who brought the landmark lawsuits in the early and mid-1980's that forced the city to shelter the homeless.
In 1989, the city spent more than $320 million to shelter a total of about 25,000 single homeless people and 11,000 homeless families.
In a report released last week, the Partnership for the Homeless, a coalition of churches and synagogues, applauded some mayoral initiatives, including a city-state agreement to provide 5,200 new beds for mentally ill people in group homes and single-room-occupancy apartment buildings.
But the Partnership lamented that the Dinkins administration had not come up with a plan to eliminate the use of huge shelters in armories and other cavernous public buildings. It criticized the city's continued reliance on welfare hotels, and it begged the administration not to allow the budget crisis to become an excuse for tolerating the status quo.
"Resolve to come up with some creative ways to do more with less," the report urged.
Ms. Wackstein said she, too, was frustrated that financial constraints made it impossible for the administration to do as much as it would like. But she also said she thought some of the criticism was premature.
 
A 'Catch-All Phrase'
 "That's what I think is so unfair, to think that within one year we were going to dismantle the shelter system," Ms. Wackstein said.
"Homelessness is really the end result of a whole lot of failed systems," she said. "When people talk about eradicating homelessness, we also have to talk about eradicating poverty, drug abuse, mental illness and family problems. Homelessness becomes this catch-all phrase, and behind it is the whole range of social problems, plus the housing shortage.
"I think we in this administration are beginning for the first time to deal with those complexities," she said. "The disappointing part is that I don't know where we will be able to find the money to do these wonderful things."
Ms. Wackstein said the administration had made several significant changes in recent months. It decided for the first time to give priority to the "hidden homeless" -- families living doubled up with family or friends -- for new apartments produced by the city. Only homeless families already in the city's shelter system had previously qualified.
 
Working on Prevention
 The city is also starting a new effort to prevent people from becoming homeless. It will provide lawyers to help those threatened with eviction and sometimes will give financial benefits beyond those in the basic welfare grant to enable poor families to pay their rent.
The most important initiative from the Dinkins administration, Ms. Wackstein said, is the five-year plan that will probably be completed by February.
Ms. Wackstein said she was hopeful that the city could eventually shut down the worst of the large shelters for homeless single adults, replacing them with smaller and better temporary housing centers. She also hopes to provide more social services like drug treatment and job counseling.
But with the city's fiscal woes and the shelter system "bursting at the seams," as Ms. Wackstein described it, the administration may find it difficult to make broad changes.
A tally taken on Thursday night, the night of the snowstorm, showed that 9,950 single men and women were staying in the 25 shelters operated by the city. At the annual peak, which comes in February or March, as many as 11,000 adults are usually in the shelters.
An additional 3,853 families, more than 12,000 people, were in the city's 13 family shelters and in hotels Thursday night.
 
Different Categories
 The two categories of homeless people -- single adults and families -- are strikingly different, as have been the city's efforts for each group.
While many of the single people are capable of working and living on their own, a sizable portion and perhaps a majority are afflicted with emotional or psychological problems, often complicated by drug or alcohol abuse.
Some of the young mothers who become homeless also have deep personal problems, but many others simply lack a place to live. Typically, they become homeless after being evicted from an apartment or after living doubled up with family or friends.
In recent years, new permanent housing and new apartment-style temporary shelters created by the city have provided significant relief for many homeless families. But there has been little new permanent housing produced for single adults.
The new housing for families made it possible for the city to reduce the number of families in welfare hotels from a peak of more than 3,600 three years ago to 147 last July.
But the city was ultimately unable to make good on a promise -- and a court agreement -- to keep families out of the hotels altogether. Last week, about 640 families were in hotels. City officials attribute that primarily to an increase in the number of families coming into the system. But some homeless advocates say the real problem lay in the failure of the Koch and Dinkins administrations to construct enough new apartment-style temporary shelters in time.
 
'Question of Planning'
 "The city tried to stop using the hotels without bringing the new temporary units on line," said Steven Banks, coordinating attorney for the Legal Aid Society's Homeless Family Rights Project. "It's clearly more a question of planning and management than anything else."
Mr. Banks's group is awaiting a decision on a lawsuit it filed that seeks to force the city to stop using the hotels.
The city in recent years has built much more temporary and permanent housing for homeless families than it has for single adults. Some single-room-occupancy hotels have been rebuilt, but most of the single homeless population continues to live on the streets or in large shelters. No one knows how many people are on the streets and in the subway; the advocates' estimates run in the tens of thousands.
Mr. Hayes and other representatives of the homeless acknowledge that the city's budget crisis is real. But they also said that the economic retrenchment makes it more incumbent on the city to find creative new ways to help the homeless.
Mr. Hayes suggested, for example, that the city take advantage of the weakened real-estate market by giving rent subsidies to homeless people and leasing apartments for them.
George McDonald, the president of the Doe Fund, another homeless advocacy group, has long championed the creation of small, new factory-built single-room occupancy hotels, known as S.R.O.'s, for homeless adults.
Last week, the city turned down Mr. McDonald's proposal to build such a center in Brooklyn. City housing officials said their policy was first to renovate existing S.R.O. hotels and then to create new ones from abandoned buildings. Only then, they said, would it make sense to authorize new construction.
"If for no other reason, they should allow this to be done to see if it works," Mr. McDonald said in an interview. "Where is the creativity?"

HEADLINE: Fairhope Journal;
From a Utopian Dream, A Single Tax Remains

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: FAIRHOPE, Ala., Dec. 28

 BODY:
The end of the year is tax time here and it is perhaps fitting that the busiest place in town is the office of the city's largest landowner and taxpayer, the Fairhope Single Tax Corporation.
The corporation is the successor to the 28 people who moved from Iowa in 1894 and established Fairhope as a utopian colony. The community was based largely on the ideas of Henry George, a 19th-century journalist and economist who excoriated land speculation as a great evil of the time and the cause of poverty and inequality in society. 
 George argued that land ought to be held as a public trust by the community rather than in private hands, and that it should be leased for homes, farms, factories and stores.
And that is what is done in Fairhope, with the corporation owning about 4,300 acres, about a third of all the land here. The corporation leases it to about 1,300 homeowners, business owners and farmers.
The tenants pay a single annual tax to cover the costs of public services like water, roads, electricity, schools and parks. The tax is based solely on the value of the land and not on the value of buildings, crops, or businesses.
Perched on a gully-riven bluff called the Ecor Rouge, or Red Cliffs, overlooking Mobile Bay, Fairhope stands as a tribute to George's ideas on urban planning and land use. Because of the corporation's control of the land, the town has open vistas, open beaches and abundant parkland.
In other interesting ways time, progress and prosperity have drowned out the envisioned utopia. Fairhope is clearly no longer the haven of radical political experimenters and artists that it once was. Recent lawsuits filed by residents have attacked the tax-setting practices of the corporation, or the "colony" as it is often called.
Some critics, pointing to steep tax increases on some farmland, suggest that even the corporation has become infected by the greed and land-lust that George despised. It is still a wonderful and beautiful place to live, they said, but Fairhope has lost its sense of mission.
"It was once a place of radical ideas that now votes Republican and gave Ronald Reagan huge margins some years ago," said Paul Gaston, a history professor at the University of Virginia whose grandfather, Ernest B. Gaston, was one of the town's founders. "It was once a place where people came to try to solve social problems. Now it is a place where people go to escape social problems."
The offices of the Single Tax Corporation were abuzz this week with activity as people trooped in and out, and the corporation's secretary, Gale W. Rowe, sortedthrough tax bills.
This time of year, when the corporation finishes collecting and begins to turn over about $470,000 to the various taxing authorities in the area, is the time when the George legacy is most obvious.
With the exception of their Federal income tax, all leaseholders truly pay a single tax, from $500 to $1,000, for a multitude of services.
And even though the corporation does not own all of the town, the single tax concept has influence other purely local tax decisions. "You know we don't have a sales tax in Fairhope partly because the corporation has worked to oppose one," Mr. Rowe said. He noted that the corporation still required those wanting to join to pay the same $100 fee that was required in 1894 and to take a short course instructing them in the principles of Henry George.
This feature along with the annual collection procedure makes Fairhope the "purest" of the Georgist communities around, said Pat Aller, assistant director of the Rober Schalkenbach Foundation, a New York City-based organization that promotes and publicizes George's views. There were never very many laboratories for George's view and today two others -- Arden, Del. and Free Acres, N.J. -- still exist.
"But Georgist practices and ideas are fading or have already faded in those communities," Ms. Aller said.
Founded at a time of unbridled individualism of robber barons and laissez-faire capitalism, Fairhope was one of a number of utopian experiments tried around the country. But its location in the South was unusual.
"There were not a lot of utopian communities in the South since most such communities had as a premise that they had to spring from free men and women," said Steven Suitts, head of the Southern Regional Council, a non-profit educational organization. "That was a very hard premise to find in the South with its agrarian and low-wage economy and its history of slavery."
Mr. Rowe said that from the beginning the colony made a lot of compromises and adaptations in order to survive and it is likely that those adaptations helped doom its success as a laboratory of the single tax idea.
For one thing, the founders decided that they would open up the colony's land so that it could be leased by people who were not members of the corporation. The idea, Mr. Rowe said, was that eventually these people would see the wisdom of George's approach and freely join. But that never happened and today, only 10 percent, or those on 1,360 leaseholds, are members of the corporation. At one time members were given the first opportunity to lease any new property, but today membership has no special privileges.
Mr. Gaston said another compromise was to incorporate the colony as the town of Fairhope in 1908, an attempt to blunt opposition that had grown over the fact that only corporation members and not nonmember leaseholders could vote on colony decisions. The community was being viewed warily by those in the state capital as some sort of "communist community." But in creating the town, the town mixed leased land with deeded property and the mixture of two kinds of land ownership proved a problem over the years.
"The colony and its association dominated things for a long time but eventually the larger community overtook the colony," said Mr. Gaston.
