HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
AS JAN. 15 NEARS, SOME SEE A CHANCE FOR GULF SOLUTION

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BAGHDAD, Iraq, Dec. 29

 BODY:
With 17 days before the United Nations deadline for an Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait, a diplomatic solution to the Persian Gulf crisis remains distinctly possible, Iraqi officials and European diplomats say.
These officials suggest that despite tougher public statements by President Bush and President Saddam Hussein of Iraq in the last week, the deadlock over arranging a final set of high-level meetings in Washington and Baghdad can be broken by a European diplomatic initiative. "This is the only chance for a breakthrough," a longtime Eastern European envoy said. 
 [In an interview with Time magazine, Mr. Bush said his "gut" feeling was that Mr. Hussein would leave Kuwait without a war.]
 
European Dialogue Sought
 A Western European Ambassador here said today that he believed the 12-member European Community would review its decision against holding its own dialogue with Iraq if Mr. Bush and Mr. Hussein could not agree on an exchange of foreign ministers by the end of the year on Monday night.
Iraq asked 10 days ago for a separate dialogue between its Foreign Minister, Tariq Aziz, and European Community officials. Some European officials said they felt that the meeting would enable the community to mediate the dispute over the dates, but the community declined the offer and American officials expressed satisfaction with its decision.
In the disagreement over the timing of the American-Iraqi meetings, Washington has said it wants Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d to see Mr. Hussein in Baghdad no later than Jan. 3. Baghdad has said Mr. Hussein is too busy to meet before Jan. 12, three days before the United Nations deadline.
 
Tug of War Over Meeting
 Washington says it feels that a meeting so close to the deadline would be used by Mr. Hussein to postpone a troop withdrawal beyond Jan. 15. The Baghdad talks were to be preceded by a visit to Washington by Mr. Aziz.
Earlier this week, Mr. Hussein recalled his ambassadors from Western and other capitals, presumably to brief them on gulf policy, and sent them back to their posts without public disclosing their instructions. On Wednesday, the American envoy in Baghdad, Joseph C. Wilson 4th, the deputy chief of mission, said, "I have not given up hope on the diplomatic process."
The United Nations resolution authorizes the use of force against the Iraqis if they remain in Kuwait past Jan. 15..
The Bush Administration has indicated that American forces leading the anti-Iraq military coalition in the gulf may attack at any time after the deadline expires. But the Administration has not definitively stated when, or even if, such an attack will come.
Iraq has insisted on linking withdrawal of its troops to a solution of the Arab-Israeli dispute and the problems of Palestinians in the Israeli-occupied territories. Washington rejects any linkage of the issues.
 
Popular Sentiment Against War
 Here in Baghdad, strong popular sentiment against a new war is adding a new element of pressure for a diplomatic settlement. Tonight, acknowledging public anxiety over war, the Government was denied persistent rumors that it was organizing mass demonstrations for Jan. 10 to plead with Mr. Hussein to pull out of Kuwait as a means to preserve his leadership.
A statement issued by the ruling Revolutionary Command Council said, "This sick thinking exists only in the minds of the planners of evil and their suspect circles."
Whatever optimism there is that the crisis will be resolved at the 11th hour, however, is tempered here by the realities of the enormous task ahead, and concern about misunderstanding or miscalculation by either side.
Japan's Ambassador to Baghdad, Kunio Katakura, said in an interview this week that he felt a growing pessimism about the chances for a diplomatic solution, because "the gap is so great" between the minimum requirements of both sides before they will pull back from their vows to go to war if necessary over Iraq's occupation of its smaller neighbor.
Mr. Katakura, the longest-serving Arabist in Japan's Foreign Ministry, said he was worried that the Iraqis "take very lightly" the Jan. 15 deadline and the prospect of imminent miltiary action afterward.
 
'Dangerous to Take It Lightly'
 After a long meeting this week with Iraq's First Deputy Prime Minister, Taha Yassin Ramadan, the Japanese envoy said: "They are convinced that the Soviet Union and China will not allow the use of force automatically after the 15th. I think it is very dangerous to take it so lightly."
At the outset of the final two weeks of diplomacy, the minimum requirement of the Bush Administration is an unconditional Iraqi withdrawal from all of Kuwait and a restoration of the ruling family of Sheik Jaber al-Ahmed al-Sabah.
Though he denied that he was offering an incentive to Mr. Hussein, Mr. Bush, in an address to the United Nations in September, expressed the hope that a successful resolution of the gulf crisis might create the momentum for new efforts to solve the overall Arab-Israeli dispute.
In what could be construed as a further incentive to Iraq on security arrangements after the crisis, Mr. Baker pledged in a television interview last month that if Iraq withdrew its armed forces from Kuwait, the United States would not attack Iraq.
Mr. Hussein's minimum requirements for withdrawing his forces from Kuwait are far more murky.
On Aug. 12, the Iraqi leader acknowledged for the first time that "arrangements for the case of Kuwait" could be made -- but only if "arrangements shall be made for the immediate and unconditional withdrawal of Israel from the occupied Arab territories in Palestine, Syria and Lebanon; for the withdrawal of Syria from Lebanon, and for the withdrawal between Iraq and Iran."
Shortly afterward, Mr. Hussein pulled his remaining forces out of Iran, but he has continued to insist that any resolution of the gulf crisis must be linked to the Palestinian question and other regional disputes.
In recent weeks, Mr. Hussein and senior Iraqi officials have been increasingly ambiguous on how much progress would have to be made on the Palestinian question before Iraq would seize the fig leaf that many here believe it is seeking to cover its retreat from Kuwait.
An Iraqi familiar with Government thinking said today that the effort here is "to get the rights of the Palestinians to be focused on" and to "get movement that should satisfy the Palestinians that they are involved in the peace process." He added that Iraq is "not worried about specifics at this point."
This Iraqi said Iraq was willing to make "sacrifices," a word used by Mr. Hussein and others in a manner to mean "concessions," but without the negative political connotation that this word carries. "Sacrfice is the term we use," the Iraqi said. "We are not ready for concessions."
"If others are willing to sacrifice, Iraq for its part will be willing to sacrifice when serious and meaningful talks will start," the Iraqi said, adding, "It is premature to speak about sacrifices which Iraq would make."
In any face-to-face meeting, Iraq also would probably press for security guarantees in the region that would enshrine Mr. Baker's pledge that Iraq would not be attacked in the future, and perhaps seek to extend such a pledge to address Israel's future intentions toward Iraq.
 
Iraq Fears Israeli Attack
 Foreign Minister Aziz, in an interview with the Rome newspaper La Repubblica, expressed the concern that "if the United States does not attack us now, sooner or later Israel will." That official theme has infected relations between the United States and Iraq since the end of the Iran-Iraq war in August 1988.
A Western Ambassador said he would expect Mr. Hussein to try to extract a pledge from the Administration to forswear any future attempts to destabilize Iraq economically or politically, or to seek Mr. Hussein's overthrow.
"I think security guarantees are needed for Iraq as a state and the whole region," the knowledgeable Iraqi said. "How can you establish peace in the region at large unless there are security guarantees?"
An inescapable element in any diplomatic settlement will be the role of Mr. Hussein's onetime allies in the Arab world, Saudi Arabia and Egypt in particular, which now are arrayed against him in military alliance with the United States.
King Fahd of Saudi Arabia, though strongly in the American camp, has sent more than one signal to Mr. Hussein that an Arab rapproachment is still possible, but the King has resisted the temptation for dialogue with Mr. Hussein until Iraq leaves Kuwait.
Most recently, in his address to the heads of state of the six-nation Gulf Cooperation Council, King Fahd said he would like Mr. Hussein "to know that we have proved to him, when he chose the path of confrontation, that we are capable of making a decision on confrontation, and that as soon as he decides on concord, we, in turn, will be able to make a decision on concord."
For the moment, the lull of the holiday season and the tactical standoff between Washington and Baghdad have frozen diplomacy.
"Practically there is no diplomacy in the normal sense of the word," an Iraqi official said today. "The two Governments, United States and Iraq, are not talking to each other."
 
BUSH EXPECTS WITHDRAWAL
 President Bush has told Time magazine that his "gut" feeling is that President Hussein will withdraw from Kuwait before war.
In an interview in the current edition of Time, Mr. Bush was asked if there would be a war with Iraq. "My gut says he will get out of there," the President said of the Iraqi leader.
While he acknowledged that "that flies in the face of what some of the Arab leaders tell me, which is that he cannot get out," Mr. Bush said he held a different view.
"He cannot do in Kuwait what he did in Iran," he said. "He cannot do it and survive domestically. I just think that any person who has fought a war, once he understands what he is up against in terms of power, is going to have to find a way to see that he does not fight another one."
Mr. Bush also said he was confident that he had made the right choice in the crisis, even if it meant going to war. "Maybe that is why I am -- I will not say relaxed, but determined," he said. "And I am not churning about it."
"I know what has to be done," he added. "And I know the promise of a new world order if it is done right. I know the devastating effect on the world if it is done wrong. We will do what we have got to do."

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 27; Column 5; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 668 words

HEADLINE: Dental Clinic Is Rebuffed In Bid to Avoid Liability

BYLINE: By RONALD SULLIVAN

 BODY:
A Manhattan appeals court has made it easier for patients of a teaching clinic that provides low-cost dental care to sue the clinic for malpractice.
In a unanimous decision, the Appellate Division of State Supreme Court in Manhattan said that the New York University Dental Center's practice of requiring patients to sign waivers, in which they give up the right to sue for malpractice, was "against public policy" and that the waivers cannot be enforced. Such waivers are generally not used by hospitals, medical clinics and private doctors and dentists. 
 "It is clear that the state's substantial interest in protecting the welfare of all its citizens, irrespective of economic status, extends to insuring that they be provided with health care in a safe and professional manner," the court said in its ruling on Thursday.
 
Earlier Ruling Reversed
 "We find that the exculpatory agreement sought to be enforced between a dental clinic and its patient implicates both the state's interest in the health and welfare of its citizens as well as the special relationship between physician and patient and that it would be against public policy to uphold such an agreement," the court said.
The ruling would directly apply to dental clinics operated in Manhattan by New York and Columbia Universities.
It overturned a 1989 decision by State Supreme Court Justice Ira Gammerman that dismissed a malpractice suit by an 82-year-old patient against the New York University Dental Center, at 345 East 24th Street.
The patient, Arthur Ash, a retired salesman, was referred to the clinic in 1986 by his dentist, Dr. Charles Lennon, so that he could have about $6,000 worth of dental care done for half the cost. In return for the savings, the center required Mr. Ash to sign away any claim of liability.
According to the court, when Mr. Ash was treated by Dr. Lennon and dental students at the center on Oct. 15, 1986, two gold crowns slipped down his throat and lodged in his right lung. They had to be removed surgically.
Justice Gammerman ruled that the center's agreement protected it from a malpractice suit.
 
Services for Poor Patients
 But the appellate court, in a decision written by Justice Betty Weinberg Ellerin, found that "the agreement in this case is in violation of public policy and should not be enforced." The court ordered the suit reinstated.
The court took note of the services that the dental center provided to poor patients, but said that such services "cannot serve as a basis for excusing such providers from complying with those minimum professional standards of care which the state has seen fit to establish."
Poor patients, the court said, "cannot be considered to have freely bargained for a substandard level of care for a financial savings."
If dental centers were absolved of any liability, the court said, it would create an "invidious" system in which "services received by the less affluent are permitted to be governed by lesser minimal standards of care and skill than that received by other segments of society."
 
Malpractice Insurance
 Generally, the courts have upheld agreements in which consumers give up the right to sue for damages, provided certain conditions are met. One is that the consumers have sufficent choices. Another is that there is an "arm's length" distance between the parties involved, as in the case of owners and patrons of parking lots. Courts also insist that the agreements are specific on what they cover.
Howard R. Cohen, the lawyer for the dental center, said the ruling would hurt poor patients by requiring the center to charge more for its services in order to pay for malpractice insurance. He said the center had not decided whether to appeal the ruling or to defend itself against Mr. Ash's suit at trial.
Mr. Ash's lawyer, Steven DiJoseph, said the ruling upheld the right of poor people "who can't afford better treatment to sue when they are denied it because of malpractice or negligence."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%); 

STATE: NEW YORK, USA (94%); 

CITY: NEW YORK, NY, USA (94%);

SECTION: Section 8; Page 1; Column 2; Sports Desk

LENGTH: 2333 words

HEADLINE: 1990/THE YEAR IN SPORTS;
New Names, Troubled Times

BYLINE: By DAVE ANDERSON

 BODY:
As the 1990's began, many of the usual sports names persisted. George Steinbrenner was sentenced to baseball limbo and Pete Rose was sentenced to Federal prison. Lou Piniella guided the Cincinnati Reds to a stunning World Series sweep. The San Francisco 49ers, Detroit Pistons and Edmonton Oilers were again measured for championship rings. Jerry Tarkanian and the college basketball titleholders, the University of Nevada-Las Vegas, argued with National Collegiate Athletic Association investigators. Greg LeMond repeated in cycling's Tour de France. West Germany won soccer's World Cup. The usual number was multimillion, as in salaries; sometimes even a billion, as in television contracts.
But different names surfaced in 1990, different names that stirred new controversies or new scenarios. 
 Howard Spira, a self-described gambler from the Bronx, accepted $40,000 from Steinbrenner for information discrediting Dave Winfield, and because he made the payment the Yankee principal owner was barred by Commissioner Fay Vincent from participating in the day-to-day operation of the franchise, a ruling that Steinbrenner signed but is now debating.
James (Buster) Douglas, never more than a journeyman boxer, swatted the heavyweight crown off Mike Tyson's head with a 10th-round knockout in Tokyo that the promoter Don King loudly protested before being shouted down. But Douglas, too flabby and too docile, was later dethroned by Evander Holyfield in a third-round knockout in Las Vegas, Nev.
Hank Gathers, a basketball all-American at Loyola Marymount, collapsed on the court and died soon after, prompting an examination of his school's concern for his known irregular heartbeat, which required medication.
Hall Thompson, the founder of the Shoal Creek Country Club in Birmingham, Ala., acknowledged a club policy that discriminated against blacks, stirring civil-rights protests before the Professional Golfers Assocation Championship there. That led to the acceptance of an honorary black member and altered the membership policies of several host clubs on the pro golf tours.
Lisa Olson, a sportswriter for The Boston Herald, was sexually harassed by three members of the New England Patriots in their locker room, prompting Commissioner Paul Tagliabue to fine the players and club a total of $72,500. Sam Wyche, the Cincinnati coach, was fined about $30,000 for barring another sportswriter, Denise Tom of USA Today, from the Bengals' locker room.
Pete Sampras, a mannerly 19-year-old tennis player with a blistering serve who was ranked No. 81 only a year ago, established himself as the youngest United States Open king, while Jennifer Capriati, turning pro at age 13, enchanted fans and established herself as a threat to dominate women's tennis in the near future.
And the city of Atlanta, never the answer to an Olympics word-association test in other years, was awarded the 1996 Summer Games despite the challenge of Athens, which had hoped to stage the 100th anniversary of the modern Olympics where they began in 1896.
In baseball, the Reds, who conquered the Pittsburgh Pirates for the National League pennant, used their Nasty Boy bullpen of Randy Myers, Rob Dibble and Norm Charlton to cleanse baseball's oldest franchise, muddied again by Pete Rose's conviction for Federal tax evasion, mostly of income from trading-card shows. Already exiled by baseball for his gambling habits, the former Reds manager was sentenced to five months in an Illinois prison, three months in a treatment center and 1,000 hours of community service in Cincinnati schools.
Even before a 32-day lockout delayed the start of the schedule by a week, Steinbrenner assured Bucky Dent of being the Yankee manager all season. But on June 6, Dent was gone and Stump Merrill was hired. And on July 30 Steinbrenner was gone, soon to be succeeded by Robert Nederlander, the Broadway impresario. As the team skidded into last place, its low point came when Andy Hawkins pitched a no-hitter and lost, 4-0, to the Chicago White Sox in a comedy of outfield misplays. After the season, Dave Righetti joined the San Francisco Giants for $10 million over four years.
The Mets also changed managers, hiring Bud Harrelson on May 29 to succeed Davey Johnson. The switch inspired a quick streak to the top of the National League East, but the Mets soon settled into a second-place stride. As a free agent, Darryl Strawberry departed to join his hometown Los Angeles Dodgers for $20.25 million over five years. But the Mets' front office responded by signing Vince Coleman for $11.95 million over four years and obtaining Hubie Brooks in a trade with the Dodgers.
Until the World Series, the Athletics were baseball's most dominant team, winning the American League West by nine games and sweeping the Boston Red Sox for the pennant. In the playoff finale, Roger Clemens, the Red Sox's ace right-hander, was ejected by the plate umpire, Terry Cooney, for having argued a close pitch with vulgarities. Fined $10,000 and suspended for the first five games next season, Clemens has appealed, charging that Cooney provoked him.
Jose Canseco, the Oakland slugger, signed a five-year, $23.5 million contract, which inspired the baseball boom: 29 players will average at least $3 million a year for the duration of their current contracts. But there was no explanation for a record nine no-hitters, including a record sixth at age 43 for Nolan Ryan, who also notched his 300th victory. For the first time in modern baseball history, two no-hitters occurred on the same date, June 29, when Dave Stewart of the Athletics and Fernando Valenzuela of the Dodgers each pitched his first. Cecil Fielder, returning from Japan, hit 51 homers for the Detroit Tigers.
In pro football, the 49ers routed the Denver Broncos, 55-10, for their second straight Super Bowl title. Entering today's final games of the current season, the 49ers, with a 13-2 record, were the early favorite for what would be a record third consecutive Super Bowl triumph if Joe Montana could avert the bane of quarterbacks on other contenders -- the severe injury.
The 49ers and the Giants each opened with a 10-0 start before losing a week before their showdown, which the 49ers won, 7-3. But the Giants salvaged their second straight Eastern Division title and will enter the playoffs with Jeff Hostetler at quarterback. The Jets, a struggling 5-10 going into today's finale, hired Bruce Coslet from the Bengals' staff to be their head coach.
 Two offensive trends developed: the run-and-shoot as popularized by the Houston Oilers, Detroit Lions and Atlanta Falcons, and the no-huddle as originated by the Bengals and refined by the Bills.
Commissioner Paul Tagliabue, meanwhile, recommended to N.F.L. owners that the 1993 Super Bowl game be moved out of Tempe, Ariz., after Arizona voters rejected Martin Luther King Day as a paid state holiday.
In college football, the mythical national champion will be determined in the New Year's Day bowl games with Colorado (which defeated Missouri, 33-31, on a fifth-down play), Georgia Tech, Miami and Texas each having a chance to wave index fingers as the No. 1 team. Ty Detmer, the Brigham Young quarterback who set 30 N.C.A.A. passing records, was voted the Heisman Trophy.
In college basketball, the Runnin' Rebels from Las Vegas routed Duke, 103-73, for the national title but now, as then, Coach Jerry Tarkanian has been surrounded by N.C.A.A. investigators. After first declaring U.N.L.V. ineligible to defend its championship in 1991, the N.C.A.A. ruled that the Runnin' Rebels would be ineligible for the 1992 tournament instead. Only a few weeks later, U.N.L.V. was confronted by N.C.A.A. sleuths with a new series of allegations of recruiting violations.
Jim Valvano resigned as North Carolina State's basketball coach after disclosures of his shoddy attitude toward his players' academic progress, and also after allegations of point-shaving by Wolfpack players that have yet to be substantiated by law-enforcement officials.
Hank Gathers, who had been diagnosed as having an irregular heartbeat, collapsed during a game on March 4. His death inspired Loyola Marymount's drive to the Western Regional final amid controversy over whether his fatal heart attack could have been averted. In the N.C.A.A. tournament, the Lions routed New Mexico State and the defending champion, Michigan, and edged Alabama before losing to U.N.L.V.
In the National Basketball Association, the Pistons repeated as champions, dominating the Portland Trail Blazers in five games, but the triumph set off a celebratory riot in Detroit that left 8 people dead and 127 injured. Pat Riley, who had four championship rings, resigned as the Lakers' coach; Mike Dunleavy succeeded him. Early in the current season, John MacLeod was hired to replace Stu Jackson as the coach of the Knicks.
For the fifth time in seven years, the Edmonton Oilers won the Stanley Cup as Mark Messier, the runner-up to his ex-linemate Wayne Gretzky of the Los Angeles Kings for the regular-season scoring title, was voted the Hart Trophy as the National Hockey League's most valuable player. The Oilers conquered the Boston Bruins in five games after sweeping the Kings.
Bolstered by the late-season acquisition of Bernie Nicholls and Mike Gartner, the Rangers finished atop the Patrick Division, the first time they had finished first anywhere since 1942. But after eliminating the Islanders in the playoffs, the Rangers lost to the Washington Capitals, who had ousted the New Jersey Devils.
In July, Mario Lemieux, Pittsburgh's superstar center, had surgery for a herniated disk in his back, which plagued him last year and has kept him off the ice this season.
In tennis, the United States finally recaptured the Davis Cup for the first time since 1982, with Andre Agassi and Michael Chang winning the opening singles matches of the final with Australia on clay in the Suncoast Dome in St. Petersburg, Fla. For the first time since the Open era of tennis began in 1968, the men's and women's Grand Slam events were won by eight different players: Sampras and Gabriela Sabatini in the United States Open, Stefan Edberg and Martina Navratilova (a record-breaking ninth All-England singles title for her) at Wimbledon; Andres Gomez and Monica Seles in the French Open; Ivan Lendl and Steffi Graf in the Australian Open.
In golf, Nick Faldo repeated as the Masters champion and also won the British Open, the first to win two majors in the same year since Tom Watson in 1982. At age 45, Hale Irwin emerged as the United States Open's oldest champion in a 19-hole playoff against Mike Donald. After the Shoal Creek controversy, an Australian, Wayne Grady, took the P.G.A. title. Lee Trevino was golf's top money winner with $1,190,518 in dominating the Senior PGA Tour with seven victories, including the Senior Open in a duel with Jack Nicklaus. Beth Daniel earned a record $863,578 while winning seven tournaments, including the L.P.G.A. championship, but Betsy King won the Women's Open.
At the Breeders' Cup at Belmont, the death of three horses, notably the champion filly Go for Wand in front of millions watching on national television, saddened what is known as thoroughbred racing's greatest day. At age 92, Frances Genter finally owned a Kentucky Derby winner, Unbridled, which later finished second to Summer Squall in the Preakness and fourth to Go and Go in the Belmont Stakes. At age 58, after 8,833 victories, Bill Shoemaker rode for the last time on Feb. 3, a fourth-place finish on Patchy Groundfog in the fifth at Santa Anita.
For the first time in 40 years, the United States soccer team qualified for the World Cup finals of Italia '90 but it quickly lost all three of its first-round games. In another early game, the small African nation of Cameroon stunned Argentina, 1-0, but the defending champions recovered to advance to the final before losing to West Germany, 1-0, in Rome, a result typical of the low-scoring games that dulled the anticipation of the World Cup's debut at various United States sites in 1994.
In boxing, Buster Douglas's knockout of Mike Tyson provoked a courtroom power struggle for Douglas's first title defense that the promoter Don King lost to the Las Vegas casino-hotel owner Steve Wynn. But it wasn't worth the legal fees. Douglas was leveled by Evander Holyfield's one-punch knockout. But when Holyfield signed to defend against 42-year-old George Foreman instead of Tyson, squabbles resumed. Julio Cesar Chavez, the super-lightweight champion, extended his record to 73-0, including a streak-saving knockout of Meldrick Taylor with 2 seconds remaining.
In auto racing, Arie Luyendyk, a 36-year-old Dutchman, won the Indianapolis 500 with a record average speed of 185.981 miles per hour, more than 15 m.p.h. faster than the previous high. Dale Earnhardt drove to his fourth stock-car championship.
Randy Barnes set a world shot-put record with a heave of 75 feet 10 1/4 inches but he and Butch Reynolds, the world-record holder at 400 meters, later were suspended for having failed steroids tests. Each is appealing. Ben Johnson was cleared to return to the coming indoor circuit following his two-year suspension for having tested positive for steroids after winning the 100-meter dash at the 1988 Olympics in Seoul, South Korea. Douglas Wakiihuri and Wanda Panfil won the New York City Marathon. And Lynn Jennings, the world crosscountry champion, emerged as the dominant force in distance running.
Television contracts soared to $3.6 billion for N.F.L. games over four years, $1 billion for N.C.A.A. basketball over seven years, $175 million for college football over five years, plus a separate $38 million just for Notre Dame games over five years. All this while the nation's economy was suffering. But as 1991 approached, sports was as persistent in booming as so many of its usual names had been in making headlines along with so many different names.

HEADLINE: Narrow vs. Wide In Tire Safety

 BODY:
To the Sports Editor:
Marshall Schuon provided some sound advice about winter driving ("Getting From Here to There -- Safely," About Cars, Dec. 16), but he perpetuated a common fallacy that wide tires "provide less downweight per square inch" than narrow tires. Actually the weight per square inch of any tire is always equal to the air pressure in the tire. Any tire inflated to 32 pounds per square inch, for example, presses down on the pavement with 32 pounds per square inch. Also, tires of various aspect ratios but with the same inflation pressure and supporting the same weight will have contact patches of different shapes but with the same area.
Narrower tires, better for snow, sink deeper because of their different proportions of length and width of the contact patch's shape. The edges of the patch along the sidewall lead to an almost vertical wall that lets the tire sink into snow; the front and rear edges of the patch at the tread lead to a very gradual slope, following the curvature of the tire, that buoys the tire. A narrow tire's contact patch is long and narrow, maximizing steep and minimizing sloping edges. This minimizes flotation and the tire sinks into the hard packed snow underneath where the tread's lugs can get a bite. In contrast, a low profile tire's contact patch is short and wide, minimizing the steep and maximizing the gently sloping edges. This increases flotation and the tire's tread stays in the loose upper layers of snow.
 
KENNETH ALAN CROSSNER
Somerset, N.J.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: Don't Make Madonna a Stick to Beat Feminism;
Decadence and Profit

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Madonna as an economic enterprise is formidable (Op-Ed, Dec. 14). So was Donald Trump. The 1980's are over, and just as Mr. Trump's Taj Mahal is an inflated fantasy with faltering success and little value to the quality of life of most Americans, so are the for-profit lyrics of Madonna and hits like "Papa Don't Preach, I'm Going to Keep My Baby."
As a society, let's dump our romance with an illusion and decadence that are in service to the profit of a few. The new American poor consist of single mothers and their children. The age of these women declines every year, and 20 percent of all children in this country live in poverty.
This is not just a feminist issue, but one that affects every American. Unavailability of prenatal care, low-birthweight babies, welfare, insufficient health care and a burgeoning prison population, as these children fall through the cracks, is a future that belongs to all of us.
And Madonna tells these kids to keep having babies.
 
SUSAN CALZA
Bloomington, Ind., Dec. 14, 1990

LENGTH: 782 words

HEADLINE: The Region;
The Mob Is Back, And Not Just for Fun

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON

 BODY:
WAS it one of those only-in-New-York coincidences, or what? Three of the city's most-talked-about stories last week seemed so tightly interwoven that the seam between fact and fiction became all but invisible: John Gotti spent Christmas in ratty quarters behind bars instead of natty clothes in front of bars of a different kind. The film "The Godfather Part III" played to, pardon the expression, mobs of movie-goers. And officials declared that the city's privately run school bus companies are controlled by organized crime.
Out-of-towners have long been puzzled that mob figures in New York have about the same name recognition as, say, elected officials. With organized crime so much in the headlines, perhaps it is no surprise that a favorite dinner-party joke is that the economy is so bad that the Mafia had to lay off three judges. And even a part of the labor force not usually mentioned in the same breath as garbage-haulers or cement-truck drivers -- authors -- admit to mob influence. The mob was not mentioned in "The Encyclopedia of Bad Taste" by Jane and Michael Stern because, Mrs. Stern says, "Michael and I don't want to wind up at the bottom of the Hudson River wearing cement shoes." 
 But this mob business is not so funny, nor so romantic. There was a shootout last week at a Long Island theater showing "The Godfather Part III," but no one was sure it was real until the lights came up. One 15-year-old was killed, and four other people in the theater were wounded, including one who did not come forward for at least 48 hours.
 Law-enforcement officials have long maintained that New Yorkers pay for the pervasiveness of organized crime. The costs of basics like food, clothing and shelter are inflated, prosecutors say, because of sweetheart deals with trucking and construction unions. One executive who works with nonprofit housing projects says organized crime constitutes a "hidden infrastructure" that means only one thing: higher costs. "I once said 30 percent to a former high official in housing and he said more, at least 40," said the executive, who refused to be quoted by name. "Nobody knows. Of course, new construction is dead for the time being, so maybe they'll lessen their greed."
Contractors pad the bottom line with no-show workers; the authorities claim that school-bus companies use "ghost buses" and "phantom riders." Officials say the cheating is possible because they lack inspectors to check on how many buses are dispatched or how many students they haul.
Still, no one has claimed that the buses are unsafe, and at least one parent boasts that his child's bus is never more than 30 seconds late. The driver, this parent says, has a special incentive for on-time performance.
One school-bus company executive told prosecutors that he got together from time to time with top Gambino crime family members "for coffee." Among them, he said, were Gene and Peter Gotti, brothers of the man the F.B.I. says is the nation's most powerful mobster, the chief executive of a half-billion-dollar empire of loan-sharking, extortion, hijacking, robbery and murder.
 
Love-Hate Relationship
 John Gotti has become an object of unparalleled fascination: he is at once decried and glorified. The 50-year-old self-described businessman is, law-enforcement officials say, a rough-spoken descendant of a notorious line that includes Lucky Luciano, Albert Anastasia, Carlo Gambino and Paul Castellano. On the basis of sound bites recorded on his way to court, he is characterized as the kind of guy who orders executions the way other New Yorkers order coffee.
Now prosecutors are accusing him of carrying out the execution of Mr. Castellano and "The Godfather Part III" is lampooning his Hollywood-grade flamboyance. Is this art imitating life, or the other way around?
"Gotti has the image he has," said David Feldman, an author and social historian, "because people believe he does what he's accused of doing, and there doesn't seem to be much revulsion about it. That's scary because we're countenancing it. We get so upset by the murder of the week or Humphrey the Whale or whatever the lead is on the 5 o'clock news, and yet we are able to enjoy the Gotti sideshow."
Some New Yorkers say the year-end preoccupation with the mob -- in real life and at the movies -- is a diversion from the city's fizzling economy and a mayor less charismatic than his predecessor. Whatever the reason, Tony Hiss, the author of "The Experience of Place," said: "There are two things going on: the media event and the underlying reality of what it means to life in New York, and I don't think the difference is very well understood."

HEADLINE: The World;
More Than Ever, Personality Drives World Events

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
PERHAPS, as Josip Broz Tito once said, "an intelligent man cannot accept the theory that personalities create history." Or perhaps Harry S. Truman was right when he said that "men make history and not the other way around."
Historians have argued endlessly about whether great individuals or great forces shape history, and future generations are sure to continue the fight. 
 For at the beginning of the 1990's, at a pause in history on the verge of what promises to be a period of fundamental change, a case can be made that the world is truly feeling the power of individual men and women.
Some people simply force their way onto the world's stage; Saddam Hussein, not the head of a nuclear or economic superpower, nevertheless altered the course of post-cold war history when he brought the world to the brink of war.
There are those whose strength is their voice, even at a time when eloquence has been supplanted by sound bites: Vaclav Havel, the playwright turned President in Czechoslovakia; Nelson Mandela, who emerged from a South African prison to the adulation of a world hungry for heroes; Margaret Thatcher, more powerful in defeat than many politicians are in victory.
It can be argued that these people's voices have helped guide history; it seems indisputable that they at least colored the way much of the world saw the events in which they were involved.
It may even be that the history of how the post-cold-war era began will prove less that of personal vision than of personality:
* Helmut Kohl, who by his own admission lacks ideology, demonstrated a skill at wardroom politics that may have been the perfect formula to guide German unification.
* Mikhail S. Gorbachev, whose vision for the Soviet Union still seems obscure, held his country together seemingly through sheer force of will.
* And George Bush bounded through his second year in office with a helter-skelter syntax that was the antithesis of Presidential oratory. Continuing to defy "the vision thing," he produced a Persian Gulf strategy based largely on the fact that he is friends with most world leaders and has the home numbers of the rest in his Rolodex.
Mr. Hussein thrust himself into the world's consciousness with a blinding display of violence that seemed driven more than anything else by the particular nature of his character: his wounded pride at Kuwait's refusal to come to terms on his financial demands; his ambition to assume the pan-Arab mantle that had eluded so many other Middle Eastern leaders, and then his thirst for a one-on-one showdown with Mr. Bush.
On a larger stage, Mr. Gorbachev has come as close as anyone to refuting Tito's view of history, although some, including President Bush and Ronald Reagan before him, argue that it was Western pressure and military strength that forced the collapse of Communism.
Mr. Gorbachev has made the creation of a new Soviet federation a test of his personal leadership and, in the view of his critics, is on the road to accumulating as much, if not more, power than even Stalin had.
For the Bush Administration, Mr. Gorbachev virtually embodies the Soviet Union; when Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze resigned, Washington was shocked by the loss of the only other central link in the East-West chain.
As for Mr. Bush, his operating style is based almost entirely on the force of personality, expressed in his contacts with other politicians, especially world leaders.
The President has often spoken of the resurgence of the United Nations, of the rule of international law, and of the weight of nations brought to bear against Iraq. But in his view, the alliance against Baghdad is one of individual politicians, not governments. He knows the leaders, calls many by their first names, and talks to them often on the telephone, his favored instrument of diplomacy.
Chancellor Kohl, too, has affected history through skill as a politician rather than vision or ideology. Was Germany's unification inevitable? Perhaps, and no doubt Mr. Kohl was aided by Mr. Gorbachev, by Mr. Bush and by his ability to spend German money. But many in Europe argue that the ideological drives of a Willy Brandt or even of a Hans-Dietrich Genscher could not have done what Mr. Kohl did.
While Mr. Bush bristles at the suggestion that he lacks vision, Mr. Kohl seems serene. This is not to say that Mr. Kohl has no conceptual framework. He does, a typically plain one. Asked what his vision of Germany's future was, Mr. Kohl said: "That we simply don't stick out."
In Eastern Europe, Mr. Havel's public agonizing over moving from revolutionary idealism to political compromise seemed to symbolize the disappointments that produced a bleak morning after to the celebrations of 1989.
On New Year's Day, Mr. Havel told his countrymen to stop lying to themselves about their economy. Then he faced the possibility that his country would fragment. Even when Czechoslovakia celebrated the first anniversary of its revolution, Mr. Havel was somber. "Are we really a nation capable of reviving its virtues only once in 20 years' time?" he asked.
Mr. Mandela, too, faced a grim reality on his release from prison: a country nearly as rent by fighting among blacks as by their struggle against apartheid. He, too, faced political maneuverings within his movement. But he seemed to influence the rest of the world by the power of his vision. He told the United States Congress he had come "as a particle of a people whom we know to be noble and heroic, enduring, multiplying, permanent, rejoicing in the expectation and knowledge that their humanity will be reaffirmed and enlarged by open and unfettered communion with the nations of the world."
Mrs. Thatcher, before leaving 10 Downing Street in November, had made sure her voice was heard in the debate over the decline of Communism, the rise of a common Europe and the crisis in the gulf. Even Mr. Bush drew strength from her after the invasion of Kuwait, about which she was characteristically blunt in her warnings to Saddam Hussein.
In "War and Peace," Tolstoy wrote that a belief that free will shapes events "is the same to history as the recognition of a free force moving the heavenly bodies would be for astronomy."
But some historians watch the emergence of global personalities with bemusement, like Gabor S. Boritt, a professor at Gettysburg College, who in times like these delights in conducting guided tours of the battlefield near his office.
"I love to go to specific spots in the field and say, 'This one man did that one thing that made history in that one square mile,' " Mr. Boritt said. "It may be the subject of argument on a grander scale, but the power of the individual is self-evident out there in the battlefield."

HEADLINE: Headliners;
Sweet Charity

 BODY:
In the last year it has been widely reported that measured in dollars, Japan has become the No. 1 donor of foreign assistance. But in some ways its status can be attributed to the high value of the yen and cutbacks by the budget-squeezed United States Government. Last week, some Japanese officials said they hoped that the selection of a Japanese woman, Sadako Ogata, as United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees would show that Japan is serious about extending its world leadership beyond the domain of business. "I think it would be pretty good if Japan, in becoming an economic power, becomes a humanitarian power as well," said Ms. Ogata, dean of foreign studies at Sophia University in Tokyo. Ms. Ogata, who spent five years in the 1930's in San Francisco and Portland, later earning a doctorate in political science from the University of California at Berkeley, is accustomed to a cosmopolitan perspective. Her husband, Shijuro Ogata, an economist, has been mentioned for a possible role as director of the International Monetary Fund.

HEADLINE: The Region;
The Poor Mainly Recycle Poverty

BYLINE: By ALLAN R. GOLD

 BODY:
ONE of the biggest disappointments for New York City's fledgling recycling program has been the low participation by residents in poorer, densely populated neighborhoods. In areas like Harlem or Bedford-Stuyvesant, already overwhelmed by life and death issues, recycling seems trivial.
"They're not talking about landfills," said Eddie O'Jay, a Harlem resident on Community Board 10's sanitation committee. 
 As city officials look for ways to cut recycling costs while improving compliance, some experts suggest that expensive curbside pickup of recyclable material in low-income neighborhoods should be replaced with programs that offer residents economic incentives to recycle. The idea is simple: People are more likely to recycle if they are paid to do so.
 "Everyone recognizes it is much tougher in a poorer neighborhood than an affluent one," said the city's Sanitation Commissioner, Steven M. Polan. "It's obvious."
The concept of providing an economic motivation to recycle is receiving strong interest. The city's 21-month-old recycling law calls for establishing in each borough at least one center to which residents can take recyclable material like newspapers in exchange for cash. Such businesses, called buyback centers, are also intended to create jobs for depressed communities.
 
The High-Rise Problem
 The nation does not have much experience with high-density urban recycling, and as a result, New York City is generally viewed as a pioneer. For example, there are many successful suburban recycling programs, but few places have tried recycling on a large-scale in neighborhoods dominated by high-rise apartments. Buyback centers have been around for years, but in no great numbers in cities. Even Newark, which boasts a recycling rate exceeding 50 percent, has poor participation in low-income, high-density areas, a study by that city has found.
"We don't know of a single city that approximates New York in complexity in waste handling," said Nancy A. Wolf, executive director of the Environmental Action Coalition, a local nonprofit group that organizes recycling programs.
That complexity is translating into big problems for New York City's mandatory recycling program. Although tonnage goals were met in the first year, collection costs have been much higher than anticipated. Without productivity gains, the Sanitation Department expects the program, once it is fully in effect, to cost more than four times what had been projected.
Under the best financial circumstances, the city would be hard-pressed to afford that. Now, when budget paring is the priority, the Sanitation Department must make do with less. Continued phase-in of the recycling program has already been set back at least until July. Currently, 29 of 59 community boards, or 2 million households, have curbside recycling for at least some materials.
If New York City is to reach its goal of 25-percent recycling by April 1994, Sanitation Department officials must figure out how to increase participation in low-income neighborhoods. In recent testimony before the City Council, department officials said residents of Staten Island, where single-family homes predominate, were recycling 18 percent of their garbage -- the best in the city -- while residents of northern Manhattan, including Harlem, were at 5 percent. As a result, the cost per ton of collected materials is much higher in neighborhoods with low compliance than in neighborhoods that generate a lot of recyclables.
Reasons for the poor compliance range from the basic social and economic problems of the neighborhood to uncooperative building management to insufficient education by the Sanitation Department.
Mr. O'Jay, the Harlem community board member, also noted that some residents have started to view recycling as a city revenue-raising effort because fines are now being assessed to violators. Others doubt recycling is actually occurring when they see separated materials tossed into regular garbage trucks instead of specially marked trucks, he said.
Perhaps most important, "the outreach program has been a dismal failure," Mr. O'Jay said. "You can't just expect people to understand a problem by handing out literature."
Mr. Polan, the Sanitation Commissioner, acknowledged that educational efforts have been lacking. The delay in expanding the program in the next six months will enable department workers to return to Harlem to promote the recycling program, he said.
It would be premature to discontinue curbside recycling in Harlem, Mr. Polan said. "I don't think we've given it a fair test in any neighborhood, low-income included." He also said the Sanitation Department wants to establish more buyback centers, but has found strong community opposition to locating them near residential areas, where neighbors are concerned about additional truck traffic, noise and foul smells.
Supporters of buyback centers take a different view. David M. Muchnick, chief executive officer of a successful center in the South Bronx, said that in low-income neighborhoods the buyback approach is "cheaper and more cost-effective" than curbside pickup programs.
"Places like ours can be immensely effective in generating a lot of tonnage," he said.
Mr. Muchnick runs Recoverable Resources/Borough Bronx 2000 Inc., a for-profit enterprise started eight years ago as a subsidiary of the nonprofit South Bronx 2000 Local Development Corporation. Since April 1982, the center has sold about $5 million in paper, plastic, glass, metal and wood to recyclers while paying $800,000 to people who bring in such materials. R2B2, as it is called, is a partner in a joint venture to open a Brooklyn buyback center next month.
"We know that poor people will participate in recycling efforts," Mr. Muchnick said, but "one should maximize the cash opportunities."

GRAPHIC: Photo: A worker processing empty glass bottles at the R2B2 Recycling Plant in the Bronx last week. (Keith Meyers/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: The T-Shirt Industry Sweats It Out

BYLINE: By ISADORE BARMASH

 BODY:
Their appeal transcends income, education and taste. While other fashions have come and gone, the lowly T-shirt keeps selling. But even this garment's sales may be vulnerable to a recession. Indeed, a surge in domestic capacity and the flow of imports have made T-shirt marketing a sweaty contact sport. Analysts are expecting cutbacks in domestic T-shirt output to continue in 1991.
"The T-shirt market this year is soft due to too much capacity and the erosion in general economic conditions," said Robert Blanchard, president of the National Knitwear Manufacturers Association, of Morristown, N.J. "Retailers knew that they can always get the supply they need and are controlling their inventories." 
 Because T-shirts are relatively cheap and readily available in a wide range of styles and designs, they appeal to many different people. For example, wearing a T-shirt is a way to assert one's identity or or back up a boast, whether it be about surviving the New York Marathon, visiting Dolly Parton's Dollywood in Tennessee or proclaiming an allegiance to Calvin Klein. "Over the last five years, demand for T-shirts has been very strong," said Deborah Bronston, apparel analyst for Prudential-Bache Securities Inc., in New York City.
Meeting the demand can yield stunning profit margins. Start with a simple white cotton or cotton-polyester shirt, dye it in the factory or run it through a screen or thermal printing machine. With little added expense, a basic $3-wholesale shirt can emerge as a garment that sells for several times more.
This year, about a billion T-shirts have been made for the United States market, or four for every back. Domestic mills produced about 609 million outerwear-style T- shirts and produced another 300 million meant to be worn as underwear. About 108 million shirts have been imported.
T-shirts are the only clothing sold not just in stores, but also on the street, in gasoline stations, bowling alleys, movie theaters and zoos. A single shirt can fetch as little as $5 or as much as $150 when sold in boutiques. Even better, in Manhattan's Times Square, three shirts can sell for as low as $10.
Large makers like Fruit of the Loom Inc. and the Hanes division of Sara Lee Corporation, together account for more than 50 percent of the domestic outerwear T-shirt market, with Fruit of the Loom having a somewhat higher share than Hanes. But retailers buy only about 15 percent of their shirts directly from the manufacturers. The rest filter through wholesalers who either print T-shirts themselves or sell to independent, entrepreneurial style printers.
Some executives and analysts forecast continuing strong sales. Millions of young and older Americans constantly add to their T-shirt wardrobes -- attesting to the enduring American passion for the casual. "T-shirts and jeans naturally go together, since each seems to be just right for the other," observed Richard Ruster, the president of the Tee Corporation of America, a producer and importer in New York City. "They're the leisure wear for the 1990's."
Few fashions are so adaptable. What began as outerwear for the fitness craze has become an all-purpose garment for the home and many work places. Indeed, the greatest growth in T-shirt sales has been in outerwear. Jack Hershlag, executive director of the National Association of Men's Sportswear Buyers, speaks of "the two worlds of T-shirts."
One, he said, is the world of fashion T-shirts, proprietary creations marketed by companies or designers, usually as part of coordinated wardrobes. The other includes the blank shirts that are printed with cartoon characters, team names, product logos, special events, political slogans and jokes. "Bart Simpson is probably outselling any other cartoon character," Mr. Hershlag said. "It's a case of the product creating its own market, and the more bright ideas there are, the bigger the market will be."
Some analysts say that fashion and novelty will continue to fill T-shirts and T-shirt demand. "Even though some see an oversupply, I see a continuing, solid trend because of the tie-ins with popular movies or television shows," said Arthur Britten, a New York retailing and apparel consultant. "The 'Dick Tracy' and now 'The Simpsons' shirts have had sales in the millions."
As demand grew in the late 1980's, factories expanded more than 10 percent a year but still ran at capacity. Domestic outerwear production boomed from 382 million shirts in 1986 to 648 million in 1989. Output fell closer to 600 million this year as the manufacturers, fearing oversupply, cut back.
Importers have had to retreat as well. The biggest source of imported T-shirts is Pakistan, followed by China, Hong Kong and the Philippines. While the foreign flow increased from 14.9 percent of the outerwear market in 1986 to 18.2 percent in 1989, it has slowed this year, dropping an estimated 6 to 8 percent through June, Mr. Blanchard said.
Domestic T-shirt makers have depended on automation to fend off the imports that have inundated many other types of apparel companies. T-shirts are made from knit jersey, either an all-cotton or polyester blend, by high-speed knitting machines that, in a plant with 500 employees, turn out 17 million shirts a year. But lower fiber costs help keep the price of imported shirts a dollar or two below the cost of domestic products.
Some American companies are trying to compete by shifting their production overseas. Mr. Ruster said that imports are more likely to thrive in 1991 than domestic production. His three-year-old T-shirt company produces all its garments from abroad. Foreign makers often pay more for import rights -- which, under national quotas, can be bought and traded -- than for actual shirt production. In Mr. Ruster's view, "That shows the demand still exists for the import."
For manufacturers like Mr. Ruster, T-shirts will never go out of style. "It's still a very hot business," he said. "The T-shirt business is at its peak and everyone still wants to be in it."
 
 
 
DESIGNER T-SHIRTS' TRUE WORTH
 Nobody would price a Claude Monet painting by totaling the value of its canvas, pigment, frame and labor, as well as the retail markup. Likewise, the Monets of the fashion world insist that standard accounting fails to capture their creations' true worth. Far from treating T-shirts as designer doodlings, clever retailers promote them as essential elements in wardrobes.
Earlier this year, at the Claude Montana boutique in Beverly Hills, Calif., $150 bought a white T-shirt with a big M on the front. However, the French designer will no longer produce these shirts, because, as the boutique manager said, "We do not renew last season's styles."
Those in the business stress that the finished masterpiece justifies a shirt's high price. "The designer who decides to do a T-shirt will design it totally, from the fabric, construction, shape and trim," said Jack Hershlag, of the National Association of Men's Sportswear Buyers. "Basic T-shirts are mass-produced and chopped out in standard shapes. But the designer might want his creation longer, shorter, fuller or deeper, or with a higher neck."
Not that this effort is always evident. "You are paying for the designer's name, which is usually on it, and for the pleasure of the reaction you get," Mr. Hershlag said. "It's a matter of fashion appeal and probably snob appeal. Is it better made? Yes, but probably not much more than the best-made, moderate-priced T-shirt."
Mr. Hershlag said that consumers will never tire of letting designers advertise on their chests. "Not one bit," he said. "People want that name on their jeans or their T-shirts. Manufacturers are falling all over themselves to get the permission of designers to use their names on the products they make."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Richard Ruster, a T-shirt maker. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Joseph Long, 78, Philanthropist And Founder of Drugstore Chain

BYLINE: By MARVINE HOWE

 BODY:
Joseph M. Long, the chairman and co-founder of Longs Drug Stores and a prominent philanthropist, died Thursday at the John Muir Medical Center in Walnut Creek, Calif. He was 78 years old and lived in nearby Danville.
He died of a brain hemorrhage, a company spokesman said. 
 With his brother Thomas, Mr. Long founded the retail drug company in Oakland, Calif., in 1938. The Longs chain, now based in Walnut Creek, employs 14,000 people in 257 retail drug stores in six states and did $2 billion in business last year.
Several years ago, Mr. Long's son Robert was named president and chief executive officer of the company but the father continued to exercise oversight. Thomas Long is retired but continues as a member of the board.
Joseph Long was also a former director of Safeway Stores, the Western Department Stores, Cutter Laboratories and served on the boards of several other companies.
 
Education and Environment
 An advocate of educational and environmental causes, Mr. Long donated millions of dollars to the University of California and wildlife institutions. Among his contributions was $3.2 million to the University of California at San Francisco Medical Center.
He also established the Foundation for Excellence at the School of Pharmacy at the University of California at San Francisco and set up a marine laboratory at the University of California at Santa Cruz.
Mr. Long served as chairman and co-founder of the California State Parks Foundation and was co-founder and president of the California Waterfowl Association.
A native of Covelo, Calif., he graduated in 1933 from the University of California at Berkeley and stayed on for three years of graduate studies in economics and public administration.
He is survived by two sons: Robert M. of Lafayette, Calif., and Kirk of Spain; two daughters, Nancy Fink of Berkeley and Marion Souyoultzis of Piedmont, Calif.; two brothers, Thomas J. of Alamo and Ralph of Millbrae, Calif.; two sisters, Sarah Sweeney of El Sobrante, Calif., and Leta Lowry of Walnut Creek, and nine grandchildren.

HEADLINE: Cuomo Facing His Toughest Year: Making Up Deficit and His Mind

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Dec. 29

 BODY:
The Governor's inauguration is traditionally the high point of New York's election cycle, an occasion that has all the pomp a state government can muster: commemorative key chains, Indian chiefs in full regalia, geysers of punch served up in little plastic cups.
This year, however, there will be no pomp, no key chains, no chiefs. In a gesture of austerity, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has canceled the inauguration and will take his oath of office in a modest ceremony on Monday. 
 The spartan swearing-in ceremony is a fitting prelude to the demanding year that lies ahead for the Governor. Somewhat diminished at home by a disappointing re-election but still a prominent national figure, Mr. Cuomo is entering what is likely to be the most arduous year of his tenure, a year of grave austerity and government hardship.
When he presents his budget in January, the Governor will have to close a budget gap that by many estimates has already grown to almost $4 billion. That is more than 10 percent of the general tax revenues the state expects to collect in the fiscal year beginning April 1. Not one to minimize a challenge, Mr. Cuomo said recently, "In terms of the numbers, this is the toughest since the 30's."
 
Decision Time on '92
 At the same time, Mr. Cuomo will have to decide, perhaps for good, whether to run for President. Most political analysts, including those closest to the Governor, say that in order to run a credible campaign in 1992 he must make his plans known by September.
"You ought to know this summer whether he's running," said Robert S. Strauss, a former chairman of the Democratic National Committee. "Beyond that it gets pretty late, particularly for someone who's never been around the track before."
The awkward confluence of decisions facing the Governor in 1991 -- political and fiscal, presidential and parochial -- makes the coming months peculiarly treacherous for him. A misstep in Albany, which the Governor says is facing its most serious budget problems since the Depression, is likely to have harmful effects in Washington. Conversely, a false move on the national front could weaken him at home.
As one associate said, "The next three or four months could kill him."
Mr. Cuomo's situation both as Governor and as a potential presidential candidate is further complicated by the deepening fiscal worries facing New York City.
As a practical matter, the state government is inevitably drawn into any crisis facing the city; the city relies on the state for a significant part of its budget, and the state relies on the city's tax base for a substantial part of its revenues.
 
'Can He Manage a Tailspin?'
 That Mr. Cuomo cannot avoid being tugged into the city's troubles was amply demonstrated last Wednesday, when the Governor rebuked Mayor David N. Dinkins, saying the Mayor was relying on state aid that simply did not exist. The rebuke led to a chorus of protest from those who said that the Governor, instead of criticizing the Mayor, should solve the problem.
"New York City becomes the test of Governor Cuomo's vision," said the Rev. Jesse Jackson, who called reporters from his Washington headquarters to register his criticism. "It is a reflection of his priorities."
With New York State and New York City dragging each other closer to insolvency, the task facing the Governor is nothing less than to arrest the slide.
"The issue is, Can he manage a tailspin?" said the Assembly Speaker, Mel Miller, a Brooklyn Democrat. "If he does it well, then it will be phenomenal."
When it comes to the Presidency, Mr. Cuomo's fondness for secrecy is legendary. Those who are closest to him insist that the Presidency is an issue he does not even discuss privately. Indeed, they say, whether he will run in 1992 is something that the Governor himself does not yet know.
 
Key to a Presidential Run
 Still, there appears to be a consensus among Mr. Cuomo's advisers that if he resolves the state's budget problems this spring -- or at least is perceived as having done so -- he could be a formidable candidate if he chooses. But if he does not appear to master the budget, he has effectively disqualified himself from a national campaign. Since the outcome of state budget negotiations will not be known until April or May, the Governor, his advisers say, will probably wait until the summer to make his plans known.
"If you wanted to speculate that the Governor would wait until after the budget before saying anything, you'd probably be right," one of his closest advisers said. "Mario is very good at not saying anything. And since you don't have to say anything, why do it?"
But even as the budget unfolds and Mr. Cuomo ducks the inevitable questions about 1992, few expect him to give up the long flirtation with the Presidency that he began six years ago.
In June, when Mr. Cuomo declared his candidacy for governor, he repeatedly evaded questions about why he was not declaring, as he had in 1986, that by running for Governor he was indicating that he would not run for President.
Since then, Mr. Cuomo has discussed national political issues at least as much as Albany's. Even when he was presenting $1 billion worth of midyear budget cuts last month, he made the event a forum on Washington, saying: "It's obvious that the problem is national and will need national remedies."
 
Taking Objections to Washington
 Three weeks ago, Mr. Cuomo took his criticism of the Federal Government directly to Washington, speaking to the National Press Club on themes that made him the toast of Democratic dinner parties: the failures of Reaganomics, the nation's faltering economy, President Bush's preoccupation with foreign policy.
"There is a long-term national economic crisis as dangerous to this country as Saddam Hussein," Mr. Cuomo told the Washington press. "But so far there is little evidence that Washington is prepared to deal with it."
Aides denied that the Governor's speech was intended to reinvigorate speculation about his national ambitions. Yet they complained when the speech did not receive the kind of display they said it deserved. And the address, which sounded curiously like the blueprint for a Cuomo candidacy, quickly became the talk of national political circles.
"I think he reminded a lot of people why they would like to see him run," said Harrison Hickman, a Democratic poll-taker. "He seemed big enough to fit in the presidential chair without a couple of phone books under him."
Mr. Cuomo has always been well served by his status as a potential presidential candidate, and, his advisers note, there is no reason for him to change this status as he enters his administration's most challenging year.
 
'I'm Not Good at Being Coy'
 "Why would you do it?" one adviser said. "Who knows what it would do?"
As a potential candidate, the Governor has always enjoyed a national audience that has amplified his image in New York. But Mr. Cuomo has also been able to avoid the kind of rigorous questioning on national and international affairs that would follow a declaration of intent to run.
"I don't think the Governor has to say anything," Mr. Miller, the Assembly Speaker, said. "He gets all the attention anyway."
After the Governor's agonizingly coquettish dance with the 1988 presidential race, he told reporters that he had rued the whole episode. "Did I have fun with the media?" he said. "Yes. When it was all over, did I regret it? Yes. I'm not good at being coy."
When asked recently on national television whether he planned to run, Mr. Cuomo answered: "What's more important than who runs is, am I right about the economy? Am I right about education?" If he is not good at being coy, he is still practicing.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 35; Column 3; Arts & Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 1196 words

HEADLINE: TELEVISION/1990;
As Documentaries Soared, Newscasters Went on Ego Trips

BYLINE: By WALTER GOODMAN

 BODY:
Thanks to Ken Burns and company, choosing the year's best documentary presents no headaches. "The Civil War" made a powerful narrative out of the evocative photographs and moving words of the men and women, famous and obscure, who played a part in the nation's epic conflict. In addition to serving as an absorbing course in American history and a tribute to those who bore the pain and achieved the glory, these 11 hours, which attracted a record number of viewers for a PBS limited series, are a landmark of American television.
 Although "The Civil War" was special, public broadcasting came through with many other first-rate documentaries. Several, most notably the eight-part series "Eyes on the Prize II," focused on black Americans. This strong sequel by Henry Hampton to his memorable 1987 series, relied on newsreels to carry forward the eventful history from the ascendance of Malcolm X in the mid-1960's up to 1980, when race relations seemed less explosive yet still unsettled and unsettling. 
Two other solid documentaries looked at race relations from a fresh perspective. "Politics: The New Black Power" was a sophisticated account by Juan Williams, a Washington Post reporter, of the phenomenon of the "crossover politician" -- black officials, like Gov. L. Douglas Wilder of Virginia, Mayor Kurt Schmoke of Baltimore and Ron Brown, the chairman of the Democratic National Committee, who are making it in the political mainstream. Mr. Williams suggested that whatever hope there is for greater black participation in American politics can be found in the careers of these successful politicians rather than in those of more sectarian figures.
 Another unorthodox approach to black-white relations came from Shelby Steele, a professor of English at San Jose State University, who reflected on issues growing out of the killing in Brooklyn of 16-year-old Yusuf K. Hawkins by a gang of young white men. In "Seven Days in Bensonhurst," Mr. Steele concluded that racial provocateurs like the Rev. Al Sharpton, who exploit such events, may be obscuring changes in white attitudes and misdirecting energies needed by blacks for economic development.
 In quite a different spirit, " 'Amazing Grace' With Bill Moyers" found the busy journalist on a search for the origins and the appeal of the hymn, written by a reformed 18th-century English slave-trader, that has found so abiding a place in America's gospel tradition, white as well as black.
 The public stations also had their share of less impressive documentaries. The year's superdud was "Race to Save the Planet," 10 hours' worth of worthiness about the efforts of nations around the globe to save mankind from self-destruction. For all the programs' good intentions, much of the material seemed recycled, and the overall effect may have been to send people rushing out of their living rooms to chop down trees in preference to grinding their teeth.
 Over on the commercial stations, the most stirring news specials were inspired by the freeing of Nelson Mandela after many years in South Africa's prisons. All the networks were there, and it was emotion-rich television, with a world-class hero at its center. It cannot be said that the networks' analysis of the ramifications of Mr. Mandela's release or their treatment of South African politics in the months since have matched those early hours. But television news is generally better at capturing the moment than explicating its significance.
 For viewers with the time, C-Span's unabridged attention to the Senate Ethics Committee hearings into the relations of five United States senators with Charles Keating, of savings-and-loan infamy, offered an instructive if sometimes dispiriting look at the marriage of politics and money. With its straightforward coverage of the workings of Congress and other institutions through the year, C-Span reinforced its special niche among the cable channels.
 Several big news names basked in their celebrity this year, while others tried out new ventures, with mixed results. "The Best of Nightline With Ted Koppel, 1980 to 1990" was an engaging review of the ABC program that began with the taking of American hostages in Iran and continues to deliver nightly vitamin supplements to the once-over-fast helpings of network news.
 Again this year, ABC News gave time, via Mr. Koppel and Peter Jennings, to important stories, including the abortion issue, America's role in supporting the Khmer Rouge in Cambodia and the political commotions in Eastern Europe.
 Among the more worthless documentaries of 1990, on the other hand, was NBC's "New Hollywood," Tom Brokaw's report on nothing new. Dan Rather of CBS News was particularly busy racing around the Middle East in search of stories, which he occasionally found.
 Another self-tribute, "Mike Wallace, Then and Now," offered evidence of the place that the CBS veteran has won in the hearts of his fans. Some of the excerpts misguidedly tried to show Mr. Wallace in a soft light, but the fun of the hour lay in examples of his performances as Mean Mike, the Scourge of the Tube. The most resounding quote of the hour came from Richard Nixon as he was taking office in 1968: "I hope to restore respect to the Presidency."
 A less successful exercise in self-celebration came this month, on PBS, in the form of "15 Years of MacNeil/Lehrer." It featured puffy comments by biggies of the tube like Walter Cronkite, Barbara Walters, Jane Pauley and Mr. Jennings. Why the estimable Mr. MacNeil and Mr. Lehrer should have sat still for a public ego massage is perplexing. It was the latest display of the embarrassing lengths to which public broadcasting is driven in its endless quest for viewer contributions.
 Among the most-publicized events of the television year were the appearances of three network glamourpusses, Ms. Pauley, Connie Chung and Maria Shriver, in new formats that can be collectively thought of as the "With" shows.
 The premiere hour of "Real Life With Jane Pauley," which began with a report on America's young parents working harder than ever, was a lightly handled mix of once-over-easy sociology, mild reporting and just fooling around. Nothing particularly important, but unpretentious and unpatronizing, in the Pauley manner.
 "Face to Face With Connie Chung" led off with an interview with Gene Wilder, who had lost his wife, the comedian Gilda Radner, to cancer. "You miss her, or she's with you anyway?" Ms. Chung had the face to inquire.
 "Cutting Edge With Maria Shriver" had pretty Ms. Shriver rushing around interviewing several near-celebrities, with camera equipment often on camera. The tricky production and slightly offbeat guests could not conceal the conventionality of spirit. "So many of your songs do express pain and loss," Ms. Shriver informed the pop singer Sinead O'Connor and then asked, "Is it painful for you to write it?" Probably not as painful as hearing that sort of question.
 With "The Civil War" on one end and show-biz style specials on the other, television once again showed its range and its potential, for better and, all too commonly, for worse.
