LENGTH: 1222 words

HEADLINE: Focus: Cleveland;
Bucking the Trend on Shopping Malls

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STOFFEL

 BODY:
WHEN banks in Cleveland were approached in 1986 to invest in redeveloping 34 downtown acres into an ambitious mix of shopping, restaurants, offices and a hotel, they reacted cautiously.
As in most cities, suburban shopping mall development had for several decades drawn business out of the city's center, and attracting new retailers and bringing shoppers back to downtown was clearly going against the tide. Yet the downtown project was attractive to bankers because they were already heavily invested in this city's turnaround. 
 Crowned by the landmark 52-story Terminal Tower -- since the 30's a prominent office building atop the central transit station -- the redevelopment was seen as crucial to revitalizing a much-beleaguered city center. Its site spans several blocks between the Cuyahoga River and Public Square.
 Ameritrust was the first of several local banks -- Society Corporation, Bank One, Huntington National Bank and National City Bank -- that, along with the Chase Manhattan Bank, invested a total of $59 million in the $400 million project.
 Now, just nine months after opening, Tower City Centre's 380,000 square feet of retail space is 98 percent leased to such stores as Brooks Brothers, Bally of Switzerland, the Gap and Gap Kids, some of them new to the city. According to Forest City Enterprises of Cleveland, the owner and developer, the leasing pace is three years ahead of projections. And despite gloomy national retail forecasts, individual stores report that sales for the center's first Christmas season are better than expected.
 But any further expansion of the project has been postponed indefinitely, including an agreement from Neiman-Marcus to build a 120,000-square-foot anchor store that was to have opened in 1992. The original development plan called for three additional anchor stores.
In fact, Forest City's development plans nationwide have come to a near halt. "Very few transactions are taking place," said Albert B. Ratner, president and chief executive officer of Forest City. "It's a flaky market."
 After more than three decades of almost frenzied building of shopping malls, the financial hurdles are higher for getting any project off the ground. Besides an ever-shrinking supply of attractive markets without malls, a stagnating retail industry plagued by takeovers and liquidations has lenders less willing to finance projects without stable anchors firmly in place. Blaming tougher regulators and examiners, lenders are also demanding that developers finance as much as 25 percent of their projects.
 "There's a simple reason," said John Jardine, vice president and managing partner at Teachers Insurance & Annuity Association of New York, a heavy investor in malls. "The banking, pension and insurance industries are all less liquid than they were 18, 24 months ago. As a result, everyone is slowing down."
 The change of heart has hit developers nationwide. In a survey released in August by the International Council of Shopping Centers, the industry trade group based in New York, 43 percent of developers said credit became tougher to come by in mid-1990. Forty percent said their most recent loan application had been turned down.
 "We never could have done this project, in fact most of what we have today in our portfolio we couldn't finance in this climate," Mr. Ratner said.
 As a result, shopping center construction has plummeted. The number of construction contracts awarded nationwide in the third quarter of 1990 was 41.4 percent lower than in the same period a year ago. The Midwest saw a drop of 45 percent, faring better than only the Northeast, where starts dropped 53.9 percent in the third quarter compared with a year earlier. The South saw a 37.2 percent decline in construction, the West, 30.5 percent.
Ohio has managed to hold onto its rank as third behind Florida and California in the number of new centers under construction. Yet if the trend continues, the decline nationwide will represent the lowest number of new project starts since 1982, the council said..
 "We're looking at a turmoil in retailing, in particular affecting the larger department stores, and a credit crunch choking the developers," said Isaac Legnado, director of research at the shopping center council. "In normal circumstances one or the other would be serious enough."
In the late 50's and early 60's, as the nation moved to the suburbs, where land was plentiful, mall speculation was a sure thing. Twenty years ago, most urban areas were ripe for a regional mall of 400,000 square feet or more in an affluent suburb. Today there are often five or six regional malls in the same metropolitan area.
AS rising land prices accompanied the shrinking availability of the 60- to 90-acre tracts needed for such regional centers, the popularity of smaller, less-expensive, unenclosed strip centers grew. Strip centers, typically smaller than 100,000 square feet and anchored by such convenience stores as groceries and discount druggists, represent 63 percent of the country's 36,650 shopping malls.
 The retail industry's upheaval has also taken its toll on centers already in place. The 1.5-million-square-foot Forest Fair Mall in Cincinnati celebrated a gala opening in 1989 and this year lost three of its six anchors -- Bonwit Teller, B. Altman and Parisian. In November the principal lender, the Bank of Nova Scotia, moved to foreclose on the entire development.
 Despite such highly visible difficulties, developers deny there is widespread vacancy. The average vacancy rate for shopping centers of all sizes nationwide was 10.6 percent in 1989, nearly unchanged from 10.5 percent in 1988, according to the shopping center council. The vacancy rate for strip centers was 12.3 percent in 1989 -- double the rate for larger malls.
 Smaller developments and smaller developers are being hit hardest. The council survey found that developers who own less than a million square feet are less likely to have their loan applications approved.
 "There is no question, a lot of strip centers -- and malls for that matter -- should not have been built," said Irving Maizlish, chairman of Leo Eisenberg & Company of Kansas City, Mo., a strip center developer.
 While the current squeeze is being felt throughout the shopping mall industry, it may translate into fewer, better-marketed centers. And for those projects, it seems, money is available.
"WE'RE always interested in a good deal, one with a superior land position and good connections to department stores," said Mr. Jardine of the Teachers Insurance & Annuity Association. "Regional shopping malls continue to be a preferred investment, much superior to offices or hotels."
 For his part, Mr. Maizlish of Eisenberg & Company reported that development would be off 15 to 20 percent in 1991 from the typical rate of 20 centers a year he kept up through the 1980's. To raise cash, he will continue to sell completed centers to private investors.
"We're continuing to go on building with blinders on," he said, "just not as many as last year."
 Forest City expects the slowdown in development of new centers to continue for at least a year, Mr. Ratner said, so its focus will be on renovating existing centers to make them more attractive to shoppers and prospective tenants.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 11; Column 2; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 1082 words

HEADLINE: Does Community Policing Work?;
Efficient, Cooperative

BYLINE: By Herman Goldstein; Herman Goldstein, professor of law at the University of Wisconsin, is author of "Problem-Oriented Policing."

DATELINE: MADISON, Wis.

 BODY:
If New York and other cities want to succeed in aggressive campaigns to reclaim the streets for all citizens, they must make the most of a relatively new idea: community policing. This concept, also known as neighborhood policing, empowers the police to do more than just make arrests.
Officers are permanently assigned to one neighborhood so they can get to know the people living and working in it. They tackle problems once deemed unimportant: noise complaints, trespassing, teen-agers' hullabaloo. The public is given easy access to them through storefront stations and officers' telephone numbers at work, not the impersonal and frequently busy 911. 
 Community policing, which is expensive, cannot replace traditional investigations into such violent crimes as homicide, armed robbery and sexual assault. But by emphasizing crime prevention, it can improve a neighborhood's quality of life, an urgent issue in America's cities.
New York's 1,457-member Community Patrol Officer Program (called CPOP), which such evaluators as New York's Vera Institute of Justice considers promising, is staffed by special units. In other cities, the entire force does community policing.
Some programs are ambitious and far-reaching. Others are narrow and superficial. They are little more than public-relations efforts such as police athletic leagues and foot patrols intended to evoke nostalgic recollections of the friendly cop on the beat. These shallow efforts threaten the movement's credibility.
In the most advanced neighborhood programs, the police systematically identify and analyze particular crimes such as theft from cars and robbery of the elderly, and develop tailored responses. This fine-tuning approach, specifically labeled problem-oriented policing, is best exemplified by New York City; Baltimore County, Md.; San Diego, Calif.; Tulsa, Okla.; Newport News, Va., and Edmonton, Alberta.
The CPOP program began in June 1984, and within about a year it began making its mark: Vincent Esposito, a CPOP officer assigned to the 72d Precinct in Brooklyn, N.Y., foiled drug dealing in Rainbow Park, on Sixth Avenue. First, he arrested drug dealers, but that time-consuming process hardly stopped the traffic. After gaining the trust of elderly residents in neighboring apartment houses, he got them to telephone him confidentially and tell him where dealers were stashing drugs. He entered the park, ignored the dealers and seized the drugs. The dealers, suffering heavy losses, soon gave up and went away, and the park was restored to young mothers and elderly residents of the neighborhood.
Baltimore County's COPE (Citizen Oriented Police Enforcement) unit, begun in 1981, has reduced crime and repeated calls for police assistance and residents' fear in apartment complexes beset by burglaries, drug-dealing, vandalism, petty theft and conflicts among residents. It has done so by working with management to make repairs, improve lighting, modify areas where intruders could hide, and improve the appearance of buildings. It has organized tenants, mediated disputes between tenants and between tenants and management and made sure both sides honor leases. It has involved the fire, building inspection, zoning and health departments, and has connected residents with social services.
The police traditionally deal with the public the way many parents treat their children. Armed with authority, they have cultivated an omnipotent air, hoping the threat of arrest and punishment would keep things under control. Their bluff has been called. In many inner cities, they cannot control violence, curb disorder and reduce fear simply by a show of authority and the implicit threats that go with it.
Yet, large segments of the public have not yet acknowledged these limitations. They prefer old-fashioned approaches: a greater police presence, more arrests, more prosecutions -- even though investing more and more resources in such methods does not reduce street crime and violence. This is wasteful. It ignores everything learned about the complexity of policing in recent decades, and caters to the notion that more officers mean less crime. That sounds good, but it's not necessarily so.
Community policing discards the assumption that only criminal-law enforcement defines the police's social role and priorities. It recognizes that officers face an incredible range of knotty problems and that the law is but one means for engaging them.
Today, there are not enough officers to protect the public properly. Consider New York's Police Department, staffed by 26,992 officers of all ranks. Allow for days off, vacations, sick leave, court appearances, administrative support and supervision, and important special assignments such as investigative and organized-crime units. Apportion the remaining officers to cover the three daily shifts. Spread that number on the streets of five boroughs.
No more than 3,000 officers are on patrol on any shift to protect the lives and property of 7.5 million residents of widely varied cultural, educational and economic backgrounds in a volatile city. Would adding 2,000, 4,000, 6,000 officers dramatically reduce the crisis of the streets? I doubt it.
Besides being spread so thin, the police are preoccupied with responding to 911 calls, not all of which merit their attention. While foot and motorized patrols may reduce fear, researchers question whether they significantly reduce street crime.
Generally, officers lack legal authority to deal with many offensive street situations: harassment by panhandlers, disorderly behavior by the homeless. When they have proper authority, they must depend on the criminal justice system to enforce it. However, the overwhelmed courts can handle only the most serious offenders. Thus, traditional policing makes no sense in certain situations.
When officers arrange for the quick removal of abandoned cars and the speedy demolition or repair of abandoned buildings -- "quality of life" issues that can affect self-respect and crime rates -- they are not doing "soft social work," as critics charge.
Such community policing can succeed only by cultivating a neighborhood's respect and trust -- a tall order given the tensions between the police and certain ethnic groups. Alone, it cannot overcome such endemic problems as racial tensions. It is, at best, a holding operation to keep the peace until national social forces and political will address the underlying problems.

HEADLINE: Focus: Cleveland;
Bucking the National Trend on Shopping Malls

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STOFFEL

 BODY:
When banks in Cleveland were approached in 1986 to invest in redeveloping 34 downtown acres into an ambitious mix of shopping, restaurants, offices and a hotel, they reacted cautiously.
As in most cities, suburban shopping mall development had for several decades drawn business out of the city's center, and attracting new retailers and bringing shoppers back to downtown was clearly going against the tide. Yet the downtown project was attractive to bankers because they were already heavily invested in this city's turnaround. 
 Crowned by the landmark 52-story Terminal Tower -- since the 30's a prominent office building atop the central transit station -- the redevelopment was seen as crucial to revitalizing a much-beleaguered city center. Its site spans several blocks between the Cuyahoga River and Public Square.
 Ameritrust was the first of several local banks -- Society Corporation, Bank One, Huntington National Bank and National City Bank -- that, along with the Chase Manhattan Bank, invested a total of $59 million in the $400 million project.
 Now, just nine months after opening, Tower City Centre's 380,000 square feet of retail space is 98 percent leased to such stores as Brooks Brothers, Bally of Switzerland, the Gap and Gap Kids, some of them new to the city. According to Forest City Enterprises of Cleveland, the owner and developer, the leasing pace is three years ahead of projections. And despite gloomy national retail forecasts, individual stores report that sales for the center's first Christmas season are better than expected.
But any further expansion of the project has been postponed indefinitely, including an agreement from Neiman-Marcus to build a 120,000-square-foot anchor store that was to have opened in 1992. The original development plan called for three additional anchor stores.
In fact, Forest City's development plans nationwide have come to a near halt. "Very few transactions are taking place," said Albert B. Ratner, president and chief executive officer of Forest City. "It's a flaky market."
 After more than three decades of almost frenzied building of shopping malls, the financial hurdles are higher for getting any project off the ground. Besides an ever-shrinking supply of attractive markets without malls, a stagnating retail industry plagued by takeovers and liquidations has lenders less willing to finance projects without stable anchors firmly in place. Blaming tougher regulators and examiners, lenders are also demanding that developers finance as much as 25 percent of their projects.
 "There's a simple reason," said John Jardine, vice president and managing partner at Teachers Insurance & Annuity Association of New York, a heavy investor in malls. "The banking, pension and insurance industries are all less liquid than they were 18, 24 months ago. As a result, everyone is slowing down."
 The change of heart has hit developers nationwide. In a survey released in August by the International Council of Shopping Centers, the industry trade group based in New York, 43 percent of developers said credit became tougher to come by in mid-1990. Forty percent said their most recent loan application had been turned down.
 "We never could have done this project, in fact most of what we have today in our portfolio we couldn't finance in this climate," Mr. Ratner said.
As a result, shopping center construction has plummeted. The number of construction contracts awarded nationwide in the third quarter of 1990 was 41.4 percent lower than in the same period a year ago. The Midwest saw a drop of 45 percent, faring better than only the Northeast, where starts dropped 53.9 percent in the third quarter compared with a year earlier. The South saw a 37.2 percent decline in construction, the West, 30.5 percent.
Ohio has managed to hold onto its rank as third behind Florida and California in the number of new centers under construction. Yet if the trend continues, the decline nationwide will represent the lowest number of new project starts since 1982, the council said..
 "We're looking at a turmoil in retailing, in particular affecting the larger department stores, and a credit crunch choking the developers," said Isaac Legnado, director of research at the shopping center council. "In normal circumstances one or the other would be serious enough."
In the late 50's and early 60's, as the nation moved to the suburbs, where land was plentiful, mall speculation was a sure thing. Twenty years ago, most urban areas were ripe for a regional mall of 400,000 square feet or more in an affluent suburb. Today there are often five or six regional malls in the same metropolitan area.
As rising land prices accompanied the shrinking availability of the 60- to 90-acre tracts needed for such regional centers, the popularity of smaller, less-expensive, unenclosed strip centers grew. Strip centers, typically smaller than 100,000 square feet and anchored by such convenience stores as groceries and discount druggists, represent 63 percent of the country's 36,650 shopping malls.
 The retail industry's upheaval has also taken its toll on centers already in place. The 1.5-million-square-foot Forest Fair Mall in Cincinnati with its sports-themed entertainment centercelebrated a gala opening in 1989 and this year lost three of its six anchors -- Bonwit Teller, B. Altman and Parisian. In November the principal lender, the Bank of Nova Scotia, moved to foreclose on the entire development.
 Despite such highly visible difficulties, developers deny there is widespread vacancy. The average vacancy rate for shopping centers of all sizes nationwide was 10.6 percent in 1989, nearly unchanged from 10.5 percent in 1988, according to the shopping center council. The vacancy rate for strip centers was 12.3 percent in 1989 -- double the rate for larger malls.
 Smaller developments and smaller developers are being hit hardest. The council survey found that developers who own less than a million square feet are less likely to have their loan applications approved.
 "There is no question, a lot of strip centers -- and malls for that matter -- should not have been built," said Irving Maizlish, chairman of Leo Eisenberg & Company of Kansas City, Mo., a strip center developer.
 While the current squeeze is being felt throughout the shopping mall industry, it may translate into fewer, better-marketed centers. And for those projects, it seems, money is available.
"We're always interested in a good deal, one with a superior land position and good connections to department stores," said Mr. Jardine of the Teachers Insurance & AnnuityAssociation. "Regional shopping malls continue to be a preferred investment, much superior to offices or hotels."
 For his part, Mr. Maizlish of Eisenberg & Company reported that development would be off 15 to 20 percent in 1991 from the typical rate of 20 centers a year he kept up through the 1980's. To raise cash, he will continue to sell completed centers to private investors.
"We're continuing to go on building with blinders on," he said, "just not as many as last year."
 Forest City expects the slowdown in development of new centers to continue for at least a year, Mr. Ratner said, so its focus will be on renovating existing centers to make them more attractive to shoppers and prospective tenants.

HEADLINE: NATIONAL NOTEBOOK: MOBILE, ALA.;
A New Center For Downtown

BYLINE: By LETTICE STUART

 BODY:
A $60 MILLION waterfront convention center on which construction is to start early next year is being hailed by city officials and business leaders as the key to downtown revitalization.
This city of 200,000 people, situated at the head of Mobile Bay 31 miles from the Gulf of Mexico, is Alabama's major port and the state's largest metropolitan area. But the downtown is a classic example of the decay of inner cities, in part because of retail flight to suburban malls, said Wilbert Wetzel, manager of the city's Office of Community Services. 
 "Lack of retailing means a lack of pedestrians, and the reversal of that process begins with putting people back on the street," he said.
City officials hope the convention center will do that by attracting hotels, retail shops, restaurants and entertainment establishments.
Albert R. St. Clair, executive director of the newly formed Mobile Downtown Redevelopment Commission, said the city is working with Charles K. Brelant Jr., who bought the 200-room Sheraton Hotel four blocks from the convention-center site, on finding funds to restore the building. The hotel closed in 1983.
The city is also negotiating a lease of its vacant, 68,000-square-foot Kress Building a block from center's site with Jerry Valent of Miami and James Van Antwerp of Mobile. Initially, they plan to spend $5 million to convert half the space for lease to upscale shops and specialty restaurants.
Mr. St. Clair said several people are also interested in developing the 11-acre C. S. X. railroad yards across from the convention center site into a riverwalk with shops and restaurants. The railroad is asking $9 million.
The 317,000-square-foot convention center will be built on a four-acre site along the Mobile River that the city bought from the state in 1987 for $2.85 million. The three-story building, topped with a lighthouse-style tower and featuring outdoor terraces and landscaped river walks, was designed by the Architects Group of Mobile and Thompson, Zentulett and Stanback of Atlanta. It is to have a 286-car underground parking garage and to be connected to the existing 376-room Stouffer's Riverview Plaza Hotel and a proposed 400-room hotel by elevated walkways. Completion is set for the fall of 1992.
The project is being financed by a $48.6 million bond issue to be paid off with revenues from the city's 2 percent hotel and restaurant tax and a 2 percent sales tax on all prepared food items.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Rendering of Mobile, Ala., convention center, hailed by city officials as key to downtown redevelopment. (Thompson, Ventulett, Stanback & Associates)

HEADLINE: DINING OUT;
Innovative Creations in a Hotel Setting

BYLINE: By JOANNE STARKEY

 BODY:
ALTHOUGH 1990's economic indicators pointed downward, the news for Long Island restaurantgoers was not all bad. Hard times have spawned two positives. Some luxury restaurants have lowered their prices, and a handful of Manhattan's star chefs have, of necessity, made the trek to the Island.
The Garden City Hotel has enticed one of them, Ali Barker, who earned his stripes at the Union Square Cafe and 150 Wooster in New York City. He has been dishing up his innovative creations at the Polo Grill since September. 
 Unfortunately, the trend toward lower prices is not part of the new regime. Entrees are competitively priced at $16 to $25, but other courses make the tab soar. Most appetizers are $8 to $11; mixed drinks, $6; glasses of wine, $5 to $7.50, and special desserts, $7.50.
The new chef and menu are not the only changes at the Polo Grill. This lobby dining room now has a wraparound cozy feeling. It is still possible to see the passing parade of hotel guests and hear the piano music from the adjoining bar, but tapestry banquettes, brass railings, wood paneling and etched glass contain and define the room.
The service staff is abundant and attentive, with a few inconsistent lapses. One evening, the extraordinary service included fresh napkins after the entrees. On another, water was by request, and then served only to the person asking for it.
Yet, the staff and decor are mere acolytes to the exciting new menu at the Polo Grill. It features many of Mr. Barker's signature dishes: a heartwarming appetizer of "risotto" of barley and sausage with a roasted red-and-yellow-pepper fillip, entrees of duck breast topped with both green olives and slices of roasted garlic and Tuna Diane, a takeoff on the Cognac, mustard and green-peppercorn sauce that usually adorns Steak Diane.
But execution of these exciting dishes sometimes falls short. The tuna was dreadfully overcooked and dry. So, too, was a grilled salmon steak charred to the point of tasting acrid.
Diners who ordered those fish were understandably more taken with their vegetable accompaniments, wisps of deep-fried zucchini with the tuna and a warm compote of cannelini and pinto beans beneath the salmon. Soothing mashed turnips topped with shallot crisps also outclassed the menu's very ordinary shell steak.
Main courses rating hurrahs for both concept and delivery are the juicy roast chicken under its mountain of thinly cut French fries, the delicate red snapper kissed with red wine and contrasted with crisp minicubes of potato, flaky sea bass on a fresh-tasting, almost-crunchy, ratatouille and an evening special that our table nicknamed "Turkey in the Straw." Turkey breast scaloppine glossed with a shallot ginger sauce was buried beneath a thatch of crisply fried vegetables.
Creamy leek soup spiked with white port and complemented by Roquefort croutons and crackly fried leeks is the one to order. Black-bean soup and corn chowder are routine alternatives.
Hardy pastas like homemade fettuccine bathed in a lusty lamb ragout and tricolored fettuccini perky with bacon, white wine and cream are also fine openers. Thumbs up, too, for the lobster-fennel ravioli and the roasted oysters mated with fried parsnip chips. In spite of a lemon-pepper aioli that shares honors with the usual red sauce, the shrimp cocktail ($11) is pedestrian.
All salads shine, but the leader is the spinach, with its warm brown-sugar-and-bacon dressing.
Except for a silken creme brulee with crackly crust, desserts are routine. The raspberry mousse cake was rubbery, and the chocolate mousse cake and fruit tart was run-of-the-mill.
The prestigious 200-choice wine list mirrors the restaurant itself. It is well thought out and intelligently selected with occasional surprises. Opus One (1987), the magnificent Mondavi-Rothschild collaboration that sells for as much as $60 a bottle retail, is priced at $90, less than the standard 100 percent markup. But a 1988 Antinori Orvietto that is $9 at many stores costs $25 at the Polo Grill. Steer toward the Seppelt Australian chardonnay for $25, one of the most modestly priced chardonnays here and one of the most outstanding.

 
The Polo Grill
Very Good
Garden City Hotel, 45 Seventh Street, Garden City. 747-3000.
Atmosphere: Elegant, extravagant lobby dining.
Service: Attentive, occasional lapses on basics.
Recommended dishes: "Risotto" of barley, roasted oysters, lobster-fennel ravioli, fettuccine with lamb ragout, tricolored fettuccine, leek soup, all salads, roast chicken, red snapper, duck breast with green olives, sea bass, turkey scaloppine, creme brulee.
Price range: Lunch entrees are $12.50 to $17.50. Dinner: Appetizers are $4.50 to $11 and entrees are $16 to $25.
Credit cards: All major cards.
Hours: Breakfast, 6:30 to 11 A.M.; lunch, noon to 3 P.M.; dinner, 6 to 10 P.M. every day. Afternoon tea is served 3 to 5 P.M. Saturdays.
Reservations: Needed.
Wheelchair accessibility: No steps.

HEADLINE: Securities From Abroad

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Americans bought a net $810 million of foreign stocks in the third quarter, down sharply from the $2.4 billion they bought a year earlier, the Securities Industry Association said last week.
The figure was also down sharply from the second quarter, when American investors bought a net $6 billion in foreign stocks. Net purchases of foreign stocks were near record highs for the first nine months of the year.
 "U.S. investors have faith in foreign equity markets despite the precariousness of the world's political and economic situation," said David Storin, the association's director of international finance.
The trade group's Foreign Activity Report said purchases for the first nine months totaled $7.3 billion, second only to the record $9.4 billion in the 1989 period.
Foreign investors sold a net $1.3 billion of American stocks in August and more than $2.5 billion worth in September, the report said.
For the third quarter, net sales by foreigners of American stocks totaled $2.4 billion, compared with net sales of $3.8 billion in the second quarter and net purchases of $5.8 billion in the third quarter of 1989.
American investors bought a net $665 million of foreign bonds in the third quarter, down from $5.7 billion in the second quarter.

HEADLINE: Barometer's 8-Year Low

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Dec. 30

 BODY:
The Chicagoland Business barometer, a measure of economic conditions in the industrial center of the Midwest, has fallen to its lowest level in eight years, the Association of Purchasing Management of Chicago said on Friday.
The group said its monthly index fell to an unadjusted 41 percent in December, from 45.1 percent in November. A reading below 50 percent signals a slowdown in the manufacturing sector, while a level above 50 percent suggests expansion.
The index reflects conditions in 200 industrial businesses in northern Illinois and northwest Indiana.
The overall index "appears to indicate a recession is upon us," the Chicago association said.
The National Association of Purchasing Management does not use data from the Chicago report for its monthly survey, which will next be released on Jan. 2, but conducts its own research.
None of the five components that make up the Chicagoland business barometer were at or above 50 percent in November.
Production rates fell to 44 percent in December from 48 percent last month. New orders, to which the association gives the most weight, fell to 40 percent from 44 percent.
The report showed order backlogs had declined to 35 percent, from 38 percent in November, and employment had slid to 40 percent, from 43 percent. Supplier deliveries, which used to be called vendor performance, fell to 45 percent, from 50 percent in November.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Primary Dealers' Profits Reportedly High in '90

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
One bright spot in the gloom that has hung over Wall Street for much of this year was the financial performance of the 41 primary dealers of Government securities.
A number of people at these firms say that the trading profits of primary dealers for 1990 were the highest since 1986, a time when financial firms of all stripes seemed to be printing money. 
 The financial condition of the primary dealers is monitored on a daily basis by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. But the New York Fed, which also has the responsibility of awarding primary dealership status to firms that seek the designation, allows the public a glimpse only once a year at how the dealers are doing . Even then, the information covers just the first eight months. The firms that are designated as primary dealers by the New York Fed make markets in Government securities. In addition, the Fed conducts its open market operations exclusively through those firms.
 
Released Each November
 The figures are released each November at the annual meeting of the Public Securities Association, an industry group.
The New York Fed said at that time that the combined profit of the primary dealers was $600 million through August. A steady decline in interest rates since then has helped to raise profits further, because the direction of interest rates has been obvious, and market participants were saying late last week that profits for all of 1990 probably amounted to $800 million or more.
In contrast, the New York Fed said primary dealers posted a small loss in the comparable period of 1989, while in 1988 aggregate profits amounted to $300 million.
The New York Fed declined to comment further on 1990 earnings or on industry speculation about profits.
 
Plagued by Overcapacity
 The rebound in the earnings of primary dealers has been hard-won. As with much of Wall Street, as the 1980's drew to a close primary dealers were plagued by overcapacity. Too many firms were chasing the limited amount of business, and profit margins became razor thin.
A shakeout, which began in 1989, continued this year. Five firms gave up their dealerships in 1990, while two, Deutsche Bank and SBC Government Securities Inc., a subsidiary of the Swiss Bank Corporation, were added to the list. Some people in the industry expect that the number of primary dealers will shrink, within the next year or so, from 41 to the mid-30's.
Almost certainly, not all of the firms that remain primary dealers made money in 1990. "I would bet that a large number were either in the red or broke even, at best," said Leonard J. Santow, a managing director at Griggs & Santow Inc., a financial consulting firm that has a number of primary dealers as clients. "Maybe a dozen firms accounted for all of the profits; 1990 was a respectable year, but the last three years were miserable."
Richard M. Kelly, president of Aubrey G. Lanston & Company, a primary dealer, said that "assuming that most dealers were profitable in the last four months, we can say that 1990 was a better year than any of the past four." He continued, "This business has been in a recession since 1986, but in my judgment there is light at the end of this tunnel."
 
Aided by Correct Bets
 Mr. Kelly added that the contraction in the business in the last few years had slightly widened profit margins on trades. In addition, dealers were helped by their correct bets in August and September, when the markets were roiled by rising oil prices and fear of war in the Middle East. Finally, he said, with fewer players in the business the prospects for making money at a Treasury auction had increased.
"In past years, competition among dealers was so intense that bidding in the auction process was a losing proposition, even though it was something you had to do," he said. "In 1990, as the competition became somewhat less intense, dealers had greater success in underwriting Treasury securities."
A trader in Government bonds at a primary dealer firm, who insisted that he not be identified, said, "This year it seemed like 9 out of 10 times you made money when you bought securities at the auctions."
 
Focus on Other Matters
 Aside from celebrating the holidays, shoring up the bottom line has been the main focus for dealers in the last two weeks. But now, with the books on 1990 all but shut, the focus will shift to other matters.
The first reports on how the economy fared in December will be the object of much attention this week. Analysts said the data, especially a report expected Friday on employment conditions in December, will probably be very weak, rekindling thoughts among market participants that the Fed will again lower the Federal funds rate from its current target level of 7 percent.
"Barring a seasonal fluke, the December employment report should be substantially a replay of the prior month's debacle," Robert DiClemente, an economist at Salomon Brothers, wrote in the firm's weekly "Comments on Credit."
 
Further Easing in Rates Seen
 "The broad-based slowing in business spending and consumer outlays, together with the deterioration in direct measures of the labor market, points to further easing in the funds rate to at least 6.75 percent soon after Friday's report," he said.
Salomon analysts are forecasting that nonfarm employment probably fell by an additional 200,000 jobs in December, after a drop of 267,000 reported in November. A contraction of that size would raise the unemployment rate, which last month stood at 5.9 percent, to 6.1 percent.
"We think that by Friday the market will take a different attitude with respect to the Fed," Mr. Santow said. "After the employment data we think the market will be absolutely convinced the Fed will ease by the second week in January."

HEADLINE: Business Scene;
The Long Wait For the PC Payoff

BYLINE: By Louis Uchitelle

 BODY:
THE personal computer, so ubiquitous in the United States, is one of those historic breakthrough technologies that should be revamping the lives of millions of Americans and making them wealthier. But so far, the great payoff has not materialized and, if history is a guide, which it usually is, the payoff will probably not appear until the 21st century.
It is inconvenient that this vigorous young technology is not offering much help in reversing the economy's current decline. Yet it is in good company. The railroad, the electric motor and the car were similarly anemic when they were in their youths. Ingrained national habits had to change before they generated economic growth, and in each case that took a generation or more to achieve. The personal computer is still in its first decade as a widely used technology. 
 "It is not so strange that these desktop computers and the tiny chips that make them run have not led right away to greater productivity and economic growth," said Paul A. David, an historian in economics at Stanford University. The reassurance is important. The personal computer is not the only new technology likely to make the economy grow, but it is the most promising. Biotechnology might vastly increase food production. Trains that run on a bed of air undoubtedly will travel three or four times as fast as those that run on rails. And "hypersonic" aircraft, now being developed, will someday fly travelers from New York to Tokyo in two hours.
But in the opinion of economists, none of these inventions seems to match the potential of personal computers to expand the economy and also to reorganize it. Electricity, or more precisely the electric motor, eventually realized a similar potential. But first it was an obstacle to economic growth, Mr. David notes. For example, manufacturing productivity -- that is, the output of a factory with a given number of workers -- actually declined in the early 20th century. For more than two decades, electric power was a hindrance to production rather than the great accelerator that it finally became in the 1920's.
By then electric power had reorganized the manufacturing process. It meant that each machine in a factory could have its own motor. Until then, machines were driven by belts or chains connected to a single power source for the entire factory -- a big steam engine or a water mill that made the network of belts rotate.
With the advent of the motor and of utilities that furnished inexpensive electricity, factories no longer had to be compact, multi-storied structures that kept each machine near the central power source. In time they became the long, one-story buildings common today, with the machines arranged to make possible the modern assembly line and much more rapid production.
But in the interim, from the 1890's to the 1920's, managers and workers had to operate the old system while learning and installing the new one. That was time consuming, and it slowed production. Something similar is beginning to happen with desktop computers and the corporate office.Although computers are commonplace, the office is still organized around memos and paper work, secretaries and clerks, and paper filed in metal cabinets. The corporate office in 2010, if the personal computer reaches its full potential, will no longer have paper or be housed in a single building. It can be spread across a city or a nation, if that makes workers more efficient.
The office, in effect, will become a series of computers connected by a fiber-optic network -- a network that carries information, images and voices at speeds many times faster and in much greater quantities than today's copper telephone lines.
The information on 900 paper pages, for instance, can go from one personal computer screen to another in less than a minute, and "tele-conferencing" will become standard practice, with people seeing and talking to each other as if they were around the same table.
But productivity is dragged down until the old system is phased out and the new one fully absorbed. "It doesn't make for greater output to have expensive computers everywhere and executives who still dictate letters to secretaries taking shorthand," said Paul Krugman, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.
 Perhaps most important, the fiber-optic network still has to be installed. Only the beginnings are in place. And here, too, there are parallels with the past. Just as private companies installed electric power lines early in the century, telephone companies are stringing fiber-optic cable today. Just as the Government set electric power rates, it will probably have to set rules or priorities for the fiber-optic network.
Some hold that the Government's role is to stand aside, letting the telephone companies install the network and operate it as they please. Others favor Federal subsidies to speed up the installation of a network, which could cost $200 billion, and Federal rules governing its use.
So far, the Bush Administration has not produced a policy. "It is not clear what Government imprimatur is needed," said Robert E. Kahn, president of the Corporation for National Research Initiatives. "But some guidance is necessary."

HEADLINE: Market Place;
'89 Stock Winners Still on Top in '90

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS

 BODY:
If 1990 was a year when it was best not to own stocks, it was also a year when those who did own them were well advised to stay with the winners of 1989.
The old reliable consumer growth stocks, the ones that make products everyone buys, again led the 30 stocks in the Dow Jones industrial average, as they did in 1989. Procter & Gamble, Coca-Cola and Philip Morris were the top three stocks in 1990, as they were the year before. 
 The Dow is by far the best-known stock market average in America, but its inadequacy as a market measure is limited by its very nature: 30 large companies in a market that includes thousands. This year, that unrepresentative nature led to a picture of a market that was much kinder and gentler to investors than the reality.
Through Friday, the Dow was off just 4.5 percent for 1990, and 11 of the 30 stocks showed gains for the year. The Standard & Poor's 500, a more broadly based index of large stocks, was down 7 percent. And the Russell 2,000, which measures the small stocks ignored by larger indexes, fell 22.5 percent. All the figures include price performance only, ignoring dividends.
Within the Dow, the idea of going with a winner and avoiding losers was well illustrated. In 1989, a fine year for stocks, seven Dow stocks went down. Of those, five went down further in 1990. The worst performer in 1989, Navistar, improved its relative performance only a bit this year. It was next to last. Goodyear Tire and Rubber, the fourth-worst performer in 1989, moved into last place this year, with a stunning 56 percent drop.
The biggest turnaround on the up side this year came from International Business Machines, the company with the largest market capitalization in America and, in recent years, one of the more disappointing. It rose 20.5 percent, giving Big Blue shareholders their best year since 1985.
I.B.M. benefited from the successful introduction of its next generation of mainframes, as well as from the perception that its efforts to cut costs were finally paying off. And, after years of under-performance -- this was the first year since 1984 that I.B.M. shares outpaced the Dow average -- expectations may finally have been brought down to a level that was excessively pessimistic.
Over the years, one strategy of playing the Dow that has worked more often than not has been to buy the stocks least popular with investors, assuming that unloved stocks may grow to be worth more than expected.
Michael O'Higgins, an Albany investment manager and author of "Beating the Dow," to be published this week by HarperCollins, has developed a strategy of buying the 10 Dow stocks with the highest indicated yields at the end of each year, and holding them for a year. That assumes that the stocks with high yields have been driven to low prices by negative expectations in the market, and that at least some of those expectations will turn out to be unreasonable.
This year, the strategy was not a particularly great performer, with the 10 highest-yielding stocks of a year ago showing an average decline of 7.8 percent, before dividends, compared with a loss for the Dow of 4.5 percent. Including dividends, Mr. O'Higgins estimates, the 10 stocks had an average loss of 2.7 percent, while the Dow fell less than 1 percent.
The figure was pulled down by poor performances from Sears, Roebuck; Union Carbide; Allied-Signal, and General Motors, but helped by I.B.M. and a strong contingent of oil stocks that benefited from the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait. Those included Texaco, Exxon and Chevron. The other stocks among the 10 were Du Pont and Eastman Kodak.
 
Gain of 1,511 Percent
 Even with that poor performance, Mr. O'Higgins calculates that an investor who followed the strategy for the last 18 years would have a cumulative gain of 1,511 percent, compared with a rise of 495 percent in the Dow itself, having outperformed the Dow in 12 of the years.
The portfolio of high yielders for next year is led by Goodyear, which now has an indicated yield of 9.6 percent, based on a $1.80 annual dividend. There is more than a little doubt on Wall Street that Goodyear, burdened by debt from a corporate overhaul, will be able to pay the dividend if the recession continues, but so far it has not cut it.
The other stocks with high dividends as 1990 ends are General Motors, Sears, Allied-Signal, Texaco, Union Carbide, Exxon, Westinghouse Electric, Eastman Kodak and USX.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Time Warner's Merger Payoff

BYLINE: By RANDALL ROTHENBERG

 BODY:
When communications companies merged with wild abandon in the 1980's, executives attributed much of their enthusiasm for the combinations to an untested phenomenon called synergy.
Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation could now offer advertisers space in TV Guide packaged with time on the Fox television network, so the theory went. Capital Cities/ABC could promote ABC's soap operas in a special magazine from its publishing division. By vaulting the boundaries separating print, film, television and records, these new giants could make one plus one equal three. 
 A year after the merger of Time Inc. and Warner Communications created the world's largest communications company, Time Warner Inc. is finding that the combination adds up to a lot less than three, but something more than two.
 
Small and Unusual Payoffs
 Profits from such synergies are hard to pinpoint. The major projects held out by the company as examples could have been accomplished by either Time or Warner alone.
Yet the merger appears to be paying off in numerous small and unusual projects that, while not providing large returns for the company, could help Time Warner with its $10.7 billion merger debt.
Since the merger, Time has packaged ads for its newest magazine in Warner videotapes; Warner's Looney Tunes cartoon characters are appearing in books published by Time, and Time has tapped its customer data base to promote Warner's movies by mail.
But the deal the company has heralded as the best example of combined strength consists of a gallon of Time but only a pinch of Warner. Last month, Time Warner announced a marketing arrangement with the Chrysler Corporation that involved advertising and promotions using the media company's magazines, movies, books and direct-marketing capabilities. Executives said the deal exemplified what Time Warner's 1989 annual report called "a synthesis that would lift us to a position neither could achieve alone."
 
Sole Sponsor of Special Issues
 Yet the most innovative elements of the Chrysler package revolve around the Time Inc. magazines. Chrysler will be the sole sponsor of special issues of Fortune, People and Life, tied to the promotion's theme, "Rediscover America."
As part of the package, the automobile company will place advertising inserts in several Time Inc. magazines beginning in February, asking through personalized cards for readers' opinions on various issues. The tabulated responses will be distributed at the 1992 political conventions, and will also become part of a computer data base that Time Warner and Chrysler can use to develop advertisements aimed at particular types of people, said Michael D. Drexler, the worldwide media director of Bozell, Chrysler's ad agency.
Videotapes of Warner Brothers movies will be among the premiums offered to consumers responding to test-drive promotions, but other than that, Warner's involvement consists of several "maybes."
Warner may produce a "Rediscover America" television special and television spots featuring public figures discussing the readers' survey. Chrysler also has the right to buy product placements in a half-dozen Warner Brothers movies.
 
Greatest Marketing Obstacle
 Donald M. Elliman Jr., the executive vice president for marketing of the Time Inc. Magazine Company, attributed the lack of Warner elements in the Chrysler project to what may be the greatest obstacle facing marketing ventures involving the two sides of the merged company. Warner's records, movies and television production, he said, "have a little less applicability to advertising than do the old Time Inc. elements," especially the magazines.
This does not surprise media analysts, many of whom have been skeptical that communications companies could earn significant new revenues from cross-media projects.
"The truth is, the synergies, or economies of scale, usually take place at the very top of the company and from combining duplicated operations, rather than by forcing several different media to work together," said Larry Gerbrandt, an analyst at Paul Kagan Associates.
Still, with advertising growth at a virtual standstill, many communications executives say media companies must package their assets creatively simply to remain afloat.
One midlevel Time Warner executive, who insisted on not being identified, described the situation bluntly. "Marketing budgets for clients are static; that's a fact of life and it's directing the thinking of most media companies," he said. "We're saying that if a company normally spends $10 million on our magazines, let's get them to spend an additional $5 million on a bigger idea and force them to take that money from other media companies. We want to squeeze out our competitors."
 
Millions in Ad Revenues
 The Chrysler deal, for example, was reported by the advertising trade press to be worth $40 million to $50 million in ad revenues. Privately, some executives said the auto maker was increasing its spending at Time Warner by a third to a half over its normal expenditures. Time Warner and ad agency executives said similar deals were in the works but would not identify the advertisers.
The best examples of true cross-media ventures at Time Warner have been quite small. "The synergistic things happening between the two companies run from the monster level to the mundane, and the mundane things are the most cost-effective and efficient," said Sanford E. Reisenbach, the executive vice president of marketing and planning at Warner Brothers.
Warner Home Video included a subscription offer for Entertainment Weekly, Time's new magazine, in the videotape release of the film "Lethal Weapon 2," and the publishing division is enclosing promotions for Warner movies with its magazine subscription billings.
The greatest benefits from the merger have been realized through Time Warner Direct, which oversees the Book-of-the-Month Club and the Quality Paperback Book Club, as well as the television offers for parapsychology books and other ventures that sell by mail or telephone.
 
Promotion for 'Memphis Belle'
 Access to Time Warner's data base of 13 million direct-marketing customers enabled Warner Brothers to send tens of thousands of promotions for "Memphis Belle," a Warner film about the crew of a World War II bomber, to many of the 550,000 people who had bought war books or movies, Mr. Reisenbach said.
Warner has also been the source of numerous new products promoted by Time's direct-marketing operation, including a videotape series featuring such Warner Brothers properties as World War II movies, Clint Eastwood films and Looney Tunes cartoons, said Paul R. Stewart, the chairman of Time Life Entertainment.
The cartoon characters are also featured in a direct-mail book series for children, which another unit, Time Life for Children, started test-marketing two months ago.
"We'd never done cartoons in home video before, and it wouldn't have occurred to us if we hadn't gotten together with Warner and understood them," said Christopher T. Linen, the president and chief executive of Time Warner Direct.
 
Some Disappointments
 The effort to create cross-media ventures has not been without disappointments. One letdown has been "Listen Up: The Lives of Quincy Jones," a biography of the musician-producer that was done as a film by Warner Brothers, a cassette tape and compact disk by Reprise Records and a book by Warner Books, all of which were produced by Courtney Sale Ross, the wife of Time Warner's co-chairman, Steven J. Ross. One analyst who insisted on anonymity estimated that the company spent $4.5 million producing the movie and $1 million advertising it, for a return of only $250,000 in film rentals.
Nonetheless, "the project has a value beyond the box office," said Aubrey Balkind, the president of Frankfurt, Gips, Balkind, the New York ad agency and design firm that created the book, did the film's titles and ads and produced a music video.
The benefit appears to have been only the opportunity to experiment, however expensively, at crossing corporate lines. The company's cable subsidiary offered time on several of its systems for advertising for "Listen Up." In turn, the cable companies were able to host premieres of the film on behalf of charities in Houston, Kansas City, Mo., and other cities. Executives said the events had public-relations value, particularly with local regulators who wield enormous control over cable franchises.
Time Warner executives say that they generally do not receive discounts when they buy products or advertising space and time from other divisions. Nonetheless, there are financial benefits from the internal deals. Because money is being spent inside the corporation, some divisions are authorized to spend more on promotions for other divisions' products, Mr. Linen said.
 
'The Jury Is Still Out'
 Many media experts remain cautious about the eventual profitability of such projects. "To some extent, the jury is still out," said Steven Rattner, a general partner and head of the media department at Lazard Freres & Company, the investment firm. "This is still a relatively new concept that has yet to be fully tested. Thus far, the impact on the bottom line is difficult to quantify."
Beyond the Chrysler deal, Time Warner executives are refusing to comment on how much revenue the company has gained from synergies in its first year. They concede the amount is small, but maintain that money comes second, after the new methodology.
"This today is not a mammoth part of Time Warner's profit picture," Mr. Elliman of the Time Inc. Magazine Company said. "So maybe the critics are right. But we are blurring lines, and this company was never run that way before."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
Trade-Talk Role Seen For Ottawa

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Officials of the United States, Canada and Mexico say they are fast sweeping away obstacles to Canadian participation in the free-trade negotiations expected to open in 1991 between the United States and Mexico.
The three countries are expected to begin shaping a single agreement that would create a North American common market of 350 million people. "We're clearing away the underbrush and will start on the big trees very quickly," said Canada's Minister of International Trade, John C. Crosbie. 
 Since August the three countries have held a series of unpublicized high-level meetings, the latest just before Christmas, to try to work out the shape of the new negotiating table. They have announced nothing, but trade officials say they have set a deadline of Feb. 1 by which time they expect to reach agreement on the ground rules for a three-way deal.
 
Addition of Mexico Preferred
 Most American officials say they would prefer to bring Mexico into an agreement based on the accord that Canada and the United States signed two years ago, rather than negotiating a separate pact with Mexico.
Separate agreements with Canada and Mexico would mean two sets of rules, rights and obligations, complicating the tasks of exporters seeking markets in all three countries.
"If you look at it from a long-term perspective, we're going to be more regional," said a senior American trade official, who asked to remain anonymous. "So why not start being regional now? From the point of efficiency and use of resources, it makes a lot of sense."
 
'Studying the Modalities'
 Antonio Ocaranza, press attache at the Mexican Embassy here, said: "From the beginning we have been interested in liberalizing trade in the three countries, and our long-term goal is a North American free-trade area. But for the specifics of the present negotiations, we are still studying the modalities and the requirements that such modalities would entail."
The United States is also considering something similar in the "Enterprise for the Americas" program of prospective trade agreements with South American countries willing to cut their trade barriers.
The apparent collapse in early December of a four-year effort to liberalize and revamp world trade has added impetus to the North American discussions, which would give the three partners greater leverage in dealing with the 12-nation European Community and others.
The deadlock in the global trade talks, which centered on the European Community's refusal to cut sharply its farm subsidies as much as the United States, Canada and other food exporters wanted, is likely to generate more trade conflicts as well as regional trade groupings.
The goal in North America, trade officials say, is a common set of rules embracing the maximum number of common objectives.
The three-nation free-trade agreement would include such basics as duty-free and quota-free trade in goods, a common set of customs rules, nondiscrimination in trade in services and in the treatment of investors, protection of patents, copyrights and other intellectual property and procedures for the resolution of disputes. Most of the elements already exist in the Canadian-American agreement. Where they do not, the Canadian pact might be amended, trade officials say.
 
Complications Seen
 Yet some people worry that the three-way talks might delay freer trade between the United States and Mexico. "A three-way negotiation invariably would be more complicated, increasing the likelihood that already difficult negotiations would encounter additonal delays and complications," said Senator Lloyd Bentsen, Democrat of Texas and chairman of the Senate Finance Committee. Mr. Crosbie countered that "there isn't any reason for trilateral discussions to take any longer than bilateral ones."
One possibility, said Jeffrey J. Schott, a trade specialist at the Institute for International Economics, a Washington policy research group, is a two-track negotiation. The United States and Mexico would be left to decide tariff-cutting and other specific bilateral trade issues while the three Governments would work together on administrative and institutional provisions to insure a commonality with the existing United States-Canadian free-trade pact.
President Bush and President Carlos Salinas de Gortari of Mexico announced that they would seek a free-trade pact only six months ago. In August, Canada's Prime Minister, Brian Mulroney, said he wanted to explore the possibility of participating, suggesting that what Mexico and the United States decided would inevitably touch Canada.
Canadian labor organizations are worried, for example, that products made by cheap Mexican labor -- wages of less than $1 an hour -- would flood Canada through the United States. Wages in both countries average above $10 an hour. The wage differential is a touchy point also with American labor groups, which fear the impact of low-cost imports on such industries as textile, steel, cement and on fruit and vegetable growing in such states as Texas, Florida and California.
What Canada hopes to gain from freer trade with Mexico is similar to what excites American industry. Mexico is a market with more than 80 million people who are growing more prosperous under the stimulus of trade and economic changes.
Canada's trade with Mexico is still modest -- $2.5 billion of imports and exports -- but the volume has been growing by more than 10 percent annually since the mid-1980's and it expanded by 25 percent in 1989.
Canada and the United States are each other's largest customers, exchanging more than $150 billion of goods and services. The United States is Mexico's biggest export outlet. Mexico is the third-ranking customer of the United States after Canada and Japan. The annual two-way trade of Mexico and the United States amounts to more than $60 billion.
President Salinas has fixed on free trade with the United States as the centerpiece of his efforts to open up the Mexican economy, following cuts in tax rates, relaxed currency and other controls and the aggressive sale of state enterprises. He sees free trade as a spur to both investment and growth.
At a meeting in November in Monterrey, Mexico, Presidents Salinas and Bush envisaged conclusion of a pact by the end of 1992. Mr. Bush notifed Congress on Sept. 25 that he intended to begin the trade talks with Mexico.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
World Markets Begin 1991 Pressed by Problems of 1990

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
World stock markets begin 1991 facing the threat of a war in the Persian Gulf, recessions or economic slowdowns around the world, and dismal earnings -- the same factors that drove stock markets down sharply in 1990. Yet many market watchers expect a better year than the one ending today.
In 1990, these pressures resulted in a worse year for stocks than even 1987, when stock markets crashed in October. 
 The Financial Times World index for 24 markets, excluding the United States, dropped 30.8 percent in local currency in 1990, compared with a 1.7 percent dip by the end of 1987. For American investors, the sharp drop in the dollar cut the 1990 decline to 24.6 percent. But in 1987, the dollar's fall and a smaller decline in equities resulted in a 24.2 percent gain. While stock markets are open today in the United States, Britain and Cananda, most markets ended the year on Friday.
Many forecasts for 1991 are based on what could turn out to be optimistic assumptions: a quick recovery from a recession in the United States, a peaking of interest rates worldwide, no war or a quick war in the Persian Gulf and a cap on oil prices. If these assumptions do not hold, markets could be in for another troubling year.
The abrupt resignation earlier this month of the Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze, provided a vivid reminder of the fragility of President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's experiment in the Soviet Union. An unraveling there -- or more problems in Eastern Europe -- could also send stock markets tumbling, especially in Germany and Europe.
Striking a pessimistic note, Madhav Dhar, global strategist for Morgan Stanley & Company in New York, said: "The economic, political and corporate outlook is not right. The markets are not reacting as if the news is gloomy. We think the fundamental troubles around the world are not fully discounted in the prices. We think the markets are poised to respond to good news and that is a mistake."
Sander Cseh of Cseh International in Philadelphia was more optimistic. "We could have a lot of good things happening, even if there is a war," he said. "If it is a short war, you are going to have a huge decline in oil prices, and that will benefit cyclical companies. If gasoline prices drop, consumers will be more optimistic."
The uncertainties in the outlook for 1991 revolve around the assumptions for the pace of the slowdowns in Europe and Japan, the length and depth of the recessions in the United States and elsewhere, and the course of interest rates. Stock analysts also differ over the impact of a capital shortage around the world as both the Japanese and the Germans keep money at home that used to buoy markets abroad.
But analysts seem to agree that the motivation for buying equities in 1991 is the attractive values one can find, not the prospect of strong earnings. "The most important factor in the market in 1991 is the valuations," said Guy Rigden, director of strategy at UBS Phillips & Drew in London.
In addition, many analysts think that stock markets have further to fall because they have rebounded since the lows reached last fall.
While there is a split on the outlook for 1991, there is general agreement, in hindsight, on much of what went wrong in 1990, a year that began with some forecasts of 15 percent increases in markets.
Not only did the experts not foresee the invasion of Kuwait, but they missed the quickness of the economic decline and the high level at which interest rates have remained. Even before Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, major stock markets around the world were in a swoon or ready for one because of the high interest rates and the deterioration of the economy over all.
 
'A Match Touched to Tinder'
 "I believe that the Aug. 2 hostilities were simply a match touched to tinder that was already dry," said Lawrence W. Harris 3d, director of of Lombard Odier International in London.
In the end, 1990 was a year of new highs and new lows for many stock markets. In Japan, the Nikkei 225 index of leading stocks plunged 28 percent from a record high of 38,915.87 at the end of 1989 to 28,002.07 by April 2. After the Iraqi invasion it slumped to below 20,000 before closing Friday for the year at 23,848.71, down 38.7 percent for the year.
New Zealand's Barclay's index is at its lowest level in more than six years. In Australia, the All Ordinaries dropped to a 30-month low. And in Norway, despite the benefit its oil-rich economy receives from higher prices, the All Share index fell from a record high on the day of the Iraqi invasion to a string of 1990 lows at the end of the year.
One surprise is that two countries now thought to be in recessions -- the United States and Britain -- have performed relatively well. For the year through Friday, the Financial Times index for Wall Street is down 7.2 percent, the sixth-best performance of the 24 markets in local currencies. Britain's market is No. 4 in dollar terms, up 4.6 percent because the strong pound wiped out the market's local losses when results were turned to dollars.
 
Interest Rates
 A main factor in the performance of markets last year was interest rates, which are likely to have a strong effect next year, too. In Europe, the interest rate impact is heightened by the discipline of the European Monetary System, which links the currencies of 10 countries. In effect, it has forced Europe to match the high-interest-rate policy of Germany, even though the Government in Britain, where the economy is in a recession, and the Governments in France and Italy, where the economies are slowing down, would like to cut rates to lessen the economic damage.
The Milan stock market, which had been the pick of analysts last spring, wound up falling 28.8 percent in local terms, according to the Financial Times index, the fourth-worst performance of the year. In dollar terms, the loss was reduced to 20 percent. For 1991, many economists predict Italy will have one of the slowest-growing economies in Europe.
The French market, another popular one earlier in the year, also fell sharply as France's economy began to slow and it felt the backlash of the Persian Gulf crisis. The Paris Bourse was down 25.7 percent for the year in local terms and 15.7 percent in dollars, according to the Financial Times Index.
In Britain, despite the emerging recession, the market was one of the best performers of the year for dollar investors, with a rise of 4.6 percent. But for locals it was not that bad either, declining 12.2 percent in pounds, the eighth-best performance in the index. A key reason the market suffered less than others was North Sea oil production, which insulated Britain and its stock market from the impact of the sharp jump in oil prices.
Britain is also one of the markets of choice for many managers next year, partly because of the good values in the market.
 
Shipping Stocks Hurt
 Norway is another oil exporter whose stock market fared better than others. Unlike Britain's, Norway's market had a sharp decline after the invasion because the crisis hurt shipping stocks. The British market had most of its decline before the invasion because of its faltering economy and high interest rates. Norway's market finished fifth in local currency and dollars, declining 8.9 percent kroner and increasing 1.9 percent in dollar terms.
The Frankfurt market soared to a record high in March amid euphoria about Eastern Europe, only to succumb to the worries about the cost of German unificiation later in the spring.
Rallies after that were halted when economic worries came to the fore and Iraq invaded Kuwait. For the year, the market is down 20.1 percent in marks. The strong mark halved that loss to 9.7 percent for dollar investors.
Analysts are divided on the outlook for 1991, with some still worried about the negative consequences for equities of the cost of German unification and the potential for even another rise in German interest rates. But the longer-term benefits of German unification and the fact that the economy is expected to be one of the strongest performers in Europe make it attractive to some.
Tokyo's stock market was the worst performing stock market in the Financial Times index, falling 40.4 percent in yen terms, a notch worse than the 39.2 percent plunge in New Zealand's stock market.
The Japanese market's fall from a record high at the end of 1989 was swift, as the market was undermined by a weakening yen, rising interest rates, higher oil prices and politicial uncertainty.
As with Germany, there is a divided opinion over the Japanese outlook. Many managers prefer to stay away because of the high cost of Japanese stocks. But others, including Merrill Lynch, are betting that interest rates in Japan may finally be at their peak, given recent data showing the economy to be slowing.
 
Mexico Earns the Top Rank
 Mexico's market rose 97 percent in pesos and 79.2 percent in dollar terms, giving it the top ranking for the third year in a row. The Government's success in dealing with its debt and its effort to open the economy have made the stock market a great bet.
Hong Kong eked out a gain of 4.9 percent in local terms and 5 percent in dollars, making it the second- and third-ranked market, respectively. Its performance was based on the strong rally in the first half that finally brought it back from the low levels it hit after the massacre in Tiananmen Square in Beijing in June 1989.
"Hong Kong is marching to a different tune than to what is going on in the rest of the world," said Mr. Harris of Lombard Odier Internatinal. The market, he added, is less affected by high interest rates and the Persian Gulf crisis than most other stock markets. Analysts still like it because its stocks are cheap and they are a little less worried about the impact from the takeover by China in 1997.
Austria, one of the smallest European markets, also did well relatively, falling only 4.4 percent in schillings and rising 7.7 percent in dollars, making it the second-best performer of the year. But part of the reason it held up so well was the cushion it gained from a huge rally at the beginning of the year on the back of Eastern Europe euphoria. While the German market was up just 5 percent in the first six months of the year, Austria's market jumped almost 33 percent.

HEADLINE: Sandinistas Admit Missiles Went to Salvador

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
In their first such admission, Sandinista army officers have acknowledged that advanced weapons were smuggled recently from Nicaragua to guerrillas fighting in El Salvador, Administration officials said today. But the Sandinistas have pledged to United States diplomats that such aid has been halted, the officials said.
The Bush Administration was gratified by the admission, which the officials said came at a meeting between senior Sandinista officers and United States diplomats in Managua twoweeks ago. But officials expressed disbelief that the Nicaraguans had stopped their arms smuggling entirely. 
 Attempts to contact Sandinista officials for comment were unsuccessful.
The Administration officials also said there was evidence that the Sandinistas were transferring explosives to a small guerrilla group in Honduras to attack American military personnel based there.
An Administration official said, "I don't believe it is their intention to stop or that this is an isolated incident."
The reported smuggling occurred since President Violeta Barrios de Chamorro of Nicaragua took office bydefeating the Sandinista leader, Daniel Ortega de Saavedra, in elections last February. Although she is the national leader, she has limited control over the armed forces. Mr. Ortega's brother, Gen. Humberto Ortega de Saavedra, remains the army commander.
Mrs. Chamorro has privately told President Alfredo Cristiani of El Salvador that she will do all she can to halt the arms flow.
Mrs. Chamorro's seeming inability to rein in the Sandinista military has caused growing friction among conservatives serving in her coalition Government and has also led to growing protests and violence among the former contra rebels who once fought to oust the Sandinistas.
"The Sandinista Front," said Bernard W. Aronson, Assistant Secretary of State for Inter-American Affairs, "is squandering every ounce of good will they won when they accepted the results of the Nicaraguan elections by continuing to arm and supply violent revolutionaries in El Salvador."
During the Reagan Administration, charges that Nicaragua served as a conduit for Soviet bloc arms shipments to the Salvadoran rebels led to a cutoff of United States economic aid to Managua and the launching of the contra rebels.
Sandinista arms shipments arose again as a vital issue in United States-Nicaraguan relations in the last five weeks when guerrillas shot down two Salvadoran Air Force aircraft withSoviet-manufactured SA-14 missiles. The deployment of the antiaircraft missiles, the most sophisticated ever seen in El Salvador, has forced Salvadoran pilots to fly well above heights considered desirable for maximum battle effectiveness.
The new check against the air force represented a major advantage for the Farabundo Marti National Liberation Front rebels as they began their most effective offensive in more than a year.
The increase in fighting led the Administration to announce it would rush $48.1 million in military aid to San Salvador months ahead of schedule. The Salvadoran guerrillas announced Saturday that they were ending their offensive to give peace talks another chance.
The Sandinistas called for the meeting with United States officials shortlyafter the Soviet Foreign Ministry identified the serial number of a SA-14 missile casing found near a downed Salvadoran AC-46 gunship as the same imprinted on a missile Moscow donated to the Nicaraguan Army in 1985.
An Administration official said the Sandinista officers, whom he would not identify, had said, "Yes, indeed there had been a diversion, but it had been a small one."
The officers said an air force captain had smuggled six anti-aircraft missiles to El Salvador in the last few months before his unauthorized action was discovered. They promised that the smuggling was being investigated and that all involved would be prosecuted and punished.
The Salvadoran guerrillas have publicly denied that they received the missiles from Nicaragua.
