HEADLINE: Successor To Dukakis Ready to Be Unpopular

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass., Dec. 30

 BODY:
With the Massachusetts economy battered by recession and the state budget hemorrhaging red ink, William F. Weld prepares to take office on Thursday as the state's first Republican governor in almost two decades.
So grave is the situation that even some of Mr. Weld's advisers worry that he will be only a sacrificial one-term Governor. But sitting in the book-lined living room of his spacious house here, the 45-year-old former United States Attorney said he believed the job "is doable." 
 To balance the budget, Mr. Weld said, "I'm certainly prepared to take some fiscal actions that are wildly unpopular." He said he planned to offer a package of tough budget cuts early in January.
"We'll have to take away some things that some people have gotten used to," Mr. Weld said.
 
Proposal on Deficit
 He indicated that some of the reductions he would recommend to the Legislature, which is still solidly controlled by Democrats, would come in the area of social services, like Medicaid and welfare. Others would come through reorganizing state government, like changing the way the state now pays for much of Boston's transit system.
But he would not say how big he believed the deficit in the $13.4 billion state budget actually is, a matter of widespread dispute. Estimates range from $100 million to $700 million.
Representative Richard A. Voke, the chairman of the State House Ways and Means Committee, said he believed that without large cuts the state would end the current fiscal year on June 30 with a deficit of up to $500 million. Because half the fiscal year is already gone, Mr. Weld will have to make reductions of about $1 billion on an annual basis, or 7.5 percent of the total budget, to get it in balance.
"I don't know how you make those kinds of cuts in that period of time," Mr. Voke said.
Moreover, Mr. Weld is required to submit his budget proposal for the fiscal year 1992 on Feb. 27, just 55 days after he takes office.
 
Previous Budget Cuts
 The crisis confronting the incoming Governor is compounded by several other factors. Gov. Michael S. Dukakis, a Democrat who decided against running for re-election, has already cut $1 billion out of the budget in the past two years, including laying off 6,000 state employees and reducing spending for higher education by 15 percent.
"So a lot of the so-called fat that Bill talked about has already been trimmed and the next cuts are going to be from the bone, whole programs like closing a state college," said a Boston businessman with close ties to Mr. Weld who spoke on condition of anonymity.
In addition, Mr. Weld takes office at a time when the recession in Massachusetts is continuing to deepen, meaning that state tax revenues are likely to drop further, while the demand for social services like unemployment compensation and Medicaid will increase, thus adding to the deficit.
Unemployment in the state jumped from 6.3 percent in October to 7 percent in November, more than double the rate of two years ago, the United States Labor Department says. And business failures in Massachusetts in the first nine months of the year more than tripled from the same period in 1989, by far the worst rate in the nation, according to the Dun & Bradstreet Corporation. Further large layoffs are expected in the state's troubled high technology companies in January.
 
Pledge on Taxes
 Mr. Weld possibly added to his troubles when he pledged in the campaign that he would not raises taxes during the next four years.
"It will be fiscal triage, a revolution," said an adviser to Mr. Weld who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "To get the budget under control, it will require a radically reduced state work force and radically reduced and restructured state services."
Richard Manley, the president of the Massachusetts Taxpayers Foundation, a nonpartisan group financed by businesses, agreed. "I think it will be four years of brutal cuts," he said.
At the heart of the trouble, Mr. Manley said, is a deficit stemming from social programs that must be eliminated. This deficit, of at least $1 billion, was created as large programs like Medicaid, welfare, and pensions and health insurance for state employees grew twice as fast as state revenues, and that was in normal times, Mr. Manley said. In the past two years state revenues have fallen.
 
Voter Anger Over Dukakis
 But Mr. Manley said he is confident Mr. Weld will succeed. "Sometimes just a change of faces is a big improvement," he said, pointing out that much of the public anger in Massachusetts was directed at Governor Dukakis, who campaigned for President in 1988 on the strength of the "Massachusetts Miracle," the state's enormous economic growth in the 1980's.
"I also think that if Weld gets the budget in balance, despite the pain of the cuts, the public reaction won't be as harsh as you might think," Mr. Manley said.
Mr. Weld said he thinks his job will be made somewhat easier because he received a popular mandate to restructure state government. Mr. Weld defeated John R. Silber, the president of Boston University, by a ratio of only 52 percent to 48 percent. But Mr. Weld said those figures were deceptive, since he believed "the real election" occurred in the September primary, when voters picked him and Dr. Silber as the two candidates most seen as outsiders and likely to bring about change.
Mr. Weld said he has spoken extensively with the Democratic leaders of the State House and Senate and is convinced that they share his view that decisive action must be taken in January and February to cut the deficit.
 
Proposed Cuts in Social Service
 If the Governor and the Legislature do not act before March, he said, it would be difficult for him to go to Wall Street to borrow further to cover the deficit. Massachusetts already has the lowest credit rating of any state.
Mr. Weld said many of his proposed cuts would come from the social services area, like Medicaid, welfare and health insurance for state employees. "We've outdone ourselves in an attempt to be the most generous state in the nation," Mr. Weld said. "I think we've gone beyond providing for the needy, down on their luck, to the notion that human services benefits have become a middle-class entitlement."
One of his proposals will be to reorganize the state Medicaid system, turning it into a managed health care system like a commercial health maintenance organization.

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Post 24.1% Decline

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools dropped 24.1 percent in November from the October level, as concerns about the weakening economy and the possibility of war in the Persian Gulf dampened demand for factory machinery.
Orders totaled $203.4 million last month, compared with $268.1 million in October, according to a monthly report by the Association for Manufacturing Technology, a trade group in McLean, Va. 
 Compared with November 1989, when machine tool orders amounted to $184.4 million, orders last month rose 10.3 percent.
Machine tools are sophisticated machines, costing $500,000 or more, that cut or shape the metal parts that are used to make a variety of products, ranging from aircraft to kitchen appliances. Economists consider orders for these machines a good indicator of manufacturers' willingness to invest in their factories.
Actual shipments of machine tools rose 5.9 percent in November, to $268.7 million, from $253.8 million in October. But compared with a year earlier, when shipments amounted to $313.7 million, orders fell 14.3 percent.
While orders for machine tools are carefully watched for hints on plans for capital spending, they tend to vary widely from month to month.
"Orders for any individual month are really quite meaningless because of the large chunk of orders that can come in at any time," said Richard C. Kleinfeldt, vice president of finance for Giddings & Lewis Inc., a machine tool company in Fond du Lac, Wis., which supplies automated systems used to assemble General Motors' new line of Saturn automobiles.
Orders often drop in November and December because the automobile companies, which are large buyers, historically shut down for several weeks at the end of the year, said James J. Paper, a machine-tool industry analyst with Kirkpatrick, Pettis, Smith, Polian Inc., an investment firm in Omaha.
Mr. Paper attributed the sharp decline in November orders to the weak economy, the historical seasonal slowdown and the volatility of monthly orders for machine tools.
"The important thing is that this November is better than last November," he said.
 
'Understandable Wariness'
 Mr. Paper said industry executives had not reported large cancellations of orders, which often occur in a severe economic downturn. He also said that the number of inquiries from customers asking for price quotes on new machines was growing but that it was not clear how many of the inquiries would result in orders.
"Concern about the economy and the uncertainty in the Persian Gulf have created understandable wariness in recent months," said Albert W. Moore, president of the Association for Manufacturing Technology. He added, however, that "there are fundamental reasons for optimism" in the machine tools business, citing the drop in the value of the dollar, which has made American tools cheaper overseas and has spurred exports.
November orders for metal-cutting tools were $137.5 million, down 24.5 percent from the $182 million in October. Orders were up 6.7 percent from November 1989. when they were $128.9 million.
Orders for metal-forming tools amounted to $66 million, a 23 percent drop from the $85.7 million in October. Orders increased 18.9 percent, from $55.5 million a year ago.
The machine tool backlog for November 1990 was $1.52 billion, compared with $1.59 billion in October. The backlog in November 1989 was $1.98 billion.

HEADLINE: St. Louis Journal;
Moving to Future, City Finds Gap in the Present

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Dec. 30

 BODY:
In the long term, St. Louis of ficials and boost ers expect to see a downtown convention complex doubled in size, complete with a 70,000-seat domed stadium and a new 1,100-room hotel, a complex large enough to make the city a leader in the nation's convention business.
In recent years, St. Louis has seemed to have everything to offer major conventions like the national political conventions or the American Bar Association -- everything but space. Now it is trying to remedy that. 
 But the effort to reach that goal has cost St. Louis a 614-room hotel in mint condition, the only large hotel adjacent to the existing convention center and one for which conventions were already scheduled. The hotel, the Sheraton Convention Center, was scheduled to close a year from now to make room for the new stadium, but its owners are closing it on Wednesday, a year early.
"There was a very dramatic erosion in the kind of business we normally were having," said Brice R. Smith Jr., chief executive of the Sverdrup Corporation, the major investor in the hotel, which is managed by the I.T.T. Corporation. "The facts of the matter are people have already decided the building has closed."
Occupancy was down by 15 percent since 1989, Mr. Smith said. The declining occupancy rate coupled with the a traditionally slow first quarter meant the 13-year-old hotel would lose at least $2.5 million by staying open another 11 months.
The Sheraton and the Cervantes Convention Center have been linked since their birth in the late 1970's, and city officials say building the center would have been out of the question without a hotel of the Sheraton's size across the street. The future of the 19-story Sheraton has been in doubt since the mid-1980's when plans to expand the convention center were first discussed. But it was the stadium that doomed it.
The city hopes to regain a National Football League franchise with the new stadium as a lure, and when there are no games it would be used for conventions. Once the stadium became part of the $500 million expansion project, the only feasible location, the city decided, included the hotel site.
Officials counted on the Sheraton to remain open through November 1991, however, to narrow the gap between it being torn down and a larger replacement being built. Plans for the larger hotel have fallen through once, and officals are talking to developers about the prospects for it.
The sudden announcement on Dec. 13 that the hotel would close on Jan. 2 sent tourism officials scrambling to find enough rooms in other hotels for the 38 conventions already booked at the Sheraton for 1991. So far, 34 have been relocated.
Mr. Smith said he agonized over the closing, especially because of the 300 employees who would lose their jobs. "It's really kind of a bad thing to do at Christmas," he said. "I hated that."
The announcement stunned the hotel's employees. "It surprised us because we were taking reservations for up to the end of 1991. It's a shock," said Mrs. Vickie Stephenson, an administrative assistant in the executive offices who has worked at the Sheraton for nine and a half years.
The employees were not the only ones taken by surprise. So was Marvin Lewis, the chairman of the national convention commission of the Brotherhood of Elks who has spent nearly four years arranging the organization's convention in July 1991. The convention would attract about 20,000 Elks, relatives and guests, he said, and would likely bring $10 million to the St. Louis economy.
"We're still trying to work with the city of St. Louis," Mr. Lewis said . "I like the people and the amusements for families. You have one of the finest zoos in the world; you have the river."
Keith Arnold, president the St. Louis Convention and Visitors Commission, and his staff hastily put together a new plan for the Elks, which found substitutes for every lost room and kept each state delegation together.
While Mr. Lewis and the Elks have yet to accept the new proposal, Mr. Smith and St. Louis officials prefer to see the closing as a temporary setback.
Some people even see an immediate silver lining. J. Christopher Grace, head of the city's St. Louis Development Corportion, expects the closing to boost the 1991 occupancy rates for the remaining hotels in the city. He added that the closing of the Sheraton might make it easier to find a developer for a larger hotel, because of the greater urgency of getting the project going.
As for the Elks, Mr. Grace said, "You never want to lose something, but in an economy in the billions, $10 million is insignificant."

HEADLINE: EMPLOYERS SHUN SUB-MINIMUM WAGE

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Although a law that took effect on April 1 allows employers to hire teen-agers at less than the minimum wage of $3.80 an hour, few of them have elected to go below this level, an initial survey indicates.
A survey of 167 fast-food restaurants by two university economists found that only three were using the sub-minimum wage, which ranges between $3.35 an hour and $3.80. Most of the restaurants, all part of the Burger King, Kentucky Fried Chicken or Wendy's chains, said they did not think they could attract acceptable workers at the new sub-minimum wage. 
 They also expressed concern that paying some young employees less than others would be demoralizing and disruptive -- a criticism that leaders of the American Federation of Labor and Congress of Industrial Organizations have often leveled at the concept of two-tier pay scales.
The behavior of the companies is in sharp contrast to the importance President Bush attached to the sub-minimum wage. When Congress passed a bill last year that raised the minimum wage to $3.80 on April 1, 1990, and $4.25 next April 1, Mr. Bush threatened a veto unless a sub-minimum wage was provided for teen-agers. Congress agreed to include that provision for three years.
The President contended that companies would resist hiring and training disadvantaged teen-agers unless they were allowed to pay less than the new minimum for the first six months of the new worker's employment. A White House spokesman, Stephen Hart, declined to comment today on the survey, which studied the 167 restaurants in Texas, where many fast-food outlets have historically paid young workers no more than Federal law requires. Fast-food companies had been among the supporters of the separate wage scale for teen-agers.
 
A Social Role
 Lawrence F. Katz, a Harvard Univerity labor economist, and Alan B. Krueger of Princeton University, the co-authors of the survey, said the response of the fast-food restaurants suggested that the minimum wage played a social role beyond the supply-and-demand laws of economics. The Federal minimum wage has come to be accepted by most employers and employees as a sort of guide to the least that should be paid for the most menial jobs, they added.
Officials at the Labor Department's Bureau of Labor Statistics said they knew of no other study that had attempted to measure the use of the new sub-minimum wage. Indeed, future studies might turn up more use of the sub-minimum wage. But the bureau's wage and employment data tend to confirm the survey's findings.
Month after month, for example, bureau data show that about 2 percent of the nation's employed workers earn less than the minimum wage, including 6 percent of the 16-to-19-year-old wage group, the one most affected by the sub-minimum wage. This 2 percent receives less than the legal minimum because of loopholes in the law or violations of it. And since April 1, the percentage has not risen.
The bureau's unemployment data also fails to turn up evidence bearing out the Administration's concern that joblessness would rise among teen-agers without the sub-minimum wage to offset the April 1 rise in the minimum to $3.80 an hour, from $3.35.
Although few employers appear to have resorted to the sub-minimum wage, the teen-age unemployment rate remained unchanged until late summer. Bureau officials and most private economists attribute the increase not to wage levels, but to the downturn in the national economy.
 
No Unusual Changes Reported
 Mr. Katz and Mr. Krueger, who presented their survey at the annual conference of the American Economic Association, which ended here today, found that the fast-food outlets they surveyed did not report unusual changes in employment levels and hours worked after the new minimum wage went into effect.
Nor did they report cutbacks in fringe benefits, although a majority of the employees at fast-food restaurants work part-time and do not qualify for health insurance and other benefits.
Still, some economists argue that the April 1 increase in the minimum wage put relatively little pressure on employer costs because the minimum had remained at the old level, $3.35 an hour, since 1981, and during that unusually long period many companies found themselves moving their wage scales above the minimum. In this view, the minimum might begin to hurt employers when it rises to $4.25 an hour on April 1. That might prompt more companies to resort to the sub-minimum, which will rise at that time to $3.80 an hour, some economists say.
 
Little Training Required
 Finally, most people earning only the minimum wage are in retail trades, with a heavy concentration among fast-food outlets. The jobs they occupy usually require only a few days of training to master. The sub-minimum wage provision, on the other hand, allows an employer to pay a teen-ager $3.35 to $3.80 an hour for the first three months on the job, even without offering training. To extend this special pay level pay for an additional three months, training must be involved.
"The fact there is very little use of the sub-minimum means that employers are not chomping at the bit to train young people and being prevented from doing so by the minimum wage," Mr. Krueger said.
Sixty of the 167 outlets surveyed had been paying the old minimum of $3.35 an hour and they reported jumping to $3.80 an hour on April 1. Most of the rest had been paying above the old minimum to teen-agers and they generally increased their wages by enough to keep their pay above the new minimum.
 
Upward Shift for Thousands
 The hourly wages of thousands of people earning just above the minimum shift upward when the minimum does. The Texas survey found this phenomenon and other studies, in the past, have also reported it.
Mr. Katz and Mr. Krueger selected Texas because it is one of the 25 states that does not have a minimum wage law that could override the national minimum.
The new sub-minimum wage scale replaces one that had been in existence since 1961 but was limited to full-time students who worked part-time. Studies showed that the only employers to use that wage were universities and movie-theater chains.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
With the Bedouins Banished, Jordan's Caves Lose Tourists

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WADI MUSA, Jordan, Dec. 28

 BODY:
Two thousand years ago, when the Nabatean civilization was at its peak, some 25,000 people lived here in Petra, long Jordan's most celebrated tourist attraction. Last year at this time, 1,200 people a day came here. This year on New Year's Eve, the Petra Forum Hotel has reservations for five.
"The gulf crisis is more of a disaster for us than for Iraq," said Nyazi Shabaan, the 35-year-old director of tourism for Petra and this town of 12,000, which depend on tourists for their survival. "I don't know what we will do if they don't come back." 
 This town is a microcosm of Jordan's ruined tourist economy, a major source of hard currency. Only three months ago, a delegation from Unesco came here to consider ways to limit the 130,000 tourists who poured each year into these long-hidden remains of one of the great trading cities of ancient times. But Petra is now nearly as silent as it was for the 1,000 years when it languished as a lost city.
Already devastated by the Persian Gulf crisis, Jordan was dealt a further blow this week when the State Department asked all Americans on nonessential business in this country, Yemen, Mauritania, and the Sudan -- all deemed sympathetic to President Saddam Hussein of Iraq -- to leave the area.
This town has been impoverished by the gulf crisis. The Petra Forum Hotel, normally the busiest hotel in Jordan, whose 82 rooms are usually booked weeks in advance and which began expanding to 160 rooms before Aug. 2, is now practically empty. One night last week, there were only 25 guests, of whom 14 were members of a Japanese group, one of the few tours that did not cancel.
 
Halt to Construction
 Two more expensive hotels were planned by private Jordanian investors, but the plans are now on hold. Three more modest guest houses in Wadi Musa were already being built, but this construction, too, has stopped.
In normal times, the tourism office employed 15 to 20 guides a season, Mr. Shabaan said. It has now been forced to let most of them go. The town's 350 horses, each of which used to bring its owner about $200 a month, now earn less than $10 each. "It costs between $30 and $40 a month just to feed a horse," he said.
Mr. Shabaan is just old enough to remember what life was like in Petra before tourists began coming in such vast numbers. An archeologist trained at the University of Amman, he is no ordinary tourism director. He was born in one of the many caves to which tourists have flocked and in which the Nabateans lived, and he is one of the eight sons of an very poor Bedouin family, which like most of the Bedouins only a decade ago made the caves their home.
He and his family used to sleep by a "very cozy" fire in the middle of the cave. "It was wonderful to have this place to yourself at night," he said.
His interest in archeology came from his curiosity about the people who used to come from all over the world to stand in awe of Petra's lost civilization -- its rulers' tombs, its ancient paved streets and its giant amphitheater, which seats 4,000 people.
 
Reserved for Tourists
 The Bedouins no longer inhabit the caves. The Jordanian Government, eager to preserve its special archeological legacy and to turn Petra into a major tourist site, ordered them out of Petra five years ago. Just outside the ancient city, the Government built for them a modern village of cinder block and concrete houses, complete with running water and electricity, a school, mosque and stores.
Bedul Village, as it is called after the local tribe, now has about 1,000 inhabitants. But some Bedouins have refused to move there.
"You see the same faces in the same place," complained Ruwaih Bedul, who lives near Petra in a Bedouin tent made of coarse black wool from his own sheep. The inside of his tent was even warmer than the cave, especially since he shares it with his wife, 8 children, 25 goats and 10 sheep.
Mr. Bedul's simple life might not seem dependent on tourism. But in fact he used to earn extra money as a guard at the archeological sites, money that provided the extra tiny glass tumbler of tea, heated inside the tent in a black iron pot boiled over a fire of twigs.
"We all miss the tourists," Mr. Bedul said.
What is a disaster for the city, however, is a boon to the rare traveler here. For the celebrated rose rock city carved out of the sandstone cliffs can be seen today just as David Roberts, the English artist, must have seen it in the last century when he came here to draw its wonders.
"Petra is this country's natural treasure," Mr. Shabaan said. "It is more precious than oil. I can't help but believe people will always want to see it and will come back, God willing."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 29; Column 1; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 651 words

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
For much of Eastern Europe, 1991 is certain to be a year of swift economic change, one that aims to keep pace with the monumental political changes in the region. So far, with the first year of Eastern Europe's post-Communist era drawing to a close, the region's economies, except for Poland's, have moved only a few steps down the road to capitalism. [Page 1.]
Officials of the U.S., Canada and Mexico say they are fast sweeping away obstacles to Canadian participation in free-trade talks expected to open in 1991 between the U.S. and Mexico. The three countries are expected to begin shaping an agreement that would create a North American common market. [29.]
World stock markets begin 1991 facing the threat of war, recessions or economic slowdowns, and dismal earnings. Yet many people expect a better year than the one that ends today. [29.]
 
The Economy

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Dole Cautious on U.S. Role in Gulf Combat

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Senator Bob Dole, the Senate Republican leader, said today that putting the Emir of Kuwait back on his throne was not "worth one American life, as far as I am concerned."
Appearing on the NBC News program "Meet the Press," Senator Dole said the American people "want to make certain that President Bush has done everything, pursued every avenue, for peace before the firing starts." 
 He said that one of the things Mr. Bush should do is "pursue, at least one additional time, figuring out a date" that Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d could go to Baghdad to meet with President Saddam Hussein.
"I have sort of a gut feeling the American people are not yet committed to war," the Kansas Republican said.
Senator Dole disclosed that he had telephoned Iraq's Ambassador to the United States, who is in Baghdad, and taken from the conversation the conviction that "there is some flexibility" on setting a date for talks.
He also appeared to suggest that President Bush should show some flexibility on a date for sending Mr. Baker to Baghdad for negotiations with Iraq. Mr. Bush has said that the date of Jan. 12 proposed by Iraq is too close to the Jan. 15 deadline for Iraq's withdrawal from Kuwait.
Senator Dole said the President's ability to handle the "twin problems" of the gulf crisis and the worsening domestic economy represented "make-or-break time for George Bush."

HEADLINE: Comments on Puerto Ricans Embroil Hispanic Network

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO

 BODY:
A Cuban-American television commentator's remarks about Puerto Rican women have enraged Puerto Ricans in many cities and embroiled the nation's largest Spanish-language network in a dispute that threatens its advertising and viewer base.
The remarks, broadcast Nov. 5 nationwide on the Univision network, have led to demonstrations and demands for the dismissal of the commentator, Carlos Alberto Montaner. Network officials have offered explanations, met with protesters and repeatedly broadcast opposing views, but they have refused to take any action against Mr. Montaner. 
 Mr. Montaner's comments were made on "Portada" ("Cover" in Spanish), a one-hour weekly news program that is broadcast on Monday nights. Mr. Montaner, a syndicated columnist whose work appears in newspapers in the United States, Latin America and Europe, discussed why Puerto Ricans in the United States suffer worse economic conditions than other Hispanic groups.
"There's probably more than one explanation," he said, "but the one that seems the most important to me is this: because there is a grave family problem in the Puerto Rican ghettos of the United States, where there are thousands of single mothers, very young, who try to escape poverty through welfare or through new partners who then leave, and leave behind other children to worsen the problem."
The statement was immediately rebutted as "sexist" by another commentator, Sergio Munoz, editor of La Opinion, a Spanish-language paper in Los Angeles, and it sparked a flurry of condemnations from Puerto Rican leaders in major cities. They said Mr. Montaner's argument perpetuated stereotypes and implied that Puerto Rican women were promiscuous.
"You can cite the statistics, but where he went off base was targeting the Puerto Rican woman and making comments about sexual behavior," said Elizabeth Colon, head of the Association of Puerto Rican Executive Directors in New York City. "He then infers that we have lost our sense of family, our values."
Although Mr. Montaner later apologized for his comments, the furor has not died down, and the protests seem to be having an impact. Starting Wednesday, Goya Foods of Secaucus, N.J., one of the nation's leading producers of foods intended primarily for the Hispanic market, will no longer advertise on WXTV Channel 41, Univision's station in Secaucus.
 
Insulting a Community
 "His comments wre an insult to the Puerto Rican community," said Rafael Toro, a company spokesman. "Freedom of speech is not the right to insult a community." Officials of the company said the decision would affect only the Secaucus station and that Goya offices in other cities with Univision stations were free to make their own advertising decisions.
A coalition of 25 Puerto Rican organizations is pressing other major advertisers to follow suit, including The Coca-Cola Company and Philip Morris Companies.
Univision Holdings Inc., whose majority owner is Hallmark Cards Inc., owns eight television stations and the network, which broadcasts in more than 30 markets. The network has production studios in Miami and sales offices in New York City. New York, Miami and Los Angeles are the network's three largest markets, said Carmen Herrero Biaggi, a spokeswoman.
As a result of Mr. Montaner's remarks, El Diario/La Prensa in New York City stopped publishing his weekly column.
 
'Ignored, Abused and Mutilated'
 On a Dec. 24 broadcast of "Portada," Dr. Antonia C. Novello, the United States Surgeon General and a native of Puerto Rico, said: "There are many Antonia Novellos in this country, Puerto Ricans in New York, Cubans in Miami, Mexicans in Los Angeles. They will also succeed, despite the prejudices expressed by Mr. Montaner."
The intense emotions provoked by the Montaner comments took some Hispanic-Americans by surprise, but on reflection they said it was understandable, in light of other perceived biases against mainland Puerto Ricans. Some pointed to remarks by the former New York City Police Commissioner, Benjamin Ward, that linked them to drug buying, and to opposition in Puerto Rico to mainland Puerto Ricans' voting on the island's political future.
"The Puerto Rican community really feels like it's been ignored, abused and mutilated, and that it has to bear all these social problems and there's no real understanding of what the community has gone through," said Angelo Falcon, head of the Institute for Puerto Rican Policy, a research and advocacy group in New York.
Others said that part of the response stemmed from shock. They said Hispanics expect more sensitivity from Spanish-language media organizations, which often act as an advocate for the Hispanic-American community.
 
Network Cites Censorship
 "What appalls me even more is that the station and this gentleman are Latinos and should be interpreting facts correctly and intelligently," said John Garcia, a Puerto Rican who is a Daily News reporter on strike and Northeast region representative for the National Association of Hispanic Journalists.
Univision officials said they are willing to discuss improvements in programming but that dismissing Mr. Montaner would violate his right to give his views without fear. In an editorial broadcast Dec. 18, the network president, Joaquin Blaya, a Chilean-American, said:
"Montaner may or may not be mistaken. We personally believe that he is, and we deeply regret the effect of his words. For this we apologize. But with equal conviction we believe in his right to express his opinion and not be censored."
In a telephone interview last week Mr. Montaner, 47 years old, said that he never meant to give the impression that Puerto Rican mothers "voluntarily" try to escape poverty through welfare or by taking new boyfriends. His point, he said, was that there is a higher percentage of families headed by women among Puerto Ricans than in other Hispanic groups and, as a result, these families have less income than those led by a couple.
 
'Clumsy' Remarks
 The Census Bureau's 1989 population survey showed that 31 percent of Puerto Rican families in the United States were led by a woman, compared with 20 percent for Central and South Americans, 16 percent for Mexicans and 14 percent for Cubans.
"When the woman is left alone and has small children and can't go out to work, if she doesn't work, the income is less and the children have much fewer possibilities of a better education and of access to cultural riches," he said.
Conceding this was not what he said in his commentary, he said he was "clumsy in organizing the information." But he termed allegations that he was racist and sexist ridiculous and said he had been greatly surprised by the uproar.
After the Nov. 5 program, he went returned to "Portada" to apologize and explain his comments, but the criticism did not subside.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Carlos Alberto Montaner, whose remarks about Puerto Rican women in the United States were broadcast nationwide on the Univision network on Nov. 5. (Susan Greenwood for The New York Times) (pg. 10)

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Quayle Meets With Saudi Royalty And Seeks More Aid for Military

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: RIYADH, Saudi Arabia, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Vice President Dan Quayle met with this country's rulers today and said he wanted the Saudis and Kuwait's exiled Government to provide greater financial support to the United States military effort in the Persian Gulf.
"They will have to be making more contributions" because of the likelihood that American troops will remain in the gulf longer than initially anticipated, the Vice President told reporters before his meetings. 
 "We do expect collective responsibility to be exhibited," he said.
American officials traveling with Mr. Quayle said King Fahd had given them the impression that the Saudis would provide more financial aid, although no figures were discussed.
 
Three-Day Visit
 Mr. Quayle's three-day visit to the gulf comes two weeks before the Jan. 15 deadline set by the United Nations Security Council for an Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait.
He met separately with King Fahd and Crown Prince Abdullah shortly after his arrival. He planned to meet with the exiled Emir of Kuwait on Tuesday after a New Year's visit to some of the more than 300,000 United States troops in the region.
 
Duration of Deployment
 Aboard Air Force Two during his flight to Saudi Arabia, Mr. Quayle said he would discuss with the Saudis the American determination to get the forces of President Saddam Hussein of Iraq out of Kuwait and explain that the United States expected greater financial support as its effort grew in size and duration.
The Pentagon previously projected the cost of the deployment at $8.2 billion through the end of 1990. But the costs are expected to increase because of the additional troops currently being sent. It has been estimated that a full-scale war would cost as much as $1 billion to $2 billion a day.
"You are seeing other nations making contributions," Mr. Quayle said on the flight. He said he would thank the Saudis and Kuwaitis "for the contributions they have made thus far, but point out that more contributions will have to be made because the crisis is going to continue."
 
Billions Already Contributed
 So far, Saudi Arabia has contributed $9 billion, nearly half of it in cash and the rest in military support for United States troops in Saudi Arabia, according to the State Department. Kuwait has given $2.5 billion, United States officials said.
Saudi Arabia also has spent $3.9 billion and Kuwait $3.3 billion to help countries like Turkey and Egypt, which have suffered severe economic repercussions from the United Nations-imposed economic sanctions against Iraq.
Mr. Quayle will spend much of New Year's Day visiting with American troops. Among those deployed to the region are units of the Indiana National Guard, in which he served during the Vietnam War. He was later embroiled in controversy over whether he had used family connections to get into the Guard unit rather than risk being drafted and sent to Vietnam.

HEADLINE: Selling Books to be Cherished, Not Just Read

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES

 BODY:
The world of rare books, battered by soaring prices, a scarcity of good volumes and a shift by American collectors away from foreign language manuscripts and books, is struggling to redefine itself.
The large houses that once dominated the trade have been hurt by auction houses that now sell directly to the collector and by the growing number of small dealers that specialize in one or two lines of books, such as Roman histories or medical texts. 
 The number of good books available on the market has fallen dramatically and those that are available have shot up in price, in large part because of big acquisitions in the last two decades by academic institutions and libraries.
But most worrying to dealers in ancient and classical texts, the taste in the American market has changed as fewer collectors are familiar with, or interested in, Greek, Latin and foreign-language books.
 
The Decline of a Bookseller
 "The really rare and important material has tended to diminish on the market," said Kenneth A. Lohf, the librarian for rare books and manuscripts at Columbia University. "There are not as many key items being sold today."
H. P. Kraus, at 16 East 46th Street, Manhattan, once had stock that ranged from illuminated manuscripts to James Joyce. At its height, two decades ago, it employed 16 people in its New York City store, 200 in Mamaroneck, N.Y., where it produced reprints and periodicals, and 100 more in Liechtenstein who were dedicated to the same task.
But H. P. Kraus, like many of the larger rare-book houses, is now a shadow of its former self, especially with the death of its founder and owner, Hans P. Kraus, in 1988.
"I am not so sure we will ever be able to achieve something like these great bookstores again," Mr. Lohf said. "It takes a great deal of money to stock a book shop like H. P. Kraus, almost impossible today."
 
A Glory of the Past
 Many of the great shops that had vast, diverse quantities of manuscripts and books, such as the baronial John F. Fleming Rare Book Company, which was at 322 East 57th Street in Manhattan, have closed and have not been replaced.
Mr. Fleming's shop had 20-foot ceilings, English woodwork, heavy tapestries and paintings, including a life-size portrait of the actor David Garrick. Mr. Fleming used to work at a 20-foot-long Spanish refectory table surrounded by polished oak bookcases. The shop was closed after Mr. Fleming died in 1987 and its contents were sold at auction.
"The whole trade used to be enormous," said Lucien Goldschmidt, whose Lucien Goldschmidt Inc. was one of the best known rare-book shops in New York until 1987. "When I first came to New York in 1937 you could spend weeks going from one rare-book dealer to another."
H. P. Kraus has scaled back, dealing only in manuscripts and early printed books. The current owners are selling off some of Mr. Kraus's collection and estimate they do two-thirds of their business with European collectors, said an owner, Mary Ann Folter.
 
The Demand Slackens
 "It is much more difficult now to buy old books," said Roland Folter, the shop's director. "If we had the same demand we had 20 or 30 years ago it would be practically impossible to fill that demand."
But the demand is not what it was. The libraries that spent lavishly 20 years ago to aquire rare volumes and manuscripts are no longer major factors in the market. And their aquisitions were so extensive that the available volumes on the market were significantly reduced.
The business is not large financially.
Worldwide sales for rare books do not top $500 million, said George S. Lowry, the president of Swann Galleries, 104 East 25th Street, Manhattan, the largest book-auction house in the country.
"The book auctions in the United States amount to about $50 million a year in sales," Mr. Lowry said. "The book sellers do about $150 million a year in sales."
"The biggest book sellers probably do under $5 million a year in sales," Mr. Lowry said. "The smaller ones probably do under $100,000 a year in sales."
 
Relying on Fewer to Make More
 And many of these houses now depend on a handful of very high priced items to make their profits, rather than the vast amounts of scholarly material that sold so well in the past. In the 1979-80 season, for example, Swann sold 17,600 lots for a total of $2.9 million; a decade later, it sold about 10,000 lots for $6.1 million.
But those collectors who can afford the high prices often have very different interests from their predecessors. And the lack of versatility by dealers has been matched by a change in the academic background of many collectors.
"Americans are not well versed in foreign languages," said T. Peter Kraus, a distant relative of Hans P. Kraus and the owner of Ursus Rare Books Ltd., at 981 Madison Avenue, near 77th Street, one of the few open rare-book stores left in New York. "The European emigrants who came over before the war, and were the major rare-book dealers, had a general education in foreign and classical languages. Today most dealers only speak English and this limits them to English-language books."
"The decline in classical education has meant that books in Latin, Greek or Italian hold less appeal to today's collectors," added William J. Wyer, the head of rare books at Ursus.
 
Specialists Are More Common
 And even those who continue to collect rare books in the United States increasingly lack the broad knowledge that typified older collectors.
"You have people who specialize in one author," said Mr. Wyer, "who know everything about Charles Dickens's books, but a general connoisseurship is rarely found in collectors today."
"Collectors are now going the same route as the dealers," Mr. Lohf said. "They are specializing."
"There are fewer people who want to assemble the tangible proofs of the history of our intellectual development," the 78-year-old Mr. Goldschmidt said. "Many people only want to collect a few high points. This is part of the whole downgrading of the role of literature in American society and the increase in the role of the image. Now people are far less tempted to read. They are forever looking at pictures. You go into a fashionable bookstore and a large part of the books are hardly books at all."
The decline of the established houses, which could handle large stocks and ran shops that were accessible to the public, has hurt the collector's ability to gain general knowledge about the field.
 
What Was Once Goldschmidt
 Mr. Goldschmidt, whose shop at 1117 Madison Avenue, at 84th Street, combined rare books with the illustrated page, the association copy, original prints from Schongauer and Durer to Picasso and Matisse.
The shop was on the ground floor of a modern building. It was about 20 feet wide and about 60 feet deep. The books were behind glass cases and numerous framed prints or drawings, which ranged from the 16th to the 20th century, covered the walls.
"There were always over 100 items on display," Mr. Goldschmidt said, "whether we had an exhibition or not. This was very important to the graduate students and younger people who could not buy anything but who could wander around the shop."
Mr. Goldschmidt said that since World War II the number of major rare-book dealers in the New York area has fallen from about 50 well-stocked shops to about 20.
 
Emphasis on 'Star Items'
 "Keeping a good, diversified stock has become too expensive," Mr. Goldschmidt said. "The economics have killed the scholarly antiquarian bookseller. The shops that have fared best are those that have the star items."
"The auction houses have taken over many of the functions we used to have," he said, seated in his apartment overlooking Riverside Drive, "and being large and aggressive organizations they have done just about everything we hoped they wouldn't do. They go after collections, do appraisals and hire well-respected scholars to handle their stock."
The smaller shops, which specialize in limited numbers of books, can frequently only be visited by appointment and often do not sell lower-priced volumes that would appeal to collectors of more modest means or those who were first stepping into the market.
"If you want to become a collector there are no longer many stores where you can just drop in and browse," Mr. Wyer said. "The loss of the open rare-book shop is tragic. A whole culture has disappeared."

GRAPHIC: Photo: T. Peter Kraus, owner of Ursus Rare Books Ltd., one of New York's few remaining rare-book stores. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Not All Fun and Games for Sports Commissioners

BYLINE: By CLAIRE SMITH

 BODY:
Being the commissioner of a major sport has to be the greatest job in the world, right? All the games you want to see. Best seat in the house. Travel. World Series. Super Bowl. Slam-dunk contests. Hockey night at the Forum any night you want.
 "That's the fun part," Pete Rozelle, who served as commissioner of the National Football League for 30 years, said with a hearty laugh. "I also remember the litigation, the legislation, the internal problems." 
 Indeed, the officials who run major sports leagues have to be equal parts chief executive officer, public-relations expert, tax-law and antitrust expert, economist, litigator, judge, police officer and, lastly, diplomat within the growing international sports venues.
 So, while the best seats in the house may be enviable to some, the ability to sit in them and also turn in what are often 12-hour workdays, and nights, requires the deft touch of the most gifted juggler.
 
With a Capital 'T'
 "The thing that is so difficult, and definitely never on the agenda, is trouble," Fay Vincent, the commissioner of baseball, said. "It's the constant disruption that cuts across everything. I have no way to evaluate how much of my time will get caught up in that, but it's more than I would ever have imagined."
 Vincent's path in particular has been strewn with rocks. Forced to make the transition on the run because of the death of his predecessor and close friend, Bart Giamatti, in September 1989, Vincent has had to deal with the earthquake-marred 1989 World Series, labor strife that included last spring's lockout, collusion and the investigation and subsequent disciplining of George Steinbrenner.
The year will end with Vincent awaiting release from a hospital in Greenwich, Conn., where he is recovering from a bacterial infection that resulted in the removal of his spleen.
 "In terms of my health, I'm going to be fine," said the 52-year-old Vincent, who hopes to be back to a full schedule by the end of January. "None of this had anything to do with being commissioner. The doctors assure me that the bacteria, which caused an abscess on the spleen, was a fluke. It could have happened to me whether I was commissioner or in retirement in Cape Cod."
John Ziegler, the president of the National Hockey League, cannot relate to having to pull a World Series through an earthquake. But he, too, knows of trouble and the various sorts of demanding issues that confront commissioners daily.
 "This is my 14th year and I can only remember one summer where something crucial or some crisis didn't use up the off season," said Ziegler, who is dean of the chief executives of the four major professional sports.
 
'Some Unique Rigors'
 Paul Tagliabue, who succeeded Rozelle in November 1989, has also learned just how precious a commodity time is. "You have some unique rigors," Tagliabue said. "The number of teams, different ownerships, the issues create intense pressures. Once you're in this job, you realize it may be more difficult and rigorous than one held by a major officer of a large corporation."
 It has not always been that way. When Rozelle succeeded Bert Bell, there were 12 teams in the N.F.L. Now there are 28. "The first couple of years, I visited training camps and had more personal contact with the players," Rozelle said. "Then it got bigger. The high visibility has increased greatly with the general public, the owners, the media. Now, almost everything is done in the glare of the spotlight. But you can't feel sorry for yourself. You cope with it. That's a given."
 It has also been a given that as the sport expands, the central structure has to become more sophisticated.
 In the late 1960's, Larry O'Brien, then the commissioner of the National Basketball Association, heeding an internal study that advised that the small-office approach was outmoded, appointed a deputy commissioner/chief operating officer.
David Stern, the current commissioner, was not available last week to comment on the demands of his position. Russ Granik, who serves as Stern's deputy, said: "The way we're set up, I tend to be involved with the day-to-day running of the league, dealing with public relations, broadcasting, security. David becomes involved when issues become major."
 
Rewarded for Their Troubles
 Each commissioner, of course, receives handsome compensation for his work. Stern last winter received a contract reportedly worth a whopping $25 million for 10 years, plus a $10 million bonus. Tagliabue has a five-year contract that pays him approximately $700,00 a year, and Vincent earns $650,000. Ziegler's compensation has not been reported but is presumed to be less than that of the other executives.
When Ziegler was offered the N.H.L. presidency in 1977, he accepted on the condition that the sport modernize. Thus was Ziegler empowered to participate in labor negotiations and to involve ownership more in league activities.
"We operate by committee," Ziegler said. "I don't chair every one of the approximately 17, but I do chair probably two-thirds." The N.H.L.'s central administration also includes an executive vice president (Brian O'Neill) as well as vice presidents of marketing and public relations (Steve Ryan), broadcasting (Joel Nixon), legal counseling (Gil Stein), finance (Ken Sawyer) and hockey operations (Jim Gregory).
 
Many Hands to Shake
 Granik and Stern have worked together at the N.B.A. for 14 years, both having served as general counsel under O'Brien. Granik said Stern's time was taken up by a great deal of ceremonial and public appearances, including meetings with corporate sponors. "But when we have problems," Granik said, "David doesn't run away from them, he gets involved."
In baseball, Giamatti also delegated, bringing Vincent in as the game's first deputy and chief operating officer in 1989. Steve Greenberg now fills that position and handles nuts-and-bolts issues, like the Steinbrenner investigation.
 As for the N.F.L., Rozelle said that during the latter stages of his administration he also wanted to reorganize. But he was also contemplating retirement and, said the 64-year-old Rozelle, "I didn't want to make changes with a new commissioner coming in."
 Tagliabue did after his first season. Last March, he created several senior positions and upgraded others. The N.F.L. now has an executive vice president and league counsel (Jay Moyer) and a director of club administration and international development (Roger Goodell), as well as vice presidents of broadcasting and productions (Val Pinchbeck) and communications and development (Joe Browne).
 Despite the diversification, it's hard for a commissioner to discharge his obligations to owners, Government officials, sponsors and the public. Dealing with so many constituencies can intrude on private lives.
 "You make a conscious effort when it comes to family, but it doesn't mean you're always successful," said Rozelle. "My wife, children and stepchildren were always understanding. Still, it was difficult to establish a personal life the last 10 years on the job."
 While trying to strike that delicate balance, the commissioners still manage to cram more and more into their schedules. By Tagliabue's estimate, he will attend approximately 18 games this season. Ziegler estimates he sees 35 to 40 hockey games in a season.
 
Making the Rounds
 Vincent, too, has made games and appearances a major part of his routine. Partly paralyzed in both legs because of an injury suffered in college, Vincent nonetheless accomplished in 1990 something none of his seven predecessors did: visiting all the teams' home parks (now 26) in a single season.
 Cognizant that people might believe such demands caused his illness, Vincent said such thoughts are erroneous.
"The doctors first mentioned travel when they were doing their usual crossword puzzle when trying to find a cause," he said. "It was just one of the checklist points, but then they found the abscess, which was the cause of the illness."
 Vincent said he expected to work from his home during January, being "very much involved by telephone." And he said he was eager to hit the road again and vowed to do so when the first pitch is thrown. Tagliabue said he understood fully.
"Our philosophies are similar," the football commissioner said. "We both love the sport. It's important to get out to the games. Yes, it can be relaxing, but it's also a part of doing the job."

(The New York Times; Associated Press)

HEADLINE: ESSAY;
Office Pool, 1991

BYLINE: By William Safire

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Disregard last year; it was not my finest prognosticating hour. I was right about the bust into recession and the sudden German unification, but wrong about everything else.
 Serve me no humble pie; when Dolph Camilli, the Brooklyn Dodger home-run hitter, struck out three times in a row, we in the Ebbets Field bleachers used to nod sagely and say, "He's due." In 1991, I'm due.
 
 1. The Supreme Court will overturn all but one of the following lower-court decisions: (a) Rust v. Sullivan, the gag rule on abortion-rights advice; (b) Board of Ed. v. Dowell, in which school systems are forever run by courts; (c) Int'l Union v. Johnson Controls, permitting companies to discriminate against women for their own protection; (d) Masson v. Malcolm, denying interviewees libel claims for non-verbatim quotation. (Pick the one that the Court affirms and you get to pick the black female strict constructionist to replace Thurgood Marshall.)
 2. The nonfiction blockbuster of the year will be: (a) Clark Clifford's autobiography; (b) Scotty Reston's autobiography; (c) Harry Truman's biography by David McCullough; (d) Suzanne Garment's "The Politics of Scandal," which leads us to (e) "One of Us," Tom Wicker's stunning reassessment of Richard Nixon.
 3. Novel of the year will be: (a) "Prized Possessions" by Avery Corman, about date rape; (b) "You'll Never Eat Lunch in This Town Again" by Julia Phillips, for its ravenous title; (c) "Trial" by Clifford Irving, for its sure-fire megabuck movie sale; (d) "Harlot's Ghost," the longie by Norman Mailer about the C.I.A., for breaking ground as the first $30 spy novel, and (e) John le Carre's "The Secret Pilgrim," the last spy novel.
 4. Winner of the 1991 Nobel Peace Prize will be: (a) Vytautas Landsbergis of Lithuania, for resisting the great Gorbachev crackdown; (b) James Baker, for his last-minute deal with Saddam Hussein; (c) George Bush, for establishing a New World Order by decisively defeating aggression.
 5. The looming issue in the coming Presidential elections will be: (a) who egregiously tried to undercut the President in waging a successful war; (b) who wisely tried to prevent the President from wading into a quagmire; (c) quotas as the paradigm for civil rights overreach; (d) stagpression.
 6. Most surprising winner in World-order War I will be: (a) Iraq, by identifying with the world's losers; (b) Turkey, received into the European Community by a guilty Germany after it shortens the war with a second front; (c) Israel, which gets hit first through Jordan, then lets Palestinians found their East Bank state; (d) the Kurds, whose timely uprising earns them a free Kurdistan at last.
 7. Political fallout from the war in the U.S. will be led by: (a) Democratic investigations into the appeasement of Saddam Hussein before Kuwait; (b) indictments in February of merchants of death who used taxpayer-guaranteed dollars to ship nuclear and chemical weaponry to Iraq, financed through the Atlanta Lavoro bank; (c) an amalgam of defense-cutters shocked at the waste and ineffectiveness of our weaponry revealed in the desert war.
 8. Democratic front-runner will be: (a) Sam Nunn, foremost military second-guesser, chaining himself to the gates of Burning Tree Club in egalitarian protest; (b) Mario Cuomo, talking to the skull of poor Yorick, his campaign manager; (c) Gephardt-san, running for the Cromwellian role as Great Protector; (d) patriotic Gore, green thumb up; (e) General Norman (Ethelred the Unready) Schwartzkopf, matching the slows of McClellan.
 9. The economy in the second half of 1991 will: (a) sink into the worst recession since the last one, with Dow industrials plunging below 2,000; (b) be whipsawed by growing unemployment and inflation, better balance of payments and worse deficits, with the Dow at present levels; (c) be out of the woods and off to a new boom, the stock average breaking through 3,000, in time for the '92 conventions.
 10. Mikhail Gorbachev will be: (a) straddling, improvising, balancing forces as the Indispensable Man; (b) taking "stern measures" the world will see as Stalinism with flair; (c) ousted by Yeltsin-Soubchek-Shevardnadze reformers following public revulsion after a bloody crackdown; (d) ousted by the K.G.B.-Army-Party apparat after refusing to order the army to fire at separatists.
 My picks: 1d, 2e, 3c, 4a, 5a, 6d, 7b, 8b, 9c, 10c. Gotta play to criticize. Occasionally bet your long-shot hopes; it will lower your average but raise your sights.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF: EUROPEANS PLAN A SPECIAL SESSION ON CRISIS IN GULF;
White House's View

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
An Administration official said tonight that the White House was not alarmed by the prospect of the European Community meeting and indicated that the Bush Administration had assurances from European Community nations that they would not undertake any diplomatic initiatives that departed from the Persian Gulf policy of the United States and the United Nations.
The official said the United States had no specific agreement with the community to act on Washington's behalf. But he indicated that with Jan. 3 approaching quickly, the United States would welcome a friendly third party's involvement in the quest for a peaceful settlement of the crisis. President Bush has proposed Jan. 3 as the latest reasonable date for direct talks with Iraq. 
 "Our position is as it was: no negotiations," the official said. "Having said that, anything that can facilitate a peaceful solution would obviously be a welcome step. Any such move or undertaking by the E.C. should be geared toward confirming for Saddam the seriousness of the situation, that the international community is aligned, that the international community is ready to work in unison both diplomatically and militarily against him, and that he has to take this very seriously."
In that context, the official said, a European initiative "could be beneficial." He said the European Community carried "economic and financial clout as an institution that could drive home the message to Saddam in a way that so far we seem to have failed to do through other means."
"But the understanding has to be as always that the European Community members as individual countries, and as a group through NATO, are supportive of United States policy and of the U.N. resolutions," the official said.
The official added, "Irrespective of the shape that this new move takes, or its venue, we're confident that they're not going to do something as individuals or as a group that goes against United States policy."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
World Markets Begin 1991 Pressed by Problems of 1990

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
World stock markets begin 1991 facing the threat of a war in the Persian Gulf, recessions or economic slowdowns around the world, and dismal earnings -- the same factors that drove stock markets down sharply in 1990. Yet many market watchers expect a better year than the one ending today.
In 1990, these pressures resulted in a worse year for stocks than even 1987, when stock markets crashed in October. 
 The Financial Times World index for 24 markets, excluding the United States, dropped 30.8 percent in local currency in 1990, compared with a 1.7 percent dip by the end of 1987. For American investors, the sharp drop in the dollar cut the 1990 decline to 24.6 percent. But in 1987, the dollar's fall and a smaller decline in equities resulted in a 24.2 percent gain. While stock markets are open today in the United States, Britain and Cananda, most markets ended the year on Friday.
Many forecasts for 1991 are based on what could turn out to be optimistic assumptions: a quick recovery from a recession in the United States, a peaking of interest rates worldwide, no war or a quick war in the Persian Gulf and a cap on oil prices. If these assumptions do not hold, markets could be in for another troubling year.
The abrupt resignation earlier this month of the Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze, provided a vivid reminder of the fragility of President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's experiment in the Soviet Union. An unraveling there -- or more problems in Eastern Europe -- could also send stock markets tumbling, especially in Germany and Europe.
Striking a pessimistic note, Madhav Dhar, global strategist for Morgan Stanley & Company in New York, said: "The economic, political and corporate outlook is not right. The markets are not reacting as if the news is gloomy. We think the fundamental troubles around the world are not fully discounted in the prices. We think the markets are poised to respond to good news and that is a mistake."
Sander Cseh of Cseh International in Philadelphia was more optimistic. "We could have a lot of good things happening, even if there is a war," he said. "If it is a short war, you are going to have a huge decline in oil prices, and that will benefit cyclical companies. If gasoline prices drop, consumers will be more optimistic."
The uncertainties in the outlook for 1991 revolve around the assumptions for the pace of the slowdowns in Europe and Japan, the length and depth of the recessions in the United States and elsewhere, and the course of interest rates. Stock analysts also differ over the impact of a capital shortage around the world as both the Japanese and the Germans keep money at home that used to buoy markets abroad.
But analysts seem to agree that the motivation for buying equities in 1991 is the attractive values one can find, not the prospect of strong earnings. "The most important factor in the market in 1991 is the valuations," said Guy Rigden, director of strategy at UBS Phillips & Drew in London.
In addition, many analysts think that stock markets have further to fall because they have rebounded since the lows reached last fall.
While there is a split on the outlook for 1991, there is general agreement, in hindsight, on much of what went wrong in 1990, a year that began with some forecasts of 15 percent increases in markets.
Not only did the experts not foresee the invasion of Kuwait, but they missed the quickness of the economic decline and the high level at which interest rates have remained. Even before Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, major stock markets around the world were in a swoon or ready for one because of the high interest rates and the deterioration of the economy over all.
 
'A Match Touched to Tinder'
 "I believe that the Aug. 2 hostilities were simply a match touched to tinder that was already dry," said Lawrence W. Harris 3d, director of of Lombard Odier International in London.
In the end, 1990 was a year of new highs and new lows for many stock markets. In Japan, the Nikkei 225 index of leading stocks plunged 28 percent from a record high of 38,915.87 at the end of 1989 to 28,002.07 by April 2. After the Iraqi invasion it slumped to below 20,000 before closing Friday for the year at 23,848.71, down 38.7 percent for the year.
New Zealand's Barclay's index is at its lowest level in more than six years. In Australia, the All Ordinaries dropped to a 30-month low. And in Norway, despite the benefit its oil-rich economy receives from higher prices, the All Share index fell from a record high on the day of the Iraqi invasion to a string of 1990 lows at the end of the year.
One surprise is that two countries now thought to be in recessions -- the United States and Britain -- have performed relatively well. For the year through Friday, the Financial Times index for Wall Street is down 7.2 percent, the sixth-best performance of the 24 markets in local currencies. Britain's market is No. 4 in dollar terms, up 4.6 percent because the strong pound wiped out the market's local losses when results were turned to dollars.
 
Interest Rates
 A main factor in the performance of markets last year was interest rates, which are likely to have a strong effect next year, too. In Europe, the interest rate impact is heightened by the discipline of the European Monetary System, which links the currencies of 10 countries. In effect, it has forced Europe to match the high-interest-rate policy of Germany, even though the Government in Britain, where the economy is in a recession, and the Governments in France and Italy, where the economies are slowing down, would like to cut rates to lessen the economic damage.
The Milan stock market, which had been the pick of analysts last spring, wound up falling 28.8 percent in local terms, according to the Financial Times index, the fourth-worst performance of the year. In dollar terms, the loss was reduced to 20 percent. For 1991, many economists predict Italy will have one of the slowest-growing economies in Europe.
The French market, another popular one earlier in the year, also fell sharply as France's economy began to slow and it felt the backlash of the Persian Gulf crisis. The Paris Bourse was down 25.7 percent for the year in local terms and 15.7 percent in dollars, according to the Financial Times Index.
In Britain, despite the emerging recession, the market was one of the best performers of the year for dollar investors, with a rise of 4.6 percent. But for locals it was not that bad either, declining 12.2 percent in pounds, the eighth-best performance in the index. A key reason the market suffered less than others was North Sea oil production, which insulated Britain and its stock market from the impact of the sharp jump in oil prices.
Britain is also one of the markets of choice for many managers next year, partly because of the good values in the market.
 
Shipping Stocks Hurt
 Norway is another oil exporter whose stock market fared better than others. Unlike Britain's, Norway's market had a sharp decline after the invasion because the crisis hurt shipping stocks. The British market had most of its decline before the invasion because of its faltering economy and high interest rates. Norway's market finished fifth in local currency and dollars, declining 8.9 percent kroner and increasing 1.9 percent in dollar terms.
The Frankfurt market soared to a record high in March amid euphoria about Eastern Europe, only to succumb to the worries about the cost of German unificiation later in the spring.
Rallies after that were halted when economic worries came to the fore and Iraq invaded Kuwait. For the year, the market is down 20.1 percent in marks. The strong mark halved that loss to 9.7 percent for dollar investors.
Analysts are divided on the outlook for 1991, with some still worried about the negative consequences for equities of the cost of German unification and the potential for even another rise in German interest rates. But the longer-term benefits of German unification and the fact that the economy is expected to be one of the strongest performers in Europe make it attractive to some.
Tokyo's stock market was the worst performing stock market in the Financial Times index, falling 40.4 percent in yen terms, a notch worse than the 39.2 percent plunge in New Zealand's stock market.
The Japanese market's fall from a record high at the end of 1989 was swift, as the market was undermined by a weakening yen, rising interest rates, higher oil prices and politicial uncertainty.
As with Germany, there is a divided opinion over the Japanese outlook. Many managers prefer to stay away because of the high cost of Japanese stocks. But others, including Merrill Lynch, are betting that interest rates in Japan may finally be at their peak, given recent data showing the economy to be slowing.
 
Mexico Earns the Top Rank
 Mexico's market rose 97 percent in pesos and 79.2 percent in dollar terms, giving it the top ranking for the third year in a row. The Government's success in dealing with its debt and its effort to open the economy have made the stock market a great bet.
Hong Kong eked out a gain of 4.9 percent in local terms and 5 percent in dollars, making it the second- and third-ranked market, respectively. Its performance was based on the strong rally in the first half that finally brought it back from the low levels it hit after the massacre in Tiananmen Square in Beijing in June 1989.
"Hong Kong is marching to a different tune than to what is going on in the rest of the world," said Mr. Harris of Lombard Odier Internatinal. The market, he added, is less affected by high interest rates and the Persian Gulf crisis than most other stock markets. Analysts still like it because its stocks are cheap and they are a little less worried about the impact from the takeover by China in 1997.
Austria, one of the smallest European markets, also did well relatively, falling only 4.4 percent in schillings and rising 7.7 percent in dollars, making it the second-best performer of the year. But part of the reason it held up so well was the cushion it gained from a huge rally at the beginning of the year on the back of Eastern Europe euphoria. While the German market was up just 5 percent in the first six months of the year, Austria's market jumped almost 33 percent.

HEADLINE: David Saperstein, 89, a Lawyer Who Helped to Create the S.E.C.

BYLINE: By WOLFGANG SAXON

 BODY:
David Saperstein, a corporate lawyer who helped put together the Securities Exchange Commission and then fought questionable market practices as one of its top officials, died on Friday at Palisades General Hospital in North Bergen, N.J. He was 89 years old and lived in Weehawken.
Taking office at the height of the country's economic crisis, President Franklin D. Roosevelt demanded prompt action. The job to regulate the banks and stock exchanges fell to the Senate Committee on Banking and Currency and its counsel, Ferdinand Pecora, named as his assistant, Mr. Saperstein, a young lawyer with experience in banking cases and municipal corporations. 
The committee set out to write the Securities Exchange Act of 1934 that created the commission. Mr. Saperstein helped draft the legislation and was at the center of the hearings and investigations that occupied the Senators in 1933.
Mr. Saperstein's role for the Senate committee grew as the committee stepped up its inquiries into banking and stock market dealings. He supervised the committee staff members in collecting information until he joined the S.E.C. in 1934 as its trading and exchange division director.
 
Watched Trading Activities
 On Oct. 1 of that year, the S.E.C. took full control of the securities markets. To put bite into S.E.C. regulations, Mr. Pecora chose Mr. Saperstein for the broad knowledge he had gained of the stock exchanges and their ways. Mr. Saperstein's division was charged with watching trading activities on the markets nationwide. His interpretations of S.E.C. rules and regulations deeply affected those activities.
By 1935, Mr. Saperstein posted a "rogues' gallery" of some 30,000 names to weed out fake stock salesmen and get-rich-quick promoters accused of preying on unwitting clients during the boom days. His division examined applications for registration as securities traders and held hearings on those suspected of shady practices.
It also drafted the rules for over-the-counter markets and, in general, was a linchpin of the S.E.C. apparatus.
In 1937, Mr. Saperstein was prominently mentioned as a candidate for Commissioner. But he said consideration for his family convinced him to leave Government service, and he returned to private practice.
He continued as a corporate lawyer until he retired in 1982 from what was then the firm of Silver, Saperstein, Barnett & Solomon in Manhattan.
Mr. Saperstein is survived by his wife, the former Louise Lederer; a daughter, Sue Bernstein of Torrance, Calif.; a son, Roy, of Harbor City, Calif.; a sister, Shirley Weinstein of Chicago; six grandchildren, and two great-grandchildren.

HEADLINE: Joseph M. Long, 78; Philanthropist Led Chain of Drug Stores

BYLINE: By MARVINE HOWE

 BODY:
Joseph M. Long, the chairman and co-founder of Longs Drug Stores and a prominent philanthropist, died Thursday at the John Muir Medical Center in Walnut Creek, Calif., near San Francisco. He was 78 years old and lived in nearby Danville.
He died of a brain hemorrhage, a company spokesman said. 
 With his brother Thomas, Mr. Long founded the retail drug company in Oakland, Calif., in 1938. The Longs chain, now based in Walnut Creek, employs 14,000 people in 257 retail drug stores in six Western states and did $2 billion in business last year.
Several years ago, Mr. Long's son Robert was named president and chief executive officer of the company, but the father continued to oversee operations. Thomas Long is retired but continues as a member of the board.
Joseph Long was also a former director of Safeway Stores, Western Department Stores, Cutter Laboratories and several other companies.
 
Education and Environment
 An advocate of educational and environmental causes, Mr. Long donated millions of dollars to the University of California and wildlife institutions. Among his contributions was $3.2 million to the University of California at San Francisco Medical Center.
He also established the Foundation for Excellence at the School of Pharmacy at the University of California at San Francisco and set up a marine laboratory at the University of California at Santa Cruz.
Mr. Long served as chairman and co-founder of the California State Parks Foundation and was co-founder and president of the California Waterfowl Association.
A native of Covelo, Calif., he graduated in 1933 from the University of California at Berkeley and stayed on for three years of graduate studies in economics and public administration.
He is survived by two sons: Robert M. of Lafayette, Calif., and Kirk, who lives in Spain; two daughters, Nancy Fink of Berkeley and Marion Souyoultzis of Piedmont, Calif.; two brothers, Thomas J. of Alamo and Ralph of Millbrae, Calif.; two sisters, Sarah Sweeney of El Sobrante, Calif., and Leta Lowry of Walnut Creek, and nine grandchildren.
