HEADLINE: ON YOUR OWN;
Even Weekend Skiers Are Off to the Races

BYLINE: By JANET NELSON

 BODY:
RECREATIONAL ski racing is exploding, not only in the number of skiers running gates, but also in the quantity and variety of races available. Practically on any weekend skiers can find downhill races, either as part of a sponsored series or as individual events staged by ski areas for clubs, corporations or just for fun. There are even national programs specifically for bartenders and chefs.
"As people get to be better skiers, they want more challenge," said Steve Lathrop, an organizer of the Corporate Challenge team races and former member of the United States Ski Team. "At the same time, with the gas prices and economic problems, people are skiing closer to home, which means they are on smaller hills. When that gets boring, they set up some poles to create more challenge." 
 Organized ski racing for amateurs began 22 years ago when the National Standard Race (Nastar) was created by Ski magazine. Through a handicapping system, skiers of all ages and abilities could compete, win medals and compare themselves to other skiers, as well as measure their own improvement from race to race.
When Nastar started in 1968, eight ski areas held races and about 2,000 skiers competed. Last season 180 ski areas held Nastar races for more than 250,000 adults and children. "Every year we set new growth goals," said Lisa Kaye, director of public relations for World Wide Ski Corporation, which runs the Nastar program. "And every year, we exceed those goals."
One factor that has contributed to the growth of recreational racing is sponsor financing of elaborate races and national finals for top finishers. Usually there is an entry fee for competitors that may include lift tickets, parties and merchandise.
"A lot of sponsors are targeting programs to recreactional racers because they are people who spend money," said Cindy Kaiser-Stark, who runs the Ski Club Challenge, a nationwide series for club teams.
 Prizes and awards as well as expense-paid trips to national finals inspire many amateurs to train every weekend and attend racing clinics and camps. This makes the events more exciting to watch with time differentials in hundredths of seconds, but also eliminates less-skilled and less-dedicated skiers from the top spots.
"Over the years, the quality of the skiers has been improving," said Lathrop. "Sometimes recreational skiers get on a course and they don't do so well. This humbles them and they try to improve their skiing."
Here are the major race series and events with telephone contacts for schedules, locations, eligibility requirements and entry forms.
Nastar
Races are held at 180 ski areas, usually on a daily basis. Entry fees range from $3 to $5. Racers are handicapped according to age, sex and ability for gold, silver and bronze medals. Within each region, top finalists who entered at least three races during the season receive expense-paid trips to the Miller Lite Nastar finals, Dec. 11-15, 1991, at Snowbird, Utah. Contact: (303) 925-7864.
United States Ski Association
Sponsors a citizen racing program for members of the association in which ski clubs and other groups hold races through the winter. Entry fees are about $10; anyone can join the U.S.S.A. for $25 annually. Each region sends top racers at their own expense to the National Citizen's Championships at Copper Mountain, Colo., April 3-6. Contact: (801) 649-9090.
Plymouth All-American Series
Races are held at 40 resorts in six regions. The entry fee of $6 is waived for members of the United States Recreational Ski Association, whose membership is open to anyone for an annual fee of $34, or $40 in southern California. After each race, competitors are divided into three categories according to ability. The top three men and three women from each region earn trips to the national finals, April 3-7 in Keystone, Colo. Contact: (714) 634-1050.
Jeep Ski Club Challenge
Ski clubs enter teams of three men and three women in any of the six races held across the country. Entry fee is $60 a team. The winning team in each race earns trips to the nationals at Steamboat, Colo., April 4-7. Contact: (516) 536-2130.
Michelob Light
Corporate Challenge
Held at 40 eastern ski areas, these races for corporate teams are either a series of midweek night races or three days of races over a weekend. Entry fees range from $125 for the series to $175 for the weekends and include lift tickets and instruction. Winning teams go to the eastern championships at Attitash, N.H., March 21-23, and from there to the Hertz Nationals at Snowbird, Utah, April 11-14. Contact: (617) 354-4111.
The Ski Classic
Corporate team events for western skiers, these races are held at more than 30 ski areas in the Rockies and far west. Entry fees are about $150 to $200 a team. The teams that win regional events also go to the Hertz Nationals at Snowbird, Utah, from April 11-14. Contact: (415) 243-0465.
Winter World Corporate Games
Individuals representing themselves or an organization enter skiing and other winter competitions at Steamboat, Colo., Jan. 12-19. Registration fee is $100 plus $10 per event. Contact: (808) 536-1990.
 Alpine Masters
Races sanctioned by the United States Ski Association are open to skiers in age categories from 25 to 80 and older. Entry fees range from $10 to $25. Winners from 10 regions compete in the national championships at Vail/Beaver Creek, Colo., March 24-29. Individuals may also qualify for the F.I.S. International Masters at Winter Park, Colo., March 20-22. Contact: (801) 649-9090.
Senior Winter Games
Modeled on the Olympic format, this event is a pilot for a semi-annual competition. Anyone older than 55 may enter the winter games this year at Ski Incline, Calif., Feb. 26-March 3, for a fee of about $40 per event. Contact: (800) 468-2463.
Senior Winter Games
At the Summit
This winter competition started 11 years ago for Colorado residents older than 55. Entry fee is $15 for all events, Feb. 9-11, in Breckenridge, Colo. Contact: (303) 668-5486.
Jimmie Heuga's
Mazda Ski Express
This 19-state, 31-event charity series for three-person coed teams raises money for the Jimmie Heuga Center, a nonprofit research facility that helps people with Multiple Sclerosis and other physical challenges. Contact: (800) 248-0459.
